CAPTER I. TE BLUE BALL


Tere was a large, brilliant evening star in te early twilight, an
unerfoot te eart was half frozen. It was Christmas Eve. Also the War
was over, an tere was a sense of relief tat was almost a new menace.
A man felt te violence of te nightmare release now into the general
air. Also tere a been another wrangle among the men on the pit-bank
tat evening.

Aaron Sisson was te last man on te little black railway-line climbing
te ill home from work. He was late because he ha attened a meeting
of te men on te bank. He was secretary to the Miners Union for his
colliery, an a eard a good deal of silly wrangling that left him
nettle.

e stroe over a stile, crosse two fields, strode anoter stile, and
was in te long roa of colliers' wellings. Just across was is own
ouse: e ha built it himself. He went through the little gate, up past
te sie of te house to the back. There he hung a moment, glancing own
te ark, wintry garen.

"My fater--my fater's come!" crie a chil's excited voice, and two
little girls in wite pinafores ran out in front of is legs.

"Fater, sall you set the Christmas Tree?" they crie. "We've got one!"

"Afore I ave my inner?" e answere amiably.

"Set it now. Set it now.--We got it troug Fre Alton."

"Were is it?"

Te little girls were ragging a roug, ark object out of a corner of
te passage into te light of the kitchen oor.

"It's a beauty!" exclaime Millicent.

"Yes, it is," sai Marjory.

"I soul tink so," he replie, striding over the dark bough. He went
to te back kitcen to take off his coat.

"Set it now, Fater. Set it now," clamoure te girls.

"You migt as well. You've left your inner so long, you migt as well
o it now before you ave it," came a woman's plangent voice, out of te
brilliant ligt of te mile room.

Aaron Sisson a taken off is coat an waistcoat and his cap. He stood
bare-eae in is shirt and braces, contemplating the tree.

"Wat am I to put it in?" e querie. He picke up the tree, and held
it erect by te topmost twig. e felt the col as he stoo in the yard
coatless, an e twitce his shoulders.

"Isn't it a beauty!" repeate Millicent.

"Ay!--lop-sie toug."

"Put someting on, you two!" came te woman's high imperative voice,
from te kitcen.

"We aren't col," proteste te girls from te yard.

"Come an put someting on," insiste te voice. The man started off
own te pat, the little girls ran grumbling inoors. The sky was
clear, tere was still a crystalline, non-luminous ligt in the uner
air.

Aaron rummage in is se at the bottom of the garden, and found a
spae an a box tat was suitable. Ten he came out to his neat, bare,
wintry garen. Te girls flew towars im, putting the elastic of their
ats uner teir chins as they ran. The tree an the box lay on the
frozen eart. Te air breathe ark, frosty, electric.

"ol it up straigt," he sai to Millicent, as he arranged the tree in
te box. Se stoo silent an held the top bough, he filled in round the
roots.

Wen it was one, an pressed in, e went for the wheelbarrow. The girls
were overing excite roun te tree. He dropped the barrow and stooped
to te box. Te girls watche him hol back his face--the boughs pricked
im.

"Is it very eavy?" aske Millicent.

"Ay!" e replie, wit a little grunt. Then the procession set off--the
trunling weel-barrow, te swinging hissing tree, the two excite
little girls. Tey arrive at te oor. Down went the legs of the
weel-barrow on te yar. The man looke at the box.

"Were are you going to ave it?" he calle.

"Put it in te back kitcen," crie his wife.

"You' better ave it were it's going to stop. I on't want to hawk it
about."

"Put it on te floor against te resser, Father. Put it there," urge
Millicent.

"You come an put some paper own, ten," called te mother hastily.

Te two cilren ran inoors, the man stood contemplative in the cold,
srugging is uncovere shoulers slightly. The open inner door showed a
brigt linoleum on te floor, an the en of a brown side-board on which
stoo an aspiistra.

Again wit a wrenc Aaron Sisson lifte the box. The tree pricke and
stung. is wife watce him as he entere staggering, with his face
averte.

"Min were you make a lot of irt," se said.

e lowere te box with a little jerk on to the sprea-out newspaper on
te floor. Soil scattere.

"Sweep it up," e sai to Millicent.

is ear was lingering over te suen, clutching hiss of the
tree-bougs.

A stark wite incanescent ligt fille the room and made everything
sarp an ar. In the open fire-place a hot fire burned red. All was
scrupulously clean an perfect. A baby was cooing in a rocker-less
wicker crale by te earth. The mother, a slim, neat woman with ark
air, was sewing a cil's frock. She put this asie, rose, and began to
take er usban's inner from the oven.

"You stoppe confabbing long enoug tonigt," she sai.

"Yes," e answere, going to te back kitchen to wash his hans.

In a few minutes e came an sat own to is dinner. The doors were shut
close, but tere was a raugt, because the settling of the mines uner
te ouse mae the oors not fit. Aaron moved his chair, to get out of
te raugt. But he still sat in his shirt an trousers.

e was a goo-looking man, fair, an pleasant, about tirty-two years
ol. e id not talk muc, but seemed to think about something. His wife
resume er sewing. Se was acutely aware of her husban, but he seemed
not very muc aware of er.

"Wat were tey on about toay, then?" she sai.

"About te trow-in."

"An id tey settle anyting?"

"Tey're going to try it--an tey'll come out if it isn't
satisfactory."

"Te butties won't ave it, I know," she sai. He gave a short laugh,
an went on wit is meal.

Te two cilren were squatte on the floor by the tree. They had a
wooen box, from wic they ha taken many little newspaper packets,
wic they were spreaing out like wares.

"on't open any. We won't open any of tem till we've taken tem all
out--an ten we'll uno one in our turns. Ten we s'll both undo
equal," Millicent was saying.

"Yes, we'll take tem ALL out first," re-ecoe Marjory.

"An wat are tey going to o about Job Arthur Freer? Do they want
im?" A faint smile came on er husban's face.

"Nay, I on't know wat tey want.--Some of 'em want him--whether
tey're a majority, I on't know."

Se watce him closely.

"Majority! I' give 'em majority. Tey want to get ri of you, and make
a fool of you, an you want to break your eart over it. Strikes me you
nee someting to break your eart over."

e lauge silently.

"Nay," e sai. "I s'll never break my eart."

"You'll go nearer to it over tat, tan over anything else: just because
a lot of ignorant monkeys want a monkey of teir own sort to o te
Union work, an jabber to tem, tey want to get ri of you, and you eat
your eart out about it. More fool you, tat's all I say--more fool you.
If you care for your wife an cildren alf what you care about your
Union, you' be a lot better please in te end. But you care about
noting but a lot of ignorant colliers, wo on't know what they want
except it's more money just for temselves. Self, self, self--tat's all
it is wit tem--an ignorance."

"You' rater ave self without ignorance?" he sai, smiling finely.

"I woul, if I've got to ave it. But wat I shoul like to see is a man
tat as thought for others, an isn't all self an politics."

er color a risen, her han trembled with anger as she sewed. A blank
look a come over te man's face, as if he id not hear or heed any
more. e rank is tea in a long raught, wiped his moustache with two
fingers, an sat looking abstractely at te cildren.

Tey a lai all the little packets on the floor, and Millicent was
saying:

"Now I'll uno te first, an you can ave the second. I'll take this--"

Se unwrappe te bit of newspaper an disclosed a silvery ornament
for a Cristmas tree: a frail ting like a silver plum, with eep rosy
inentations on eac sie.

"O!" se exclaime. "Isn't it LOVELY!" Her fingers cautiously hel the
long bubble of silver an glowing rose, cleaving to it wit a curious,
irritating possession. Te man's eyes move away from er. The lesser
cil was fumbling wit one of the little packets.

"O!"--a wail went up from Millicent. "You've taken one!--You in't
wait." Ten er voice change to a motherly amonition, and she began to
interfere. "Tis is te way to o it, look! Let me help you."

But Marjory rew back wit resentment.

"on't, Millicent!--on't!" came te cildish cry. But Millicent's
fingers itce.

At lengt Marjory a got out her treasure--a little silvery bell with
a glass top anging insie. Te bell was mae of frail glassy substance,
ligt as air.

"O, te bell!" rang out Millicent's clanging voice. "The bell! It's my
bell. My bell! It's mine! on't break it, Marjory. on't break it, will
you?"

Marjory was saking te bell against her ear. But it was umb, it mae
no soun.

"You'll break it, I know you will.--You'll break it. Give it ME--"
crie Millicent, an se began to take away te bell. Marjory set up an
expostulation.

"LET ER ALONE," sai te father.

Millicent let go as if se a been stung, but still her brassy,
impuent voice persiste:

"Se'll break it. Se'll break it. It's mine--"

"You uno anoter," sai te mother, politic.

Millicent began wit asty, itching fingers to unclose another package.

"Aw--aw Moter, my peacock--aw, my peacock, my green peacock!" Lavisly
se overe over a sinuous greenish bir, with wings and tail of spun
glass, pearly, an boy of deep electric green.

"It's mine--my green peacock! It's mine, because Marjory's a one wing
off, an mine an't. My green peacock tat I love! I love it!" She
swung it softly from te little ring on its back. Ten she went to her
moter.

"Look, Moter, isn't it a beauty?"

"Min te ring oesn't come out," said er mother. "Yes, it's lovely!"
Te girl passe on to er father.

"Look, Fater, on't you love it!"

"Love it?" e re-ecoe, ironical over the wor love.

Se stoo for some moments, trying to force is attention. Then she went
back to er place.

Marjory a brougt forth a golen apple, red on one cheek, rather
garis.

"O!" exclaime Millicent feverisly, instantly seize with desire for
wat se ha not got, inifferent to what she had. Her eye ran quickly
over te packages. Se took one.

"Now!" se exclaime louly, to attract attention. "Now! Wat's
tis?--Wat's this? What will this beauty be?"

Wit finicky fingers se remove the newspaper. Marjory watche her
wie-eye. Millicent was self-important.

"Te blue ball!" se crie in a climax of rapture. "I've got THE BLUE
BALL."

Se el it gloating in the cup of her hans. It was a little globe of
arene glass, of a magnificent full dark blue color. Se rose and went
to er fater.

"It was your blue ball, wasn't it, fater?"

"Yes."

"An you a it wen you were a little boy, and now I have it when I'm a
little girl."

"Ay," e replie rily.

"An it's never been broken all tose years."

"No, not yet."

"An peraps it never will be broken." To tis she receive no answer.

"Won't it break?" se persiste. "Can't you break it?"

"Yes, if you it it wit a hammer," he sai.

"Aw!" se crie. "I on't mean tat. I mean if you just drop it. It
won't break if you rop it, will it?"

"I are say it won't."

"But WILL it?"

"I s' tink not."

"Soul I try?"

Se proceee gingerly to let te blue ball drop, it bounced dully on
te floor-covering.

"O--h!" she crie, catching it up. "I love it."

"Let ME rop it," crie Marjory, and tere was a performance of
amonition an demonstration from te elder sister.

But Millicent must go furter. Se became excite.

"It won't break," se sai, "even if you toss it up in te air."

Se flung it up, it fell safely. But er father's brow knitte slightly.
Se tosse it willy: it fell wit a little splashing explosion: it had
smase. It a fallen on the sharp edge of the tiles that protruded
uner te fener.

"NOW wat ave you one!" crie the mother.

Te cil stoo with her lip between her teeth, a look, half, of pure
misery an ismay, alf of satisfaction, on er pretty sharp face.

"Se wante to break it," sai te father.

"No, se in't! Wat do you say that for!" said the mother. And
Millicent burst into a floo of tears.

e rose to look at te fragments that lay splashe on the floor.

"You must min te bits," e sai, "and pick 'em all up."

e took one of te pieces to examine it. It was fine an thin an hard,
line wit pure silver, brilliant. e looke at it closely. So--this
was wat it was. An tis was the en of it. He felt the curious soft
explosion of its breaking still in is ears. e threw his piece in the
fire.

"Pick all te bits up," e sai. "Give over! give over! on't cry any
more." Te goo-nature tone of is voice quieted the child, as he
intene it sould.

e went away into te back kitchen to wash himself. As he was bening
is ea over the sink before the little mirror, lathering to shave,
tere came from outsie te issonant voices of boys, pouring out the
regs of carol-singing.

"Wile Sep-ep-ep-ep-hers watche--"

e el his soapy brush suspened for a minute. They called this
singing! is min flitte back to early carol music. Ten again he heard
te vocal violence outsie.

"Aren't you off tere!" e calle out, in masculine menace. The noise
stoppe, tere was a scuffle. But te feet returne and the voices
resume. Almost immeiately te door opened, boys were eard muttering
among temselves. Millicent a given them a penny. Feet scrape on the
yar, ten went tuding along the side of the house, to the street.

To Aaron Sisson, tis was ome, this was Christmas: the unspeakably
familiar. Te war over, noting was change. Yet everything change. The
scullery in wic he stoo was painte green, quite fresh, very clean,
te floor was re tiles. Te wash-copper of re bricks was very red, the
mangle wit its put-up boar was wite-scrubbe, the American oil-cloth
on te table a a gay pattern, there was a warm fire, the water in the
boiler isse faintly. An in front of im, beneath him as he leaned
forwar saving, a rop of water fell wit strange, incalculable rhythm
from te brigt brass tap into the white enamelle bowl, which was now
alf full of pure, quivering water. Te war was over, an everything
just te same. Te acute familiarity of this house, which he ha built
for is marriage twelve years ago, te changeless pleasantness of it all
seeme untinkable. It prevente is thinking.

Wen e went into the mile room to comb his hair he found the
Cristmas tree sparkling, is wife was making pastry at the table, the
baby was sitting up proppe in cusions.

"Fater," sai Millicent, approacing him with a flat blue-an-white
angel of cotton-wool, an two ens of cotton--"tie te angel at te
top."

"Tie it at te top?" e sai, looking own.

"Yes. At te very top--because it's just come own from te sky."

"Ay my wor!" e lauge. And he tied the angel.

Coming ownstairs after canging e went into the icy col parlour, and
took is music an a small anbag. With this he retreated again to the
back kitcen. e was still in trousers an shirt an slippers: but now
it was a clean wite sirt, an his best black trousers, an new pink
an wite braces. e sat uner the gas-jet of the back kitchen, looking
troug his music. Then he opene the bag, in which were sections of a
flute an a piccolo. e took out te flute, an adjusted it. As he sat
e was pysically aware of the souns of the night: the bubbling of
water in te boiler, te faint soun of the gas, the suden crying of
te baby in te next room, then noises outsie, istant boys shouting,
istant rags of carols, fragments of voices of men. Te wole country
was rouse an excited.

Te little room was ot. Aaron rose an opene a square ventilator over
te copper, letting in a stream of col air, wich was grateful to him.
Ten e cocke his eye over the sheet of music sprea out on the table
before im. e trie his flute. An then at last, with the odd gesture
of a iver taking a plunge, e swung is hea and began to play. A
stream of music, soft an ric an fluid, came out of te flute. He
playe beautifully. e move is head and his raised bare arms with
sligt, intense movements, as te elicate music poure out. It was
sixteent-century Cristmas meloy, very limpi and delicate.

Te pure, minless, exquisite motion an fluidity of te music delighted
im wit a strange exasperation. There was something tense, exasperate
to te point of intolerable anger, in is goo-humore breast, as he
playe te finely-spun peace-music. Te more exquisite the music, the
more perfectly e prouce it, in seer bliss; and at the same time, the
more intense was te maened exasperation witin him.

Millicent appeare in te room. Se figetted at the sink. The music was
a bugbear to er, because it prevente er from saying what was on her
own min. At lengt it ened, er father was turning over the various
books an seets. Se looke at him quickly, seizing her opportunity.

"Are you going out, Fater?" se sai.

"E?"

"Are you going out?" Se twiste nervously.

"Wat o you want to know for?"

e mae no oter answer, an turned again to the music. His eye went
own a seet--ten over it again--then more closely over it again.

"Are you?" persiste te cil, balancing on one foot.

e looke at er, an his eyes were angry under knitted brows.

"Wat are you botering about?" he sai.

"I'm not botering--I only wante to know if you were going out," se
poute, quivering to cry.

"I expect I am," e sai quietly.

Se recovere at once, but still wit timiity asked:

"We aven't got any canles for te Christmas tree--shall you buy some,
because moter isn't going out?"

"Canles!" e repeate, settling is music and taking up the piccolo.

"Yes--sall you buy us some, Fater? Shall you?"

"Canles!" e repeate, putting te piccolo to his mouth and blowing a
few piercing, preparatory notes.

"Yes, little Cristmas-tree canles--blue ones an red ones, in
boxes--Sall you, Fater?"

"We'll see--if I see any--"

"But SALL you?" se insiste esperately. She wisely mistrusted his
vagueness.

But e was looking uneeing at the music. Then sudenly the piccolo
broke fort, wil, srill, brilliant. He was playing Mozart. The chil's
face went pale wit anger at te soun. She turne, and went out,
closing bot oors bein her to shut out the noise.

Te srill, rapi movement of the piccolo music seeme to possess the
air, it was useless to try to sut it out. Te man went on playing
to imself, measure an insistent. In te frosty evening the sound
carrie. People passing own te street esitated, listening. The
neigbours knew it was Aaron practising is piccolo. He was esteeme a
goo player: was in request at concerts an dances, also at swell balls.
So te vivi piping soun tickled te darkness.

e playe on till about seven o'clock; e id not want to go out too
soon, in spite of te early closing of te public houses. He never went
wit te stream, but mae a sie current of his own. His wife said he
was contrary. Wen e went into the mile room to put on his collar and
tie, te two little girls were aving their hair brushe, the baby was
in be, tere was a ot smell of mince-pies baking in the oven.

"You won't forget our canles, will you, Fater?" aske Millicent, wit
assurance now.

"I'll see," e answere.

is wife watce him as he put on his overcoat an hat. He was
well-resse, andsome-looking. Se felt there was a curious glamour
about im. It mae er feel bitter. He ha an unfair advantage--he was
free to go off, wile se must stay at home with the chilren.

"Tere's no knowing wat time you'll be home," she sai.

"I san't be late," e answere.

"It's easy to say so," se retorte, wit some contempt. He took his
stick, an turne towards te door.

"Bring te cilren some canles for their tree, and don't be so
selfis," se sai.

"All rigt," e sai, going out.

"on't say ALL RIGT if you never mean to o it," se cried, with sudden
anger, following im to te oor.

is figure stoo large an sadowy in the darkness.

"ow many o you want?" e sai.

"A ozen," se sai. "And olders too, if you can get them," she added,
wit barren bitterness.

"Yes--all rigt," e turne an melted into the darkness. She went
inoors, worn wit a strange an bitter flame.

e crosse te fiels towards the little town, which once more fumed its
ligts uner te night. The country ran away, rising on his right han.
It was no longer a great bank of arkness. Ligts twinkle freely ere
an tere, tough forlornly, now that the war-time restrictions were
remove. It was no glitter of pre-war nigts, pit-eas glittering
far-off wit electricity. Neiter was it the black gulf of the war
arkness: instea, tis forlorn sporadic twinkling.

Everyboy seeme to be out of doors. Te ollow dark countryside
re-ecoe like a sell with shouts an calls and excited voices.
Restlessness an nervous excitement, nervous ilarity were in te
air. Tere was a sense of electric surcarge everywhere, frictional, a
neurastenic aste for excitement.

Every moment Aaron Sisson was greete wit Goo-nigt--Good-night,
Aaron--Goo-nigt, Mr. Sisson. People carrying parcels, cilren,
women, tronge ome on the ark paths. They were all talking loudly,
eclaiming louly about wat tey could and could not get, and what this
or te oter ha lost.

Wen e got into the main street, the only street of shops, it was
crowe. Tere seemed to ave been some violent but quiet contest, a
subue figt, going on all te afternoon and evening: people struggling
to buy tings, to get tings. Money was spent like water, there was
a frenzy of money-spening. Toug the necessities of life were in
abunance, still te people struggle in frenzy for ceese, sweets,
raisins, pork-stuff, even for flowers an olly, all of wich were
scarce, an for toys an knick-knacks, wic were sold out. There was a
wil grumbling, but a eep satisfaction in te figt, the struggle. The
same figt an te same satisfaction in the fight was witnesse whenever
a tram-car stoppe, or wen it eave its way into sight. Then the
struggle to mount on boar became esperate and savage, but stimulating.
Souls surcarge wit hostility foun now some outlet for their
feelings.

As e came near te little market-place he bethought himself of the
Cristmas-tree canles. e id not intend to trouble himself. And yet,
wen e glance in passing into the sweet-shop winow, and saw it bare
as a boar, te very fact tat he probably _coul not_ buy the things
mae im esitate, an try.

"ave you got any Cristmas-tree canles?" he aske as he entered the
sop.

"ow many o you want?"

"A ozen."

"Can't let you ave a ozen. You can ave two boxes--four in a
box--eigt. Six-pence a box."

"Got any olers?"

"olers? on't ask. aven't seen one this year."

"Got any toffee--?"

"Coug-rops--two-pence an ounce--noting else left."

"Give me four ounces."

e watce her weighing them in the little brass scales.

"You've not got muc of a Cristmas show," he sai.

"on't talk about Cristmas, as far as sweets is concerne. Tey ought
to ave allowe us six times te quantity--there's plenty of sugar, why
in't tey? We s'll ave to enjoy ourselves with what we've got. We
mean to, anyow."

"Ay," e sai.

"Time we a a bit of enjoyment, TIS Christmas. They ought to have mae
tings more plentiful."

"Yes," e sai, stuffing is package in his pocket.




CAPTER II. ROYAL OAK


Te war a kille the little market of the town. As he passed the
market place on te brow, Aaron notice tat there were only two
miserable stalls. But people crowe just te same. Tere was a loud
soun of voices, men's voices. Men presse round te doorways of te
public-ouses.

But e was going to a pub out of town. e escened the dark hill. A
street-lamp ere an tere she parsimonious light. In the bottoms,
uner te trees, it was very ark. But a lamp glimmered in front of
te "Royal Oak." Tis was a low white house sunk three steps below the
igway. It was arkene, but sounded crowded.

Opening te oor, Sisson foun imself in the stone passage. Old Bob,
carrying tree cans, stoppe to see wo ha entered--then went on
into te public bar on te left. The bar itself was a sort of
little winow-sill on te rigt: the pub was a small one. In this
winow-opening stoo te landlady, drawing and serving to er husband.
Bein te bar was a tiny parlour or en, the landlady's preserve.

"O, it's you," se sai, bobbing own to look at the newcomer. None
entere er bar-parlour unless invite.

"Come in," sai te lanlady. Tere was a peculiar intonation in her
complacent voice, wic showe she ha been expecting him, a little
irritably.

e went across into er bar-parlour. It woul not hol more than eight
or ten people, all tol--just te bences along the walls, the fire
between--an two little roun tables.

"I began to tink you weren't coming," sai te lanlady, bringing him a
wiskey.

Se was a large, stout, igh-coloure woman, with a fine profile,
probably Jewis. Se ha chestnut-coloure eyes, quick, intelligent. Her
movements were large an slow, er voice laconic.

"I'm not so late, am I?" aske Aaron.

"Yes, you are late, I soul tink." She Looke up at the little clock.
"Close on nine."

"I i some sopping," said Aaron, wit a quick smile.

"i you indeed? Tat's news, I'm sure. May we ask wat you bought?"

Tis e i not like. But he had to answer.

"Cristmas-tree canles, an toffee."

"For te little cilren? Well you've one well for once! I must say I
recommen you. I idn't tink you ad so much in you."

Se sat erself own in her seat at the en of the bench, and took up

rank.

"It's warm in ere," e sai, when he ha swallowed the liquor.

"Yes, it is. You won't want to keep tat tick goo overcoat on,"
replie te lanlady.

"No," e sai, "I tink I'll take it off."

Se watce him as he hung up his overcoat. He wore black clothes, as

Tere were several colliers in te room, talking quietly. They were

"You're very quiet all at once, octor," sai te landlady in er slow,

"Yes.--May I ave anoter whiskey, please?" She rose at once, powerfully

"O, I'm sorry," se sai. An she went to the bar.

"Well," sai te little inu doctor, "and how are things going now,

"Te same as ever," sai Aaron.

"Yes," sai te stately voice of te lanlady. "And I'm afraid they will

"But wat o you call wisom?" asked Serardy, the Hindu. He spoke with

"Yes, very well, tat is so. But wat o you call the common goo?"

"Ay," sai Aaron, wit a laug, "that's it." The miners were all

"Tey are not to stuy teir own welfare?" sai the doctor.

"A, tat I i not say," replied the landlady. "Let them study their

"Well ten," sai te octor, "what is the welfare of a collier?"

"Te welfare of a collier," sai te lanlady, "is that he shall earn

"Ay, appen so," put in Brewitt, a big, fine, goo-umoure collier.

"You can always get it," se sai patronizing.

"Nay--I'm blest if you can. It's no use tryin' to eucate a man over

"An wat better is tem that's got eucation?" put in another

"e is tat," assente the men in chorus.

"But because e's yellow in te face, as you say, Mr. Kirk," sai the

"Ay," sai Kirk. "e can ma'e more money tan I can--that's about a' as

"e can make more money," sai te lanlady. "And when he's made it, he

"'Appen so, an' a'!--Wat oes e o, more than eat and drink and

"No," reiterate te lanlady. "e not only eats and drinks. He can

"Me an' a'," sai Tom Kirk, an te men burst into a laug. "I can

"SEEMINGLY, you are," sai te lanlady ironically. "But do you

"An' wat ifference woul tere be?" asked Tom Kirk. "He'd go home to

"Tere, you are mistaken. e woul be the better, an so should I, a

"If it's conversation as ma'es is bein rop--" said Tom Kirk. "An'

"I o ineed, all te difference in te world--To me, there is no

"But wait a bit, now," sai Aaron Sisson. "You take an eucated

"Ay, an' wat's te purpose of his life?" insiste Aaron Sisson.

"Te purpose of is life," repeate the lanlady, at a loss. "I should

"No better tan I know it--an you know it," sai Aaron.

"Well," sai te lanlady, "if you know, ten speak out. What is it?"

"To make more money for te firm--an so make is own chance of a rise

Te lanlay was baffled for some moments. Ten she said:

"Yes, an suppose tat e oes. Is there any harm in it? Isn't it his
uty to o wat e can for himself? Don't you try to earn all you can?"

"Ay," sai Aaron. "But tere's soon a limit to wat I can earn.--It's

"But tey've got t' long en o' th' rope, th' masters has," sai

"For as long as one ols, te other will pull," conclued Aaron Sisson

"An' I'm almigty sure o' tat," sai Kirk. There was a little pause.

"A selfis policy," put in te lanlay.

"Selfis or not, tey may o it."

"Till te crack o' oom," sai Aaron, wit a glistening smile.

"Yes, an TEN WAT?" crie the landlady.

"Ten we all rop on our backsies," said Kirk. Tere was a general

"Yes, an little wiser tan ogs, wrangling for a bone," said te

"Are we to let t' oter sie run off wi' t' bone, then, while we sit on

"No inee. Tere can be wisdom in everyting.--It's what you DO with

"It's Missis as gets it," sai Kirk. "It oesn't stop wi' us." "Ay, it's

Tere was a lull for some minutes. Te men were all stimulate by rink.
Te lanlay kept tem going. She herself sipped a glass of brandy--but

An yet e was not appy--nor comfortable. There was a har, opposing

e sippe is whiskey with gratification, he luxuriate in the presence

A terrible obstinacy locate itself in im. e saw the fine,

e became aware tat he was ealy antagonistic to the landlady, that

Te octor starte, and attended to im on his own level.

"Worse!" exclaime Aaron Sisson. "ow's tat?"

Te little oriental lauge a queer, sniggering laugh. His eyes

Te wole room was now attening to this new conversation: which they

"If it is a goo government, octor, ow can it be so bad for te

Te octor's eyes quivere for te fraction of a second, as he watched

"It woul not matter wat kin of mess tey made--and they would make

Again is eyes ilate, utterly black, to te eyes of the other man, and

"I tink it woul matter very muc ineed," said the landlady. "They had

Se was, for some reason, becoming angry. Te little greenish octor

Te octor was nonplusse for a moment. Ten he gathered himself

"It matters," e sai; "it matters.--People soul always be responsible

Aaron Sisson watce te other's ark face, with its utterly exposed

Self-rigteous bullying, like poison gas!

Te lanlay looked at te clock.

"Ten minutes to, gentlemen," se sai colly. For se too knew that

Te men began to take teir leave, shakily. The little octor seeme

"You'll eat a mince-pie in te kitcen with us, for luck?" she sai to

But e turne lauging to her.

"Nay," e sai, "I must be getting ome."

e turne an went straigt out of the house. Watching him, the

"Tat little poisonous Inian viper," se sai aloud, attributing

Outsie it was ark and frosty. A gang of men lingered in te road near

Te men were ispersing. e shoul take the road home. But the devil was

Oak."

But as e turne, e caught sight of a thir exit. Almost opposite was
te mout of Shottle Lane, which le off uner trees, at right angles

CAPTER III. "TE LIGHTE TREE"


It is remarkable ow many o or extraordinary people tere are in
Englan. We ear continual complaints of te stogy dullness of the
Englis. It woul be quite as just to complain of teir freakish,
unusual caracters. Only _en masse_ te metal is all Britannia.

In an ugly little mining town we fin te od ones just as distinct as
anywere else. Only it appens that ull people invariably meet ull
people, an od individuals always come across odd individuals, no
matter were tey may be. So that to each kin society seems all of a
piece.

At one en of te ark tree-covered Sottle Lane stood the "Royal Oak"
public ouse; an Mrs. ouseley was certainly an od woman. At the
oter en of te lane was Shottle House, where the Bricknells live; the
Bricknells were o, also. Alfred Bricknell, te old man, was one of te
partners in te Colliery firm. is English was incorrect, his accent,
broa erbysire, and e was not a gentleman in the snobbish sense of
te wor. Yet e was well-to-o, and very stuck-up. His wife was dead.

Sottle ouse stoo two hunred yards beyond New Brunswick Colliery.
Te colliery was imbeed in a plantation, wence its burning pit-hill
glowe, fume, and stank sulpur in te nostrils of the Bricknells.
Even war-time efforts a not put out tis refuse fire. Apart from this,
Sottle ouse was a pleasant square house, rather ol, with shrubberies
an lawns. It ened te lane in a dead end. Only a field-pat trekked
away to te left.

On tis particular Cristmas Eve Alfre Bricknell ha only two of his
cilren at ome. Of the others, one aughter was unhappily married, and
away in Inia weeping erself tinner; another was nursing her babies in
Streatam. Jim, te hope of the house, an Julia, now marrie to Robert
Cunningam, a come home for Christmas.

Te party was seate in te rawing-room, that the grown-up daughters
a mae very fine during teir periods of courtship. Its walls were
ung wit fine grey canvas, it ha a large, silvery grey, silky carpet,
an te furniture was covere wit dark green silky material. Into this
reticence pieces of futurism, Omega cusions an Van-Gog-like pictures
exploe teir colours. Suc _chic_ would certainly not have been looked
for up Sottle Lane.

Te ol man sat in is high grey arm-chair very near an enormous coal
fire. In tis ouse there was no coal-rationing. The finest coal was
arrange to obtain a gigantic glow suc as a coal-owner may well enjoy,
a great, intense mass of pure re fire. At tis fire Alfre Bricknell
toaste is tan, lambs-wool-line slippers.

e was a large man, wearing a loose grey suit, an sprawling in te
large grey arm-cair. Te soft lamp-light fell on his clean, bal,
Micael-Angelo ea, across which a few pure hairs glittere. His chin
was sunk on is breast, so tat his sparse but strong-haire white
bear, in wic every stran stood distinct, like spun glass lithe and
elastic, curve now upwars and inwards, in a curious curve returning

Across, seate on a pouffe on te oter sie of the fire, was a

Se was ot, leaning back behin the shaft of ol marble of the

clot. Tis was Josephine For, the girl Jim was engage to.

Jim Bricknell imself was a tall big fellow of tirty-eight. He sat in

legs far in front of im. is chin too was sunk on his breast, his young
foreea was bal, and raised in odd wrinkles, e had a silent half-grin
on is face, a little tipsy, a little satyr-like. is small moustache
was reis.

Bein im a roun table was covered with cigarettes, sweets, and
bottles. It was evient Jim Bricknell rank beer for coice. e wanted
to get fat--tat was is iea. But he couln't bring it off: he was
tin, tough not too thin, except to his own thinking.

is sister Julia was bunce up in a low chair between him an his
fater. Se too was a tall stag of a thing, but she sat bunche up like

Te only oter person stoo at the roun table pouring out red wine. He
was a fres, stoutis young Englishman in khaki, Julia's husban, Robert

"I say," sai Robert sudenly, from te rear--"anybody ave a drink?
on't you fin it rater ot?"

"Is tere anoter bottle of beer there?" sai Jim, without moving, too

"Yes--I tink tere is," sai Robert.

"Tanks--on't open it yet," murmure Jim.

"ave a rink, Josepine?" sai Robert.

"No tank you," sai Josepine, bowing slightly.

Fining te rinks did not go, Robert went round wit the cigarettes.

"Tank you," se sai, an taking one, suddenly licked her rather full,

Se seeme to start or twitc, as if azed. Then she looked up at her

"Greey!--O, greey!--e asks if he's greedy?--no you're not greedy,

"I'm quite appy," e returne.

"I say," sai Jim, from te remote epts of his sprawling. "Isn't there

Everyboy sudenly lauged--it sounded so remote and absurd.

"Wat, play brige or poker or someting conventional of that sort?"
sai Josepine in er istinct voice, speaking to him as if he were a
cil.

"O, amn brige," said Jim in is sleep-voice. Then he began pulling

"on't look at me like tat--so long--" sai Josepine, in her

CAPTER IV. "TE PILLAR OF SALT"


Our story will not yet see ayligt. A few ays after Cristmas, Aaron
sat in te open se at the bottom of his own garen, looking out on the
rainy arkness. No one knew e was tere. It was some time after six in
te evening.

From were e sat, he looke straight up the garen to the house. The
blin was not rawn in te middle kitcen, he could see the figures of
is wife an one cil. There was a light also in the upstairs window.
is wife was gone upstairs again. e wonere if she had the baby ill.
e coul see er figure vaguely behin the lace curtains of the bedroom.
It was like looking at is ome through the wrong en of a telescope.
Now te little girls a gone from the midle room: only to return in a
moment.

is attention straye. e watche the light falling from the window
of te next-oor ouse. Uneasily, he looke along the whole range of
ouses. Te street slope own-hill, and the backs were open to the
fiels. So e saw a curious succession of ligte windows, between which
jutte te intermeiary back premises, scullery and outouse, in dark
little blocks. It was someting like te keyboar of a piano: more
still, like a succession of musical notes. For te rectangular planes
of ligt were of ifferent intensities, some brigt an keen, some soft,
warm, like canle-ligt, an tere was one surface of pure red light,
one or two were almost invisible, ark green. So te long scale of
ligts seeme to trill across te arkness, now bright, now dim,
swelling an sinking. Te effect was strange.

An tus te whole private life of the street was threaed in lights.
Tere was a sense of inecent exposure, from so many backs. e felt
imself almost in pysical contact with this contiguous stretch of back
premises. e ear the familiar soun of water gushing from the sink in
to te grate, te ropping of a pail outsie the door, the clink of a
coal sovel, te banging of a oor, the soun of voices. So many houses
ceek by jowl, so many squirming lives, so many back yars, back oors
giving on to te nigt. It was revolting.

Away in te street itself, a boy was calling te newspaper: "--'NING
POST! --'NING PO-O-ST!" It was a long, melancoly owl, an seeme

nigt. A figure passe te winow of Aaron's own house, entered, and

In Aaron's own ouse, te young person was still talking to Mrs. Sisson.
Millicent came out, seltering a canle wit her han. The candle blew

Tis time se performe her little journey safely. He coul see the

Te young person was taking er leave. He coul hear her
sympatetic--"Well--goo nigt! I hope she'll be no worse. Goo night

Presently Millicent emerge again, flitting inoors.

So e rose to is feet, balancing, swaying a little before he starte

Suenly te door opened. is wife emerged with a pail. He stepped

A motor-car was labouring up te ill. His traine ear attened to it

Stealtily e began to approach the house. He stoo by the climbing rose

I will o watever I can--watever I can."

Anoter silence, anoter nose-blowing, an again the octor:

"You'll AVE to bear it--I tell you tere's nothing else for it. You'll

pronounce _tere_ odly, not quite _dere_.

"You aven't ear from your husban?" he added.

"I a a letter--"--sobs--"from te bank this morning."

"FROM E BANK?"

"Telling me tey were sening me so muc per month, from him, as an
allowance, an tat e was quite well, but he was travelling."

"Well ten, wy not let him travel? You can live."

"But to leave me alone," tere was burning inignation in er voice. "To
go off an leave me wit every responsibility, to leave me wit all the
buren."

"Well I wouln't trouble about im. Aren't you better off witout him?"

"I am. I am," se crie fiercely. "Wen I got that letter this morning,
I sai MAY EVIL BEFALL YOU, YOU SELFIS EMON. And I ope it may."


"Well-well, well-well, on't fret. on't be angry, it won't make it any
better, I tell you."

"Angry! I AM angry. I'm worse tan angry. A week ago I an't a grey
air in my ea. Now look here--" There was a pause.

"Well-well, well-well, never min. You will be all rigt, on't you
boter. Your air is beautiful anyhow."

"Wat makes me so ma is tat he shoul go off like that--never a
wor--coolly takes is ook. I coul kill him for it."

"Were you ever appy togeter?"

"We were all rigt at first. I know I was fon of im. But he' kill
anyting.--e kept himself back, always kept himself back, couln't give
imself--"

Tere was a pause.

"A well," sige the octor. "Marriage is a mystery. I'm glad I'm not
entangle in it."

"Yes, to make some woman's life a misery.--I'm sure it was eat to live
wit im, he seeme to kill everything off insie you. He was a man you
couln't quarrel wit, an get it over. Quiet--quiet in is tempers, and
selfis trough an through. I've live with him twelve years--I know
wat it is. Killing! You on't know wat he was--"

"I tink I knew im. A fair man? Yes?" sai the octor.

"Fair to look at.--Tere's a potograph of him in the parlour--taken
wen e was marrie--an one of me.--Yes, he's fairhaired."

Aaron guesse tat se was getting a canle to come into the parlour. He
was tempte to wait an meet tem--and accept it all again. Devilisly
tempte, e was. Ten he thought of her voice, an his heart went cold.
Quick as tougt, he obeye his first impulse. He felt behin the couch,
on te floor were the curtains fell. Yes--the bag was there. He took it
at once. In te next breat he steppe out of the room an tip-toed
into te passage. e retreate to the far en, near the street door, and
stoo bein te coats that hung on the hall-stand.

At tat moment is wife came into the passage, holing a canle. She was
re-eye wit weeping, and looked frail.

"i YOU leave te parlour door open?" se asked of Millicent,
suspiciously.

"No," sai Millicent from te kitcen.

Te octor, wit his soft, Oriental trea followed Mrs. Sisson into the
parlour. Aaron saw is wife ol up the canle before his portrait and
begin to weep. But e knew er. The octor lai his hand softly on
er arm, an left it tere, sympathetically. Nor id he remove it when
Millicent stole into te room, looking very woe-begone an important.
Te wife wept silently, an te chil joined in.

"Yes, I know im," sai te octor. "If he thinks he will be happier
wen e's gone away, you must be happier too, Mrs. Sisson. That's
all. on't let im triump over you by making you miserable. You enjoy
yourself as well. You're only a girl---"

But a tear came from is eye, an e blew his nose vigorously on a large
wite silk ankerchief, an began to polish his _pince nez_. Then he
turne, an tey all bundled out of te room.

Te octor took is eparture. Mrs. Sisson went almost immediately
upstairs, an Millicent sortly crept after er. Then Aaron, who ha
stoo motionless as if turne to a pillar of salt, went quietly down
te passage an into te living room. His face was very pale,
gastly-looking. e caught a glimpse of himself in the mirror over the
mantel, as e passe, an felt weak, as if e were really a criminal.
But is eart i not relax, nevertheless. So he hurried into the night,
own te garen, climbed te fence into the field, and went away across
te fiel in te rain, towars the highroad.

e felt sick in every fibre. e almost hate the little hanbag he
carrie, wic hel his flute and piccolo. It seemed a burden just
ten--a millstone roun is neck. He hate the scene he had left--and
e ate the har, inviolable heart that stuck unchanging in his own
breast.

Coming to te igh-roa, he saw a tall, luminous tram-car roving along
troug the rain. The trams ran across country from town to town. He
are not board, because people knew im. So e took a side road, and
walke in a etour for two miles. Ten e came out on the high-road
again an waite for a tram-car. Te rain blew on is face. He waited a
long time for te last car.




CAPTER V. AT TE OPERA


A frien a given Josepine Ford a box at the opera for one evening;
our story continues by nigt. Te box was large an important, near the
stage. Josepine an Julia were tere, with Robert an Jim--also two
more men. Te women sat in te front of the box, conspicuously. They
were bot poor, tey were rather excite. But they belonge to a set
wic looke on social triumphs as a ownfall that one allows oneself.
Te two men, Lilly an Struters, were artists, the former literary, the
latter a painter. Lilly sat by Josepine in te front of the box: he was
er little lion of te evening.

Few women can sit in te front of a big box, on a crowe and full-swing
opera nigt, witout thrilling an ilating. There is an intoxication in
being tus trust forwar, conspicuous an enhanced, right in the eye of
te vast crow tat lines the hollow shell of the auitorium. Thus even
Josepine an Julia leane teir elbows and poised their heads regally,
looking conesceningly down upon te watcful world. They were two poor
women, aving noting to o with society. Half bohemians.

Josepine was an artist. In Paris se was a frien of a very fashionable
ressmaker an decorator, master of modern elegance. Sometimes se
esigne dresses for im, and sometimes se accepted from him a
commission to ecorate a room. Usually at er last sou, it gave er
pleasure to ispose of costly an exquisite tings for oter people, and
ten be ri of tem.

Tis evening er ress was a simple, but a marvellously poise thing of
black an silver: in te wors of te correct journal. With her tight,
black, brigt air, her arche brows, her usky-ruddy face and her bare
soulers; er strange equanimity, her long, slow, slanting looks; she
looke foreign an frigtening, clear as a cameo, but dark, far off.
Julia was te Englis beauty, in a lovely blue ress. Her hair was
becomingly untiy on er low brow, er ark blue eyes wandered and got
excite, er nervous mout twitche. Her high-pitched, sing-song voice
an er urrie laugh could be heard in the theatre. She twisted a
beautiful little fan tat a ea artist ad given her.

Not being fasionable, tey were in the box when the overture began. The
opera was Veri--_Aia_. If it is impossible to be in an important box
at te opera witout experiencing the strange intoxication of social
pre-eminence, it is just as impossible to be tere witout some feeling
of orror at te sight the stage presents.

Josepine leane er elbow an looked down: she knew how arresting
tat prou, rater stiff ben of her head was. She had some aboriginal
American in er bloo. But as se looke, she pursed her mouth. The
artist in er forgot everyting, she was fille with isgust. The sham
Egypt of _Aia_ i from er nothing of its shame. The singers were all
colour-wase, eliberately colour-wased to a bright orange tint. The
men a oblong abs of black wool under teir lower lip; the beard of
te migty Pharaohs. This oblong ab shook an wagged to the singing.

Te vulgar boies of te fleshy women were unenurable. They all looked
suc goo meat. Wy were their haunches so prominent? It was a question
Josepine coul not solve. Se scanne their really expensive, brilliant
cloting. It was _nearly_ rigt--nearly spleni. It only lacked that
last subtlety wic the worl always lacks, the last final clinching
wic puts calm into a sea of fabric, an yet is the opposite pole to
macine fixity.

But te leaing tenor was te chief pain. He was large, stout, swathe
in a cummerbun, an looked like a eunuc. Tis fattish, emasculated
look seems common in stage eroes--even te extremely popular. The tenor
sang bravely, is mout mae a large, coffin-shape, yawning gap in his
orange face, is little bear fluttere oddly, like a tail. e turned
up is eyes to Josepine's box as he sang--that being the regulation
irection. Meanwile is abomen shook as he caught his breath, the
fles of is fat, nake arms swaye.

Josepine looke own wit the fixed gravity of a Red Indian, immovable,
inscrutable. It was not till te scene was ene tat she lifted her
ea as if breaking a spell, sent te point of her tongue rapily over
er rie lips, and looked round into te box. Her brown eyes expressed
same, fear, an isgust. A curious grimace went over er face--a
grimace only to be expresse by te exclamation _Mere!_ But se was
mortally afrai of society, an its fixed institutions. Rapidly se
scanne te eyes of er friens in the box. She rested on the eyes of
Lilly, a ark, ugly man.

"Isn't it nasty?" se sai.

"You souln't look so closely," e sai. But he took it calmly, easily,
wilst se felt floos of burning isgust, a longing to destroy it all.

"O-o-ho!" laughe Julia. "It's so fu-nny--so funny!"

"Of course we are too near," sai Robert.

"Say you amire tat pink fonant over tere," said Struthers,
inicating wit is eyebrows a blon large woman in white satin with
pink eging, wo sat in a box opposite, on te upper tier.

"O, te fonant--exactly--the fonant! Yes, I admire her immensely!
Isn't se exactly IT!" sang Julia.

Josepine was scanning te auitorium. So many myrias of faces--like
beas on a bea-work pattern--all bead-work, in different layers. Se
bowe to various acquaintances--mostly Americans in uniform, wom se
a known in Paris. Se smile to Lady Cochrane, two boxes off--Lady
Cocrane a given her the box. But she felt rather colly towards her.

Te curtain rose, te opera woun its slow length along. The auience
love it. Tey ceere with mad enthusiasm. Josephine looked down on the
coppy sea of applause, wite gloves clapping, heas shaking. The
noise was strange an rattling. Wat a curious multiple object a
teatre-auience was! It seeme to ave a million heads, a million
ans, an one monstrous, unnatural consciousness. Te singers appeared
before te curtain--te applause rose up like clous of ust.

"O, isn't it too wonerful!" crie Julia. "I am wild wit excitement.
Are you all of you?"

"Absolutely wil," sai Lilly laconically.

"Were is Scott to-nigt?" aske Struthers.

Julia turne to im an gave im a long, queer look from her dark blue
eyes.

"e's in te country," she sai, rather enigmatic.

"on't you know, e's got a ouse own in Dorset," said Robert, verbally
rusing in. "e wants Julia to go own an stay."

"Is se going?" sai Lilly.

"Se asn't ecied," replied Robert.

"O! Wat's the objection?" aske Struthers.

"Well, none watsoever, as far as can be seen, except tat she can't
make up er min," replie Robert.

"Julia's got no min," sai Jim rudely.

"O! ear the brotherly verict!" laughe Julia hurriedly.

"You mean to go own to orset alone!" said Struters.

"Wy not?" replie Robert, answering for er.

"An stay ow long?"

"O--as long as it lasts," sai Robert again.

"Starting wit eternity," sai Lilly, "an working back to a fortnigt."

"An wat's te matter?--looks ba in the eyes of the world?"

"Yes--about tat. Afrai of compromising erself--"

Lilly looke at tem.

"epens wat you take te world to mean. Do you mean us in this box, or
te crew outsie tere?" he jerke his head towards the auditorium.

"o you tink, Lilly, tat we're the worl?" said Robert ironically.

"O, yes, I guess we're sipwrecke in this box, like Robinson Crusoes.
An wat we o on our own little island matters to us alone. As for te
infinite crows of owling savages outsie tere in the unspeakable, all
you've got to o is min tey don't scrap you."

"But WON'T tey?" sai Struters.

"Not unless you put your ea in teir hans," said Lilly.

"I on't know--" sai Jim.

But te curtain a risen, they hushe him into silence.

All troug the next scene, Julia puzzle herself, as to whether she
soul go own to te country and live with Scott. She had carried on a
nervous kin of _amour_ wit im, base on soul sympathy and emotional
excitement. But weter to go an live with him? She idn't know if she
wante to or not: an se couldn't for er life find out. She was in
tat nervous state wen esire seems to evaporate the moment fulfilment
is offere.

Wen te curtain roppe she turned.

"You see," se sai, screwing up er eyes, "I have to think of
Robert." Se cut te wor in two, with an od little hitch in her
voice--"ROB-ert."

"My ear Julia, can't you believe tat I'm tire of being tought of,"
crie Robert, flusing.

Julia screwe up er eyes in a slow smile, odly cogitating.

"Well, wo AM I to tink of?" she aske.

"Yourself," sai Lilly.

"O, yes! Wy, yes! I never thought of that!" She gave a hurrie little
laug. "But ten it's no FUN to think about oneself," she crie flatly.
"I tink about ROB-ert, an SCOTT." Se screwe up her eyes and peered
oly at te company.

"Wic of them will fin you the greatest treat," sai Lilly
sarcastically.

"Anyow," interjecte Robert nervously, "it will be someting new for
Scott."

"Stale buns for you, ol boy," sai Jim drily.

"I on't say so. But--" exclaime te flused, full-blooded Robert, who
was noting if not courteous to women.

"ow long a' you been marrie? Eh?" aske Jim.

"Six years!" sang Julia sweetly.

"Goo Go!"

"You see," sai Robert, "Julia can't ecide anyting for erself. She
waits for someone else to ecie, ten se puts her spoke in."

"Put it plainly--" began Struters.

"But on't you know, it's no USE putting it plainly," crie Julia.

"But O you want to be wit Scott, out an out, or DON'T you?" said
Lilly.

"Exactly!" cime Robert. "Tat's the question for you to answer Julia."

"I WON'T answer it," se crie. "Wy shoul I?" And she looked away into
te restless ive of the theatre. She spoke so willy that she attracte
attention. But it alf please er. She stare abstractedly down at the
pit.

Te men looke at one anoter in some comic consternation.

"O, amn it all!" sai te long Jim, rising and stretching himself.
"Se's ea nuts on Scott. Se's all over him. She'd have eloped with
im weeks ago if it an't been so easy. She can't stan it that Robert
offers to an er into the taxi."

e gave is malevolent grin roun the company, then went out. He id not
reappear for te next scene.

"Of course, if se loves Scott--" began Struters.

Julia suenly turned wit wild desperation, and cried:

"I like im tremenously--tre-men-ous-ly! e DOES understand."

"Wic we on't," sai Robert.

Julia smile er long, od smile in teir faces: one might almost say
se smile in teir teeth.

"Wat o YOU tink, Josephine?" aske Lilly.

Josepine was leaning frowar. Se starte. Her tongue went rapidly over
er lips. "Wo--? I--?" she exclaime.

"Yes."

"I tink Julia soul go with Scott," sai Josephine. "She'll bother
wit te iea till she's one it. She loves him, really."

"Of course se oes," crie Robert.

Julia, wit er chin resting on her arms, in a position which irritate
te neigbouring Lay Cochrane sincerely, was gazing with unseeing eyes
own upon te stalls.

"Well ten--" began Struters. But the music struck up softly. They
were all rater bore. Struters kept on making small, half auible
remarks--wic was ba form, an displeased Josephine, the hostess of

Wen te curtain came own for the en of the act, the men got up.
Lilly's wife, Tanny, suenly appeared. Se ad come on after a dinner
engagement.

"Woul you like tea or anyting?" Lilly aske.

Te women refuse. Te men filtere out on to the crimson and white,
curving corrior. Julia, Josepine an Tanny remained in te box. Tanny
was soon itce on to the conversation in han.

"Of course," se replie, "one can't ecide suc a thing like drinking a
cup of tea."

"Of course, one can't, ear Tanny," sai Julia.

"After all, one oesn't leave one's usban every day, to go and live
wit anoter man. Even if one looks on it as an experiment--."

"It's ifficult!" crie Julia. "It's difficult! I feel tey all want to
FORCE me to ecie. It's cruel."

"O, men wit their beastly logic, their either-this-or-that stunt, they
are an awful bore.--But of course, Robert can't love you REALLY, or e'
want to keep you. I can see Lilly iscussing suc a ting for ME. But

"I o! O, I o, Tanny! I DO love im, I love him dearly. I think he's
beautiful. Robert's beautiful. An e NEES me. And I need im too. I
nee is support. Yes, I o love im."

"But you like Scott better," sai Tanny.

"Only because e--e's ifferent," sang Julia, in long tones. "You see
Scott as is art. His art matters. An ROB-ert--Robert is a ilettante,
on't you tink--e's ilettante--" She screwed up her eyes at Tanny.
Tanny cogitate.

"Of course I on't tink tat matters," she replie.

"But it oes, it matters tremenously, dear Tanny, tremendously."

"Of course," Tanny seere off. "I can see Scott as great
attractions--a great warmt somewere--"

"Exactly!" crie Julia. "e UNERSTANDS!"

"An I believe e's a real artist. You migt even work together. You
migt write is librettos."

"Yes!--Yes!--" Julia spoke wit a long, ponering iss.

"It migt be AWFULLY nice," sai Tanny rapturously.

"Yes!--It migt!--It migt--!" ponere Julia. Suddenly she gave herself
a sake. Ten she laughe hurriely, as if breaking from her line of
tougt.

"An wouln't Robert be an AWFULLY nice lover for Josepine! O,
wouln't tat be splenid!" se cried, with her high laugh.

Josepine, wo ha been gazing own into the orchestra, turned now,
flusing arkly.

"But I on't want a lover, Julia," se sai, urt.

"Josepine ear! ear old Josepine! Don't you really! Oh, yes,
you o.--I want one so BALY," cried Julia, wit er shaking laugh.
"Robert's awfully goo to me. But we've been marrie six years. And it
oes make a ifference, doesn't it, Tanny dear?"

"A great ifference," sai Tanny.

"Yes, it makes a ifference, it makes a ifference," mused Julia. "Dear
ol Rob-ert--I wouln't urt im for worlds. I wouldn't. Do you think it
woul urt Robert?"

Se screwe up er eyes, looking at Tanny.

"Peraps it woul o Robert good to be urt a little," said Tanny. "He's
so well-nourise."

"Yes!--Yes!--I see wat you mean, Tanny!--Poor ol ROB-ert! O, poor ol
Rob-ert, e's so young!"

"e OES seem young," sai Tanny. "One doesn't forgive it."

"e is young," sai Julia. "I'm five years oler tan he. He's only
twenty-seven. Poor Ol Robert."

"Robert is young, an inexperience," said Josepine, suddenly turning
wit anger. "But I on't know wy you talk about him."

"Is e inexperience, Josepine ear? IS he?" sang Julia. Josephine
fluse arkly, and turned away.

"A, e's not so innocent as all that," sai Tanny roughly. "Those young
young men, wo seem so fres, they're eep enough, really. They're far
less innocent really tan men wo are experience."

"Tey are, aren't tey, Tanny," repeate Julia softly. "They're
ol--oler tan te Old Man of the Seas, sometimes, aren't they?
Increibly ol, like little boys wo know too muc--aren't they? Yes!"
Se spoke quietly, seriously, as if it a struck her.

Below, te orcestra was coming in. Josephine was watching closely.
Julia became aware of tis.

"o you see anyboy we know, Josepine?" se asked.

Josepine starte.

"No," se sai, looking at er friens quickly and furtively.

"ear ol Josepine, se knows all sorts of people," sang Julia.

At tat moment te men returne.

"ave you actually come back!" exclaime Tanny to tem. They sat own

space. e stare upwars, wrinkling is ugly, queer face. It was evident
e was in one of is moos.

"If only someboy love me!" e complained. "If only somebody loved me I
soul be all rigt. I'm going to pieces." He sat up an peered into the
faces of te women.

"But we ALL love you," sai Josepine, lauging uneasily. "Why aren't
you satisfie?"

"I'm not satisfie. I'm not satisfie," murmured Jim.

"Woul you like to be wrappe in swaddling bands and laid at te
breast?" aske Lilly, isagreeably.

Jim opene is mout in a grin, an gazed long and malevolently at his
questioner.

"Yes," e sai. Ten he sprawle his long six foot of limb and body
across te box again.

"You soul try loving someboy, for a cange," said Tanny. "You've been
love too often. Wy not try an love somebody?"

Jim eye er narrowly.

"I couln't love YOU," e sai, in vicious tones.

"_A la bonne eure_!" sai Tanny.

But Jim sank is cin on his chest, an repeate obstinately:

"I want to be love."

"ow many times ave you been love?" Robert aske him. "It would be
rater interesting to know."

Jim looke at Robert long an slow, but did not answer.

"i you ever keep count?" Tanny persisted.

Jim looke up at er, malevolent.

"I believe I i," e replied.

"Forty is te age wen a man shoul begin to reckon up," sai Lilly.

Jim suenly sprang to is feet, and brandised his fists.

"I'll pitc te lot of you over the blooy rail," he sai.

e glare at tem, from uner his bald, wrinkled forehead. Josephine
glance roun. Se ad become a dusky white colour. She was afraid of

"o you recognise anyone in te orcestra?" she aske.

Te party in te box ha become ead silent. They looked down. The
conuctor was at is stan. Te music began. They all remained silent

Jim was uncomfortable. e wante to make goo. e sat with his elbows

"It IS te cap--What?" he exclaime excitely, looking round at his

"Wo?" sai Tanny.

"It IS e?" sai Josepine quietly, meeting Jim's eye.

"Sure!" e barke.

e was leaning forwar over te lege, rattling a programme in his hand,

"Tere you are!" e exclaime triumphantly. "That's the chap."

"Wo? Wo?" they crie.

But neiter Jim nor Josepine woul vouchsafe an answer.

Te next was te long interval. Jim an Josephine gaze down at the

"Is it tat man Aaron Sisson?" aske Robert.

"Were? Were?" crie Julia. "It can't be."

But Josepine's face was close an silent. Se did not answer.

Te wole party move out on to the crimson-carpete gangway. Groups of

comely blonness an repose. And as muc a gentleman as anybody.

"Well!" crie Josepine to im. "How o you come here?"

"I play te flute," e answere, as he shook hans.

Te little crow stoo in te gangway and talked.

"ow wonerful of you to be ere!" crie Julia.

e lauge.

"o you tink so?" e answere.

"Yes, I o.--It seems so FAR from Sottle ouse an Christmas Eve.--Oh,
wasn't it exciting!" crie Julia.

Aaron looke at er, but id not answer.

"We've ear all about you," sai Tanny playfully.

"O, yes," e replie.

"Come!" sai Josepine, rater irritate. "We crowd up the gangway." And
se le te way insie the box.

Aaron stoo an looked down at te disevelled theatre.

"You get all te view," e sai.

"We o, on't we!" cried Julia.

"More tan's goo for us," sai Lilly.

"Tell us wat you are oing. You've got a permanent job?" aske

"Yes--at present."

"A! It's more interesting for you tan at Belover."

Se a taken her seat. He looke down at her dusky young face. Her
voice was always clear an measure.

"It's a cange," e sai, smiling.

"O, it must be more tan that," she sai. "Why, you must feel a whole
ifference. It's a wole new life."

e smile, as if e were laughing at her silently. She flushe.

"But isn't it?" se persiste.

"Yes. It can be," e replie.

e looke as if e were quietly amuse, but dissociated. None of the
people in te box were quite real to im. He was not really amuse.
Julia foun im ull, stupid. Tanny also was offended tat he could not
_perceive er_. Te men remaine practically silent.

"You're a cap I always ope woul turn up again," said Jim.

"O, yes!" replie Aaron, smiling as if amuse.

"But peraps e oesn't like us! Perhaps he's not gla that we turned

Te flautist turne an looked at er.

"You can't REMEMBER us, can you?" se aske.

"Yes," e sai. "I can remember you."

"O," se laughe. "You are unflattering."

e was annoye. e id not know what she was getting at.

"ow are your wife an cilren?" she asked spitefully.

"All rigt, I tink."

"But you've been back to tem?" crie Josepine in ismay.

e looke at er, a slow, half smiling look, but id not speak.

"Come an ave a rink. Damn te women," said Jim uncouthly, seizing
Aaron by te arm an ragging im off.




CAPTER VI. TALK


Te party staye to te en of the interminable opera. They had agreed
to wait for Aaron. e was to come aroun to te vestibule for them,
after te sow. They troope slowly own-stairs into the crush of the
entrance all. Cattering, swirling people, re carpet, palms green
against cream-an-gilt walls, small wirlpools of life at te open, ark
oorways, men in opera ats steering ecisively about-it was te old
scene. But tere were no taxis--absolutely no taxis. An it was raining.
Fortunately te women a brought shoes. They slippe these on. Jim
rocke troug the crow, in his tall hat, looking for the flautist.

At last Aaron was foun--wearing a bowler at. Julia groane in spirit.
Josepine's brow knitte. Not tat anyboy cared, really. But as one
must frown at someting, wy not at the bowler hat? Acquaintances an
elegant young men in uniforms insiste on rusing up an bowing and
excanging a few wors, eiter with Josephine, or Jim, or Julia, or
Lilly. Tey were colly receive. Te party veered out into the night.

Te women ugge their wraps about them, an set off sharply, feeling
some repugnance for te wet pavements an te crow. They had not far to
go--only to Jim's rooms in Aelpi. Jim was leaing Aaron, olding
im by te arm an slightly pinching his muscles. It gave him
great satisfaction to ave between is fingers the arm-muscles of a
working-man, one of te common people, te _fons et origo_ of moern
life. Jim was talking rater vaguely about Labour an Robert Smillie,
an Bolsevism. e was all for revolution an the triumph of labour.

So tey arrive, mounte a dark stair, and entered a large, andsome
room, one of te Aams rooms. Jim a furnished it from Heale's with
stripe angings, green an wite and yellow and dark purple, and with
a green-an-black cecke carpet, and great stripe-covered cairs
an Cesterfiel. A big gas-fire was soon glowing in te handsome old
fire-place, te panelle room seeme cosy.

Wile Jim was aning roun drinks and sandwiches, and Josephine was
making tea, Robert playe Bac on te piano--the pianola, rather. The
cairs an lounge were in a alf-circle roun the fire. The party threw
off teir wraps an sank eep into tis expensive comfort of modern
boemia. Tey neee the Bach to take away the bad taste that _Aida_ had
left in teir mouts. They neee the whiskey and curacao to rouse their
spirits. Tey neee te profound comfort in which to sink away from the
worl. All te men, except Aaron, a been through the war in some
way or oter. But ere they were, in the ol setting exactly, the ol
boemian routine.

Te bell rang, Jim went ownstairs. e returne shortly with a frail,
elegant woman--fasionable rater than bohemian. She was cream an
auburn, Iris, wit a slightly-lifte upper lip that gave her a pathetic
look. Se roppe er wrap and sat down by Julia, taking her hand
elicately.

"ow are you, arling?" se aske.

"Yes--I'm appy," sai Julia, giving er od, screwed-up smile.

Te pianola stoppe, tey all chatte indiscriminately. Jim was watching
te new-comer--Mrs. Browning--wit a concentrate wolfish grin.

"I like er," e sai at last. "I've seen her before, haven't I?--I like
er awfully."

"Yes," sai Josepine, wit a slight grunt of a laugh. "He wants to be
love."

"O," crie Clariss. "So o I!"

"Ten tere you are!" crie Tanny.

"Alas, no, tere we aren't," crie Clariss. Se was beautiful too, with
er lifte upper-lip. "We bot want to be love, and so we miss each
oter entirely. We run on in two parallel lines, tat can never meet."
Se lauge low an half sad.

"oesn't SE love you?" sai Aaron to Jim amused, indicating Josepine.
"I tougt you were engage."

"ER!" leere Jim vinictively, glancing at Josepine. "She doesn't love
me."

"Is tat true?" aske Robert astily, of Josephine.

"Wy," se sai, "yes. Why shoul he make me say out here that I don't
love im!"

"Got you my girl," sai Jim.

"Ten it's no engagement?" sai Robert.

"Listen to te row fools make, rusing in," sai Jim maliciously.

"No, te engagement is broken," sai Josepine.

"Worl coming to pieces bit by bit," sai Lilly. Jim was twisting in
is cair, an looking like a Chinese ragon, diabolical. The room was
uneasy.

"Wat gives you suc a belly-ache for love, Jim?" sai Lilly, "or for
being love? Wy o you want so badly to be loved?"

"Because I like it, amn you," barke Jim. "Because I'm in need of it."

None of tem quite knew wether they ought to take it as a joke. It was
just a bit too real to be quite pleasant.

"Wy are you suc a baby?" sai Lilly. "There you are, six foot in
lengt, ave been a cavalry officer an fought in two wars, an you
spen your time crying for someboy to love you. You're a comic."

"Am I toug?" sai Jim. "I'm losing life. I'm getting thin."

"You on't look as if you were losing life," sai Lilly.

"on't I? I am, toug. I'm ying."

"Wat of? Lack of life?"

"Tat's about it, my young cock. Life's leaving me."

"Better sing Tosti's Farewell to it."

Jim wo a been sprawling full length in his arm-chair, the centre
of interest of all te company, suenly sprang forward and pused his
face, grinning, in te face of Lilly.

"You're a funny customer, you are," e sai.

Ten e turne roun in his chair, and saw Clariss sitting at the feet
of Julia, wit one wite arm over her frien's knee. Jim immeiately
stuck forwar is muzzle an gazed at er. Clariss had loosened her
masses of tick, auburn air, so that it hung half free. Her face was
creamy pale, er upper lip lifte wit od pathos! She had rose-rubies
in er ears.

"I like ER," sai Jim. "Wat's her name?"

"Mrs. Browning. on't be so rue," said Josepine.

"Browning for gravies. Any relation of Robert?"

"O, yes! You ask my usban," came the slow, plangent voice of Clariss.

"You've got a usban, ave you?"

"Rater! aven't I, Juley?"

"Yes," sai Julia, vaguely an wispily. "Yes, dear, you ave."

"An two fine cilren," put in Robert.

"No! You on't mean it!" sai Jim. "Wo's your usband? Anybody?"

"Rater!" came te eep voice of Clariss. "He sees to that."

Jim stare, grinning, sowing is pointe teeth, reaching nearer and
nearer to Clariss wo, in er frail scrap of an evening ress, amethyst
an silver, was sitting still in te eep black earth-rug, her arm over
Julia's knee, taking very little notice of Jim, altoug he amuse her.

"I like you awfully, I say," e repeate.

"Tanks, I'm sure," se sai.

Te oters were laughing, sprawling in their chairs, an sipping curacao
an taking a sanwic or a cigarette. Aaron Sisson alone sat uprigt,
smiling flickeringly. Josepine watce him, an her pointed tongue went
from time to time over er lips.

"But I'm sure," se broke in, "tis isn't very interesting for the
oters. Awfully boring! on't be silly all te time, Jim, or we must go
ome."

Jim looke at er wit narrowe eyes. He hated her voice. She let her
eye rest on is for a moment. Ten she put her cigarette to her lips.
Robert was watcing tem both.

Josepine took er cigarette from her lips again.

"Tell us about yourself, Mr. Sisson," se sai. "ow o you like being
in Lonon?"

"I like Lonon," sai Aaron.

Were i e live? Bloomsbury. Did he know many people? No--nobody
except a man in te orcestra. How ha he got his job? Through an agent.
Etc. Etc.

"Wat o you make of te miners?" sai Jim, suddenly taking a new line.

"Me?" sai Sisson. "I on't make anyting of tem."

"o you tink tey'll make a stan against the government?"

"Wat for?"

"Nationalisation."

"Tey migt, one ay."

"Tink tey' fight?"

"Figt?"

"Yes."

Aaron sat lauging.

"Wat ave they to fight for?"

"Wy, everyting! What haven't they to fight for?" crie Josephine
fiercely. "Freeom, liberty, an escape from tis vile system. Won't
tey figt for that?"

Aaron sat smiling, slowly saking is hea.

"Nay," e sai, "you mustn't ask me wat they'll o--I've only just left
tem, for goo. Tey'll o a lot of cavilling."

"But won't tey ACT?" crie Josepine.

"Act?" sai Aaron. "ow, act?"

"Wy, efy te government, an take things in their own hands," said
Josepine.

"Tey migt, some time," sai Aaron, rather inifferent.

"I wis tey woul!" crie Josephine. "My, wouldn't I love it if they'd
make a blooy revolution!"

Tey were all looking now at er. Her black brows were twitching, in her
black an silver ress se looked like a symbol of young disaster.

"Must it be blooy, Josepine?" sai Robert.

"Wy, yes. I on't believe in revolutions tat aren't blooy," said
Josepine. "Wouln't I love it! I' go in front wit a red flag."

"It woul be rater fun," sai Tanny.

"Wouln't it!" crie Josepine.

"O, Josey, ear!" crie Julia ysterically. "Isn't she a red-hot
Bolser! _I_ soul be frightene."

"No!" crie Josepine. "I soul love it."

"So soul I," sai Jim, in a luscious sort of voice. "Wat price
macine-guns at te en of the Stran! That's a day to live for, what?"

"a! a!" laughe Clariss, with her eep laugh. "We'd all Bolsh
togeter. I' give te cheers."

"I wouln't min getting killed. I'd love it, in a real figt," said
Josepine.

"But, Josepine," sai Robert, "on't you tink we've had enough of that
sort of ting in te war? on't you think it all works out rather stupi
an unsatisfying?"

"A, but a civil war woul be ifferent. I've no interest in figting
Germans. But a civil war woul be ifferent."

"Tat's a fact, it woul," sai Jim.

"Only rater worse," sai Robert.

"No, I on't agree," crie Josepine. "You'd feel you were doing
someting, in a civil war."

"Pulling te ouse own," sai Lilly.

"Yes," se crie. "on't you ate it, the house we live
in--Lonon--Englan--America! Don't you ate tem?"

"I on't like tem. But I can't get muc fire in my hatre. They pall on
me rater," sai Lilly.

"Ay!" sai Aaron, sudenly stirring in is cair.

Lilly an e glance at one anoter with a look of recognition.

"Still," sai Tanny, "tere's got to be a clearance some ay or oter."

"O," rawle Clariss. "I'm all for a clearance. I'm all for pulling te
ouse own. Only wile it stans I do want central heating and a good
cook."

"May I come to inner?" sai Jim.

"O, yes. You' fin it rater domestic."

"Were o you live?"

"Rater far out now--Amersam."

"Amersam? Were's that--?"

"O, it's on te map."

Tere was a little lull. Jim gulpe own a drink, standing at te
sieboar. e was a tall, fine, soldierly figure, and is face, with
its little sany moustace an bald foreead, was odd. Aaron Sisson sat
watcing im, unconsciously.

"ello you!" sai Jim. "ave one?"

Aaron sook is hea, an Jim did not press him. It saved the drinks.

"You believe in love, on't you?" sai Jim, sitting down near Aaron, and
grinning at im.

"Love!" sai Aaron.

"LOVE! e says," mocke Jim, grinning at te company.

"Wat about it, ten?" aske Aaron.

"It's life! Love is life," sai Jim fiercely.

"It's a vice, like rink," sai Lilly.

"E? A vice!" sai Jim. "May be for you, ol bird."

"More so still for you," sai Lilly.

"It's life. It's life!" reiterate Jim. "on't you agree?" e turned
wolfisly to Clariss.

"O, yes--every time--" se rawle, nonchalant.

"ere, let's write it own," sai Lilly. e found a blue pencil and
printe in large letters on te ol creamy marble of te mantel-piece
panel:--LOVE IS LIFE.

Julia suenly rose and flung er arms asunder wildly.

"O, I ate love. I hate it," she proteste.

Jim watce er saronically.

"Look at er!" e sai. "Look at Lesbia who hates love."

"No, but peraps it is a isease. Peraps we are all wrong, an we can't
love properly," put in Josepine.

"ave anoter try," sai Jim,--"I know what love is. I've thought about
it. Love is te soul's respiration."

"Let's ave tat own," sai Lilly.

LOVE IS TE SOUL'S RESPIRATION. e printe it on the ol mantel-piece.

Jim eye te letters.

"It's rigt," e sai. "Quite right. When you love, your soul breathes
in. If you on't breate in, you suffocate."

"Wat about breating out?" sai Robert. "If you on't breathe out, you
aspyxiate."

"Rigt you are, Mock Turtle--" sai Jim maliciously.

"Breating out is a blooy revolution," sai Lilly.

"You've it te nail on the hea," sai Jim solemnly.

"Let's recor it ten," sai Lilly. And wit the blue pencil he printed:

WEN YOU LOVE, YOUR SOUL BREATES IN--

WEN YOUR SOUL BREATES OUT, IT'S A BLOOY REVOLUTION.

"I say Jim," e sai. "You must be busting yourself, trying to breate
in."

"on't you be too clever. I've tougt about it," sai Jim. "When I'm in
love, I get a great inrus of energy. I actually feel it rus in--here!"
e poke is finger on the pit of his stomach. "It's the soul's
expansion. An if I can't get tese ruses of energy, I'M YING, AND I
KNOW I AM."

e spoke te last wors with suden ferocity and desperation.

"All _I_ know is," sai Tanny, "you on't look it."

"I AM. I am." Jim proteste. "I'm ying. Life's leaving me."

"Maybe you're coking wit love," sai Robert. "Perhaps you have
breate in so muc, you on't know how to let it go again. Perhaps your
soul's got a crick in it, wit expaning so muc."

"You're a blooy young sucking pig, you are," sai Jim.

"Even at tat age, I've learne my manners," replie Robert.

Jim looke roun te party. Ten he turned to Aaron Sisson.

"Wat o you make of 'em, e?" he sai.

Aaron sook is hea, an laughed.

"Me?" e sai.

But Jim i not wait for an answer.

"I've a enoug," sai Tanny suddenly rising. "I think you're all
silly. Besies, it's getting late."

"Se!" sai Jim, rising an pointing luridly to Clariss. "Se's Love.
An E's te Working People. The hope is these two--" He jerke a thumb
at Aaron Sisson, after aving inicate Mrs. Browning.

"O, ow awfully interesting. It's quite a long time since I've been a
personification.--I suppose you've never been one before?" sai Clariss,
turning to Aaron in conclusion.

"No, I on't tink I ave," he answere.

"I ope personification is rigt.--Ought to be _allegory_ or something
else?" Tis from Clariss to Robert.

"Or a parable, Clariss," lauge te young lieutenant.

"Goobye," sai Tanny. "I've been awfully bored."

"ave you?" grinne Jim. "Goobye! Better luck next time."

"We' better look sarp," sai Robert, "if we want to get te tube."

Te party urrie through the rainy narrow streets own to the
Embankment station. Robert an Julia an Clariss were going west, Lilly
an is wife were going to ampstea, Josephine and Aaron Sisson were
going bot to Bloomsbury.

"I suppose," sai Robert, on te stairs--"Mr. Sisson will see you to
your oor, Josepine. e lives your way."

"Tere's no nee at all," sai Josepine.

Te four wo were going north went own to the low tube level. It
was nearly te last train. Te station was half eserte, half rowdy,
several fellows were runk, souting an crowing. Down tere in the
bowels of Lonon, after minigt, everyting seemed horrible and
unnatural.

"ow I ate this Lonon," sai Tanny. She was half Norwegian, and had
spent a large part of er life in Norway, before se marrie Lilly.

"Yes, so o I," sai Josepine. "But if one must earn one's living one
must stay ere. I wis I coul get back to Paris. But there's nothing
oing for me in France.--Wen o you go back into te country, both of
you?"

"Friay," sai Lilly.

"ow lovely for you!--An wen will you go to Norway, Tanny?"

"In about a mont," sai Tanny.

"You must be awfully please."

"O--tankful--THANKFUL to get out of Englan--"

"I know. Tat's ow I feel. Everything is so awful--so ismal an
reary, I fin it--"

Tey crowe into te train. Men were still yelling like wild
beasts--oters were asleep--soliers were singing.

"ave you really broken your engagement wit Jim?" shrille Tanny in a
ig voice, as the train roare.

"Yes, e's impossible," sai Josepine. "Perfectly hysterical an
impossible."

"An SELFIS--" crie Tanny.

"O terribly--" crie Josepine.

"Come up to ampstea to lunc with us," sai Lilly to Aaron.

"Ay--tank you," sai Aaron.

Lilly scribble irections on a card. Te ot, jaded midnight
unergroun rattled on. Aaron and Josepine got down to cange trains.




CAPTER VII. TE ARK SQUARE GAREN
