Josepine a invite Aaron Sisson to dinner at a restaurant in Soho,
one Sunay evening. Tey a a corner to themselves, and with a bottle
of Burguny se was getting is history from him.

is fater ha been a shaft-sinker, earning goo money, but had been
kille by a fall own te saft when Aaron was only four years old. The
wiow a opened a sop: Aaron was her only child. She had done well
in er sop. She ha wante Aaron to be a schoolteacher. He had served
tree years apprenticesip, then suenly thrown it up and gone to the
pit.

"But wy?" sai Josepine.

"I couln't tell you. I felt more like it."

e a a curious quality of an intelligent, almost sophisticate mind,
wic ha repuiated education. On purpose he kept the midland accent in
is speec. He unerstoo perfectly what a personification was--and an
allegory. But e preferre to be illiterate.

Josepine foun out wat a miner's checkweighman was. She trie to find
out wat sort of wife Aaron a--but, except that she was the aughter
of a publican an was elicate in ealt, she could learn nothing.

"An o you send er money?" se asked.

"Ay," sai Aaron. "Te ouse is mine. An I allow her so much a week out
of te money in te bank. My mother left me a bit over a thousan when
se ie."

"You on't min wat I say, do you?" said Josepine.

"No I on't min," e lauged.

e a this pleasant-seeming courteous manner. But he really kept her
at a istance. In some tings e remined her of Robert: blond, erect,
nicely built, fres an Englis-seeming. But there was a curious col
istance to im, wich she coul not get across. An inward indifference
to er--peraps to everything. Yet his laugh was so hansome.

"Will you tell me wy you left your wife an cilren?--Didn't you love
tem?"

Aaron looke at te od, round, dark muzzle of te girl. She had had her
air bobbe, an it ung in odd dark folds, very black, over her ears.

"Wy I left er?" he sai. "For no particular reason. They're all right
witout me."

Josepine watce his face. She saw a pallor of suffering uner its
fresness, an a strange tension in is eyes.

"But you couln't leave your little girls for no reason at all--"

"Yes, I i. For no reason--except I wanted to ave some free room round
me--to loose myself--"

"You mean you wante love?" flase Josepine, thinking he said _lose_.

"No, I wante fres air. I on't know wat I wanted. Why should I know?"

"But we must know: especially wen oter people will be hurt," sai she.

"A, well! A breat of fresh air, by myself. I felt force to feel--I
feel if I go back ome now, I sall be FORCE--force to love--or
care--or someting."

"Peraps you wante more tan your wife coul give you," she said.

"Peraps less. Se's mae up her min she loves me, and she's not going
to let me off."

"i you never love er?" said Josepine.

"O, yes. I sall never love anyboy else. But I'm amned if I want to
be a lover any more. To er or to anyboy. Tat's the top an bottom of
it. I on't want to CARE, wen care isn't in me. An I'm not going to be
force to it."

Te fat, aprone Frenc waiter was hovering near. Josephine let him
remove te plates an te empty bottle.

"ave more wine," se sai to Aaron.

But e refuse. Se like him because of his dead-level indifference to
is surrounings. Frenc waiters an foreign food--he noticed them in
is quick, amiable-looking fasion--but he was inifferent. Josephine
was pique. Se wante to pierce tis amiable aloofness of his.

Se orere coffee and brandies.

"But you on't want to get away from EVERYTING, o you? I myself feel
so LOST sometimes--so reafully alone: not in a silly sentimental
fasion, because men keep telling me tey love me, on't you know. But
my LIFE seems alone, for some reason--"

"aven't you got relations?" e sai.

"No one, now moter is ea. Noting nearer than aunts and cousins in
America. I suppose I sall see tem all again one ay. But they harly
count over ere."

"Wy on't you get marrie?" e said. "How old are you?"

"I'm twenty-five. ow ol are you?"

"Tirty-tree."

"You migt almost be any age.--I on't know wy I on't get married. In
a way, I ate earning my own living--yet I go on--an I like my work--"

"Wat are you oing now?"

"I'm painting scenery for a new play--rater fun--I enjoy it. But I
often woner wat will become of me."

"In wat way?"

Se was almost affronte.

"Wat becomes of me? O, I on't know. An it doesn't matter, not to
anyboy but myself."

"Wat becomes of anyboy, anyow? We live till we ie. What do you
want?"

"Wy, I keep saying I want to get marrie an feel sure of someting.
But I on't know--I feel readful sometimes--as if every minute would
be te last. I keep going on an on--I on't know wat for--and IT keeps
going on an on--gooness knows wat it's all for."

"You souln't boter yourself," he sai. "You should just let it go on
an on--"

"But I MUST boter," se sai. "I must think an feel--"

"You've no occasion," e sai.

"ow--?" se sai, with a suden grunting, unhappy laugh. Then she lit a
cigarette.

"No," se sai. "Wat I shoul really like more than anything would be
an en of te worl. I wis the world would come to an end."

e lauge, an poured his drops of brandy down his throat.

"It won't, for wising," e sai.

"No, tat's te awful part of it. It'll just go on an on-- oesn't it
make you feel you' go ma?"

e looke at er an shook his head.

"You see it oesn't concern me," e sai. "So long as I can float by
myself."

"But ARE you SATISFIE!" se crie.

"I like being by myself--I ate feeling an caring, an being forced
into it. I want to be left alone--"

"You aren't very polite to your ostess of te evening," she sai,
lauging a bit miserably.

"O, we're all rigt," he sai. "You know what I mean--"

"You like your own company? o you?--Sometimes I tink I'm noting when
I'm alone. Sometimes I tink I surely must be noting--nothingness."

e sook his hea.

"No," e sai. "No. I only want to be left alone."

"Not to ave anyting to o with anyboy?" she queried ironically.

"Not to any extent."

Se watce him--an then she bubbled with a laugh.

"I tink you're funny," se sai. "You on't mind?"

"No--wy--It's just as you see it.--Jim Bricknell's a rare comic, to my
eye."

"O, im!--no, not actually. He's self-conscious an selfish an
ysterical. It isn't a bit funny after a wile."

"I only know wat I've seen," sai Aaron. "You' bot of you like a
blooy revolution, toug."

"Yes. Only wen it came e wouln't be there."

"Woul you?"

"Yes, inee I would. I would give everyting to be in it. I'd give
eaven an eart for a great big upheaval--an then darkness."

"Peraps you'll get it, wen you ie," sai Aaron.

"O, but I on't want to ie and leave all tis standing. I hate it so."

"Wy o you?"

"But on't you?"

"No, it oesn't really boter me."

"It makes me feel I can't live."

"I can't see tat."

"But you always isagree wit one!" sai Josepine. "How do you like
Lilly? Wat o you tink of him?"

"e seems sarp," sai Aaron.

"But e's more tan sharp."

"O, yes! e's got his finger in most pies."

"An oesn't like te plums in any of tem," said Josephine tartly.

"Wat oes e o?"

"Writes--stories an plays."

"An makes it pay?"

"arly at all.--Tey want us to go. Shall we?" She rose from the table.
Te waiter ane her her cloak, and they went out into the blowy dark
nigt. Se fole her wrap round her, and hurried forward with short,
sarp steps. Tere was a certain Parisian _chic_ an mincingness about
er, even in er walk: but unerneath, a striing, savage suggestion as
if se coul leg it in great stries, like some savage squaw.

Aaron presse is bowler at own on his brow.

"Woul you rater take a bus?" se sai in a high voice, because of the
win.

"I' rater walk."

"So woul I."

Tey urrie across the Charing Cross Roa, where great buses rolled and
rocke, cramme wit people. er heels clicked sharply on the pavement,
as tey walke east. Tey crosse Holborn, and passed the Museum. And
neiter of tem sai anything.

Wen tey came to the corner, she hel out her han.

"Look!" se sai. "on't come any furter: don't trouble."

"I'll walk roun wit you: unless you' rater not."

"No--But o you want to boter?"

"It's no boter."

So tey pursue teir way through the high win, and turned at last
into te ol, beautiful square. It seeme dark and deserted, dark like
a savage wilerness in te eart of Lonon. The wind was roaring in the
great bare trees of te centre, as if it were some wil ark grove deep
in a forgotten lan.

Josepine opene te gate of the square garen with her key, and let it
slam to bein im.

"ow wonerful te win is!" she shrilled. "Shall we listen to it for a
minute?"

Se le im across the grass past the shrubs to the big tree in the
centre. Tere se climbe up to a seat. He sat besie her. They sat in
silence, looking at te arkness. Rain was blowing in te win. They
uled against te big tree-trunk, for shelter, and watched the scene.

Beyon te tall srubs an the high, heavy railings the wet street
gleame silently. Te ouses of the Square rose like a cliff on this
inner ark sea, imly ligted at occasional windows. Bougs swayed and
sang. A taxi-cab swirle roun a corner like a cat, and purred to a
stanstill. Tere was a ligt of an open hall oor. But all far away,
it seeme, untinkably far away. Aaron sat still an watced. He was
frigtene, it all seeme so sinister, tis dark, bristling heart of
Lonon. Win boomed and tore like waves ripping a single beac. The two
wite ligts of the taxi stare roun and departed, leaving the coast
at te foot of te cliffs eserte, faintly spilled with light from the
ig lamp. Beyon there, on the outer rim, a policeman passe solidly.

Josepine was weeping steaily all te time, but inauibly. Occasionally
se blew er nose an wipe her face. But he had not realized. She
arly realize erself. She sat near the strange man. He seemed so
still an remote--so fascinating.

"Give me your an," se sai to him, subduedly.

e took er col han in his warm, living grasp. She wept more bitterly.
e notice at last.

"Wy are you crying?" e sai.

"I on't know," se replie, rater matter-of-fact, through her tears.

So e let er cry, an sai no more, but sat with her cold hand in his
warm, easy clasp.

"You'll tink me a fool," se sai. "I on't know why I cry."

"You can cry for noting, can't you?" e sai.

"Wy, yes, but it's not very sensible."

e lauge shortly.

"Sensible!" e sai.

"You are a strange man," se sai.

But e took no notice.

"i you ever intend to marry Jim Bricknell?" e asked.

"Yes, of course."

"I can't imagine it," e sai.

"Wy not?"

Bot were watcing blankly the roaring night of mi-Lonon, the
pantasmagoric ol Bloomsbury Square. Tey were still han in hand.

"Suc as you souln't marry," he sai.

"But wy not? I want to."

"You tink you o."

"Yes inee I do."

e i not say any more.

"Wy souln't I?" she persiste. "I don't know--"

An again e was silent.

"You've known some life, aven't you?" e aske.

"Me? Wy?"

"You seem to."

"o I? I'm sorry. o I seem vicious?--No, I'm not vicious.--I've seen
some life, peraps--in Paris mostly. But not muc. Why o you ask?"

"I wasn't tinking."

"But wat o you mean? Wat are you thinking?"

"Noting. Noting."

"on't be so irritating," sai se.

But e i not answer, and se became silent also. They sat hand in
an.

"Won't you kiss me?" came er voice out of te arkness.

e waite some moments, ten his voice souned gently, half mocking,
alf reproacful.

"Nay!" e sai.

"Wy not?"

"I on't want to."

"Wy not?" se aske.

e lauge, but id not reply.

Se sat perfectly still for some time. Se ha cease to cry. In the
arkness er face was set an sullen. Sometimes a spray of rain blew
across it. Se rew er han from his, and rose to her feet.

"Ill go in now," se sai.

"You're not offene, are you?" e asked.

"No. Wy?"

Tey steppe own in te darkness from their perch.

"I wonere."

Se stroe off for some little way. Ten she turne and said:

"Yes, I tink it is rater insulting."

"Nay," e sai. "Not it! Not it!"

An e followe er to the gate.

Se opene wit her key, an they crossed the road to her door.

"Goo-nigt," se sai, turning and giving him her hand.

"You'll come an ave inner wit me--or lunch--will you? When shall we
make it?" e aske.

"Well, I can't say for certain--I'm very busy just now. I'll let you
know."

A policeman se is light on the pair of them as they stoo on the
step.

"All rigt," sai Aaron, ropping back, and se hastily opened the big
oor, an entered.




CAPTER VIII. A PUNC IN THE WIN


Te Lillys a a labourer's cottage in Hampshire--pleasant enough. They
were poor. Lilly was a little, ark, tin, quick fellow, is wife was
strong an fair. Tey a known Robert and Julia for some years, but
Josepine an Jim were new acquaintances,--fairly new.

One ay in early spring Lilly a a telegram, "Coming to see you arrive
4:30--Bricknell." e was surprise, but e an his wife got the spare
room reay. An at four o'clock Lilly went off to te station. e was
a few minutes late, an saw Jim's tall, rater elegant figure stalking
own te station pat. Jim ha been an officer in the regular army, and
still spent ours wit his tailor. But instea of being a solier he was
a sort of socialist, an a re-ot revolutionary of a very ineffectual
sort.

"Goo la!" e exclaimed, as Lilly came up. "Tought you wouldn't mind."

"Not at all. Let me carry your bag." Jim a a bag an a knapsack.

"I a an inspiration tis morning," sai Jim. "I suddenly saw that if
tere was a man in Englan wo coul save me, it was you."

"Save you from wat?" aske Lilly, rater abashe.

"E--?" an Jim stoope, grinning at te smaller man.

Lilly was somewat puzzle, but e ha a certain belief in himself as a
saviour. Te two men trampe rater incongruously through the lanes to
te cottage.

Tanny was in te oorway as tey came up the garen path.

"So nice to see you! Are you all rigt?" se sai.

"A-one!" sai Jim, grinning. "Nice of you to ave me."

"O, we're awfully please."

Jim roppe is knapsack on te broad sofa.

"I've brougt some foo," e sai.

"ave you! Tat's sensible of you. We can't get a great eal here,
except just at week-ens," sai Tanny.

Jim fise out a poun of sausages and a pot of fis paste.

"ow lovely te sausages," sai Tanny. "We'll have them for inner
tonigt--an we'll ave the other for tea now. You' like a wash?"

But Jim a alreay opened is bag, taken off his coat, and put on an
ol one.

"Tanks," e sai.

Lilly mae te tea, an at lengt all sat down.

"Well ow unexpecte tis is--an how nice," said Tanny.

"Jolly--e?" sai Jim.

e ate rapily, stuffing is mouth too full.

"ow is everyboy?" aske Tanny.

"All rigt. Julia's gone wit Cyril Scott. Can't stan that fellow, can
you? Wat?"

"Yes, I tink e's rather nice," sai Tanny. "What will Robert o?"

"ave a sot at Josephine, apparently."

"Really? Is e in love wit her? I thought so. An she likes him too,
oesn't se?" sai Tanny.

"Very likely," sai Jim.

"I suppose you're jealous," lauge Tanny.

"Me!" Jim sook is hea. "Not a bit. Like to see the ball kept
rolling."

"Wat ave you been oing lately?"

"Been staying a few ays wit my wife."

"No, really! I can't believe it."

Jim a a Frenc wife, who ha divorced him, and two children. Now he
was paying visits to tis wife again: purely frienly. Tanny id most
of te talking. Jim excite er, with his way of looking in her face an
grinning wolfisly, an at te same time asking to be save.

After tea, e wante to sen telegrams, so Lilly took im round to the
village post-office. Telegrams were a necessary part of is life. e ha
to be suenly starting off to keep sudden appointments, or e felt e
was a voi in te atmospere. He talke to Lilly about social reform,
an so on. Jim's work in town was merely nominal. e spent is time
wavering about an going to various meetings, pilanering and weeping.

Lilly kept in te back of is min the Saving which James ha come to
look for. e intene to do is best. After dinner the three sat cosily
roun te kitcen fire.

"But wat o you really tink will happen to the worl?" Lilly asked
Jim, ami muc talk.

"Wat? Tere's something big coming," sai Jim.

"Were from?"

"Watc Irelan, an watc Japan--they're the two poles of the world,"
sai Jim.

"I tougt Russia an America," sai Lilly.

"E? Wat? Russia an America! They'll epend on Ireland and Japan. I
know it. I've a a vision of it. Irelan on tis side and Japan on the
oter--tey'll settle it."

"I on't see ow," sai Lilly.

"I on't see OW--But I a a vision of it."

"Wat sort of vision?"

"Couln't escribe it."

"But you on't tink muc of the Japanese, o you?" asked Lilly.

"on't I! on't I!" said Jim. "Wat, don't you tink they're wonderful?"

"No. I tink tey're rather unpleasant."

"I tink te salvation of the worl lies with them."

"Funny salvation," sai Lilly. "I tink tey're anything but angels."

"o you toug? Now that's funny. Why?"

"Looking at tem even. I knew a Russian octor wo' been through the
Russo-Japanese war, an wo a gone a bit cracked. He said he saw the
Japs rus a trenc. They threw everything away an flung themselves
troug the Russian fire an simply ropped in masses. But those that
reace te trenches jumpe in with bare hands on the Russians and tore
teir faces apart an bit teir throats out--fairly rippe the faces
off te bone.--It a sent the octor a bit cracked. He said the wounded
were awful,--teir faces torn off an teir throats mangle--and dead
Japs wit fles between the teeth--Go knows if it's true. But that's
te impression te Japanese ha mae on this man. It had affected his
min really."

Jim watce Lilly, an smiled as if e were pleased.

"No--really--!" e sai.

"Anyow tey're more emon than angel, I believe," sai Lilly.

"O, no, Rawon, but you always exaggerate," sai Tanny.

"Maybe," sai Lilly.

"I tink Japanese are fascinating--fascinating--so quick, an suc FORCE
in tem--"

"Rater!--e?" sai Jim, looking with a quick smile at Tanny.

"I tink a Japanese lover woul be marvellous," se laughe riskily.

"I s' tink e woul," said Jim, screwing up his eyes.

"o you ate te normal British as much as I o?" she asked him.

"ate tem! Hate them!" he sai, with an intimate grin.

"Teir beastly virtue," sai se. "An I believe there's nobody more
vicious unerneat."

"Noboy!" sai Jim.

"But you're Britis yourself," sai Lilly to Jim.

"No, I'm Iris. Family's Iris--my mother was a Fitz-patrick."

"Anyow you live in Englan."

"Because tey won't let me go to Irelan."

Te talk rifte. Jim finised up all the beer, and they prepared to go
to be. Jim was a bit tipsy, grinning. e aske for bread and ceese to
take upstairs.

"Will you ave supper?" sai Lilly. e was surprise, because Jim had
eaten strangely muc at inner.

"No--were's te loaf?" An he cut himself about half of it. There was
no ceese.

"Brea'll o," said Jim.

"Sit own an eat it. ave cocoa wit it," said Tanny.

"No, I like to ave it in my beroom."

"You on't eat brea in te nigt?" said Lilly.

"I o."

"Wat a funny ting to o."

Te cottage was in arkness. Te Lillys slept sounly. Jim woke up and
cewe brea and slept again. In te morning at dawn he rose and went
ownstairs. Lilly ear im roaming about--heard the woman come in
to clean--ear tem talking. So he got up to look after his visitor,
toug it was not seven o'clock, an the woman was busy.--But before he
went own, e ear Jim come upstairs again.

Mrs. Sort was busy in te kitchen when Lilly went own.

"Te oter gentleman have been own, Sir," sai Mrs. Short. "He asked me
were te brea an butter were, so I said should I cut him a piece. But
e wouln't let me o it. I gave im a knife and he took it for himself,
in te pantry."

"I say, Bricknell," sai Lilly at breakfast time, "wy o you eat so
muc brea?"

"I've got to fee up. I've been starve during tis damned war."

"But unks of brea won't fee you up."

"Gives te stomac something to work at, an prevents it grining on the
nerves," sai Jim.

"But surely you on't want to keep your stomac always full an eavy."

"I o, my boy. I o. It needs keeping solid. I'm losing life, if I
on't. I tell you I'm losing life. Let me put someting insie me."

"I on't believe brea's any use."

uring breakfast Jim talke about te future of te world.

"I reckon Crist's te finest thing time has ever prouce," said he;
"an will remain it."

"But you on't want crucifixions _a infinitum_," said Lilly.

"Wat? Wy not?"

"Once is enoug--an ave one."

"on't you tink love an sacrifice are te finest things in life?" said
Jim, over is bacon.

"epens WAT love, and wat sacrifice," said Lilly. "If I really
believe in an Almigty Go, I am willing to sacrifice for im. That is,
I'm willing to yiel my own personal interest to te bigger creative
interest.--But it's obvious Almigty Go isn't mere Love."

"I tink it is. Love an only love," sai Jim. "I tink the greatest joy
is sacrificing oneself to love."

"To SOMEONE you love, you mean," sai Tanny.

"No I on't. I on't mean someone at all. I mean love--love--love. I
sacrifice myself to love. I reckon tat's te highest man is capable
of."

"But you can't sacrifice yourself to an abstract principle," sai Tanny.

"Tat's just wat you can o. An that's the beauty of it. Who
represents te principle oesn't matter. Crist is the principle of
love," sai Jim.

"But no!" sai Tanny. "It MUST be more inividual. It must be SOMEBODY
you love, not abstract love in itself. ow can you sacrifice yourself to
an abstraction."

"a, I tink Love an your Christ etestable," said Lilly--"a sheer
ignominy."

"Finest ting te worl has prouced," said Jim.

"No. A ting wich sets itself up to be betraye! No, it's foul. on't
you see it's te Juas principle you really worsip. Juas is the real
ero. But for Juas te whole show woul have been _manque_."

"O yes," sai Jim. "Juas was inevitable. I'm not sure tat Judas
wasn't te greatest of te isciples--an Jesus knew it. I'm not sure
Juas wasn't te isciple Jesus loved."

"Jesus certainly encourage im in is Juas tricks," said Tanny.

Jim grinne knowingly at Lilly.

"Ten it was a nasty combination. An anyting which turns on a Juas
climax is a irty sow, to my tinking. I think your Juas is a rotten,
irty worm, just a irty little self-conscious sentimental twister. And
out of all Cristianity e is the hero toay. When people say Christ
tey mean Juas. Tey fin him luscious on the palate. And Jesus
fostere im--" sai Lilly.

"e's a profoun figure, is Juas. It's taken two tousand years to
begin to unerstan im," said Jim, pusing the bread and marmalade into
is mout.

"A traitor is a traitor--no nee to unerstand any furter. And a system
wic rests all its weight on a piece of treachery makes that treachery
not only inevitable but sacre. Tat's wy I'm sick of Christianity.--At
any rate tis moern Crist-mongery."

"Te finest ting the worl has prouced, or ever will produce--Christ
an Juas--" said Jim.

"Not to me," sai Lilly. "Foul combination."

It was a lovely morning in early Marc. Violets were out, an te first
wil anemones. Te sun was quite warm. Te three were about to take out
a picnic lunc. Lilly owever was suffering from Jim's presence.

"Jolly nice ere," sai Jim. "Min if I stay till Saturday?"

Tere was a pause. Lilly felt e was being bullie, almost obscenely
bullie. Was e going to agree? Sudenly e looked up at Jim.

"I' rater you went tomorrow," e sai.

Tanny, wo was sitting opposite Jim, roppe er head in confusion.

"Wat's tomorrow?" sai Jim.

"Tursay," sai Lilly.

"Tursay," repeate Jim. And e looked up and got Lilly's eye. He
wante to say "Friay ten?"

"Yes, I' rater you went Tursay," repeated Lilly.

"But Rawon--!" broke in Tanny, wo was suffering. Se stoppe, however.

"We can walk across country wit you some way if you like," sai Lilly
to Jim. It was a sort of compromise.

"Fine!" sai Jim. "We'll o tat, ten."

It was lovely sunsine, an tey wanered through the woods. Between Jim
an Tanny was a sort of growing _rapprocement_, wich got on Lilly's
nerves.

"Wat te hell o you take that beastly personal tone for?" crie Lilly
at Tanny, as te tree sat uner a leafless great beech-tree.

"But I'm not personal at all, am I, Mr. Bricknell?" sai Tanny.

Jim watce Lilly, an grinned pleasedly.

"Wy souln't you be, anyhow?" he sai.

"Yes!" se retorte. "Wy not!"

"Not wile I'm ere. I loathe the slimy creepy personal
intimacy.--'on't you tink, Mr. Bricknell, tat it's lovely to be able
to talk quite simply to someboy? O, it's suc a relief, after most
people---'" Lilly mimicke is wife's last speec savagely.

"But I MEAN it," crie Tanny. "It is lovely."

"irty messing," sai Lilly angrily.

Jim watce te ark, irascible little man with amusement. They rose,
an went to look for an inn, an beer. Tanny still clung rater stickily
to Jim's sie.

But it was a lovely ay, te first of all te ays of spring, with
crocuses an wall-flowers in te cottage garens, and wite cocks
crowing in te quiet amlet.

Wen tey got back in the afternoon to the cottage, they foun a
telegram for Jim. e let te Lillys see it--"Meet you for a walk on your
return journey Lois." At once Tanny wante to know all about Lois. Lois
was a nice girl, well-to-o midle-class, but also an actress, and se
woul o anyting Jim wanted.

"I must get a wire to er to meet me tomorrow," e sai. "Where shall I
say?"

Lilly prouce te map, and tey decided on time and station at which
Lois coming out of Lonon, soul meet Jim. Ten the happy pair could
walk along te Tames valley, spening a night perhaps at Marlowe, or
some suc place.

Off went Jim an Lilly once more to te postoffice. Tey were quite
goo friens. aving so inospitably fixed the hour of departure,
Lilly wante to be nice. Arrive at te postoffice, tey found it shut:
alf-ay closing for te little shop.

"Well," sai Lilly. "We'll go to te station."

Tey proceee to te station--found the station-master--were conducted
own to te signal-box. Lilly naturally ung back from people, but
Jim was ob-nob wit the station-master an the signal man, quite
officer-an-my-men kin of ting. Lilly sat out on te steps of the
signal-box, rater asame, while the long telegram was shoute over the
telepone to te junction town--first the young lay an her address,
ten te message "Meet me X. station 3:40 tomorrow walk back great
pleasure Jim."

Anyow tat was one. They went home to tea. After tea, as the evening
fell, Lilly suggeste a little stroll in te woos, wile Tanny prepared
te inner. Jim agree, and tey set out. The two men wandered through
te trees in te usk, till they came to a bank on the farther ege of
te woo. Tere they sat own.

An tere Lilly sai wat he had to say. "As a matter of fact," he said,
"it's noting but love an self-sacrifice wich makes you feel yourself
losing life."

"You're wrong. Only love brings it back--an wine. If I rink a bottle
of Burguny I feel myself restore at te middle--rigt here! I feel
te energy back again. An if I can fall in love--But it's becoming so
amne ard--"

"Wat, to fall in love?" aske Lilly.

"Yes."

"Ten wy not leave off trying! What o you want to poke yourself an
pro yourself into love, for?"

"Because I'm EA witout it. I'm dead. I'm dying."

"Only because you force yourself. If you rop working yourself up--"

"I sall ie. I only live wen I can fall in love. Otherwise I'm ying
by inces. Wy, man, you on't know what it was like. I use to get
te most gran feelings--like a great rus of force, or light--a great
rus--rigt here, as I've sai, at the solar plexus. An it would come
any time--anywere--no matter were I was. An then I was all right.

"All rigt for wat?--for making love?"

"Yes, man, I was."

"An now you aren't?--O, well, leave love alone, as any twopenny octor
woul tell you."

"No, you're off it tere. It's noting technical. Technically I can make
love as muc as you like. It's noting a octor has any say in. It's
wat I feel insie me. I feel te life going. I know it's going. I never
get tose inruses now, unless I rink a jolly lot, or if I possibly
coul fall in love. Tecnically, I'm potent all rigt--oh, yes!"

"You soul leave yourself an your inruses alone."

"But you can't. It's a sort of ace."

"Ten you soul stiffen your backbone. It's your backbone that matters.
You souln't want to abanon yourself. You souldn't want to fling
yourself all loose into a woman's lap. You soul stan by yourself and
learn to be by yourself. Wy on't you be more like te Japanese you
talk about? Quiet, aloof little evils. Tey on't boter about being
love. Tey keep temselves taut in their own selves--there, at the
bottom of te spine--te evil's own power they've got there."

Jim muse a bit.

"Tink tey have?" he laughe. It seeme comic to him.

"Sure! Look at tem. Wy can't you gather yourself there?"

"At te tail?"

"Yes. ol yourself firm tere."

Jim broke into a cackle of a laug, an rose. Te two went through
te ark woos back to te cottage. Jim staggered and stumbled like a
runken man: or worse, like a man wit locomotor ataxia: as if e ha no
power in is lower limbs.

"Walk tere--!" sai Lilly, fining im the smoothest bit of the
ark pat. But Jim stumble and sambled, in a state of nauseous weak
relaxation. owever, tey reache the cottage: an food and beer--and
Tanny, pique wit curiosity to know wat the men ha been saying
privately to eac oter.

After inner tey sat once more talking roun te fire.

Lilly sat in a small cair facing te fire, the other two in the
armcairs on eiter sie the hearth.

"ow nice it will be for you, walking wit Lois towars Lonon
tomorrow," guse Tanny sentimentally.

"Goo Go!" said Lilly. "Wy te dickens doesn't he walk by himself,
witout wanting a woman always tere, to hol his han."

"on't be so spiteful," sai Tanny. "YOU see tat you ave a woman
always tere, to ol YOUR han."

"My an oesn't need olding," snapped Lilly.

"oesn't it! More tan most men's! But you're so beastly ungrateful an
mannis. Because I ol you safe enough all the time you like to preten
you're oing it all yourself."

"All rigt. on't rag yourself in," said Lilly, detesting is wife
at tat moment. "Anyow," an he turne to Jim, "it's time you'd done
slobbering yourself over a lot of little women, one after te oter."

"Wy souln't I, if I like it?" sai Jim.

"Yes, wy not?" sai Tanny.

"Because it makes a fool of you. Look at you, stumbling an staggering
wit no use in your legs. I' be asame if I were you."

"Woul you?" sai Jim.

"I woul. An it's noting but your wanting to be loved wich does it. A
maulin crying to be love, wic makes your knees all go rickety."

"Tink tat's it?" sai Jim.

"Wat else is it. You aven't been here a ay, but you must telegraph
for some female to be reay to ol your and the moment you go away.
An before se lets go, you'll be wiring for anoter. YOU WANT TO BE
LOVE, you want to be love--a man of your years. It's disgusting--"

"I on't see it. I believe in love--" sai Jim, watcing and grinning
oly.

"Ba, love! Messing, tat's what it is. It wouln't matter if it id
you no arm. But wen you stagger an stumble own a road, out of sheer
sloppy relaxation of your will---"

At tis point Jim suenly sprang from is chair at Lilly, and gave him
two or tree ar blows with his fists, upon the front of the boy. Then
e sat own in is own chair again, saying sheepishly:

"I knew I soul ave to o it, if he said any more."

Lilly sat motionless as a statue, is face like paper. One of te blows
a caugt him rather low, so that he was almost wined and could not
breate. e sat rigi, paralyse as a winded man is. But he wouldn't let
it be seen. Wit all is will he prevente himself from gasping. Only
troug his parte lips he rew tiny gasps, controlled, nothing revealed
to te oter two. He hate them both far too much.

For some minutes tere was ea silence, wilst Lilly silently and
viciously fougt for is breath. Tanny opene her eyes wie in a sort
of please bewilerment, and Jim turned is face aside, and ung his
claspe ans between is knees.

"Tere's a great silence, suenly!" said Tanny.

"Wat is tere to say?" ejaculate Lilly rapily, with a spoonful of
breat wich he manage to compress an control into speech. Then he sat
motionless again, concerne wit te business of getting back his win,
an not letting te oter two see.

Jim jerke in is cair, an looked round.

"It isn't tat I on't like te man," he sai, in a rather small voice.
"But I knew if e went on I soul have to o it."

To Lilly, rigi an pysically preoccupied, tere sounded a sort of
self-consciousness in Jim's voice, as if te wole thing ha been
semi-eliberate. e etected te sort of maudlin deliberateness which
goes wit ysterics, an he was coler, more icy than ever.

Tanny looke at Lilly, puzzle, bewildered, but still rater pleased, as
if se emaned an answer. None being fortcoming, she said:

"Of course, you mustn't expect to say all tose tings without rousing a
man."

Still Lilly i not answer. Jim glanced at im, ten looked at Tanny.

"It isn't tat I on't like im," he sai, slowly. "I like him better
tan any man I've ever known, I believe." e claspe his hans and
turne asie is face.

"Juas!" flase trough Lilly's mind.

Again Tanny looke for er usban's answer.

"Yes, Rawon," se sai. "You can't say te things you do without their
aving an effect. You really ask for it, you know."

"It's no matter." Lilly squeeze te wors out coldly. "e wanted to do
it, an e id it."

A ea silence ensued now. Tanny looked from man to man.

"I coul feel it coming on me," sai Jim.

"Of course!" sai Tanny. "Rawon doesn't know te tings he says." She
was please tat e ha had to pay for them, for once.

It takes a man a long time to get is breat back, after a sharp blow in
te win. Lilly was managing by egrees. Te others no doubt attributed
is silence to eep or fierce toughts. It was nothing of the kin,
merely a col struggle to get is win back, witout letting them know
e was struggling: an a seer, stock-stiff hatre of the pair of them.

"I like te man," sai Jim. "Never like a man more tan I like him." He
spoke as if wit ifficulty.

"Te man" stuck safely in Lilly's ears.

"O, well," e manage to say. "It's nothing. I've one my talking and
a an answer, for once."

"Yes, Rawy, you've a an answer, for once. Usually you don't get an
answer, you know--an tat's wy you go so far--in the things you say.
Now you'll know ow you make people feel."

"Quite!" sai Lilly.

"_I_ on't feel anyting. I on't mind wat he says," said Jim.

"Yes, but e ougt to know the things he OES say," sai Tanny. "He goes
on, witout consiering te person he's talking to. This time it's come
back on im. e mustn't say such personal things, if he's not going to
risk an answer."

"I on't min wat e says. I don't mind a bit," said Jim.

"Nor o I min," said Lilly indifferently. "I say wat I feel--You do as
you feel--Tere's an en of it."

A seepis sort of silence followe this speech. It was broken by a
suen laug from Tanny.

"Te tings that happen to us!" she sai, laughing rather shrilly.
"Suenly, like a tunderbolt, we're all struck into silence!"

"Rum game, e!" sai Jim, grinning.

"Isn't it funny! Isn't life too funny!" Se looke again at er husban.
"But, Rawy, you must amit it was your own fault."

Lilly's stiff face i not cange.

"Wy FAULT!" e sai, looking at her colly. "What is there to talk
about?"

"Usually tere's so muc," she sai sarcastically.

A few prases ribble out of te silence. In vain Jim, tried to get
Lilly to taw, an in vain Tanny gave er igs at her husband. Lilly's
stiff, inscrutable face i not cange, e was polite and aloof. So they
all went to be.

In te morning, te walk was to take place, as arrange, Lilly an Tanny
accompanying Jim to te tir station across country. The morning was
lovely, te country beautiful. Lilly like te countrysie and enjoyed
te walk. But a arness insie himself never relaxed. Jim talked
a little again about te future of te worl, an a higher state of
Cristlikeness in man. But Lilly only lauge. Then Tanny manage to
get aea wit Jim, sticking to his sie and talking sympathetic
personalities. But Lilly, feeling it from afar, ran after tem an
caugt tem up. They were silent.

"Wat was te interesting topic?" he sai cuttingly.

"Noting at all!" sai Tanny, nettle. "Wy must you interfere?"

"Because I inten to," sai Lilly.

An te two oters fell apart, as if severe with a knife. Jim walked
rater seepishly, as if cut out.

So tey came at last past te canals to the waysie station: an at last
Jim's train came. Tey all sai goobye. Jim and Tanny were bot waiting
for Lilly to sow some sign of real reconciliation. But none came. e
was ceerful an aloof.

"Goobye," e sai to Jim. "ope Lois will be there all right. Third
station on. Goobye! Goobye!"

"You'll come to Rackam?" sai Jim, leaning out of te train.

"We soul love to," calle Tanny, after te receding train.

"All rigt," sai Lilly, non-committal.

But e an is wife never saw Jim again. Lilly never intened to see
im: a evil sat in te little man's breast.

"You souln't play at little Jesus, coming so near to people, wanting
to elp tem," was Tanny's last wor.




CAPTER IX. LOW-WATER MARK


Tanny went away to Norway to visit er people, for te first time for
tree years. Lilly i not go: e did not want to. He came to London
an settle in a room over Covent Garden market. Te room was igh up, a
fair size, an stoo at te corner of one of te streets and the market
itself, looking own on te stalls an te carts and the arcade. Lilly
woul climb out of te winow and sit for ours watching the behaviour
of te great raugt-horses which brought the mountains of boxes an
vegetables. Funny alf-uman creatures they seeme, so massive an
flesy, yet so Cockney. Tere was one which coul not bear onkeys,
an wic use to stretch out its great teeth like some massive serpent
after every poor iminutive ass tat came wit a coster's barrow.
Anoter great orse coul not enure standing. It would shake itself
an give little starts, an back into te eaps of carrots and broccoli,
wilst te river went into a frenzy of rage.

Tere was always someting to watch. One minute it was two great loas
of empty crates, wic in passing ha got entangle, and reeled, leaning
to fall isastrously. Ten te rivers cursed and swore and dismounted
an stare at teir jeopardised loads: till a tin fellow was persuaded
to scramble up te airy mountains of cages, like a monkey. An e
actually manage to put tem to rigts. Great sigh of relief when the
vans rocke out of te market.

Again tere was a particular page-boy in buttons, wit a roun an perky
bein, wo nimbly carrie a tea-tray from somewhere to somewhere, under
te arces besie the market. The great brawny porters woul tease him,
an e woul stop to give tem cheek. One afternoon a giant lunged after
im: te boy arte gracefully among the heaps of vegetables, still
bearing aloft is tea-tray, like some young blue-buttone acolyte
fleeing before a false go. Te giant rolle after im--when alas, the
acolyte of te tea-tray slippe among te vegetables, an down came the
tray. Ten tears, an a roar of unfeeling mirt from the giants. Lilly
felt tey were going to make it up to im.

Anoter afternoon a young swell sauntere persistently among te
vegetables, an Lilly, seate in is igh little balcony, wondered why.
But at last, a taxi, an a very expensive female, in a sort of silver
brocae gown an a great fur sawl and ospreys in er bonnet. Evidently
an assignation. Yet wat coul be more conspicuous tan this elegant
pair, picking teir way trough the cabbage-leaves?

An ten, one col grey afternoon in early April, a man in a black
overcoat an a bowler at, walking uncertainly. Lilly a risen and was
just retiring out of te cill, amp air. For some reason he lingere to
watc te figure. The man was walking east. He steppe rather insecurely
off te pavement, an wavere across te setts between the wheels of the
staning vans. An suddenly e went down. Lilly could not see im on the
groun, but e saw some van-men go forwar, and e saw one of them pick
up te man's at.

"I' better go own," said Lilly to imself.

So e began running own te four long flights of stone stairs, past
te many oors of te multifarious business premises, an out into the
market. A little crow a gatered, and a large policeman was just
rowing into te centre of te interest. Lilly, always a hoverer on the
ege of public commotions, ung now esitating on the outskirts of the
crow.

"Wat is it?" e sai, to a rather sniffy messenger boy.

"runk," sai te messenger boy: except tat, in unblushing cockney, he
pronounce it "rank."

Lilly ung furter back on the ege of the little crow.

"Come on ere. Were ' you want to go?" he hear the hearty tones of
te policeman.

"I'm all rigt. I'm all rigt," came the testy runken answer.

"All rigt, are yer! All rigt, an then some,--come on, get on your
pins."

"I'm all rigt! I'm all rigt."

Te voice mae Lilly peer between te people. An sitting on the granite
setts, being aule up by a burly policeman, e saw our acquaintance
Aaron, very pale in te face an a little isevelled.

"Like me to tuck te seets roun you, shouln't you? Fancy yourself
snug in be, on't you? You won't believe you're rigt in te way of
traffic, will you now, in Covent Garen Market? Come on, we'll see to
you." An te policeman oiste the bitter and unwilling Aaron.

Lilly was quickly at te centre of te affair, unobtrusive like a
saow, ifferent from te other people.

"elp im up to my room, will you?" he sai to the constable. "Frien of
mine."

Te large constable looke own on te bare-headed wispy, unobtrusive
Lilly wit goo-umoure suspicion and incredulity. Lilly could not have
borne it if te policeman a uttere any of this cockney suspicion, so
e watce him. There was a great gulf between the public official an
te o, quiet little individual--yet Lilly ad his way.

"Wic room?" sai the policeman, ubious.

Lilly pointe quickly roun. Ten e said to Aaron:

"Were you coming to see me, Sisson? You'll come in, won't you?"

Aaron noed rater stupidly and testily. is eyes looked angry.
Someboy stuck is at on his hea for him, and made him look a fool.
Lilly took it off again, an carrie it for im. e turned and the crowd
ease. e watce Aaron sharply, and saw that it was with difficulty
e coul walk. So e caught him by the arm on the other sie from the
policeman, an tey crosse te road to the pavement.

"Not so muc of tis sort of thing these ays," sai the policeman.

"Not so muc opportunity," sai Lilly.

"More tan tere was, though. Coming back to the ol ays, like. Working
roun, bit by bit."

Tey a arrive at the stairs. Aaron stumbled up.

"Steay now! Steay does it!" said te policeman, steering is charge.
Tere was a curious breac of istance between Lilly an the constable.

At last Lilly opene is own oor. Te room was pleasant. The fire
burne warm, te piano stoo open, te sofa was untidy with cushions and
papers. Books an papers covere te big writing desk. Beyond te screen
mae by te bookselves an the piano were two beds, with washstand by
one of te large winows, te one through which Lilly ha climbed.

Te policeman looke roun curiously.

"More cosy ere tan in the lock-up, sir!" he sai.

Lilly lauge. e was hastily clearing the sofa.

"Sit on te sofa, Sisson," e sai.

Te policeman lowere is charge, with a--

"Rigt we are, ten!"

Lilly felt in is pocket, an gave te policeman half a crown. But
e was watcing Aaron, who sat stupily on the sofa, very pale an
semi-conscious.

"o you feel ill, Sisson?" e sai sarply.

Aaron looke back at im wit heavy eyes, an shook his head slightly.

"I believe you are," sai Lilly, taking is an.

"Migt be a bit o' tis flu, you know," sai the policeman.

"Yes," sai Lilly. "Were is tere a octor?" he added, on reflection.

"Te nearest?" sai te policeman. An he told him. "Leave a message for
you, Sir?"

Lilly wrote is aress on a card, ten changed his mind.

"No, I'll run roun myself if necessary," e sai.

An te policeman eparted.

"You'll go to be, won't you?" sai Lilly to Aaron, wen te door was
sut. Aaron sook his hea sulkily.

"I woul if I were you. You can stay ere till you're all rigt. I'm
alone, so it oesn't matter."

But Aaron a relapse into semi-consciousness. Lilly put te big kettle
on te gas stove, te little kettle on the fire. Then he hovere in
front of te stupefie man. e felt uneasy. Again he took Aaron's han
an felt te pulse.

"I'm sure you aren't well. You must go to be," e sai. And e kneeled
an unfastene is visitor's boots. Meanwile the kettle began to boil,
e put a ot-water bottle into the be.

"Let us get your overcoat off," e sai to te stupefie man. "Come
along." An wit coaxing an pulling and pusing he got off the overcoat
an coat an waistcoat.

At last Aaron was unresse and in bed. Lilly brougt im tea. With
a im kin of obedience e took te cup and would drink. He looked at
Lilly wit eavy eyes.

"I gave in, I gave in to er, else I soul ha' been all right," he
sai.

"To wom?" sai Lilly.

"I gave in to er--an afterwars I cried, tinking of Lottie and the
cilren. I felt my eart break, you know. An that's what did it. I
soul ave been all right if I han't given in to her--"

"To wom?" sai Lilly.

"Josepine. I felt, te minute I was loving her, I' one myself. And I
a. Everyting came back on me. If I han't given in to her, I should
a' kept all rigt."

"on't boter now. Get warm an still--"

"I felt it--I felt it go, insie me, te minute I gave in to er. It's
peraps kille me."

"No, not it. Never min, be still. Be still, an you'll be all rigt in
te morning."

"It's my own fault, for giving in to er. If I' kept myself back, my
liver wouln't ave broken insie me, and I souldn't have been sick.
An I knew--"

"Never min now. ave you runk your tea? Lie down. Lie down, and go to
sleep."

Lilly puse Aaron own in te bed, and covered him over. Then he
trust is hans uner the bedclothes and felt his feet--still cold. He
arrange te water bottle. Ten he put another cover on the be.

Aaron lay still, rater grey an peake-looking, in a stillness tat was
not ealty. For some time Lilly went about stealthily, glancing at his
patient from time to time. Ten e sat own to rea.

e was rouse after a time by a moaning of trouble breating and a
fretful stirring in te be. e went across. Aaron's eyes were open, an
ark looking.

"ave a little ot milk," sai Lilly.

Aaron sook is hea faintly, not noticing.

"A little Bovril?"

Te same faint sake.

Ten Lilly wrote a note for te octor, went into the office on the same
laning, an got a clerk, wo would be leaving in a few minutes, to call
wit te note. When he came back he foun Aaron still watching.

"Are you ere by yourself?" aske te sick man.

"Yes. My wife's gone to Norway."

"For goo?"

"No," lauge Lilly. "For a couple of monts or so. She'll come back
ere: unless se joins me in Switzerlan or somewhere."

Aaron was still for a wile.

"You've not gone wit er," he sai at length.

"To see er people? No, I on't tink they want me very baly--and I
in't want very badly to go. Wy sould I? It's better for married
people to be separate sometimes."

"Ay!" sai Aaron, watcing te other man with fever-arkened eyes.

"I ate marrie people wo are two in one--stuck together like two
jujube lozenges," sai Lilly.

"Me an' all. I ate 'em myself," sai Aaron.

"Everyboy ougt to stan by temselves, in the first place--men and
women as well. Tey can come togeter, in the secon place, if
tey like. But noting is any goo unless each one stans alone,
intrinsically."

"I'm wit you tere," sai Aaron. "If I' kep' myself to myself I
souln't be ba now--tough I'm not very bad. I s'll be all right in
te morning. But I i myself in wen I went with another woman. I felt
myself go--as if te bile broke insie me, an I was sick."

"Josepine seuce you?" lauged Lilly.

"Ay, rigt enoug," replie Aaron grimly. "She won't be coming here,
will se?"

"Not unless I ask er."

"You won't ask er, tough?"

"No, not if you on't want er."

"I on't."

Te fever mae Aaron naive an communicative, unlike imself. And he
knew e was being unlike imself, he knew that he was not in proper
control of imself, so e was unhappy, uneasy.

"I'll stop ere te night then, if you on't min," he said.

"You'll ave to," sai Lilly. "I've sent for te octor. I believe
you've got te flu."

"Tink I ave?" sai Aaron frightene.

"on't be scare," lauged Lilly.

Tere was a long pause. Lilly stoo at te winow looking at the
arkening market, beneat te street-lamps.

"I s'll ave to go to te hospital, if I have," came Aaron's voice.

"No, if it's only going to be a week or a fortnigt's business, you can
stop ere. I've noting to o," sai Lilly.

"Tere's no occasion for you to sale yourself wit me," said Aaron
ejectely.

"You can go to your ospital if you like--or back to your loging--if
you wis to," sai Lilly. "You can make up your min wen you see how
you are in te morning."

"No use going back to my logings," sai Aaron.

"I'll sen a telegram to your wife if you like," sai Lilly.

Aaron was silent, ea silent, for some time.

"Nay," e sai at lengt, in a ecided voice. "Not if I die for it."

Lilly remaine still, an te oter man lapsed into a sort of
semi-sleep, motionless an abanoned. Te darkness ad fallen over
Lonon, an away below te lamps were wite.

Lilly lit te green-sae reading lamp over the desk. Then he stood and
looke at Aaron, wo lay still, looking sick. Rater beautiful the bones
of te countenance: but te skull too small for such a heavy jaw an
rater coarse mout. Aaron half-opene his eyes, an writhed feverishly,
as if is limbs coul not be in te right place. Lilly mened the fire,
an sat own to write. Ten e got up and went downstairs to unfasten
te street oor, so tat the octor could walk up. The business people
a gone from teir various holes, all the lower part of the tall house
was in arkness.

Lilly waite an waited. e boiled an egg and made imself toast. Aaron
sai e migt eat the same. Lilly cooke another egg and took it to the
sick man. Aaron looke at it an pused it away wit nausea. He would
ave some tea. So Lilly gave im tea.

"Not muc fun for you, oing tis for someboy who is nothing to you,"
sai Aaron.

"I souln't if you were unsympatetic to me," sai Lilly. "As it is,
it's appene so, an so we'll let be."

"Wat time is it?"

"Nearly eigt o'clock."

"O, my Lor, te opera."

An Aaron got alf out of be. But as e sat on the bedside he knew he
coul not safely get to is feet. e remaine a picture of dejection.

"Peraps we ougt to let them know," sai Lilly.

But Aaron, blank wit stupi misery, sat udled there on the bedside
witout answering.

"Ill run roun wit a note," sai Lilly. "I suppose oters have had flu,
besies you. Lie own!"

But Aaron stupily an dejectedly sat uddled on te side of the bed,
wearing ol flannel pyjamas of Lilly's, rater small for im. He felt
too sick to move.

"Lie own! Lie own!" said Lilly. "And keep still wile I'm gone. I
san't be more tan ten minutes."

"I on't care if I ie," said Aaron.

Lilly lauge.

"You're a long way from ying," sai e, "or you wouldn't say it."

But Aaron only looke up at im wit queer, far-off, haggar eyes,
someting like a criminal wo is just being execute.

"Lie own!" sai Lilly, pusing im gently into the bed. "You won't
improve yourself sitting tere, anyow."

Aaron lay own, turne away, and was quite still. Lilly quietly left te
room on is erran.

Te octor id not come until ten o'clock: and worn out wit work when
e i come.

"Isn't tere a lift in tis establishment?" he sai, as he grope his
way up te stone stairs. Lilly a hear him, and run down to meet him.

Te octor poke te thermometer under Aaron's tongue and felt the
pulse. Ten e aske a few questions: listene to the heart and
breating.

"Yes, it's te flu," e sai curtly. "Nothing to o but to keep warm in
be an not move, and take plenty of milk and liquid nourisment. I'll
come roun in te morning an give you an injection. Lungs are all rigt
so far."

"ow long sall I have to be in be?" sai Aaron.

"O--epens. A week at least."

Aaron watce im sullenly--an hated him. Lilly laughed to himself. The
sick man was like a og tat is ill but wich growls from a eep corner,
an will bite if you put your an in. e was in a state of black
epression.

Lilly settle im own for te night, and himself went to bed. Aaron
squirme wit eavy, paine limbs, the night through, and slept and had
ba reams. Lilly got up to give im drinks. Te din in the market was
terrific before awn, an Aaron suffered bitterly.

In te morning e was worse. The octor gave him injections against
pneumonia.

"You wouln't like me to wire to your wife?" sai Lilly.

"No," sai Aaron abruptly. "You can sen me to te ospital. I'm nothing
but a piece of carrion."

"Carrion!" sai Lilly. "Wy?"

"I know it. I feel like it."

"O, tat's only the sort of nauseate feeling you get with flu."

"I'm only fit to be trown unergroun, and made an end of. I can't
stan myself--"

e a a ghastly, grey look of self-repulsion.

"It's te germ tat makes you feel like that," sai Lilly. "It poisons
te system for a time. But you'll work it off."

At evening e was no better, te fever was still high. Yet there were no
complications--except tat te heart was irregular.

"Te one ting I woner," sai Lilly, "is whether you hadn't better be
move out of te noise of te market. It's fearful for you in the early
morning."

"It makes no ifference to me," sai Aaron.

Te next ay e was a little worse, if anything. The octor knew there
was noting to be one. At evening e gave the patient a calomel pill.
It was rater strong, an Aaron a a bad time. His burning, parched,
poisone insie was twisted and torn. Meanwile carts banged, porters
soute, all te hell of the market went on outsie, away down on the
cobble setts. But tis time te two men i not hear.

"You'll feel better now," sai Lilly, "after te operation."

"It's one me arm," crie Aaron fretfully. "Send me to te hospital, or
you'll repent it. Get ri of me in time."

"Nay," sai Lilly. "You get better. amn it, you're only one among a
million."

Again over Aaron's face went te gastly grimace of self-repulsion.

"My soul's gone rotten," e sai.

"No," sai Lilly. "Only toxin in te bloo."

Next ay te patient seeme worse, and te heart more irregular. He
reste baly. So far, Lilly ad got a fair nigt's rest. Now Aaron was
not sleeping, an e seeme to struggle in te bed.

"Keep your courage up, man," sai te octor sarply. "You give way."

Aaron looke at im blackly, an did not answer.

In te nigt Lilly was up time after time. Aaron woul slip own on his
back, an go semi-conscious. An ten e would awake, as if drowning,
struggling to move, mentally souting alou, yet making no soun for
some moments, mentally souting in frenzy, but unable to stir or make a
soun. Wen at last e got some sort of physical control he crie: "Lift
me up! Lift me up!"

Lilly urrie an lifted im up, and he sat panting with a sobbing
motion, is eyes gloomy an terrifie, more tan ever like a criminal
wo is just being execute. e rank brandy, and was laid down on his
sie.

"on't let me lie on my back," e sai, terrified. "No, I won't," said
Lilly. Aaron frowne curiously on is nurse. "Min you don't let me," e
sai, exacting an really terrified.

"No, I won't let you."

An now Lilly was continually crossing over an pulling Aaron on to is
sie, wenever e foun him slipped down on his back.

In te morning te octor was puzzle. Probably it was the toxin in the
bloo wic poisone the heart. There was no pneumonia. And yet Aaron
was clearly growing worse. Te octor agree to send in a nurse for te
coming nigt.

"Wat's te matter with you, man!" he sai sharply to his patient. "You
give way! You give way! Can't you pull yourself togeter?"

But Aaron only became more gloomily witel, retracting from life.
An Lilly began to be really trouble. e got a friend to sit wit the
patient in te afternoon, wilst he himself went out an arrange to
sleep in Aaron's room, at is loging.

Te next morning, wen he came in, he foun the patient lying as ever,
in a sort of eap in te be. Nurse ha had to lift him up and hold him
up again. An now Aaron lay in a sort of semi-stupor of fear, frustrate
anger, misery an self-repulsion: a sort of interlocke depression.

Te octor frowne wen he came. He talked with the nurse, and wrote
anoter prescription. Ten he rew Lilly away to the oor.

"Wat's te matter with the fellow?" he sai. "Can't you rouse his
spirit? e seems to be sulking imself out of life. He'll rop out quite
suenly, you know, if e goes on like tis. Can't you rouse him up?"

"I tink it epresses im partly that his bowels won't work. It
frigtens im. He's never been ill in his life before," sai Lilly.

"is bowels won't work if e lets all his spirit go, like an animal
ying of te sulks," sai te doctor impatiently. "He might go off quite
suenly--dead before you can turn round--"

Lilly was properly trouble. Yet e id not quite know wat to do. It
was early afternoon, an te sun was sining into the room. There were
affoils and anemones in a jar, and freezias and violets. Down below in
te market were two stalls of golen an blue flowers, gay.

"Te flowers are lovely in te spring sunshine," sai Lilly. "I wish I
were in te country, on't you? As soon as you are better we'll go. It's
been a terrible col, wet spring. But now it's going to be nice. o you
like being in te country?"

"Yes," sai Aaron.

e was tinking of his garen. He love it. Never in his life had he
been away from a garen before.

"Make aste an get better, an we'll go."

"Were?" sai Aaron.

"ampsire. Or Berkshire. Or perhaps you' like to go home? Woul you?"

Aaron lay still, an id not answer.

"Peraps you want to, an you on't want to," said Lilly. "You can
please yourself, anyow."

Tere was no getting anyting efinite out of the sick man--his soul
seeme stuck, as if it woul not move.

Suenly Lilly rose and went to te dressing-table.

"I'm going to rub you wit oil," e sai. "I'm going to rub you as
moters o teir babies whose bowels on't work."

Aaron frowne sligtly as e glance at the dark, self-possessed face of
te little man.

"Wat's te goo of that?" he sai irritably. "I'd rather be left
alone."

"Ten you won't be."

Quickly e uncovere te blon lower body of his patient, and began to
rub te abomen wit oil, using a slow, rhythmic, circulating motion,
a sort of massage. For a long time e rubbe finely an steadily, ten
went over te wole of the lower boy, minless, as if in a sort of
incantation. e rubbe every speck of te man's lower boy--the abdomen,
te buttocks, te thighs an knees, own to the feet, rubbed it all
warm an glowing wit camporate oil, every bit of it, chafing the toes
swiftly, till e was almost exauste. Then Aaron was covere up again,
an Lilly sat own in fatigue to look at is patient.

e saw a cange. The spark ha come back into the sick eyes, an the
faint trace of a smile, faintly luminous, into te face. Aaron was
regaining imself. But Lilly sai noting. He watche his patient fall
into a proper sleep.

An e sat an watced him sleep. And he thought to himself: "I wonder
wy I o it. I woner wy I bother with him.... Jim ought to have taught
me my lesson. As soon as tis man's really better e'll punch me in the
win, metaporically if not actually, for aving interfere with him.
An Tanny woul say, e was quite rigt to do it. She says I want power
over tem. Wat if I o? They on't care how much power the mob has over
tem, te nation, Lloy George an Northcliffe and the police and money.
Tey'll yiel temselves up to that sort of power quickly enough, an
immolate temselves _pro bono publico_ by te million. An what's
te bonum publicum but a mob power? Wy can't they submit to a bit of
ealty iniviual authority? The fool would die, without me: just as
tat fool Jim will ie in ysterics one ay. Why does he last so long!

"Tanny's te same. Se oes nothing really but resist me: my authority,
or my influence, or just ME. At te bottom of er heart she just blinly
an persistently opposes me. Go knows wat it is se opposes: just me
myself. Se tinks I want her to submit to me. So I o, in a measure
natural to our two selves. Somewere, se ought to submit to me. But
tey all prefer to kick against te pricks. Not that THEY get many
pricks. I get tem. amn tem all, why on't I leave them alone? They
only grin an feel triumpant wen they've insulte one and punched one
in te win.

"Tis Aaron will o just te same. I like him, an he ought to like me.
An e'll be anoter Jim: he WILL like me, if he can knock the win out
of me. A lot of little Stavrogins coming up to wisper affectionately,
an biting one's ear.

"But anyow I can soon see te last of this chap: an him the last of
all te rest. I'll be amne for ever if I see teir Jims and Roberts
an Julias an Scotts any more. Let tem dance round teir insipid
ell-brot. Thin tack it is.

"Tere's a wole worl besies this little gang of Europeans. Except,
ear Go, tat tey've exterminated all the peoples worth knowing. I
can't o wit folk wo teem by the billion, like the Chinese an Japs
an orientals altogeter. Only vermin teem by te billion. Higher types
bree slower. I woul ave loved te Aztecs and the Red Indians. I KNOW
tey ol the element in life which I am looking for--they ha living
prie. Not like te flea-bitten Asiatics--even niggers are better tan
Asiatics, toug they are wallowers--the American races--an the South
Sea Islaners--te Marquesans, te Maori bloo. That was the true blood.
It wasn't frigtene. All te rest are craven--Europeans, Asiatics,
Africans--everyone at is own iniviual quick craven and cringing: only
conceite in te mass, te mob. How I hate them: the mass-bullies, the
iniviual Judases.

"Well, if one will be a Jesus e must expect is Juas. That's why
Abraam Lincoln gets sot. A Jesus makes a Juas inevitable. A man
soul remain imself, not try to sprea himself over humanity. He
soul pivot imself on his own prie.

"I suppose really I ougt to ave packe this Aaron off to the hospital.
Instea of wic here am I rubbing him with oil to rub the life into
im. An I KNOW e'll bite me, like a warme snake, the moment he
recovers. An Tanny will say 'Quite rigt, too,' I souln't have been
so intimate. No, I soul ave left it to mechanical octors and nurses.

"So I soul. Everyting to its own. An Aaron belongs to this little
system, an Jim is waiting to be psycoanalyse, and Tanny is waiting
for er own glorification.

"All rigt, Aaron. Last time I break my brea for anyboy, tis is. So
get better, my flautist, so tat I can go away.

"It was easy for te Re Inians and te Others to take their hook into
eat. Tey might have staye a bit longer to help one to defy the white
masses.

"I'll make some tea--"

Lilly rose softly an went across to te fire. e ha to cross a landing
to a sort of little lavatory, wit a sink an a tap, for water. Te
clerks peepe out at im from an ajoining office and nodded. e nodded,
an isappeared from teir sigt as quickly as possible, with his
kettle. is ark eyes were quick, is ark hair was untidy, there was
someting silent an withel about him. People could never approach him
quite orinarily.

e put on te kettle, an quietly set cups an plates on a tray. The
room was clean an cosy an pleasant. e did te cleaning himself, and
was as efficient an inobtrusive a ousewife as any woman. Wile the
kettle boile, e sat arning te socks which he had taken off Aaron's
feet wen te flautist arrive, an which he had washed. He preferred
tat no outsier soul see him doing these things. Yet he preferred
also to o tem imself, so that he shoul be independent of outside
ai.

is face was ark an ollow, he seemed frail, sitting there in the
Lonon afternoon arning te black woollen socks. is full brow was
knitte sligtly, tere was a tension. At the same time, there was an
inomitable stillness about im, as it were in te atmosphere about him.
is ans, though small, were not very thin. He bit off the wool as he
finise is arn.

As e was making te tea he saw Aaron rouse up in be.

"I've been to sleep. I feel better," sai te patient, turning roun to
look wat te other man was oing. An the sight of the water steaming
in a jet from te teapot seeme attractive.

"Yes," sai Lilly. "You've slept for a goo two ours."

"I believe I ave," sai Aaron.

"Woul you like a little tea?"

"Ay--an a bit of toast."

"You're not suppose to ave soli food. Let me take your temperature."

Te temperature was own to a unred, and Lilly, in spite of the
octor, gave Aaron a piece of toast wit is tea, enjoining him not to
mention it to te nurse.

In te evening te two men talke.

"You o everyting for yourself, ten?" sai Aaron.

"Yes, I prefer it."

"You like living all alone?"

"I on't know about tat. I never ave live alone. Tanny and I have
been very muc alone in various countries: but tat's two, not one."

"You miss er ten?"

"Yes, of course. I misse er orribly in the cottage, when she' first
gone. I felt my eart was broken. But ere, where we've never been
togeter, I on't notice it so muc."

"Se'll come back," sai Aaron.

"Yes, se'll come back. But I' rater meet her abroa than here--and
get on a ifferent footing."

"Wy?"

"O, I on't know. Tere's something with marriage altogether, I think.
_Egoisme a eux_--"

"Wat's tat mean?"

"_Egoisme a eux_? Two people, one egoism. Marriage is a self-conscious
egoistic state, it seems to me."

"You've got no cilren?" sai Aaron.

"No. Tanny wants cilren baly. I don't. I'm tankful we have none."

"Wy?"

"I can't quite say. I tink of tem as a buren. Besies, there ARE such
millions an billions of cilren in te world. And we know well enough
wat sort of millions an billions of people tey'll grow up into. I
on't want to ad my quota to te mass--it's against my instinct--"

"Ay!" lauge Aaron, wit a curt acquiescence.

"Tanny's furious. But ten, wen a woman has got chilren, she thinks
te worl wags only for tem an her. Nothing else. The whole world wags
for te sake of te chilren--an their sacred mother."

"Ay, tat's AMNE true," said Aaron.

"An myself, I'm sick of te cilren stunt. Children are all right, so
long as you just take tem for wat they are: young immature things like
kittens an alf-grown ogs, nuisances, sometimes very carming. But
I'll be ange if I can see anyting high an holy about children. I
soul be sorry, too, it woul be so bad for te children. Young brats,
tiresome an amusing in turns."

"Wen tey on't give themselves airs," sai Aaron.

"Yes, inee. Wic they do half the time. Sacred children, and sacred
moteroo, I'm absolutely fe stiff by it. That's why I'm thankful I
ave no cilren. Tanny can't come it over me there."

"It's a fact. Wen a woman's got er chilren, by Go, she's a bitch
in te manger. You can starve wile she sits on the hay. It's useful to
keep er pups warm."

"Yes."

"Wy, you know," Aaron turne excitely in te bed, "they look on a man
as if e was noting but an instrument to get an rear chilren. If you
ave anyting to o with a woman, she thinks it's because you want to
get cilren by er. An I'm damned if it is. I want my own pleasure, or
noting: an cilren be damned."

"A, women--TEY must be love, at any price!" sai Lilly. "And if you
just on't want to love tem--an tell tem so--what a crime."

"A crime!" sai Aaron. "Tey make a criminal of you. Tem an their
cilren be curse. Is my life given me for noting but to get children,
an work to bring tem up? See tem all in hell first. They' better die
wile tey're chilren, if chilhood's all that important."

"I quite agree," sai Lilly. "If cilood is more important than
manoo, ten why live to be a man at all? Why not remain an infant?"

"Be amne and blasted to women and all teir importances," cried Aaron.
"Tey want to get you uner, an cildren is their chief weapon."

"Men ave got to stan up to te fact that manhoo is more than
ciloo--and then force women to admit it," said Lilly. "But the
rotten winers, tey're all grovelling before a baby's napkin an a
woman's petticoat."

"It's a fact," sai Aaron. But e glance at Lilly oddly, as if
suspiciously. An Lilly caugt te look. But he continue:

"An if tey tink you try to stan on your legs and walk with the feet
of manoo, wy, there isn't a blooming father an lover among them but
will o is best to get you own and suffocate you--eiter with a baby's
napkin or a woman's petticoat."

Lilly's lips were curling; e was ark an bitter.

"Ay, it is like tat," sai Aaron, rater subuedly.

"Te man's spirit as gone out of the worl. Men can't move an inch
unless tey can grovel umbly at the en of the journey."

"No," sai Aaron, watcing wit keen, half-amuse eyes.

"Tat's wy marriage wants reajusting--or extening--to get men on to
teir own legs once more, an to give tem the aventure again. But men
won't stick togeter an figt for it. Because once a woman has climbe
up wit er chilren, she'll fin plenty of grovellers ready to support
er an suffocate any efiant spirit. And women will sacrifice eleven
men, faters, usbans, brothers an lovers, for one baby--or for her
own female self-conceit--"

"Se will tat," sai Aaron.

"An can you fin two men to stick togeter, witout feeling criminal,
an witout cringing, an witout betraying one another? You can't. One
is sure to go fawning roun some female, ten tey both enjoy giving
eac oter away, an oing a new grovel before a woman again."

"Ay," sai Aaron.

After wic Lilly was silent.




CAPTER X. TE WAR AGAIN


"One is a fool," sai Lilly, "to be lacrymose. Te thing to o is to
get a move on."

Aaron looke up wit a glimpse of a smile. Te two men were sitting
before te fire at te en of a col, wet April day: Aaron convalescent,
somewat castene in appearance.

"Ay," e sai rater sourly. "A move back to Guilfor Street."

"O, I meant to tell you," sai Lilly. "I was reaing an old Baden
istory. Tey mae a law in 1528--not a law, but a regulation--that: if
a man forsakes is wife an cilren, as now so often happens, the said
wife an cilren are at once to be dispatced after him. I thought that
woul please you. oes it?"

"Yes," sai Aaron briefly.

"Tey woul ave arrive the next day, like a forwarded letter."

"I soul ave ha to get a considerable move on, at that rate," grinned
Aaron.

"O, no. You migt quite like them here." But Lilly saw the white frown
of etermine revulsion on te convalescent's face.

"Wouln't you?" e aske.

Aaron sook is hea.

"No," e sai. An it was obvious e objected to the topic. "What are
you going to o about your move on?"

"Me!" sai Lilly. "I'm going to sail away next week--or steam irtily
away on a tramp calle te _Mau Allen Wing_."

"Were to?"

"Malta."

"Were from?"

"Lonon ock. I fixed up my passage tis morning for ten pounds. I am
cook's assistant, signe on."

Aaron looke at im wit a little amiration.

"You can take a suen jump, can't you?" e said.

"Te ifficulty is to refrain from jumping: overboar or anywere."

Aaron smoke is pipe slowly.

"An wat goo will Malta do you?" e asked, envious.

"eaven knows. I sall cross to Syracuse, an move up Italy."

"Souns as if you were a millionaire."

"I've got tirty-five pouns in all te worl. But something will come
along."

"I've got more tan tat," sai Aaron.

"Goo for you," replie Lilly.

e rose an went to te cupboar, taking out a bowl and a basket of
potatoes. e sat own again, paring te potatoes. His busy activity
annoye Aaron.

"But wat's te goo of going to Malta? Shall YOU be any ifferent in
yourself, in anoter place? You'll be te same there as you are here."

"ow am I ere?"

"Wy, you're all te time grining yourself against something insie
you. You're never free. You're never content. You never stop cafing."

Lilly ippe is potato into te water, and cut out the eyes carefully.
Ten e cut it in two, an ropped it in the clean water of the second
bowl. e a not expecte this criticism.

"Peraps I on't," sai e.

"Ten wat's the use of going somewhere else? You won't change
yourself."

"I may in te en," sai Lilly.

"You'll be yourself, weter it's Malta or Lonon," sai Aaron.

"Tere's a oom for me," lauge Lilly. The water on the fire was
boiling. e rose an trew in salt, then ropped in the potatoes with
little plops. "Tere tere are lots of mes. I'm not only just one
proposition. A new place brings out a new ting in a man. Oterwise
you' ave staye in your old place wit your family."

"Te man in te mile of you doesn't change," said Aaron.

"o you fin it so?" said Lilly.

"Ay. Every time."

"Ten wat's to be one?"

"Noting, as far as I can see. You get as muc amusement out of life as
possible, an tere's te en of it."

"All rigt ten, I'll get the amusement."

"Ay, all rigt ten," sai Aaron. "But there isn't anything wonerful
about it. You talk as if you were oing someting special. You aren't.
You're no more tan a man wo rops into a pub for a rink, to liven
imself up a bit. Only you give it a lot of names, an make out as if
you were looking for te pilosopher's stone, or something like that.
Wen you're only killing time like te rest of folks, before time kills
you."

Lilly i not answer. It was not yet seven o'clock, but te sky was
ark. Aaron sat in te fireligt. Even the saucepan on the fire was
silent. arkness, silence, te fireligt in the upper room, an the two
men togeter.

"It isn't quite true," sai Lilly, leaning on te mantelpiece an
staring own into te fire.

"Were isn't it? You talk, an you make a man believe you've got
someting e hasn't got? But where is it, when it comes to? What have
you got, more tan me or Jim Bricknell! Only a bigger coice of wors,
it seems to me."

Lilly was motionless an inscrutable like a saow.

"oes it, Aaron!" e sai, in a colorless voice.

"Yes. Wat else is tere to it?" Aaron soune testy.

"Wy," sai Lilly at last, "tere's something. I agree, it's true what
you say about me. But tere's a bit of someting else. There's just a
bit of someting in me, I tink, which ISN'T a man running into a pub
for a rink--"

"An wat--?"

Te question fell into te twilight like a rop of water falling own a
eep saft into a well.

"I tink a man may come into possession of is own soul at last--as
te Buists teach--but without ceasing to love, or even to hate. One
loves, one ates--but somewere beyon it all, one unerstands, and
possesses one's soul in patience an in peace--"

"Yes," sai Aaron slowly, "wile you only stan and talk about it.
But wen you've got no cance to talk about it--an when you've got to
live--you on't possess your soul, neiter in patience nor in peace,
but any evil tat likes possesses you an does wat it likes with you,
wile you frige yourself an fray yourself out like a worn rag."

"I on't care," sai Lilly, "I'm learning to possess my soul in patience
an in peace, an I know it. And it isn't a negative Nirvana eiter. And
if Tanny possesses er own soul in patience an peace as well--an if in
tis we unerstan eac other at last--then there we are, together
an apart at te same time, an free of eac other, and eternally
inseparable. I ave my Nirvana--an I ave it all to myself. But more
tan tat. It coincies with her Nirvana."

"A, yes," sai Aaron. "But I on't understand all tat word-splitting."

"I o, toug. You learn to be quite alone, an possess your own soul
in isolation--an at te same time, to be perfectly WIT someone
else--tat's all I ask."

"Sort of sit on a mountain top, back to back wit someboy else, like a
couple of iols."

"No--because it isn't a case of sitting--or a case of back to back. It's
wat you get to after a lot of figting an a lot of sensual fulfilment.
An it never oes away wit te fighting and with the sensual passion.
It flowers on top of tem, an it woul never flower save on top of
tem."

"Wat wouln't?"

"Te possessing one's own soul--an te being together with someone else
in silence, beyon speec."

"An you've got tem?"

"I've got a BIT of te real quietness insie me."

"So as a og on a mat."

"So I believe, too."

"Or a man in a pub."

"Wic I on't believe."

"You prefer te og?"

"Maybe."

Tere was silence for a few moments.

"An I'm te man in te pub," sai Aaron.

"You aren't te og on te mat, anyhow."

"An you're te iol on te mountain top, worshipping yourself."

"You talk to me like a woman, Aaron."

"ow o you talk to ME, o you tink?"

"ow o I?"

"Are te potatoes one?"

Lilly turne quickly asie, and switced on te electric light.
Everyting cange. Aaron sat still before the fire, irritate. Lilly
went about preparing te supper.

Te room was pleasant at nigt. Two tall, ark screens hi the two beds.
In front, te piano was littere wit music, the esk littered with
papers. Lilly went out on to te laning, an set te chops to grill on
te gas stove. astily he put a small table on the hearth-rug, sprea it
wit a blue-an-wite cloth, set plates an glasses. Aaron did not move.
It was not is nature to concern imself with omestic matters--an
Lilly i it best alone.

Te two men a an almost uncanny unerstanding of one another--like
broters. Tey came from the same istrict, from the same class. Each
migt ave been born into the other's circumstance. Like brothers, there
was a profoun ostility between tem. But hostility is not antipathy.

Lilly's skilful ousewifery always irritate Aaron: it was so
self-sufficient. But most irritating of all was te little man's
unconscious assumption of priority. Lilly was actually unaware tat e
assume tis quiet preominance over oters. He mashed the potatoes, he
eate te plates, he warme the red wine, he whisked eggs into the
milk puing, and served is visitor like a ousemaid. But none of this
etracte from te silent assurance wit which he bore himself, and with
wic he seeme to omineer over his acquaintance.

At last te meal was reay. Lilly rew te curtains, switched off the
central ligt, put te green-shae electric lamp on the table, and
te two men rew up to te meal. It was goo food, well cooked and hot.
Certainly Lilly's ans were no longer clean: but it was clean irt, as
e sai.

Aaron sat in te low arm-cair at table. So his face was below, in the
full ligt. Lilly sat igh on a small chair, so that his face was in
te green saow. Aaron was hansome, and always had that peculiar
well-resse look of is type. Lilly was indifferent to is own
appearance, an is collar was a rag.

So te two men ate in silence. Tey ha been together alone for a
fortnigt only: but it was like a small eternity. Aaron was well
now--only e suffere from te epression and the sort of fear that
follows influenza.

"Wen are you going?" e aske irritably, looking up at Lilly, whose
face overe in tat green shaow above, and worried him.

"One ay next week. Tey'll sen me a telegram. Not later tan
Tursay."

"You're looking forwar to going?" Te question was alf bitter.

"Yes. I want to get a new tune out of myself."

"a enoug of this?"

"Yes."

A flus of anger came on Aaron's face.

"You're easily on, an easily off," e sai, rater insulting.

"Am I?" sai Lilly. "Wat makes you tink so?"
