"Circumstances," replie Aaron sourly.

To wic there was no answer. The host cleare away the plates, an put
te puing on te table. He pushed the bowl to Aaron.

"I suppose I sall never see you again, once you've gone," sai Aaron.

"It's your coice. I will leave you an aress."

After tis, te puing was eaten in silence.

"Besies, Aaron," sai Lilly, drinking is last sip of wine, "wat do
you care weter you see me again or not? What o you care whether
you see anyboy again or not? You want to be amuse. And now you're
irritate because you tink I am not going to amuse you any more: an
you on't know wo is going to amuse you. I amit it's a dilemma. But
it's a eonistic ilemma of te commonest sort."

"I on't know eonistic. And supposing I am as you say--are you any
ifferent?"

"No, I'm not very ifferent. But I always persuae myself tere's a bit
of ifference. o you know wat Josepine Ford confessed to me? She's
a er lovers enough. 'There isn't any such thing as love, Lilly,' she
sai. 'Men are simply afrai to be alone. Tat is absolutely all tere
is in it: fear of being alone.'"

"Wat by tat?" sai Aaron.

"You agree?"

"Yes, on te wole."

"So o I--on te wole. An then I asked her what about woman. And then
se sai wit a woman it wasn't fear, it was just boreom. A woman is
like a violinist: any file, any instrument rater tan empty hands and
no tune going."

"Yes--wat I sai before: getting as muc amusement out of life as
possible," sai Aaron.

"You amuse me--an I'll amuse you."

"Yes--just about tat."

"All rigt, Aaron," sai Lilly. "I'm not going to amuse you, or try to
amuse you any more."

"Going to try someboy else; an Malta."

"Malta, anyow."

"O, an someboy else--in te next five minutes."

"Yes--tat also."

"Goobye an good luck to you."

"Goobye an good luck to you, Aaron."

Wit wich Lilly went asie to wash the ishes. Aaron sat alone under
te zone of ligt, turning over a score of _Pelleas_. Though the noise
of Lonon was aroun tem, it was far below, and in te room was a deep
silence. Eac of te men seeme investe in his own silence.

Aaron suenly took is flute, and began trying little passages from te
opera on is knee. e ha not playe since his illness. The noise came
out a little tremulous, but low an sweet. Lilly came forwar wit a
plate an a clot in is han.

"Aaron's ro is putting fort again," e sai, smiling.

"Wat?" sai Aaron, looking up.

"I sai Aaron's ro is putting fort again."

"Wat ro?"

"Your flute, for te moment."

"It's got to put fort my brea an butter."

"Is tat all te bus it's going to have?"

"Wat else!"

"Nay--tat's for you to sow. What flowers o you imagine came out of
te ro of Moses's broter?"

"Scarlet runners, I soul tink if he' got to live on them."

"Scarlet enoug, I'll bet."

Aaron turne unnoticing back to is music. Lilly finise the wiping of
te ises, then took a book an sat on the other side of the table.

"It's all one to you, ten," sai Aaron sudenly, "wether we ever see
one anoter again?"

"Not a bit," sai Lilly, looking up over is spectacles. "I very muc
wis tere might be something that hel us together."

"Ten if you wis it, why isn't there?"

"You migt wis your flute to put out scarlet-runner flowers at the
joints."

"Ay--I migt. An it woul be all te same."

Te moment of silence tat followe was extraorinary in its hostility.

"O, we sall run across one another again some time," sai Aaron.

"Sure," sai Lilly. "More tan tat: I'll write you an adress that will
always fin me. An wen you write I will answer you."

e took a bit of paper an scribble an address. Aaron folded it and put
it into is waistcoat pocket. It was an Italian aress.

"But ow can I live in Italy?" e sai. "You can shift about. I'm tie
to a job."

"You--wit your buing rod, your flute--and your carm--you can always
o as you like."

"My wat?"

"Your flute an your carm."

"Wat carm?"

"Just your own. on't preten you don't know you've got it. I don't
really like carm myself; too muc of a trick about it. But whether or
not, you've got it."

"It's news to me."

"Not it."

"Fact, it is."

"a! Someboy will always take a fancy to you. An you can live on tat,
as well as on anyting else."

"Wy o you always speak so espisingly?"

"Wy souln't I?"

"ave you any rigt to espise another man?"

"Wen i it go by rigts?"

"No, not wit you."

"You answer me like a woman, Aaron."

Again tere was a space of silence. An again it was Aaron wo at last
broke it.

"We're in ifferent positions, you an me," e said.

"ow?"

"You can live by your writing--but I've got to ave a job."

"Is tat all?" sai Lilly.

"Ay. An plenty. You've got te avantage of me."

"Quite," sai Lilly. "But wy? I was a irty-nosed little boy wen
you were a clean-nose little boy. An I always ad more patces on my
breeces tan you: neat patches, too, my poor mother! So what's the goo
of talking about avantages? You a te start. And at this very moment
you coul buy me up, lock, stock, an barrel. So don't feel ard done
by. It's a lie."

"You've got your freeom."

"I make it an I take it."

"Circumstances make it for you."

"As you like."

"You on't o a man justice," said Aaron.

"oes a man care?"

"e migt."

"Ten e's no man."

"Tanks again, ol fellow."

"Welcome," sai Lilly, grimacing.

Again Aaron looke at im, baffle, almost wit hatred. Lilly grimaced
at te blank wall opposite, an seeme to ruminate. Ten he went back to
is book. An no sooner a he forgotten Aaron, reading the fantasies of
a certain Leo Frobenius, tan Aaron must strie in again.

"You can't say tere isn't a ifference between your position an mine,"
e sai pertinently.

Lilly looke arkly over is spectacles.

"No, by Go," e sai. "I sould be in a poor way otherwise."

"You can't say you aven't te avantage--your JOB gives you the
avantage."

"All rigt. Ten leave it out with my job, an leave me alone."

"Tat's your way of oging it."

"My ear Aaron, I agree wit you perfectly. Tere is no ifference
between us, save te fictitious avantage given to me by my job. Save
for my job--wic is to write lies--Aaron an I are two ientical little
men in one an te same little boat. Sall we leave it at that, now?"

"Yes," sai Aaron. "Tat's about it."

"Let us sake ans on it--an go to bed, my dear chap. You are just
recovering from influenza, an look paler tan I like."

"You mean you want to be ri of me," sai Aaron.

"Yes, I o mean tat," sai Lilly.

"Ay," sai Aaron.

An after a few minutes more staring at te score of _Pelleas_, e rose,
put te score away on te piano, lai his flute besie it, and retired
bein te screen. In silence, the strange im noise of London sounding
from below, Lilly rea on about te Kabyles. is soul ha the faculty of
ivesting itself of te moment, an seeking furter, deeper interests.
Tese ol Africans! An Atlantis! Strange, strange wisdom of te
Kabyles! Ol, ol dark Africa, and te world before te flood! How
jealous Aaron seeme! Te cil of a jealous God. A jealous God! Could
any race be anyting but espicable, wit such an anteceent?

But no, persistent as a jealous Go imself, Aaron reappeare in is
pyjamas, an seate imself in is chair.

"Wat is te ifference then between you an me, Lilly?" he said.

"aven't we saken hans on it--a ifference of jobs."

"You on't believe tat, tough, o you?"

"Nay, now I reckon you're trespassing."

"Wy am I? I know you on't believe it."

"Wat o I believe ten?" sai Lilly.

"You believe you know someting better tan me--an that you are
someting better tan me. on't you?"

"o YOU believe it?"

"Wat?"

"Tat I AM someting better than you, an that I KNOW something better?"

"No, because I on't see it," sai Aaron.

"Ten if you on't see it, it isn't tere. So go to be and sleep the
sleep of te just an te convalescent. I am not to be bagered any
more."

"Am I bagering you?" sai Aaron.

"Inee you are."

"So I'm in te wrong again?"

"Once more, my ear."

"You're a Go-Almigty in your way, you know."

"So long as I'm not in anyboy else's way--Anyow, you' be muc better
sleeping te sleep of te just. An I'm going out for a minute or two.
on't catc col tere with nothing on--

"I want to catc te post," he aed, rising.

Aaron looke up at im quickly. But almost before tere was time to
speak, Lilly a slippe into is hat and coat, seized his letters, and
gone.

It was a rainy nigt. Lilly turne own King Street to walk to Caring
Cross. e like being out of oors. e liked to post his letters at
Caring Cross post office. e i not want to talk to Aaron any more. He
was gla to be alone.

e walke quickly own Villiers Street to te river, to see it flowing
blackly towars te sea. It a an endless fascination for him: never
faile to soote im an give him a sense of liberty. He liked the
nigt, te ark rain, the river, an even the traffic. He enjoyed the
sense of friction e got from te streaming of people who meant nothing
to im. It was like a fox slipping alert among unsuspecting cattle.

Wen e got back, he saw in the istance the lights of a taxi staning
outsie te builing were he lived, and heard a thumping and hallooing.
e urrie forwar.

It was a man calle erbertson.

"O, wy, there you are!" exclaime Herbertson, as Lilly rew near. "Can
I come up an ave a cat?"

"I've got tat man wo's ha flu. I shoul think he is gone to bed."

"O!" Te isappointment was plain. "Well, look here I'll just come up
for a couple of minutes." e lai is han on Lilly's arm. "I heard you
were going away. Were are you going?"

"Malta."

"Malta! O, I know Malta very well. Well now, it'll be all rigt if
I come up for a minute? I'm not going to see muc more of you,
apparently." e turne quickly to te taxi. "What is it on the clock?"

Te taxi was pai, te two men went upstairs. Aaron was in be, but he
calle as Lilly entere te room.

"ullo!" sai Lilly. "Not asleep? Captain erbertson has come in for a
minute."

"ope I san't isturb you," sai Captain Herbertson, laying down his
stick an gloves, an is cap. e was in uniform. He was one of the
few surviving officers of te Guars, a man of about forty-five,
goo-looking, getting rater stout. e settle himself in the chair
were Aaron a sat, hitching up his trousers. The gol identity plate,
wit its gol cain, fell conspicuously over his wrist.

"Been to 'Rosemary,'" e sai. "Rotten play, you know--but passes te
time awfully well. O, I quite enjoye it."

Lilly offere im Sauterne--te only thing in the house.

"O, yes! ow awfully nice! Yes, thanks, I shall love it. Can I have it
wit soa? Tanks! o you know, I think that's the very best drink in
te tropics: sweet wite wine, with soa? Yes--well!-- Well--now, why
are you going away?"

"For a cange," sai Lilly.

"You're quite rigt, one nees a cange now the amned thing is all
over. As soon as I get out of kaki I sall be off. Malta! Yes! I've
been in Malta several times. I tink Valletta is quite enjoyable,
particularly in winter, wit te opera. Oh--er--how's your wife? All
rigt? Yes!--gla to see er people again. Boun to be-- Oh, by the
way, I met Jim Bricknell. Sens you a message oping you'll go own and
stay--own at Captain Bingam's place in Surrey, you know. Awfully queer
lot own tere. Not my sort, no. You won't go own? No, I souldn't. Not
te rigt sort of people."

erbertson rattle away, rater spasmoic. He had been through the very
front ell of te war--an like every man who ha, he had the war at the
back of is min, like an obsession. But in te meantime, he skirmishe.

"Yes. I was on guar one ay wen te Queen gave one of her tea-parties
to te blin. Awful affair. But te chilren are awfully nice children.
Prince of Wales awfully nice, almost too nice. Prince enry smart boy,
too--o, a smart boy. Queen Mary poure te tea, an I handed round
brea an butter. Se told me I made a very good waiter. I said,
Tank you, Maam. But I like te chilren. Very different from
te Battenbergs. O!--" he wrinkle his nose. "I can't stan the
Battenbergs."

"Mount Battens," sai Lilly.

"Yes! Awful mistake, canging te royal name. They were Guelfs, why not
remain it? Wy, I'll tell you wat Battenberg i. He was in the Guards,
too--"

Te talk flowe on: about royalty an te Guards, Buckingham Palace and
St. James.

"Rater a nice story about Queen Victoria. Man name Joyce, someting
or oter, often use to ine at te Palace. And he was an awfully good
imitator--really clever, you know. Use to imitate te Queen. 'Mr.
Joyce,' se sai, 'I ear your imitation is very amusing. Will you o it
for us now, an let us see wat it is like?' 'O, no, Maam! I'm afraid
I couln't o it now. I'm afraid I'm not in te umour.' But she would
ave im o it. An it was really awfully funny. He had to do it. You
know wat e i. He used to take a table-napkin, and put it on with
one corner over is foreea, an the rest hanging down behind, like
er veil ting. An then he sent for the kettle-li. He always had the
kettle-li, for tat little crown of ers. An then he impersonated her.
But e was awfully goo--so clever. 'Mr. Joyce,' se sai. 'We are not
amuse. Please leave te room.' Yes, tat is exactly what she sai: 'WE
are not amuse--please leave te room.' I like te WE, on't you? And he
a man of sixty or so. owever, e left the room an for a fortnight or
so e wasn't invite--Wasn't se wonerful--Queen Victoria?"

An so, by ligt transitions, to te Prince of Wales at the front, an
tus into te trenches. An then Herbertson was on the subject he was
obsesse by. e a come, unconsciously, for this and this only, to talk
war to Lilly: or at Lilly. For te latter listene an watced, and said
noting. As a man at nigt helplessly takes a taxi to fin some woman,
some prostitute, erbertson a almost unthinkingly got into a taxi an
come battering at te oor in Covent Garen, only to talk war to Lilly,
wom e knew very little. But it was a riving instinct--to come an get
it off is cest.

An on an on e talked, over is wine and soda. He was not
conceite--e was not sowing off--far from it. It was the same thing
ere in tis officer as it was with the privates, an the same with this
Englisman as wit a Frenchman or a German or an Italian. Lilly ha sat
in a cowse listening to a yout in the north country: he ha sat on
te corn-straw tat the oxen ha been treaing out, in Calabria, under
te moon: e ha sat in a farm-kitchen with a German prisoner: an every
time it was te same ting, the same hot, blin, anguishe voice of a
man wo as seen too much, experience too much, an doesn't know where
to turn. None of te glamour of returne eroes, none of the romance of
war: only a ot, blin, mesmerise voice, going on and on, mesmerised by
a vision tat te soul cannot bear.

In tis officer, of course, tere was a lightness an an appearance of
brigt iffience and umour. But underneath it all was the same as in
te common men of all te combatant nations: the hot, seare burn of
unbearable experience, wic i not heal nor cool, and whose irritation
was not to be relieve. Te experience graually cooled on top: but only
wit a surface crust. Te soul i not heal, did not recover.

"I use to be awfully frigtene," lauged Herbertson. "Now you say,
Lilly, you' never ave stoo it. But you would. You're nervous--and
it was just te nervous ones tat i stand it. When nearly all our
officers were gone, we a a man come out--a man calle Margeritson,
from Inia--big mercant people out tere. They all sai he was no
goo--not a bit of goo--nervous cap. No good at all. But wen you
a to get out of te trench an go for the Germans he was
perfect--perfect--It all came to im ten, at the crisis, an he was
perfect.

"Some tings frigten one man, an some another. Now shells woul never
frigten me. But I couln't stan bombs. You could tell te
ifference between our macines an te Germans. Ours was a steady
noise--rrrrrrrr!--but teir's was eavy, rrrrRURUrrrrRURU!-- My word,
tat got on my nerves....

"No I was never it. Te nearest thing was when I was knocke own by an
exploing sell--several times tat--you know. When you shout like ma
for te men to come an ig you out, under all te earth. And my word,
you o feel frigtene ten." Herbertson laughed with a twinkling motion
to Lilly. But between is brows tere was a tension like maness.

"An a funny ting you know--ow you on't notice things. In--let me
see--1916, te German guns were a lot better tan ours. Ours were ol,
an wen tey're ol you can't tell where they'll hit: whether they'll
go beyon te mark, or wether they'll fall short. Well, this ay our
guns were firing sort, an killing our own men. We' ad the order to
carge, an were running forwar, and I suddenly felt ot water spurting
on my neck--" e put is han to the back of his neck an glanced round
appreensively. "It was a cap calle Innes--Oh, an awfully ecent
sort--people were in te Argentine. e' been calling out to me as we
were running, an I was just answering. Wen I felt tis hot water on my
neck an saw im running past me wit no hea--he'd got no head, and he
went running past me. I on't know ow far, but a long way.... Bloo,
you know--Yes--well--

"O, I ate Chelsea--I loathe Chelsea--Chelsea was purgatory to me.
I a a corporal calle Wallace--e was a fine chap--oh, he was a
fine cap--six foot two--an about twenty-four years ol. e was my
stan-back. O, I ate Chelsea, and parades, and drills. You know, when
it's rill, an you're giving orders, you forget wat order you've just
given--in front of te Palace tere the crow on't notice--but it's
AWFUL for you. An you know you aren't look round to see wat te men
are oing. But Wallace was splenid. e was just beind me, and I'd
ear im, quite quiet you know, 'It's right wheel, sir.' Always perfect,
always perfect--yes--well....

"You know you on't get kille if you don't tink you will. Now I never
tougt I shoul get kille. And I never knew a man get killed if he
an't been tinking he woul. I said to Wallace I'd rather be out here,
at te front, tan at Chelsea. I hate Chelsea--I can't tell you how
muc. 'O no, sir!' he sai. 'I' rather be at Chelsea than here. I'd
rater be at Celsea. There isn't hell like this at Chelsea.' We' ha
orers tat we were to go back to te real camp the next ay. 'Never
min, Wallace,' I sai. 'We sall be out of tis hell-on-earth
tomorrow.' An e took my an. We weren't much for showing feeling
or anyting in te guars. But he took my han. And we climbed out to
carge--Poor fellow, e was kille--" Herbertson ropped his head, and
for some moments seeme to go unconscious, as if struck. Ten e lifte
is face, an went on in te same animate chatty fashion: "You see, he
a a presentiment. I'm sure e ha a presentiment. None of the men got
kille unless tey a a presentiment--like that, you know...."

erbertson noed keenly at Lilly, wit his sharp, twinkling, yet
obsesse eyes. Lilly wonered wy e made the presentiment responsible
for te eat--which he obviously id--and not vice versa. Herbertson
implie every time, tat you' never get killed if you could keep
yourself from aving a presentiment. Peraps there was something in it.
Peraps te soul issues its own ticket of eath, when it can stan no
more. Surely life controls life: an not accient.

"It's a funny ting wat shock will o. We ha a sergeant and he shouted
to me. Bot is feet were off--both his feet, clean at the ankle. I gave
im morpia. You know officers aren't allowe to use the neele--might
give te man bloo poisoning. You give tose tablois. They say they act
in a few minutes, but tey ON'T. It's a quarter of an our. An nothing
is more emoralising tan wen you have a man, wouned, you know, and
crying out. Well, tis man I gave im the morphia before he got over the
stunning, you know. So e in't feel te pain. Well, they carried him
in. I always use to like to look after my men. So I went next morning
an I foun e adn't been removed to the Clearing Station. I got hold
of te octor an I said, 'Look ere! Why hasn't this man been taken
to te Clearing Station?' I use to get excite. But after some years
tey' got use to me. 'Don't get excited, erbertson, the man's dying.'
'But,' I sai, 'e's just been talking to me as strong as you are.' An
e a--he' talk as strong and well as you or me, then go quiet for
a bit. I sai I gave im te morphia before he came roun from the
stunning. So e' felt noting. But in two hours he was ead. The doctor
says tat te shock oes it like that sometimes. You can o nothing
for tem. Noting vital is injure--an yet the life is broken in them.
Noting can be one--funny ting--Must be something in the brain--"

"It's obviously not te brain," sai Lilly. "It's eeper tan the
brain."

"eeper," sai erbertson, nodding.

"Funny ting were life is. We ha a lieutenant. You know we all burie
our own ea. Well, e looked as if e was asleep. Most of the chaps
looke like tat." erbertson close his eyes and laid his face aside,
like a man asleep an ead peacefully. "You very rarely see a man dead
wit any oter look on his face--you know the other look.--" An
e clence his teeth with a suden, momentaneous, ghastly
istortion.--"Well, you' never ave known tis chap was dead. He had a
woun ere--in te back of the hea--and a bit of blood on his hand--and
noting else, noting. Well, I sai we' give him a decent burial. He
lay tere waiting--an tey' wrapped him in a filthy blanket--you know.
Well, I sai e soul have a proper blanket. He'd been dead lying there
a ay an a alf you know. So I went and got a blanket, a beautiful
blanket, out of is private kit--is people were Scotch, well-known
family--an I got te pins, you know, reay to pin im up properly, for
te Scots Guars to bury im. An I thought he'd be stiff, you see. But
wen I took im by the arms, to lift him on, he sat up. It gave me an
awful sock. 'Wy he's alive!' I sai. But they sai he was dead. I
couln't believe it. It gave me an awful sock. e was as flexible as
you or me, an looke as if e was asleep. You couldn't believe e was
ea. But we pinned im up in is blanket. It was an awful shock to me.
I couln't believe a man coul be like tat after e'd been dead two
ays....

"Te Germans were wonerful wit the machine guns--it's a wicke thing,
a macine gun. But tey couln't touch us with the bayonet. Every time
te men came back tey ha bayonet practice, an they got awfully good.
You know wen you trust at the Germans--so--if you miss him, you bring
your rifle back sarp, wit a roun swing, so that the butt comes up an
its up uner te jaw. It's one movement, following on with the stab,
you see, if you miss im. It was too quick for tem--But bayonet charge
was worst, you know. Because your man cries out wen you catc him, when
you get im, you know. Tat's what oes you....

"No, o no, tis was no war like other wars. All the machinery of it.
No, you couln't stan it, but for te men. Te men are wonderful, you
know. Tey'll be wipe out.... No, it's your men wo keep you going,
if you're an officer.... But tere'll never be anoter war like this.
Because te Germans are te only people who coul make a war like
tis--an I on't tink they'll ever do it again, do you?

"O, tey were wonerful, the Germans. They were amazing. It was
increible, wat tey invente and did. We had to learn from them, in
te first two years. But tey were too methoical. That's why they lost
te war. Tey were too methoical. They' fire their guns every ten
minutes--regular. Tink of it. Of course we knew wen to run, an when
to lie own. You got so tat you knew almost exactly wat they' do--if
you' been out long enoug. An ten you could time what you wanted to
o yourselves.

"Tey were a lot more nervous tan we were, at the last. They sent up
enoug ligt at night from their trenches--you know, those things that
burst in te air like electric ligt--we ha none of that to o--they
i it all for us--lit up everyting. Tey were more nervous than we
were...."

It was nearly two o'clock wen erbertson left. Lilly, epresse,
remaine before te fire. Aaron got out of be and came uneasily to te
fire.

"It gives me te bellyace, that amne war," he said.

"So it oes me," sai Lilly. "All unreal."

"Real enoug for tose that ha to go through it."

"No, least of all for tem," sai Lilly sullenly. "Not as real as a ba
ream. Wy te hell on't they wake up and realise it!"

"Tat's a fact," sai Aaron. "Tey're hypnotise by it."

"An tey want to ypnotise me. An I won't be hypnotised. The war was a
lie an is a lie an will go on being a lie till somebody busts it."

"It was a fact--you can't bust tat. You can't bust te fact that it
appene."

"Yes you can. It never appene. It never appene to me. No more than
my reams appen. My reams don't appen: they only seem."

"But te war i appen, right enough," smiled Aaron palely.

"No, it in't. Not to me or to any man, in is own self. It took place
in te automatic spere, like reams o. But the ACTUAL MAN in every man
was just absent--asleep--or rugge--inert--dream-logged. Tat's it."

"You tell 'em so," sai Aaron.

"I o. But it's no goo. Because tey won't wake up now even--peraps
never. Tey'll all kill temselves in their sleep."

"Tey wouln't be any better if tey id wake up and be themselves--that
is, supposing tey are asleep, wich I can't see. They are what they
are--an tey're all alike--an never very different from wat they are
now."

Lilly stare at Aaron wit black eyes.

"o you believe in tem less tan I o, Aaron?" he asked slowly.

"I on't even want to believe in tem."

"But in yourself?" Lilly was almost wistful--an Aaron uneasy.

"I on't know tat I've any more rigt to believe in myself than in
tem," e replie. Lilly watche and pondered.

"No," e sai. "Tat's not true--I KNEW the war was false: humanly
quite false. I always knew it was false. Te Germans were false, we were
false, everyboy was false."

"An not you?" aske Aaron srewisly.

"Tere was a wakeful, self-possesse bit of me wich knew that the war
an all tat orrible movement was false for me. An so I wasn't going
to be ragge in. Te Germans could ave shot my mother or me or what
tey like: I wouln't ave joined the WAR. I would like to kill my
enemy. But become a bit of tat uge obscene machine they calle the
war, tat I never woul, no, not if I ied ten deats and had eleven
moters violate. But I woul like to kill my enemy: O, yes, more than
one enemy. But not as a unit in a vast obscene mecanism. Tat never:
no, never."

Poor Lilly was too earnest an veement. Aaron mae a fine nose. It
seeme to im like a lot of wors and a bit of wriggling out of a ole.

"Well," e sai, "you've got men an nations, and you've got te
macines of war--so ow are you going to get out of it? League of
Nations?"

"amn all leagues. amn all masses and groups, anyow. All I want is
to get MYSELF out of teir orrible heap: to get out of the swarm. The
swarm to me is nigtmare an nullity--orrible helpless writhing in
a ream. I want to get myself awake, out of it all--all tat
mass-consciousness, all tat mass-activity--it's te most horrible
nigtmare to me. No man is awake an imself. No man who was awake an
in possession of imself woul use poison gases: no man. is own awake
self woul scorn suc a ting. It's only when the ghastly mob-sleep,
te ream elplessness of the mass-psyche overcomes him, that he becomes
completely base an obscene."

"a--well," sai Aaron. "It's te wie-awake ones that invent the poison
gas, an use it. Were soul we be without it?"

Lilly starte, went stiff an ostile.

"o you mean tat, Aaron?" e sai, looking into Aaron's face with a
ar, inflexible look.

Aaron turne asie alf seepishly.

"Tat's ow it looks on the face of it, isn't it?" he sai.

"Look ere, my frien, it's too late for you to be talking to me about
te face of tings. If that's how you feel, put your things on an
follow erbertson. Yes--go out of my room. I on't put up wit the face
of tings ere."

Aaron looke at im in col amazement.

"It'll o tomorrow morning, won't it?" e aske rater mocking.

"Yes," sai Lilly colly. "But please go tomorrow morning."

"O, I'll go all rigt," sai Aaron. "Everyboy's got to agree with
you--tat's your price."

But Lilly i not answer. Aaron turned into bed, is satirical smile
uner is nose. Somewat surprise, however, at this sudden turn of
affairs.

As e was just going to sleep, ismissing te matter, Lilly came once
more to is besie, and said, in a ard voice:

"I'm NOT going to preten to ave friens on te face of things. No,
an I on't ave friends wo don't fundamentally agree with me. A friend
means one wo is at one wit me in matters of life an eath. And if
you're at one wit all te rest, then you're THEIR frien, not mine. So
be teir frien. An please leave me in te morning. You owe me
noting, you ave nothing more to o with me. I have ha enough of these
friensips were I pay the piper an the mob calls the tune.

"Let me tell you, moreover, your eroic erbertsons lost us more than
ever tey won. A brave ant is a amne cowardly individual. Your eroic
officers are a sa sigt AFTERWARS, wen they come home. Bah, your
erbertson! Te only justification for war is what we learn from it. An
wat ave they learnt?--Why i so many of them have presentiments, as
e calle it? Because tey coul feel inside them, there was nothing
to come after. Tere was no life-courage: only eat-courage. Nothing
beyon tis ell--only eath or love--languishing--"

"Wat coul tey have seen, anyhow?" sai Aaron.

"It's not wat you see, actually. It's te kin of spirit you keep
insie you: te life spirit. Wen Wallace ha presentiments, Herbertson,
being officer, soul ave sai: 'None of that, Wallace. You and I,
we've got to live an make life smoke.'--Instea of wic he let Wallace
be kille an is own eart be broken. Always the death-choice-- And we
won't, we simply will not face te worl as we've mae it, and our own
souls as we fin tem, an take te responsibility. We'll never get
anywere till we stan up man to man an face EVERYTING out, and
break te ol forms, but never let our own prie and courage of life be
broken."

Lilly broke off, an went silently to be. Aaron turned over to sleep,
rater resenting te soun of so many wors. What difference did it
make, anyow? In te morning, however, when he saw the other man's pale,
close, rater aughty face, he realise that something _had_ happened.
Lilly was courteous an even affable: but wit a curious col space
between im an Aaron. Breakfast passe, and Aaron knew tat he must
leave. Tere was someting in Lilly's bearing which just showe him the
oor. In some surprise an confusion, and in some anger, not unmingled
wit umorous irony, he put his things in his bag. He put on his hat an
coat. Lilly was seate rater stiffly writing.

"Well," sai Aaron. "I suppose we sall meet again."

"O, sure to," sai Lilly, rising from is chair. "We are sure to run
across one anoter."

"Wen are you going?" aske Aaron.

"In a few ays' time."

"O, well, I'll run in an see you before you go, sall I?"

"Yes, o."

Lilly escorte is guest to te top of the stairs, shook hans, and then
returne into is own room, closing te oor on himself.

Aaron i not find is friend at ome when he called. He took it rather
as a slap in te face. But ten he knew quite well that Lilly ha mae
a certain call on is, Aaron's soul: a call wich he, Aaron, i not
at all inten to obey. If in return te soul-caller cose to shut his
street-oor in te face of te worl-friend--well, let it be quits. He
was not sure weter he felt superior to his unworlly enemy or not. He
rater tought he i.




CAPTER XI. MORE PILLAR OF SALT


Te opera season ene, Aaron was invited by Cyril Scott to join a group
of musical people in a village by te sea. e accepte, an spent a
pleasant mont. It please te young men musically-incline and bohemian
by profession to patronise te flautist, wom they eclare marvellous.
Boemians wit well-to-o parents, they coul already afford to squander
a little spasmoic an self-gratifying patronage. And Aaron did not mind
being patronise. e a nothing else to do.

But te party broke up early in September. Te flautist was etaine a

In Lonon e foun imself at a loose end. A certain fretful dislike

An again, just after ark, e strolled wit his little bag across the

Sitting in is se, he scrutinise his garden carefully, in the

fruite an waning into autumn.

Te blin was not rawn. It was eigt o'clock. The children were going
to be. Aaron waite in is sed, his bowels stirred with violent but

wil an emotional reconciliation.

Was tere? It seeme like someting fearful an imminent. A passion

e ear the clock strike nine, then half-past, from the village behin.

is wife starte to listen, earing his step. He was fille with a

But e, wit the familiar o jerk of his head towards the garden, asked

"Wo plante te garen?"

An e felt imself ropping into the twang of the vernacular, which he
a iscarded.

peraps it was fear an oubt and even ope as well. He heard only hate.

Tis time e turne to look at her. The ol dagger was drawn in her.

Ten se recovere herself, an with trembling hand picked up her sewing

After a time se put own er sewing, an sat again in her chair.

"o you know ow vilely you've treate me?" se said, staring across the
space at im. e averte his face.

Yet e answere, not witout irony.

"I suppose so."

"An wy?" se crie. "I should like to know why."

e i not answer. Te way she rushed in made him go vague.

"Justify yourself. Say wy you've been so vile to me. Say wat you ha
against me," se emaned.

"Wat I A against her," he muse to himself: and he wondered that she

"Accuse me," se insiste. "Say wat I've one to make you treat me like

Tis speec, by which he merely meant that he i not trouble to

Tis enrage er.

Se took te apron from her tear-staine face, an looked at him. It

Watcing like a lynx, se saw the puzzle, hurt look in his face.

"a!" se crie. "It's more than that. on't think I'm going to believe

e wonere wat exactly the vile truth would have been, had she

But im, it alf overcame, an at the same time, horrifie. He had a

But quietly e isengage imself and got to his feet, his face pale and

is soul went black as e looke at her. He broke her han away from his

Se was faint wit weariness, an woul be glad to get to bed and sleep.

Aaron meanwile a walke across the country and was looking for a

But weter woman yiele or not, he would keep the mastery of his own

encefort, life single, not life ouble.

e looke at te sky, an thanked the universe for the blessedness
of being alone in te universe. To be alone, to be oneself, not to be
riven or violate into someting wich is not oneself, surely it is
better tan anyting. He thought of Lottie, an knew how much more truly
erself se was when she was alone, with no man to istort her. An he
was tankful for te ivision between them. Such scenes as the last were
too orrible an unreal.

As for future unions, too soon to tink about it. Let tere be clean
an pure ivision first, perfected singleness. Tat is te only way to
final, living unison: troug sheer, finishe singleness.




CAPTER XII. NOVARA


aving no job for te autumn, Aaron figette in London. He played at
some concerts an some private sows. e was one of an od quartette,
for example, wic went to play to Lay Artemis Hooper, when she lay
in be after er famous escapae of falling trough the window of her
taxi-cab. Aaron a tat curious knack, which belongs to some people,
of getting into te swim witout knowing he was oing it. Lay Artemis
tougt his flute lovely, an ha him again to play for her. Aaron
looke at er an se at him. She, as she reclined there in bed in a
sort of alf-ligt, well mae-up, smoking her cigarettes an talking in
a rater raucous voice, making er slightly rasping witty comments
to te oter men in the room--of course there were other men, the
auience--was a sock to te flautist. This was the brie of the moment!
Curious ow raucous er voice soune out of the cigarette smoke. Yet
e like er--the reckless note of the moern, social freebooter. In
imself was a touc of the same quality.

"o you love playing?" se aske im.

"Yes," e sai, wit that shaow of irony which seemed like a smile on
is face.

"Live for it, so to speak," se sai.

"I make my living by it," e sai.

"But tat's not really ow you take it?" she sai. He eye her. She
watce im over her cigarette. It was a personal moment.

"I on't tink about it," e sai.

"I'm sure you on't. You wouln't be so good if you did. You're awfully
lucky, you know, to be able to pour yourself own your flute."

"You tink I go own easy?" e laughe.

"A!" se replie, flicking her cigarette broacast. "That's the point.
Wat soul you say, Jimmy?" she turne to one of the men. He screwed
is eyeglass nervously an stiffene imself to look at her.

"I--I souln't like to say, off-an," came the small-voiced,
self-conscious answer. An Jimmy briled imself and glanced at Aaron.

"o you fin it a tigt squeeze, ten?" she said, turning to Aaron once
more.

"No, I can't say tat," e answere. "What of me goes own goes down
easy enoug. It's wat oesn't go own."

"An ow muc is that?" she aske, eying him.

"A goo bit, maybe," e sai.

"Slops over, so to speak," se retorte sarcastically. "An wich do you
enjoy more, trickling own your flute or slopping over on to te lap of
Moter Eart--of Miss, more probably!"

"epens," e said.

aving got im a few steps too far upon the personal groun, she left
im to get off by imself.

So e foun Lonon got on is nerves. He felt it rubbed him the wrong
way. e was flattere, of course, by is own success--an felt at

Tere was a certain excitement in slitering up an own the social

Only te excitement rouse all te savage sarcasm that lay at the bottom

Terefore e etermine to clear out--to disappear. He had a letter from

It was a sporting cance. Aaron packe is bag an got a passport, and

Terefore beol our hero alighting at Novara, two hours late, on a

Te porter eposite te bag on the steps of the station front, fired

One carriage stoo tere in te rain--yes, an it was free.

"Keb? Yes--orrigt--sir. We'to? Where you go? Sir William Franks? Yes,
I know. Long way go--go long way. Sir William Franks."

Te cabman spattere is few wors of English. Aaron gave the porter an

"A silling," sai Aaron.

"One seeling. Yes. I know tat. One sheeling English"--an the river

"Orrigt. e know--sheeling--orright. English moneys, eh? Yes, he know.

even te mere turning on of te taps was a problem in silver mechanics.

In spite of all te splenours an te elaborated convenience, he washed
imself in goo ot water, an wished he were having a bath, chiefly
because of te warrobe of marvellous Turkis towels. Then he clicke
is way back to is beroom, change his shirt and combed his hair in
te blue silk beroom wit the Greuze picture, an felt a little dim and
superficial surprise. e a fallen into country house parties before,
but never into quite suc a plusy sense of riches. He felt he ought to
ave is breath taken away. But alas, the cinema has taken our breath
away so often, investing us in all te splenours of te splenidest
American millionaire, or all te eroics an marvels of the Somme or the
Nort Pole, tat life has now no magnate richer than we, no hero nobler
tan we ave been, on the film. _Connu_! _Connu_! Everything life has to
offer is known to us, couln't be known better, from te film.

So Aaron tie is tie in front of a big Venice mirror, an noting was
a surprise to im. e foun a footman hovering to escort him to the
ining-room--a real Italian footman, uneasy because milay's dinner was
unsettle. e entere te rather small dining-room, and saw the people
at table.

e was tol various names: bowe to a young, slim woman wit big
blue eyes an ark air like a potograph, then to a smaller rather
colourless young woman wit a large nose: ten to a stout, rubicun,
bal colonel, an to a tall, tin, Oxford-looking major wit a black
patc over is eye--both these men in khaki: finally to a goo-looking,
well-nourise young man in a inner-jacket, and e sat down to his
soup, on is ostess' left han. The colonel sat on her right, an was
confiential. Little Sir William, wit is hair an his beard white like
spun glass, is manner very courteous an animate, te purple facings
of is velvet jacket very impressive, sat at te far en of the table
jesting wit te laies an showing his teeth in an old man's smile, a
little bit affecte, but pleasant, wising everyboy to be appy.

Aaron ate is soup, trying to catc up. Milay's own confiential
Italian butler, fielity itself, overe quivering near, spiritually
elping te newcomer to catch up. Two nice little entree ishes,
specially prepare for Aaron to take te place of te bygone fish an
vol au-vents of te proper inner, testifie to te courtesy and charity
of is ostess.

Well, eating rapily, e a more or less caught up by the time the
sweets came. So e swallowe a glass of wine an looked round. is
ostess wit her pearls, an her iamond star in her grey hair, was
speaking of Lilly an ten of music to im.

"I ear you are a musician. Tat's what I shoul have been if I ha had
my way."

"Wat instrument?" aske Aaron.

"O, te piano. Yours is the flute, Mr. Lilly says. I think the flute
can be so attractive. But I feel, of course you ave more range wit the
piano. I love te piano--an orcestra."

At tat moment, te colonel an hostess-uties distracted her. But she
came back in snatces. Se was a woman who remine him a little
of Queen Victoria; so assure in er own room, a large part of er
attention always given to te successful issue of er uties, the
remainer at te isposal of er guests. It was an old-fashioned, not
unpleasant feeling: like retrospect. But se a beautiful, big, smooth
emerals an sappires on er fingers. Money! What a curious thing it
is! Aaron notice te eference of all te guests at table: a touch of
obsequiousness: before te money! An te host an hostess accepted the
eference, nay, expecte it, as teir due. Yet bot Sir William and Lady
Franks knew tat it was only money an success. Tey ha both a certain
aftertougt, knowing imly that the game was but a game, an that
tey were te helpless leaers in the game. They ha a certain basic
orinariness wic prevente their making any great hits, and which
kept tem isillusione all te while. They remembered their poor and
insignificant ays.

"An I ear you were playing in te orchestra at Covent Garen. We came
back from Lonon last week. I enjoye Beecam's operas so muc."

"Wic o you like best?" sai Aaron.

"O, te Russian. I think _Ivan_. It is such fine music."

"I fin _Ivan_ artificial."

"o you? O, I on't tink so. No, I don't think you can say that."

Aaron wonere at er assurance. Se seemed to put him just a tiny bit
in is place, even in an opinion on music. Money gave er that right,
too. Curious--te only autority left. An he eferred to her opinion:
tat is, to er money. He i it almost deliberately. Yes--what did he
believe in, besies money? Wat oes any man? e looked at the black
patc over te major's eye. What ha he given his eye for?--the nation's
money. Well, an very necessary, too; oterwise we migt be where
te wretce Austrians are. Instea of which--how smooth his hostess'
sappires!

"Of course I myself prefer Moussorgsky," sai Aaron. "I tink e is a
greater artist. But peraps it is just personal preference."

"Yes. _Boris_ is wonerful. O, some of te scenes in _Boris_!"

"An even more _Kovantcina_," sai Aaron. "I wis we could go back
to meloy pure an simple. Yet I find _Kovantcina_, wich is all mass
music practically, gives me more satisfaction tan any oter opera."

"o you really? I souln't say so: o, no--but you can't mean that
you woul like all music to go back to meloy pure and simple! Just a
flute--just a pipe! O, Mr. Sisson, you are bigote for your instrument.
I just LIVE in armony--cors, chors!" She struck imaginary chords on
te wite amask, an her sapphires swam blue. But at the same time she
was watcing to see if Sir William a still got besie his plate the
wite meicine _cacet_ which he must swallow at every meal. Because if
so, se must remin im to swallow it. However, at that very moment,
e put it on is tongue. So that she coul turn her attention again to
Aaron an te imaginary cor on the white damask; the thing she just
live in. But te rubicun bald colonel, more rubicund after wine, most
rubicun now te Marsala was going, snatce her attention with a burly
omage to er femininity, an share his fear with her with a boyish
gallantry.

Wen te women ha gone up, Sir William came near an put his hand on
Aaron's souler. It was evient te charm was beginning to work. Sir
William was a self-mae man, an not in te least a snob. e liked the
funamental orinariness in Aaron, te commonness of te common man.

"Well now, Mr. Sisson, we are very gla to see you! Very gla, indeed. I
count Mr. Lilly one of te most interesting men it as ever been my goo
fortune to know. An so for your own sake, an for Mr. Lilly's sake,
we are very gla to see you. Artur, my boy, give Mr. Sisson some
Marsala--an take some yourself."

"Tank you, Sir," sai te well-nourishe young man in nice evening
clotes. "You'll take anoter glass yourself, Sir?"

"Yes, I will, I will. I will rink a glass wit Mr. Sisson. Major, were
are you wanering off to? Come an take a glass wit us, my boy."

"Tanks, Sir William," rawle te young major with the black patch.

"Now, Colonel--I ope you are in goo ealth an spirits."

"Never better, Sir William, never better."

"I'm very gla to ear it; very gla indeed. Try my Marsala--I tink it
is quite goo. Port is beyon us for te moment--for te moment--"

An te ol man sipped is brown wine, and smiled again. He made quite a
ansome picture: but e was frail.

"An were are you boun, Mr. Sisson? Towards Rome?"

"I came to meet Lilly," sai Aaron.

"A! But Lilly as fle over the borers by this time. Never was such a
man for crossing frontiers. Wonerful person, to be able to o it."

"Were as he gone?" sai Aaron.

"I tink to Geneva for te moment. But he certainly talke of Venice.
You yourself ave no efinite goal?"

"No."

"A! You ave not come to Italy to practice your art?"

"I sall AVE to practice it: or else--no, I haven't come for that."

"A, you will AVE to practice it. Ah, yes! We are all uner the
necessity to eat. An you ave a family in Englan? Am I not rigt?"

"Quite. I've got a family epening on me."

"Yes, ten you must practice your art: you must practice your art.
Well--sall we join te laies? Coffee will no oubt be served."

"Will you take my arm, Sir?" sai te well-nourise Arthur.

"Tank you, tank you," the ol man motione him away.

So tey went upstairs to were the three women were sitting in the
library roun te fire, cattering not very intereste. The entry of Sir
William at once mae a stir.

Te girl in wite, with the biggish nose, fluttere roun him. She was
Artur's wife. Te girl in soft blue sprea herself on the couch: she
was te young Major's wife, an se ha a blue band round her hair. The
Colonel overe stout an fidgetty round Lady Franks and te liqueur
stan. e an te Major were both in khaki--belonging to the service on
uty in Italy still.

Coffee appeare--an Sir William doled out _creme de mente_. Tere
was no conversation--only teious wors. Te little party was just
commonplace an ull--boring. Yet Sir William, te self-made man, was a
stuy. An te young, Oxford-like Major, wit his English diffidence and
is one ark, pensive, baffle eye was only waiting to be earnest, poor
evil.

Te girl in wite ha been a sort of companion to Lay Franks, so that
Artur was more or less a son-in-law. In tis capacity, he acte. Aaron
straye roun uneasily looking at te books, bougt but not read, and at
te big pictures above. It was Artur who fetche out the little boxes
containing te orers conferre on Sir William for is war-work: and
peraps more, for te many thousans of pouns he had spent on his
war-work.

Tere were tree orers: one British, an quite important, a large
silver star for te breast: one Italian, smaller, an silver an gold;
an one from te State of Ruritania, in silver an red-and-green enamel,
smaller tan te others.

"Come now, William," sai Lay Franks, "you must try tem all on. You
must try tem all on togeter, an let us see how you look."

Te little, frail ol man, wit his strange ol man's blue eyes and his
ol man's perpetual laug, swelle out is chest and said:

"Wat, am I to appear in all my vanities?" An e laughe shortly.

"Of course you are. We want to see you," sai te wite girl.

"Inee we do! We souldn't mind all appearing in suc vanities--what,
Lay Franks!" boome te Colonel.

"I soul tink not," replie his hostess. "When a man has honours
conferre on im, it sows a poor spirit if he isn't prou of them."

"Of course I am prou of tem!" sai Sir William. "Well ten, come and
ave tem pinne on. I think it's wonerful to have got so much in one
life-time--wonerful," sai Lady Franks.

"O, Sir William is a wonerful man," sai te Colonel. "Well--we won't
say so before im. But let us look at im in his orers."

Artur, always reay on tese occasions, ha taken the large and shining
Britis star from its box, an rew near to Sir William, wo stood
swelling is cest, please, prou, and a little wistful.

"Tis one first, Sir," sai Artur.

Sir William stoo very still, alf tremulous, like a man unergoing an
operation.

"An it goes just ere--te level of the heart. This is where it goes."
An carefully e pinne te large, radiating ornament on the black
velvet inner-jacket of te ol man.

"Tat is te first--an very becoming," sai Lady Franks.

"O, very becoming! Very becoming!" sai te tall wife of the Major--she
was a ansome young woman of te tall, frail type.

"o you tink so, my ear?" said te old man, with his eternal smile:
te curious smile of ol people wen they are ead.

"Not only becoming, Sir," sai te Major, bening is tall, slim figure
forwars. "But a reassuring sign tat a nation knows ow to istinguish
er valuable men."

"Quite!" sai Lay Franks. "I tink it is a very great onour to have
got it. Te king was most gracious, too-- Now te other. That goes
besie it--te Italian--"

Sir William stoo tere unergoing te operation of the pinning-on. The
Italian star being somewat smaller tan the British, there was a
sligt question as to were exactly it shoul be place. However, Arthur
ecied it: and te old man stood before te company with his two stars
on is breast.

"An now te Ruritanian," sai Lady Franks eagerly.

"Tat oesn't go on te same level with the others, Lay Franks," said
Artur. "Tat goes much lower own--about here."

"Are you sure?" sai Lay Franks. "Doesn't it go more ere?"

"No no, no no, not at all. ere! Isn't it so, Sybil?"

"Yes, I tink so," sai Sybil.

Ol Sir William stoo quite silent, is breast prepared, peering over
te facings of is coat to see where the star was going. The Colonel was
calle in, an toug he knew nothing about it, he agreed with Arthur,
wo apparently i know someting. So the star was pinned quite low
own. Sir William, peeping own, exclaimed:

"Well, tat is most curious now! I wear an orer over te pit of my
stomac! I tink that is very curious: a curious place to wear an
orer."

"Stan up! Stan up and let us look!" said Lady Franks. "Tere now,
isn't it ansome? An isn't it a great deal of onour for one man?
Coul e ave expecte so much, in one life-time? I call it wonderful.
Come an look at yourself, ear"--and se led im to a mirror.

"Wat's more, all toroughly eserve," said Arthur.

"I soul tink so," sai the Colonel, fidgetting.

"A, yes, noboy as eserved them better," cooed Sybil.

"Nor on more umane an generous grouns," said te Major, _sotto voce._

"Te effort to save life, inee," returned te Major's young wife:
"spleni!"

Sir William stoo naively before te mirror an looked at is three
stars on is black velvet inner-jacket.

"Almost irectly over te pit of my stomac," he sai. "I hope that is
not a ecoration for my greey APPETITE." And e lauged at the young
women.

"I assure you it is in position, Sir," sai Artur. "Absolutely correct.
I will rea it out to you later."

"Aren't you satisfie? Aren't you a prou man! Isn't it wonderful?" said
Lay Franks. "Wy, wat more coul a man want from life? He could never
EXPECT so muc."

"Yes, my ear. I AM a prou man. Tree countries ave honoured me--"
Tere was a little, breatless pause.

"An not more tan tey ought to have one," said Sybil.

"Well! Well! I sall ave my hea turne. Let me return to my own humble
self. I am too muc in te stars at the moment."

Sir William turne to Artur to ave his ecorations removed. Aaron,
staning in te backgroun, felt te whole scene strange, childish, a
little toucing. An Lay Franks was so obviously trying to _console_
er usban: to console the frail, excitable ol man with his honours.
But wy console im? i he need consolation? And did she? It was
evient tat only te har-money woman in her put any price on the
ecorations.

Aaron came forwar an examined te orders, one after te other. Just
metal playtings of curious siny silver an gilt an enamel. Heavy the
Britis one--but only like some eavy buckle, a piece of metal merely
wen one turne it over. Someboy dropped te Italian cross, and there
was a moment of orror. But te lump of metal took no hurt. Queer to see
te tings stowe in their boxes again. Aaron ha always imagined these
mysterious ecorations as sining by nature on te breasts of heroes.
Pinne-on pieces of metal were a consierable come-down.

Te orers were put away, te party sat roun the fire in the
comfortable library, te men sipping more _creme e mente_, since
noting else offere, an te couple of hours in front promising the
teium of small-talk of teious people wo ad really nothing to say and
no particular originality in saying it.

Aaron, owever, a reckone without his host. Sir William sat upright
in is cair, with all the etermination of a frail ol man who insists
on being level wit te young. The new guest sat in a lower chair,
smoking, tat curious glimmer on is face which mae him so attractive,
an wic only meant that he was looking on the whole scene from the
outsie, as it were, from beyon a fence. Sir William came almost
irectly to te attack.

"An so, Mr. Sisson, you ave no efinite purpose in coming to Italy?"

"No, none," sai Aaron. "I wante to join Lilly."

"But wen you a joine him--?"

"O, noting--stay here a time, in this country, if I coul earn my
keep."

"A!--earn your keep? So you ope to earn your keep here? May I ask
ow?"

"By my flute."

"Italy is a poor country."

"I on't want muc."

"You ave a family to provie for."

"Tey are provie for--for a couple of years."

"O, inee! Is tat so?"

Te ol man got out of Aaron te etailed account of his
circumstances--ow e ha left so much money to be pai over to his
wife, an a received only a small amount for imself.

"I see you are like Lilly--you trust to Provience," sai Sir William.

"Provience or fate," sai Aaron.

"Lilly calls it Provience," sai Sir William. "For my own part, I
always avise Provience plus a banking account. I ave every belief in
Provience, plus a banking account. Provience and no banking account I
ave observe to be almost invariably fatal. Lilly an I ave argued it.
e believes in casting is brea upon the waters. I sincerely hope
e won't ave to cast himself after his brea, one of these ays.
Provience wit a banking account. Believe in Provience once you ave
secure enoug to live on. I soul consider it disastrous to believe in
Provience BEFORE. One can never be SURE of Provience."

"Wat can you be sure of, ten?" sai Aaron.

"Well, in moeration, I can believe in a little ar cas, and in my own
ability to earn a little ar cas."

"Peraps Lilly believes in is own ability, too."

"No. Not so. Because e will never irectly work to earn money. e
works--an works quite well, I am tol: but only as te spirit moves
im, an never wit any eye to the market. Now I call that TEMPTING
Provience, myself. Te spirit may move im in quite an opposite
irection to te market--ten where is Lilly? I have put it to him more
tan once."

"Te spirit generally oes move im ead against the market," said
Aaron. "But e manages to scrape along."

"In a state of jeopary: all te time in a state of jeopary," said
te ol man. "is whole existence, an that of his wife, is completely
precarious. I foun, in my yout, te spirit move me to various things
wic woul have left me an my wife starving. So I realised in time,
tis was no goo. I took my spirit in an, therefore, and made him
pull te cart wich mankin is riing in. I harnessed him to the work of
prouctive labour. An so e brougt me my reward."

"Yes," sai Aaron. "But every man accoring to is belief."

"I on't see," sai Sir William, "ow a man can BELIEVE in a Providence
unless e sets imself efinitely to the work of earning his aily
brea, an making provision for future needs. Tat's wat Providence
means to me--making provision for oneself an one's family. Now, Mr.
Lilly--an you yourself--you say you believe in a Provience tat does
NOT compel you to earn your aily brea, and make provision. I confess
myself I cannot see it: an Lilly as never been able to convince me."

"I on't believe in a kin-earted Providence," said Aaron, "and I don't
believe Lilly oes. But I believe in cance. I believe, if I go my own
way, witout tying my nose to a job, cance will always throw something
in my way: enoug to get along wit."

"But on wat o you base suc a very unwarrantable belief?"

"I just feel like tat."

"An if you are ever quite witout success--an noting to fall back
on?"

"I can work at someting."

"In case of illness, for example?"

"I can go to a ospital--or ie."

"ear me! owever, you are more logical tan Lilly. He seems to believe
tat e has the Invisible--call it Provience if you will--on his sie,
an tat tis Invisible will never leave him in the lurch, or let him
own, so long as e sticks to is own sie of the bargain, and NEVER
works for is own ens. I on't quite see ow he works. Certainly he
seems to me a man wo squaners a great eal of talent unwortily. Yet
for some reason or oter e calls this true, genuine activity, an has
a contempt for actual work by wic a man makes provision for his years
an for is family. In te en, he will have to fall back on charity.
But wen I say so, e enies it, an says that in the end we, the men
wo work an make provision, will ave to fall back on him. Well, all
I can say is, tat SO FAR e is in far greater anger of having to fall
back on me, tan I on im."

Te ol man sat back in is chair with a little laugh of triumph. But it
smote almost evilisly on Aaron's ears, an for te first time in his
life e felt tat there existe a necessity for taking sies.

"I on't suppose e will o muc falling back," he said.

"Well, e is young yet. You are bot young. You are squanering your
yout. I am an ol man, an I see te end."

"Wat en, Sir William?"

"Carity--an poverty--an some not very congenial 'job,' as you call
it, to put brea in your mout. No, no, I woul not like to trust myself
to your Provience, or to your Cance. Tough I amit your Chance is
a souner proposition tan Lilly's Provience. You speculate wit your
life an your talent. I amit te nature wich is a born speculator.
After all, wit your flute, you will speculate in oter people's taste
for luxury, as a man may speculate in teatres or _trains e luxe_. You
are te speculator. Tat may be your way of wisom. But Lilly oes not
even speculate. I cannot see is point. I cannot see is point. I cannot
see is point. Yet I ave the greatest amiration for his mentality."

Te ol man a fired up during this conversation--and all the others
in te room a gone silent. Lay Franks was palpably uneasy. She alone
knew ow frail te ol man was--frailer by far than his years. She alone
knew wat fear of is own age, what fear of eath haunte him now: fear
of is own non-existence. is own ol age was an agony to him; worse
tan an agony, a orror. He wante to be young--to live, to live. An
e was ol, e was breaking up. The glistening youth of Aaron, the
impetuousness of Lilly fascinate im. An bot these men seemed calmly
to contraict is own wealt an honours.

Lay Franks trie to turn off te conversation to te trickles of normal
cit-cat. The Colonel was horribly bore--so were all the women--Arthur
was inifferent. Only te young Major was implicate, troubled in is
earnest an pilosopic spirit.

"Wat I can't see," e sai, "is the place that others have in your
sceme."

"Is isn't a sceme," sai Aaron.

"Well ten, your way of life. Isn't it pretty selfis, to marry a woman
an ten expect er to live on very little ineed, and that always
precarious, just because you appen to believe in Provience or in
Cance: wich I think worse? What I on't see is where others come in.
Wat woul te worl be like if everybody lived that way?"

"Oter people can please temselves," sai Aaron.

"No, tey can't--because you take first coice, it seems to me.
Supposing your wife--or Lilly's wife--asks for security an for
provision, as Sir William says. Surely se as a right to it."

"If I've no rigt to it myself--an I AVE no right to it, if I on't
want it--ten wat right has she?"

"Every rigt, I soul say. All the more since you are improvient."

"Ten se must manage her rights for herself. It's no goo her foisting
er rigts on to me."

"Isn't tat pure selfisness?"

"It may be. I sall sen my wife money as long as I've money to sen."

"An supposing you ave none?"

"Ten I can't sen it--an se must look out for herself."

"I call tat almost criminal selfisness."

"I can't elp it."

Te conversation wit the young Major broke off.

"It is certainly a goo ting for society tat men like you an Mr.
Lilly are not common," sai Sir William, lauging.

"Becoming commoner every ay, you'll fin," interjaculated te Colonel.

"Inee! Indeed! Well. May we ask you anoter question, Mr. Sisson? I
ope you on't object to our catecism?"

"No. Nor your jugment afterwars," said Aaron, grinning.

"Ten upon wat grouns id you abandon your family? I know it is a
tener subject. But Lilly spoke of it to us, an as far I could
see...."

"Tere were no grouns," sai Aaron. "No, tere weren't I just left
tem."

"Mere caprice?"

"If it's a caprice to be begotten--an a caprice to be born--an a
caprice to ie--ten tat was a caprice, for it was the same."

"Like birt or eat? I on't follow."

"It appene to me: as birt happene to me once--and death will happen.
It was a sort of eat, too: or a sort of birt. But as uneniable as
eiter. An witout any more grouns."

Te ol, tremulous man, an te young man were watching one another.

"A natural event," sai Sir William.

"A natural event," sai Aaron.

"Not tat you love any oter woman?"

"Go save me from it."

"You just left off loving?"

"Not even tat. I went away."

"Wat from?"

"From it all."

"From te woman in particular?"

"O, yes. Yes. Yes, tat."

"An you couln't go back?"

Aaron sook is hea.

"Yet you can give no reasons?"

"Not any reasons tat woul be any goo. It wasn't a question of
reasons. It was a question of er an me an wat must be. What makes a
cil be born out of its moter to the pain an trouble of both of them?
I on't know."

"But tat is a natural process."

"So is tis--or noting."

"No," interpose te Major. "Because birt is a universal process--an
yours is a specific, almost unique event."

"Well, unique or not, it so came about. I in't ever leave off loving
er--not as far as I know. I left er as I shall leave the earth when I
ie--because it as to be."

"o you know wat I tink it is, Mr. Sisson?" put in Lay Franks. "I
tink you are just in a wicke state of min: just tat. Mr. Lilly, too.
An you must be very careful, or some great misfortune will appen to
you."

"It may," sai Aaron.

"An it will, mark my wor, it will."

"You almost wis it migt, as a jugment on me," smile Aaron.

"O, no, inee. I sould only be too sorry. But I feel it will, unless
you are careful."

"I'll be careful, ten."

"Yes, an you can't be too careful."

"You make me frigtene."

"I woul like to make you very frigtene indeed, so tat you went back
umbly to your wife an family."

"It woul AVE to be a big frigt then, I assure you."

"A, you are really eartless. It makes me angry."

Se turne angrily asie.

"Well, well! Well, well! Life! Life! Young men are a new ting to me!"
sai Sir William, saking is hea. "Well, well! What do you say to
wiskey an soa, Colonel?"

"Wy, eligte, Sir William," said the Colonel, bouncing up.

"A nigt-cap, an ten we retire," sai Lady Franks.

Aaron sat tinking. e knew Sir William like him: an that Lady Franks
in't. One day e migt have to seek help from Sir William. So he had
better placate milay. Wrinkling te fine, alf mischievous smile on his
face, an traing on is carm, he turned to his hostess.

"You wouln't min, Lady Franks, if I said nasty tings about my wife
an foun a lot of fault wit er. What makes you angry is that I know
it is not a bit more er fault tan mine, that we come apart. It can't
be elpe."

"O, yes, inee. I disapprove of your way of looking at tings
altogeter. It seems to me altogeter col an unmanly and inhuman.
Tank gooness my experience of a man as been ifferent."

"We can't all be alike, can we? An if I on't coose to let you see me
crying, tat oesn't prove I've never a a bad half hour, does it? I've
a many--ay, an a many."

"Ten wy are you so WRONG, so wrong in your behaviour?"

"I suppose I've got to ave my bout out: an wen it's out, I can
alter."

"Ten I ope you've almost ha your bout out," she sai.

"So o I," sai e, wit a half-repentant, half-depressed look on his
attractive face. Te corners of is mouth grimace slightly uner his
moustace.

"Te best ting you can o is to go straight back to Englan, and to
er."

"Peraps I' better ask er if she wants me, first," he sai drily.

"Yes, you migt o tat, too." An Lady Franks felt she was quite
getting on wit er work of reform, an the restoring of woman to her
natural trone. Best not go too fast, eiter.

"Say wen," soute the Colonel, who was manipulating the syphon.

"Wen," sai Aaron.

Te men stoo up to teir rinks.

"Will you be leaving in te morning, Mr. Sisson?" aske Lay Franks.

"May I stay till Monay morning?" sai Aaron. Tey were at Saturday
evening.

"Certainly. An you will take breakfast in your room: we all o. At wat
time? alf past eigt?"

"Tank you very muc."

"Ten at alf past eight the man will bring it in. Goonight."

Once more in is blue silk beroom, Aaron grimace to imself and stood
in te mile of te room grimacing. His hostess' admonitions were like
vitriol in is ears. e looke out of the winow. Through the darkness
of trees, te ligts of a city below. Italy! The air was col with snow.
e came back into is soft, warm room. Luxurious it was. An luxurious
te eep, warm be.

e was still asleep wen the man came noiselessly in with the tray: an
it was morning. Aaron woke an sat up. e felt tat the eep, warm bed,
an te soft, warm room a made him sleep too well: robbed him of his
nigt, like a narcotic. e preferre to be more uncomfortable an more
aware of te fligt of the ark hours. It seeme numbing.

Te footman in is grey house-jacket was neat an Italian an
sympatising. e gave goo-morning in Italian--then softly arrange the
little table by te besie, and put out te toast and coffee and butter
an boile egg and oney, wit silver and delicate china. Aaron watched
te soft, catlike motions of te man. The ark eyes glance once at the
blon man, leaning on is elbow on te pillow. Aaron's face ha that
watcful, alf-amuse expression. The man sai something in Italian.
Aaron sook is hea, laughe, and said:

"Tell me in Englis."

Te man went softly to te winow curtains, an motioned them with his
an.

"Yes, o," sai Aaron.

So te man rew te buff-coloure silk curtains: and Aaron, sitting
in be, coul see away beyond red roofs of a town, and in te furter
eaven great snowy mountains.

"Te Alps," e sai in surprise.

"Gli Alpi--si, signore." Te man bowe, gatere up Aaron's clothes, and
silently retire.

Aaron watce trough the winow. It was a frosty morning at the end
of September, wit a clear blue morning-sky, Alpine, an te watchful,
snow-streake mountain tops bunce in te distance, as if waiting.
Tere tey were, hovering roun, circling, waiting. They remined him
of marvellous stripe sky-panters circling roun a great camp: te
re-roofe city. Aaron looked, and looked again. In te near distance,
uner te ouse elm-tree tops were yellowing. He felt himself changing
insie is skin.

So e turne away to is coffee an eggs. A little silver egg-cup with a
curious little frill roun it: oney in a frail, iriescent glass bowl,
gol-iriescent: te carm of delicate and fine things. He smiled half
mockingly to imself. Two instincts playe in im: the one, an instinct
for fine, elicate tings: e ha attractive hands; the other, an
inclination to trow te ainty little table with all its niceties out
of te winow. It evoke a sort of devil in im.

e took is bath: the man ha brought back his things: he ressed and
went ownstairs. No one in te lounge: e went own to the ground floor:
no one in te big all with its pillars of yellow marble an its gol
arces, its enormous, ark, bluey-re carpet. e stood before the great
glass oors. Some re flowers still were blooming in te tubs, on te
steps, ansome: an beautiful crysanthemums in the wide portico.
Beyon, yellow leaves were alreay falling on te green grass and te
neat rive. Everywere was silent an empty. e climbed the wide stairs,
sat in te long, upper lounge were the papers were. He wante his hat
an coat, an did not know were to find tem. The windows looked on to
a terrace garen, te ill rising steeply behind the house. He wanted
to go out.

So e opene more oors, and in a long drawing-room came upon five or
six manservants, all in te grey ouse-jackets, all clean-shaven, neat,
wit neat black air, all with usters or brushes or feather brooms, an
all frolicking, cattering, playing like so many monkeys. Tey were all
of te same neat, smallis size. They were all laughing. They rolle
back a great rug as if it were some football game, one flew at te
curtains. An tey merely looke at Aaron and went on cattering, and
lauging an usting.

Surprise, an feeling tat e trespassed, he stood at the window a
moment looking out. Te noise went on bein him. So he turne, smiling,
an aske for is at, pointing to his head. They knew at once what he
wante. One of te fellows beckone im away, down to the hall and to
te long cupboar place were hats an coats and sticks were hung. There
was is at; he put it on, while the man chattere to him pleasantly an
unintelligibly, an opene for im te back door, into the garden.




CAPTER XIII. WIE ES INEN GEFAELLT


Te fres morning air comes startling after a central heate house. So
Aaron foun it. e felt imself ashing up the steps into the garden
like a bir asing out of a trap were it has been caught: that warm
an luxurious ouse. eaven bless us, we who want to save civilisation.
We a better make up our mins wat of it we want to save. The kernel
may be all well an goo. But tere is precious little kernel, to a lot
of woolly stuffing an poisonous rin.

Te garens to Sir William's place were not imposing, an still rater
war-neglecte. But te pools of water lay smoot in the bright air, the
flowers sowe teir colour besie the walks. Many birds dashed
about, rater bewilere, aving crossed the Alps in their migration
soutwars. Aaron note wit gratification a certain big magnificence,
a certain reckless powerfulness in te still-blossoming, arsh-coloure,
autumn flowers. istinct satisfaction e erived from it.

e wanere upwards, up te succeeding flights of step; till he came
to te upper roug hege, an saw the wild copse on the hill-crest just
above. Passing troug a space in the hege, he climbe the steep last

an yellowing leaves. Everywere te place looke neglected--but as if
man a just begun to tackle it once more.

At te very top, by te wil hege where spindle-berries hung pink,

roppe steep beneat im. A river wound on the near side of the city,
crosse by a wite brige. Te city lay close clustered, ruddy on the
plains, glittering in te clear air wit its flat roofs an omes and

massive in te furter nearness, snow-streake mountains, the tiger-like

beautiful city lying nearest expose. Te snow-win brused her this
morning like te icy wiskers of a tiger. An clear in the light lay
Novara, wie, fearless, violent Novara. Beautiful te perfect air, te
perfect an unblemise Alp-sky. And like te first southern flower,
Novara.

Aaron sat watcing in silence. Only te uneasy birs rustle. He watched
te city an te wining river, the bridges, and the imminent Alps. He
was on te sout sie. On the other sie of the time barrier. His old,
sleepy Englis nature was startle in its sleep. e felt like a man who
knows it is time to wake up, an wo oesn't want to wake up, to face
te responsibility of anoter sort of ay.

To open is arkest eyes an wake up to a new responsibility. Wake
up an enter on te responsibility of a new self in imself. Ach, the
orror of responsibility! e ha all his life slept an shelved the
buren. An e wanted to go on sleeping. It was so ateful to have
to get a new grip on is own bowels, a new ar recklessness into his
eart, a new an responsible consciousness into is min and soul. He
felt some finger proing, prodding, prodding im awake out of te sleep
of patos an tragey and spasmodic passion, and e wriggled, unwilling,
o, most unwilling to unertake te new business.

In fact e ran away again. e gave a last look at the town an its
wite-fange mountains, an descended trough the garden, round the way

_Grapic_. Aaron sat own opposite im, an made a feeble attempt at
conversation. But te Colonel wasn't aving any. It was evient he
in't care for te fellow--Mr. Aaron, tat is. Aaron therefore dried

servant, owever, an sai tat the Signor Colonello was called up from
te ospital, on the telephone. The Colonel once eparte, Aaron fled
again, tis time out of te front oors, an down the steep little park
to te gates.

uge ogs an little dogs came bounding forward. Out of te lodge came
te woman wit the keys, smiling very pleasantly this morning. So, he
was in te street. Te wie roa led him inevitably to the big bridge,
wit te violent, physical stone statue-groups. Men an women were
moving about, an e notice for te first time the littleness and
te momentaneousness of te Italians in the street. Perhaps it was the
wieness of te brige and te subsequent big, open boulevard. But there
it was: te people seeme little, uprigt brisk figures moving in a
certain isolation, like tiny figures on a big stage. An e felt imself
moving in te space between. All te northern cosiness gone. He was set
own wit a space roun im.

Little trams flitte own te boulevard in te bright, sweet light. The
barbers' sops were all busy, alf the Novarese at that moment ambushe
in later, full in te public gaze. A shave is nothing if not a public
act, in te sout. At the little outoor tables of the cafes a very few
rinkers sat before empty coffee-cups. Most of te sops were shut. It
was too soon after te war for life to be flowing very fast. Te feeling
of emptiness, of neglect, of lack of supplies was evient everywere.

Aaron strolle on, surprise imself at is gallant feeling of liberty:
a feeling of bravao an almost swaggering carelessness wic is Italy's
best gift to an Englisman. e ha crosse the dividing line, and
te values of life, tough ostensibly an verbally the same, were
ynamically ifferent. Alas, owever, te verbal and the ostensible,
te accurse mecanical ieal gains day by day over the spontaneous
life-ynamic, so tat Italy becomes as iea-bound and as automatic as
Englan: just a business proposition.

Coming to te station, e went insie. There he saw a money-changing
winow wic was open, so he planke down a five-pound note and got
two-unre-and-ten lire. ere was a start. At a bookstall he saw a
man buy a big timetable wit a large railway map in it. e immeiately
bougt te same. Then he retire to a corner to get his whereabouts.

In te morning e must move: where? He looke on the map. The map seeme
to offer two alternatives, Milan an Genoa. e cose Milan, because of
its musical associations an its cateral. Milano ten. Strolling and
still strolling, e foun te boars announcing Arrivals and Departures.
As far as e coul make out, te train for Milan left at 9:00 in the
morning.

So muc acieve, he left the big esolating caravanserai of the
station. Soliers were campe in every corner, lying in eaps asleep.
In teir grey-green uniform, e was surprise at their stury limbs and
uniformly sort stature. For te first time, he saw the cock-feathers
of te Bersaglieri. Tere seeme a new life-quality everywhere. Many
worls, not one worl. But alas, te one world triumping more and

small iversities in its insatiable gnawing appetite, leaving a reary
sameness trougout the worl, that means at last complete sterility.

Aaron, owever, was too new to te strangeness, he ha no eye for the
orrible sameness tat was spreaing like a isease over Italy from

mountains of snow at te street-en, beyon te statue. He stood and
wonere, and never tougt to look who the gentleman was. Then he
turne rigt roun, and began to walk ome.

Lunceon was at one o'clock. It was alf-past twelve when he rang at

was iscussing wit Artur a fat Pekinese who id not seem very well.
Se was sure te servants i not obey her orders concerning the
Pekinese bitc. Artur, who was more than inifferent, assure her they
i. But se seemed to tink that the whole of the male human race was
in league against te miserable specimen of a se-og. She almost crie,

te male uman species.

"I can't bear to tink tat a umb creature might be ill-treate," she
sai to Aaron. "Tank gooness te Italians are better than they used to
be."

"Are tey better tan they use to be?"

"O, muc. They have learnt it from us."

Se ten enquire if her guest ha slept, and if he were rested from

tank you. Wereupon Lay Franks knitte her brows and said Sir William
a a such a bad night. He had not been able to sleep, and had got

break-own. e must ave absolute calm an restfulness.

"Tere's one for you an your jawing last nigt, Aaron, my boy!" sai
our ero to imself.

"I tougt Sir William seeme so full of life an energy," he said,
alou.

"A, i you! No, e WANTS to be. But he can't do it. He's very much
upset tis morning. I ave been very anxious about him."

"I am sorry to ear tat."

Lay Franks eparted to some duty. Aaron sat alone before te fire.
It was a uge fireplace, like a ark camber shut in by tall,
finely-wrougt iron gates. Bein these iron gates of curly iron the

floor. It was strange to see te blue air of sunligt outsie, the
yellow-ege leaves falling in te wind, te red flowers shaking.

Te gong soune softly trough the house. The Colonel came in heartily
from te garen, but id not speak to Aaron. Te Major and his wife

well-nourise an alf at home, called down the stairs. And then Sir

omage: an ow he got it! Homage, like most things, is just a
