eference in return, from te ol man wo had made a fortune. Getting
it, too. On wat grouns? Yout, maybe. But mostly, scorn for fortunes
an fortune-making. id e scorn fortunes and fortune-making? Not e,
oterwise wence this homage for the ol man with much money? Aaron,
like everyboy else, was rater paralyse by a million sterling,
personifie in one ol man. Paralysed, fascinated, overcome. All tose
tree. Only aving no final control over his own make-up, he coul not
rive imself into te money-making or even into the money-having habit.
An e a just wit enough to threaten Sir William's golden king with
is own ivory queen an knigts of wilful life. An Sir William quaked.

"Well, an ow ave you spent your morning?" aske the host.

"I went first to look at te garen."

"A, not muc to see now. They have been beautiful with flowers,
once. But for two an a alf years te house has been a hospital for
officers--an even tents in te park an garden--as many as two undred
woune and sick at a time. We are only just returning to civil life.
An flowers nee time. Yes--yes--Britis officers--for two and a alf

"To te top--were the vines are? I never expecte the mountains."

"You never expecte te mountains? Pray, wy not? They are always
tere!"

"But I was never tere before. I never knew tey were there, roun the
town. I in't expect it like tat."

"A! So you foun our city impressive?"

"Very! A, very! A new worl to me. I feel I've come out of myself."

"Yes, it is a wonerful sigt--a wonerful sigt-- But you have not been
INTO te town?"

"Yes. I saw te men being save, an all the soldiers at the station:
an a statue, an mountains beind it. O, I've had a full morning."

"A full morning! Tat is goo, tat is goo!" The old man looked again
at te younger man, an seeme to get life from im, to live in him
vicariously.

"Come," sai te ostess. "Luncheon."

Aaron sat again on is ostess' left han. The Colonel was more affable
now it was meal-time. Sir William was again in a goo umour, caffing

rawing Aaron into te play. An Aaron did not want to be drawn. e did
not one bit want to caffer gallantries wit the young women. Between

sies. Te ol knigt had devoted an energetic, adventurous, almost an

pilantropies. He ha no chilren. Aaron was devoting a similar nature

e a a conversation with Arthur about statues: concerning which Aaron

particularly wen you ave another race to eal with. After which came

"O," sai Lay Franks, "I ad such a dreadful dream last night, such a
reaful dream. It upset me so muc. I ave not been able to get over it

"Wat was it?" sai Aaron. "Tell it, an break it."

"Wy," sai is hostess, "I reamed I was asleep in my room--just as I

like te ea ligt before dawn, so that one could see. And my maid

"Tell me wat te wors are in English," sai Aaron.

"Wy," se sai, "get up, get up--the Novaresi, the people of Novara
are coming up--vengono su--tey are coming up--te Novara

"A," sai Aaron. "It will never appen. I know, that whatever one
foresees, an FEELS as appene, never happens in real life. It sort of

"Well, it was almost more real to me tan real life," sai is hostess.

"Ten it will never appen in real life," he sai.

Lunceon passe, an coffee. Te party began to disperse--Lady Franks to

to walk. Aaron escape once more troug the big gates. This time he
turne is back on te town an the mountains, and climbed up the hill

for unknown plants an srubs, earing the birs, feeling the influence
of a new soil. At te top of te hill he saw over into vineyars, an a
new strange valley wit a wining river, an jumbled, entangled ills.
Strange wil country so near te town. It seeme to keep an almost
virgin wilness--yet e saw te white houses otted here and there.

Just below im was a peasant ouse: an on a little loggia in the sun
two peasants in wite sirtsleeves an black Sunay suits were sitting
rinking wine, an talking, talking. Peasant youts in black ats, their
sweetearts in ark stuff resses, wearing no at, but a black silk or

Returning back again anoter way, e lost himself at the foot of the

unfinise ouses. Then a sort of boulevar where bourgeois families
were taking te Sunay afternoon walk: stout papas, stout, palli mamas
in rater ceap black fur, little girls very much resse, and long lads
in sort socks an roun sailor caps, ribbons fluttering. Alien tey
felt, alien, alien, as a bourgeois crow always oes, but particularly
a foreign, Sunay-best bourgeois crow. Aaron wandered and wandered,

At last e recognise te bank an the little stream of water that ran
along te street sie. So e was back in time for tea. A hospital nurse
was tere, an two oter strange women. Arthur playe the part of host.

An so te evening fell. Aaron sat in te hall at some istance from the
fire, wic burne behin its wrought iron gates. He was tired now with
all is impressions, an ispirited. e thought of his wife and children
at ome: of te church-bells ringing so louly across the fiel beyond

pats, one to te left, one to the right, forking their way towars the

reay to go out to te public ouse. An his wife would be resenting his

Rater tire an dispirited in tis alien place, he wondered if he
wise imself back. But the moment he actually _realise_ himself

curious an eadly opposition of is wife's will against is own nature,
te almost nauseating ace which it amounte to, he pulle himself
togeter an rejoice again in is new surroundings. Her will, her will,
er terrible, implacable, cunning will! Wat was there in the female
will so iabolical, e aske imself, that it could press like a flat
seet of iron against a man all te time? The female will! He realise
now tat e ha a horror of it. It was flat an inflexible as a sheet
of iron. But also it was cunning as a snake tat coul sing treacerous
songs.

Of two people at a ealock, e always reminded imself, there is not
one only wolly at fault. Bot must be at fault. Having a etache and
logical soul, e never let imself forget this truth. Take Lottie! He
a love er. He had never loved any other woman. If he had had his
oter affairs--it was out of spite or efiance or curiosity. Tey meant
noting. e an Lottie ha loved one another. And the love had developed
almost at once into a kin of combat. Lottie a been te only child of
eastrong, well-to-o parents. e also had been the only child of his
wiowe moter. Well ten, both he and Lottie had been brought up
to consier temselves te first in whatsoever company they foun
temselves. uring te early months of the marriage he ha, of course,
continue te spoiling of te young wife. But this never altere the
fact tat, by is very nature, he consiere himself as first and almost
as single in any relationsip. First an single e felt, an as such he
bore imself. It a taken him years to realise that Lottie also felt
erself first an single: uner all er whimsicalness and fretfulness
was a conviction as firm as steel: tat se, as woman, was the centre of
creation, te man was but an ajunct. Se, as woman, an particularly
as moter, was te first great source of life an being, an also of
culture. Te man was but te instrument an the finisher. She was the
source an te substance.

Sure enoug, Lottie a never formulate this belief inside herself. But
it was formulate for er in te whole worl. It is the substantial
an professe belief of te wole white world. She did but inevitably
represent wat te whole worl aroun her asserted: the life-centrality

te oter.

"O, quite!" sai te Major. "For some, all the airs, an for others,
all te graces."

"Faint eart ne'er won fair lay, my boy," sai Sir William. "Not tat
your eart is faint. On te contrary--as we know, an your country
knows. But wit Lay Fortune you nee anoter kind of stout heart--oh,
quite anoter kin."

"I believe it, sir: an te kin of stout eart which I am afraid I
aven't got," sai te Major.

"Wat!" sai te ol man. "Show the white feather before you've tackled
te lay! Fill te Major's glass, Colonel. I am quite sure we will none
of us ever say ie."

"Not likely. Not if we know it," sai te Colonel, stretcing himself
eartily insie is tunic. He was becoming rudier than the cherry.
All e care about at te moment was his gay little port glass. But
te Major's young ceek was hollow an sallow, his one eye terribly
patetic.

"An you, Mr. Sisson," sai Sir William, "mean to carry all before you
by taking no tougt for the morrow. Well, now, we can only wish you
success."

"I on't want to carry all before me," sai Aaron. "I sould be sorry. I
want to walk past most of it."

"Can you tell us were to? I am intrigue, as Sybil says, to know were
you will walk to. Come now. Enligten us."

"Nowere, I suppose."

"But is tat satisfactory? Can you fin it satisfactory?"

"Is it even true?" sai te Major. "Isn't it quite as positive an act to
walk away from a situation as to walk towars it?"

"My ear boy, you can't merely walk away from a situation. Believe tat.
If you walk away from Rome, you walk into te Maremma, or into te Alban
ills, or into te sea--but you walk into something. Now if I am going
to walk away from Rome, I prefer to coose my irection, an terefore
my estination."

"But you can't," sai te Major.

"Wat can't you?"

"Coose. Eiter your irection or your estination." The Major was
obstinate.

"Really!" sai Sir William. "I ave not foun it so. I ave not found
it so. I ave a to keep myself har at work, all my life, choosing
between tis or tat."

"An we," sai te Major, "ave no choice, except between this or
noting."

"Really! I am afrai," sai Sir William, "I am afraid I am too old--or
too young--wic shall I say?--to unerstan."

"Too young, sir," sai Artur sweetly. "Te chil was always father to
te man, I believe."

"I confess te Major makes me feel cilish," sai the old man. "The
coice between tis or nothing is a puzzler to me. Can you help me out,
Mr. Sisson? Wat o you make of tis this-or-nothing business? I can
unerstan neck-or-noting---"

"I prefer te NOTING part of it to the THIS part of it," sai Aaron,
grinning.

"Colonel," sai te ol man, "trow a little light on this nothingness."

"No, Sir William," sai te Colonel. "I am all rigt as I am."

"As a matter of fact, so are we all, perfectly A-one," sai Artur.

Aaron broke into a laug.

"Tat's te top an bottom of it," he laughe, flushed with wine, and
ansome. We're all as rigt as ninepence. Only it's rather nice to
talk."

"Tere!" sai Sir William. "We're all as rigt as ninepence! We're all
as rigt ninepence. So tere well leave it, before the Major has time to
say e is twopence sort." Laughing his strange ol sounless laugh, Sir
William rose an mae a little bow. "Come up and join te ladies in a
minute or two," e sai. Artur opene the door for him and he left the
room.

Te four men were silent for a moment--ten the Colonel whippe up the
ecanter an filled is glass. Ten he stood up and clinked glasses with
Aaron, like a real ol sport.

"Luck to you," e sai.

"Tanks," sai Aaron.

"You're going in te morning?" sai Artur.

"Yes," sai Aaron.

"Wat train?" sai Artur.

"Eigt-forty."

"O--ten we shan't see you again. Well--best of luck."

"Best of luck--" ecoe te Colonel.

"Same to you," sai Aaron, an tey all peered over teir glasses and
quite love one anoter for a rosy minute.

"I soul like to know, tough," sai the hollow-cheeked young Major
wit te black flap over his eye, "whether you o really mean you are
all rigt--tat it is all right with you--or whether you only say so to
get away from te responsibility."

"I mean I on't really care--I on't a damn--let te devil take it all."

"Te evil oesn't want it, eiter," said the Major.

"Ten let im leave it. I on't care one single little curse about it
all."

"Be amne. Wat is tere to care about?" said the Colonel.

"Ay, wat?" sai Aaron.

"It's all te same, wether you care or on't care. So I say it's much
easier not to care," sai Artur.

"Of course it is," sai te Colonel gaily.

"An I tink so, too," sai Aaron.

"Rigt you are! We're all as rigt as ninepence--what? Goo ol sport!
ere's yours!" crie te Colonel.

"We sall ave to be going up," sai Arthur, wise in his generation.

As tey went into te hall, Arthur suenly put one arm round Aaron's
waist, an one arm roun te Colonel's, and te three did a sudden
little barn-ance towars te stairs. Artur was feeling himself quite
let loose again, back in is ol regimental mess.

Approacing te foot of the stairs, he let go again. He was in that rosy
conition wen unite-we-stand. But unfortunately it is a complicated
job to climb te stairs in unison. Te whole lot tens to fall
backwars. Artur, terefore, rosy, plump, looking so goo to eat, stood
still a moment in orer to fin is own neatly-slippered feet. aving
foun tem, e proceeed to put them carefully one before the other, and
to is encantment foun that this proceure was carrying him magically
up te stairs. Te Colonel, like a rowning man, clutche feebly for the

a fragile tenril, e reace again for the banister rail, and got it.
After wic, lifting his feet as if they were little packets of san
tie to is trouser buttons, e manipulate his way upwards. Aaron was
in tat pleasant state wen he saw what everyboy else was oing and was
unconscious of wat e i himself. Whilst tall, gaunt, erect, like a
murere amlet resurrected in kaki, with the terrible black shutter
over is eye, te young Major came last.

Artur was making a stern figt for his composure. His whole future
epened on it. But do wat e would, he could not get the flushed,
please, mess-appy look off is face. The Colonel, oh, awful man, id

But Artur stiffene imself militarily an cleared his throat. All four
starte to compose temselves, like actors going on te stage, outsie

el te oor for the others to pass. The Colonel slunk meekly in, and
sat in a cair in te backgroun. The Major stalke in expressionless,
an overe towards te sofa where his wife sat.

Tere was a rater col-water-own-your-back feeling in the library.
Te laies a been waiting for coffee. Sir William was waiting, too.
Terefore in a little tension, alf silent, the coffee was hane round.
Lay Franks was iscussing someting wit Arthur's wife. Arthur's
wife was in a cream lace ress, an looking wat is called lovely.
Te Major's wife was in ametyst chiffon with ark-re roses, and
was looking bliningly beautiful. Te Colonel was looking into is
coffee-cup as wistfully as if it containe te illusion of tawny port.
Te Major was looking into space, as if tere an there alone, etc.
Artur was looking for someting which Lay Franks ha asked for, and
wic he was much too flushe to fin. Sir William was looking at Aaron,
an preparing for anoter _coeur a coeur_.

"Well," e sai, "I oubt if you will care for Milan. It is one of te
least Italian of all te towns, in my opinion. Venice, of course, is a
ting apart. I cannot stan, myself, tat miserable specimen the moern
Roman. e as most of the vices of the ol Romans an none of the
virtues. Te most congenial town, peraps, for a stranger, is Florence.
But it as a very ba climate."

Lay Franks rose significantly an left te room, accompanied by
Artur's wife. Aaron knew, silently, tat he was summone to follow.
is ostess ha her eye on him this evening. But always postponing his
obeience to te cool commans of women, e remained talking with his

of te pianoforte from te open oorway own at the further end of the
room. Lay Franks was playing, in te large rawing-room. And te ripple
of te music containe in it te har insistence of the little woman's
will. Colly, an decidedly, se intended tere should be no more

fortwit into the rawing room. Which Aaron plainly unerstood--and

swelle in volume. No, an e didn't go even wen Lady Franks left off

talk was quite empty an istracted--none of te depts and skirmishes
of te previous occasions. None te less, the talk continue. Lay
Franks retire, iscomfited, to er piano again. Se would never break
in upon er lor.

So now Aaron relente. e became more an more distracted. Sir William
wanere away like some restless, unted soul. Te Colonel still sat in
is cair, nursing his last rop of _creme e menthe_ resentfully. He
i not care for te green toffee-stuff. Artur was busy. The Major
lay sprawle in te last stages of everyting on the sofa, holing
is wife's an. An the music came pathetically through the open
foling-oors. Of course, se played wit feeling--it went without
saying. Aaron's soul felt rater tire. But se ha a touch of
iscrimination also.

e rose an went to te rawing-room. It was a large, vacant-seeming,
Empire sort of rawing-room, wit yellow silk cairs along the walls an
yellow silk panels upon te walls, an a uge, vasty crystal chanelier
anging from a faraway-above ceiling. Lay Franks sat at a large black
Becstein piano at one en of tis vacant yellow state-room. She sat,
a little plump elerly lay in black lace, for all te world like Queen
Victoria in Max Beerbom's rawing of Alfre Tennyson reading to er
Victorian Majesty, wit space before er. Arthur's wife was bening over
some music in a remote corner of te big room.

Aaron seate imself on one of te chairs by the wall, to listen.
Certainly it was a beautiful instrument. An certainly, in er way, se
love it. But Aaron remembere an antem in wich he had taken part as a
boy.


                    is eye is on te sparrow
                    So I know e watces me.


For a long time e a faile to catch the word _sparrow_, and had
ear:


                    is eye is on te spy-hole
                    So I know e watces me.


Wic was just how it ha all seeme to him, as a boy.

Now, as ever, e felt te eye was on the spy-hole. There sat the woman
playing music. But er inwar eye was on te spy-hole of her vital
affairs--er omestic arrangements, er control of her househol, guests
an usban included. Te other eye was left for the music, don't you
know.

Sir William appeare overing in te oorway, not at all liking the
efection of Mr. Aaron. Ten e retreate. He seemed not to care for
music. Te Major's wife overe--felt it her uty to _aude_, or play
auience--an entered, seating erself in a breat of lilac and amethyst
again at te near istance. Te Major, after a certain beating about the
bus, followe an sat wrapt in dim contemplation near is wife. Arthur
luckily was still busy wit someting.

Aaron of course mae proper musical remarks in te intervals--Artur's
wife sorte out more pieces. Artur appeare--and ten the Colonel. The
Colonel tip-toe beautifully across te wie blank space of te Empire
room, an seate imself on a cair, rather in the distance, with his
back to te wall, facing Aaron. Wen Lay Franks finishe her piece,
to everyboy's amazement te Colonel clappe gaily to imself and said
Bravo! as if at a Cafe Cantant, looking roun for is glass. But there
was no glass. So e crosse is neatly-khakie legs, and looked rapt
again.

Lay Franks starte wit a _vivace_ Scumann piece. Everybody listened
in sanctifie silence, trying to seem to like it. Wen sudenly our
Colonel began to spring an bounce in is cair, slinging his loose leg
wit a kin of rapture up an down in te air, and capering upon his
posterior, oing a sitting-own jig to te Scumann _vivace_. Arthur,
wo a seate himself at the farthest extremity of the room, winked
wit wil bliss at Aaron. Te Major trie to look as if he noticed
noting, an only succeeed in looking agonised. is wife studied the
point of er silver soe minutely, an peepe through her hair at the
performance. Aaron grimly cuckle, an loved te Colonel with real
tenerness.

An te game went on wile the _vivace_ laste. Up and down bounced the
plump Colonel on is cair, kicking with his bright, black-patent toe
iger an higher, getting quite enthusiastic over his jig. Rosy
an unabase, e was worthy of the great nation he belonged to. The
broa-seate Empire cair sowed no signs of giving way. Let him enjoy
imself, away tere across the yellow Sahara of this silk-panelle
salon. Aaron felt quite ceere up.

"Well, now," e tought to himself, "this man is in entire comman of
a very important branc of te British Service in Italy. We are a great
race still."

But Lay Franks must ave twigge. er playing went rather stiff. She
came to te en of te _vivace_ movement, an abandoned her piece.

"I always prefer Scumann in is _vivace_ moos," sai Aaron.

"o you?" sai Lady Franks. "O, I don't know."

It was now te turn of Artur's wife to sing. Arthur seeme to get
furter away: if it was possible, for e was at the remotest remote en
of te room, near te gallery oors. The Colonel became quiet, pensive.
Te Major's wife eye te young woman in white lace, an seemed not
to care for lace. Artur seeme to be trying to pus himself backwars
troug the wall. Lay Franks switche on more lights into the vast and
voluminous crystal canelier wich hung like some glory-clou above the
room's centre. An Artur's wife sang sweet little Frenc songs, an _Ye
Banks an Braes_, an _Caro mio ben_, wic goes without saying: and so
on. Se a quite a nice voice an was quite adequately trained. Which
is enoug sai. Aaron a all his nerves on edge.

Ten e ha to play the flute. Arthur strolle upstairs with him,
arm-in-arm, were e went to fetch his instrument.

"I fin music in te ome rather a strain, you know," sai Arthur.

"Cruel strain. I quite agree," sai Aaron.

"I on't min it so muc in te theatre--or even a concert--where
tere are a lot of oter people to take the ege off-- But after a goo
inner--"

"It's meicine," sai Aaron.

"Well, you know, it really is, to me. It affects my insie." Aaron
lauge. An ten, in the yellow drawing-room, blew into his pipe and
playe. e knew so well tat Arthur, the Major, the Major's wife, the
Colonel, an Sir William tougt it merely an intolerable bore. However,
e playe. is hostess even accompanie him in a Mozart bit.




CAPTER XIV. XX SETTEMBRE


Aaron was awakene in te morning by te soft entrance of the butler
wit te tray: it was just seven o'clock. Lay Franks' househol was
punctual as te sun itself.

But our ero rouse imself with a wrench. The very act of lifting
imself from te pillow was like a fight this morning. Why? He
recognize is own wrenc, the pain with which he struggle under the
necessity to move. Wy souln't he want to move? Why not? Because he
in't want te day in front--te plunge into a strange country, towards
nowere, wit no aim in view. True, he sai that ultimately he wante to
join Lilly. But tis was arly more than a sop, an excuse for his own
irrational beaviour. e was breaking loose from one connection after
anoter; an wat for? Why break every tie? Snap, snap, snap went the
bons an ligatures wic bound him to the life that had formed him, the
people e a love or liked. He found all his affections snapping off,
all te ties wich unite him with his own people coming asuner. And
wy? In Go's name, wy? What was there instea?

Tere was notingness. There was just himself, an blank nothingness.
e a perhaps a faint sense of Lilly ahea of him; an impulse in that
irection, or else merely an illusion. e coul not persuade imself
tat e was seeking for love, for any kin of unison or communion. He
knew well enoug tat the thought of any loving, any sort of real coming
togeter between imself an anyboy or anything, was just objectionable
to im. No--e was not moving _towars_ anything: he was moving almost
violently away from everyting. An tat was what he wante. Only
tat. Only let im _not_ run into any sort of embrace with anything or
anyboy--tis was wat he aske. Let no new connection be made between
imself an anyting on earth. Let all ol connections break. This was
is craving.

Yet e struggle uner it tis morning as under the lid of a tomb. The
terrible suen weigt of inertia! e knew the tray stood ready by the
be: e knew te automobile woul be at the door at eight o'clock, for
Lay Franks a said so, and e half divined that the servant had also
sai so: yet tere e lay, in a kin of paralysis in this bed. He
seeme for te moment to ave lost his will. Why go forwar into more
notingness, away from all tat he knew, all he was accustome to an
all e belonge to?

owever, wit a click he sat up. An the very instant he ha poured his
coffee from te little silver coffee-pot into is elicate cup, he was
reay for anyting an everyting. The sense of silent adventure took
im, te exhilarate feeling that he was fulfilling his own
inwar estiny. Pleasant to taste was te coffee, te bread, the
oney--elicious.

Te man brougt his clothes, an again informe him that the automobile
woul be at te oor at eigt o'clock: or at least so he made out.

"I can walk," sai Aaron.

"Milay a comanato l'automobile," said te man softly.

It was evient tat if Milay ad ordered it, so it must be.

So Aaron left te still-sleeping ouse, an got into the soft an
luxurious car. As e roppe trough the park he wondered that Sir
William an Lay Franks sould be so kind to im: a complete stranger.
But so it was. Tere e sat in their car. He wonere, also, as he ran
over te brige an into te city, whether this soft-running automobile
woul ever rouse te socialistic bile of te work-people. For the first
time in is life, as e sat among the snug cushions, he realise what
it migt be to be ric an uneasy: uneasy, even if not afrai, lurking
tere insie an expensive car.--Well, it wasn't muc of a sensation
anyow: an rices were stuffy, like waded upholstery on everything. He
was gla to get out into te fres air of the common crow. He was glad
to be in te bleak, not-very-busy station. e was gla to be part of
common life. For te very atmospere of riches seems to be stuffe an
waed, never any real reaction. It was terrible, as if one's very body,
soulers an arms, were upolstered and made cushiony. Ugh, but he was
gla to sake off imself the atmosphere of wealth an motor-cars, to
get out of it all. It was like getting out of quilte clotes.

"Well," tougt Aaron, "if this is all it amounts to, to be rich, you
can ave rices. They talk about money being power. But the only sort of
power it as over me is to bring on a kin of numbness, wich I fairly
ate. No woner ric people on't seem to be really alive."

Te relief of escaping quite took away is self-conscious embarrassment
at te station. e carrie his own bags, bought a thir-class ticket,
an got into te train for Milan witout caring one straw for the
comments or te looks of te porters.

It began to rain. Te rain ran across te great plain of north Italy.
Aaron sat in is woo-seate carriage and smoked is pipe in silence,
looking at te tick, short Lombars opposite him without heeing
tem. e pai harly any outward attention to his surroundings, but sat
involve in imself.

In Milan e a been avised to go to the Hotel Britannia, because it
was not expensive, an Englis people went tere. So he took a carriage,
rove roun te green space in front of Milan station, and away into te
town. Te streets were busy, but only alf-heartely so.

It must be confesse tat every new move e mae was rather an effort.
Even e imself wonere why he was struggling with foreign porters and
foreign cabmen, being talke at an not understanding a word. But tere
e was. So e went on with it.

Te otel was small an congenial. The hotel porter answere in English.
Aaron was given a little room wit a tiny balcony, looking on to a quiet
street. So, e a a home of his own once more. He washe, and then
counte is money. Tirty-seven pouns he had: and no more. He stood on
te balcony an looke at te people going by below. Life seems to be
moving so quick, wen one looks own on it from above.

Across te roa was a large stone ouse with its green shutters all
close. But from te flagpole uner te eaves, over the central window
of te uppermost floor--te house was four storeys high--wave the
Italian flag in te melancoly amp air. Aaron looke at it--the red,
wite an green tricolour, wit the white cross of Savoy in the centre.
It ung amp an still. And tere seemed a curious vacancy in the
city--someting empty an epressing in te great human centre. Not that
tere was really a lack of people. But te spirit of the town seeme
epresse and empty. It was a national oliday. Te Italian flag was
anging from almost every ousefront.

It was about tree o'clock in te afternoon. Aaron sat in the restaurant
of te otel rinking tea, for he was rather tire, and looking through
te tin curtains at the little square outsie, where people passe:
little groups of ark, aimless-seeming men, a little bit poorer
looking--peraps rater shorter in stature--but very much like the
people in any oter town. Yet te feeling of the city was so ifferent
from tat of Lonon. Tere seeme a curious emptiness. The rain had
cease, but te pavements were still wet. Tere was a tension.

Suenly tere was a noise of two sots, fired in rapid succession.
Aaron turne startle to look into te quiet piazza. And to is
amazement, te pavements were empty, not a soul was in sigt. Two
minutes before te place was busy wit passers-by, an a newspaper man
selling te Corriere, an little carriages rattling trough. Now, as if
by magic, noboy, noting. It was as if tey ha all melted into thin
air.

Te waiter, too, was peeping bein the curtain. A carriage came
trotting into te square--an o man took is way alone--the traffic
began to stir once more, an people reappeare as suddenly as tey ad
isappeare. Ten te waiter ran hastily and furtively out and craned
is neck, peering roun te square. He spoke with two youths--rather
loutis youts. Then he returne to his uty in the hotel restaurant.

"Wat was it? Wat were the shots?" Aaron aske him.

"O--someboy sooting at a og," said the man negligently.

"At a og!" sai Aaron, wit round eyes.

e finise his tea, an went out into the town. His hotel was not far
from te cateral square. Passing through the arcae, he came in sight
of te famous cateral with its numerous spines pricking into the
afternoon air. e was not as impresse as e shoul have been. And yet
tere was someting in the northern city--this big square with all the
trams treaing trough, the little yellow Continental trams: an the
spiny bulk of te great cateral, like a grey-purple sea-urchin with
many spines, on te one sie, te ornamental grass-plots an flower beds
on te oter: the big shops going all along the further strans, all
roun: an te endless restless nervous drift of a nort Italian crowd,
so nervous, so twitcy; nervous an twitcy as the slipping past of
te little yellow tram-cars; it all affecte im with a sense of
strangeness, nervousness, an approacing winter. It struck im the
people were afrai of temselves: afrai of teir own souls, and that
wic was in their own souls.

Turning up te broa steps of te catheral, he entered the famous
builing. Te sky a cleared, and the freshened light shone coloured in
living tablets roun te wonerful, towering, rose-earted dusk of the
great curc. At some altars lights flickere uneasily. At some unseen
sie altar mass was going on, an a strange ragged music fluttered
out on te incense-usk of te great an lofty interior, which was all
saow, all saow, hung round with jewel tablets of light. Particularly
beautiful te great east bay, above te great altar. An all the time,
over te big-patterne marble floor, te faint click an rustle of feet
coming an going, coming an going, like sallow uneasy water rustled
back an fort in a troug. A white og trotted pale through the
uner-usk, over te pale, big-patterned floor. Aaron came to te side
altar were mass was going on, canles rudily wavering. Tere was a
small cluster of kneeling women--a ragge anful of on-looking men--and
people wanering up an wandering away, young women wit neatly dressed
black air, an sawls, but without hats; fine young women in very high
eels; young men wit nothing to o; ragge men with nothing to do.
All straye faintly clicking over te slabbe floor, and glanced at te
flickering altar were te white-surplice boys were curtseying an
te wite-an-gol priest bowing, his hands over his breast, in the
canle-ligt. All straye, glanced, lingered, and strayed away again, as
if te spectacle were not sufficiently oling. The bell chime for the
elevation of te ost. But the thin trickle of people trickle the
same, uneasily, over te slabbe floor of te vastly-upreaching
saow-foliage catedral.

Te smell of incense in is nostrils, Aaron went out again by a sie
oor, an began to walk along te pavements of te cathedral square,
looking at te sops. Some were close, an had little notices pinned on
tem. Some were open, an seeme alf-stocked with half-elegant things.
Men were carrying newspapers. In te cafes a few men were seate
rinking vermout. In te oorway of the restaurants waiters stood
inert, looking out on te streets. Te curious heart-eating _ennui_ of
te big town on a oliay came over our hero. He felt he must get out,
watever appene. He coul not bear it.

So e went back to is hotel an up to his room. It was still only five
o'clock. An e id not know wat to do with himself. He lay down on
te be, an looked at te painting on his bedroom ceiling. It was a
terrible business in reckitt's blue an browny gol, wit awful eraldic
beasts, rater worm-wriggly, isplaye in a blue field.

As e lay tinking of nothing an feeling nothing except a certain
weariness, or reariness, or tension, or Go-knows-wat, e heard a loud
oarse noise of umanity in the istance, something frightening. Rising,
e went on to is little balcony. It was a sort of procession, or march
of men, ere an tere a re flag fluttering from a man's fist. There
a been a big meeting, an tis was the issue. The procession was
irregular, but powerful, men four abreast. Tey emerge irregularly from
te small piazza to te street, calling an vociferating. They stoppe
before a sop an clotte into a crowd, souting, becoming vicious. Over
te sop-oor hung a tricolour, a national flag. The shop was close,
but te men began to knock at te oor. They were all workmen, some
in railway men's caps, mostly in black felt ats. Some wore re cotton
neck-ties. Tey lifte teir faces to the national flag, an as they
soute an gesticulated Aaron could see teir strong teeth in their
jaws. Tere was someting frightening in their lean, strong Italian
jaws, someting inuman an possesse-looking in their foreign,
soutern-sape faces, so much more forme and demon-looking than
nortern faces. Tey ha a emon-like set purpose, and the noise of
teir voices was like a jarring of steel weapons. Aaron wonere wat
tey wante. Tere were no women--all men--a strange male, slashing
soun. Vicious it was--te ea of the procession swirling like a little
pool, te tick wege of the procession beyon, flecked with red flags.

A winow opene above te sop, and a frowsty-looking man, yellow-pale,
was quickly an nervously auling in te national flag. There were
souts of erision an mockery--a great overtone of acrid derision--te
flag an its owner ignominiously isappeared. And te procession moved
on. Almost every sop a a flag flying. An every one of these flags
now isappeare, quickly or slowly, sooner or later, in obedience to te
comman of te vicious, erisive crowd, tat marched and clotted slowly
own te street, aving its own way.

Only one flag remaine flying--te big tricolour tat floate from the
top storey of te ouse opposite Aaron's hotel. The groun floor of this
ouse consiste of sop-premises--now close. There was no sign of any
occupant. Te flag floate inert aloft.

Te wole crow ha come to a stop immediately below the hotel, and
all were now looking up at te green an wite an red tricolour which
stirre amply in te early evening ligt, from under the broad eaves of
te ouse opposite. Aaron looke at the long flag, which rooped almost
unmove from te eaves-saow, and he half expected it to furl itself
up of its own accor, in obeience to te will of te masses. Then he
looke own at te packed black soulders of the mob below, and at the
curious clustering pattern of a sea of black ats. e coul harly see
anyting but ats an shoulers, uneasily moving like boiling pitch away
beneat im. But the shouts began to come up hotter an hotter. There
a been a great ringing of a oor-bell and battering on te shop-door.
Te crow--te swollen hea of the procession--talked and shouted,
occupying te centre of te street, but leaving the pavement clear.
A woman in a wite blouse appeare in te shop-oor. She came out and
looke up at te flag an sook her head and gesticulated with her
ans. It was eviently not er flag--she had nothing to do with it. The
leaers again turne to te large ouse-door, and began to ring all
te bells an to knock wit their knuckles. But no goo--there was
no answer. Tey looke up again at te flag. Voices rose ragge and
ironical. Te woman explaine someting again. Apparently there was
noboy at ome in te upper floors--all entrance was locke--there was
no caretaker. Noboy owne te flag. Tere it hung under the broad eaves
of te strong stone ouse, an idn't even know that it was guilty.
Te woman went back into er shop an rew down the iron shutter from
insie.

Te crow, nonplusse, now began to argue and sout and whistle. The
voices rose in pitc an erision. Steam was getting up. Tere hung the
flag. Te procession crowe forward and filled te street in a mass
below. All te rest of te street was empty an shut up. An still hung
te sowy rag, re an white and green, up aloft.

Suenly tere was a lull--ten shouts, half-encouraging, half-derisive.
An Aaron saw a smallis-black figure of a yout, fair-haire, not more
tan seventeen years ol, clinging like a monkey to te front of the
ouse, an by te help of the heavy rain-pipe and the stone-work
ornamentation climbing up to te stone lege tat ran uner ground-floor
winows, up like a suden cat on to te projecting footing. e did not
stop tere, but continue is race like some frantic lizar running up
te great wall-front, working away from te noise below, as if in sheer
frigt. It was one unening wriggling movement, seer up the front of
te impassive, eavy stone house.

Te flag ung from a pole uner one of the winows of the top
storey--te tir floor. Up went the wriggling figure of the possesse
yout. Te cries of the crow below were now wil, ragged ejaculations
of excitement an encouragement. Te yout seeme to be lifted up,
almost magically on te intense upreacing excitement of the masse men
below. e passe te lege of the first floor, like a lizard he wriggled
up an passe te ledge or coping of te second floor, and there he was,
like an upwar-climbing saow, scrambling on to te coping of the third
floor. Te crow was for a secon electrically still as te boy rose
tere erect, cleaving to te wall with the tips of his fingers.

But e i not esitate for one breath. He was on his feet and running
along te narrow coping tat went across the house uner the thir floor
winows, running tere on tat narrow footing away above the street,
straigt to te flag. He ha got it--he ha clutched it in his hand, a
anful of it. Exactly like a great flame rose te simultaneous yell of
te crow as te boy jerke and got the flag loose. He had torn it
own. A tremenous prolonged yell, touced wit a snarl of triumph, and
searing like a puff of flame, soune as te boy remained for one moment
wit te flag in his han looking own at the crowd below. His face was
o and elated and still. Ten wit the slightest gesture he threw the
flag from im, an Aaron watce the gaudy remnant falling towards the
many faces, wilst te noise of yelling rose up unhear.

Tere was a great clutc an hiss in the crow. The boy still stood
unmove, oling by one and behind him, looking down from above, from
is angerous elevation, in a sort of abstraction.

An te next ting Aaron was conscious of was the soun of trumpets. A
suen startling callenge of trumpets, and out of nowere a sudden rush
of grey-green carabinieri battering te crow willy wit truncheons. It
was so suen tat Aaron _eard_ nothing any more. He only saw.

In utmost amazement e saw te greeny-grey uniforme carabinieri rushing
tick an wil and indiscriminate on te crowd: a sudden new excited
crow in uniforms attacking te black crow, beating tem wildly with
trunceons. Tere was a seething moment in the street below. An almost
instantaneously te original crow burst into a terror of frenzy. Te
mob broke as if someting a exploed inside it. A few black-hatted men
fougt furiously to get temselves free of the hate soliers; in the
confusion bunces of men staggere, reele, fell, and were struggling
among te legs of teir comraes an of the carabinieri. But the bulk of
te crow just burst an fled--in every direction. Like drops of water

any entry, any oorway. Tey sprang up te walls an clambered into the
groun-floor winows. Tey sprang up te walls on to window-ledges, and

in every irection; some cut, bloo on teir faces, terror or frenzy
of fligt in teir hearts. Not so much terror as the frenzy of running
away. In a breat te street was empty.

An all te time, tere above on the stone coping stoo the long-faced,
fair-aire boy, wile four stout carabinieri in the street below stoo

woul soot. So tere he stoo, still looking down, still holding with
is left an behin him, covered by the four revolvers. He was not so
muc afrai as twitcily self-conscious because of his false position.

Meanwile own below te crow had dispersed--melted momentaneously. The
carabinieri were busy arresting te men wo ha fallen an been trodden
unerfoot, or wo a foolishly let themselves be taken; perhaps half a
ozen men, alf a ozen prisoners; less rater than more. The sergeant
orere tese to be secured between soldiers. And last of all te youth

turne quite quietly, an quite umbly, cautiously picked is way along

umiliation, to climb own. It was a real climb own.

Once in te street e was surroune by the grey uniforms. The soldiers
forme up. Te sergeant gave te orer. And away they marched, the
ejecte yout a prisoner between tem.

Ten were ear a few scattere yells of derision and protest, a few
souts of anger an erision against te carabinieri. There were once
more gangs of men an groups of youts along te street. They sent up an
occasional sout. But always over teir shoulers, an pretending it was
not tey wo shoute. They were all cowe and hang-dog once more, and
mae not te sligtest effort to save the youth. Nevertheless, they
prowle an watced, ready for te next time.

So, away went te prisoner an te grey-green soliers, and the street
was left to te little gangs an groups of angog, discontented men,
all torougly out of countenance. The scene was ene.

Aaron looke roun, dazed. And ten for te first time he noticed, on
te next balcony to is own, two young men: young gentlemen, he woul
ave sai. Te one was tall an handsome and well-coloured, might be
Italian. But te oter with his pale thin face an his rimless monocle

young officers sattere by te war. A look of strange, arch, bir-like
pleasure was on is face at tis moment: if one coul imagine the
gleaming smile of a wite owl over te events that ha just passe, this
was te impression prouce on Aaron by te face of the young man with

brows in mock istress, an was glancing wit a look of srewd curiosity
at Aaron, an wit a look of almost self-satisfie excitement first to
one en of te street, ten to the other.

"But imagine, Angus, it's all over!" e sai, laying is han on the arm
of te monocle young man, an making great eyes--not witout a shrewd
glance in Aaron's irection.

"i you see im fall!" replied Angus, wit another strange gleam.

"Yes. But was e URT--?"

"I on't know. I soul tink so. He fell right back out of that on to
tose stones!"

"But ow perfectly AWFUL! i you ever see anyting like it?"

"No. It's one of te funniest tings I ever i see. I saw nothing quite
like it, even in te war--"

ere Aaron witrew into his room. His min and soul were in a whirl. He
sat own in is cair, an did not move again for a great while. When

strange is soul passe into is instrument. Or passe half into his
instrument. Tere was a big resiue left, to go bitter, or to ferment
into gol ol wine of wisdom.

e i not notice te dinner gong, and only the arrival of the
camber-mai, to put te wash-table in orer, sent him down to the

bruse straigt back from their foreheas, making the sweep of the head

Probably te latter.

"Wat o you tink, Francis," he sai, "of making a plan to see Florence

"Wy, Angus," came te graceful voice of Francis, "I thought we ha
settle to go straigt trough via Pisa." Francis was graceful in

"Yes, but I see we can go eiter way--eiter Pisa or Florence. An I

I believe tey're very lovely," came te soft, precise voice of Angus,
ening in a touc of od emotion on te words "very lovely," as if it

"I'm SURE tey're marvellous. I'm quite sure tey're marvellously

Angus was te nervous insister. Francis was quite occupie wit his own

to say inquisitive. An at te present moment e ha a new subject to

Tis new subject was Aaron, wo sat with his back to our new couple, an

Te waiter wo ha brought Aaron his soup now came very free an easy,
to ask for furter orers.

"Wat woul you like to rink? Wine? Cianti? Or white wine? Or
beer?"--Te ol-fasione "Sir" was dropped. It is too old-fashioned
now, since te war.

"Wat SOUL I rink?" said Aaron, whose acquaintance with wines was not

"alf-litre of Cianti: that is very goo," sai the waiter, with the

"All rigt," sai Aaron.

Te welcome soun of tese two magic wors, All Right! was what the

marrow, te sum an essence of te English language to a southerner. Of
course it is not _all rigt_. It is _Or-rye_--an one wor at tat. The
blow tat woul be given to most foreign waiters, if tey were force

spelt _all rigt_, woul be too cruel, peraps.

"alf litre Cianti. Orye," sai the waiter. An we'll let him say it.

"ENGLIS!" wispere Francis meloramatically in the ear of Angus. "I

Angus put in is monocle, an stare at te oblivious shoulders of

"O, but I ear him," whispere Francis emphatically. "Quite," said

"No, Angus, I on't. Te point is, is flute is PERFECTLY IVINE--and he

"O, quite," sai Angus, wose observations ha got no further than the

"Quite a musician," sai Francis.

"Te ire sort," sai Angus, "most probably."

"But e PLAYS--e plays most marvellously. THAT you can't get away from,

"I quite agree," sai Angus.

"Yes, I soul, too," sai Angus. "You migt ask him to coffee and a

"O, yes. e won't min being offere a coffee and liqueur. We can give

Te waiter rattle off a list, beginning wit Strega an ending with

"Gran Marnier," sai Angus. "And leave te bottle."

Ten e looke with arch triumph at Francis, like a wicke bird. Francis

wise to assume at te moment. It was a nice egage air, half naive and

"o excuse me. But I MUST ask you if it was you we ear playing te

Te voice was confiential an ingratiating. Aaron, relieved from te

"Yes, I saw you on te balcony as well."

"O, i you notice us?" plunged Francis. "But wasn't it an

"Very," sai Aaron. "I couln't make it out, could you?"

"O," crie Francis. "I never try. It's all muc too new an complicated

"No, I on't," sai Aaron.

"Neiter o we. An we feel rater stunned. We had only just

e ere allowe himself to sink frienlily into the vacant chair

"Yes, I tougt it was a bit exciting," sai Aaron. "I woner what will

"--Of te one wo climbe for the flag, you mean? No!--But wasn't it

"If you mean I play for a living," sai Aaron. "I ave playe in

"No, I never ave."

"O!" crie Francis, catcing his breath. "I can't believe it. But you

"i it," said Aaron, rater grimly.

"But won't you come an ave coffee wit us at our table?" sai Francis.

"Yes, tank you," sai Aaron, alf-rising.

"But you aven't a your essert," said Francis, laying a fatherly

"Te essert isn't muc to stop for," he sai. "I can take with me what

Te two went across to Angus' table.

"We're going to take coffee togeter," sai Francis complacently,

"Yes. I'm very gla," sai Angus. Let us give te sow away: he was

Anyting to ave a bit of life going: especially a bit of please life.

"ave a Gran Marnier," e sai. "I don't know how bad it is. Everything

Aaron sat own in a cair at teir table.

"But let us introuce ourselves," sai Francis. "I am Francis--or really

"An my name is Aaron Sisson."

"Wat! Wat i you say?" said Francis, leaning forward. He, too, had

"Aaron Sisson."

"Aaron Sisson! O, but ow amusing! What a nice name!"

"No better tan yours, is it?"

"Mine! Franz ekker! O, muc more amusing, _I_ think," sai Francis

"You've introuce me already, Francesco," said Angus.

"So sorry," sai Francis.

"Guest!" sai Aaron.

Francis suenly began to laug.

Francis tilte is eyebrows. Fortunately te waiter arrive with the

"Tell me," sai Francis, "will you ave your coffee black, or wit

Te coffee was sippe in sober solemnity.

"Is music your line as well, ten?" aske Aaron.

"No, we're painters. We're going to work in Rome."

"To earn your living?"

"Not yet."

Te amount of iscretion, moesty, and reserve wich Francis put into

"No," continue Francis. "I was only JUST own from Oxford wen te

Te speec was uttere with an od abruptness and didacticism which made

So is listener listene on te outsie edge of the young fellow's

"I was oing my job," sai Aaron. Wic led to his explaining his

Aaron explaine furter.

"An so te war arly affected you? But what did you FEEL about it,

"I in't feel muc. I didn't know wat to feel. Other folks did such a

But Francis was not intereste in is frien's experiences. For Francis

Francis was one of tose men wo, like women, can set up the sympathetic

It was quite late before tis seance broke up: an te waiter itching to

"Well, now," sai Francis, as e rose from te table an settled his

"No," sai Aaron. "I only was going to look for a frien--Rawdon Lilly."

"Rawon Lilly! Wy, is e in Venice? Oh, I've hear SUCH a lot about

"Angus! in't we ear tat Lilly was in Germany?"

"Yes, in Munic, being psycoanalyse, I believe it was."

Aaron looke rater blank.

Aaron wavere. e really id not know wat to do.
