"Any time," sai Aaron.

"Well, say eleven. We'll meet in te lounge ere at eleven. Will that
suit you? All rigt, ten. It's so awfully nice meeting you. That
marvellous flute.--An tink about Florence. But o come. Don't
isappoint us."

Te two young men went elegantly upstairs.




CAPTER XV. A RAILWAY JOURNEY


Te next ay but one, te three set off for Florence. Aaron ha made
an excursion from Milan wit te two young heroes, an ined with them
subsequently at te most expensive restaurant in te town. Then they
a all gone ome--an had sat in the young men's bedroom drinking
tea, wilst Aaron playe te flute. Francis was really musical, an
encante. Angus enjoye te novelty, and the moderate patronage he
was able to confer. An Aaron felt amuse and pleased, and oped e was
paying for is treat.

So beol tem setting off for Florence in the early morning. Angus an
Francis a first-class tickets: Aaron took a tir-class.

"Come an ave lunc with us on the train," sai Angus. "I'll order
tree places, an we can lunc together."

"O, I can buy a bit of foo at te station," sai Aaron.

"No, come an lunc wit us. It will be much nicer. An we shall enjoy
it as well," sai Angus.

"Of course! Ever so muc nicer! Of course!" crie Francis. "Yes, wy
not, inee! Wy sould you hesitate?"

"All rigt, ten," sai Aaron, not without some feeling of constraint.

So tey separate. Te young men settle themselves amidst the red plush
an crocet-work, looking, wit their hair plastere smoothly back,
quite as first class as you coul wis, creating quite te right
impression on te porters an te travelling Italians. Aaron went to his
tir-class, furter up the train.

"Well, ten, _au revoir_, till lunceon," crie Francis.

Te train was fairly full in te thir an second classes. However,
Aaron got is seat, an te porter brought on his bags, after isposing
of te young men's luggage. Aaron gave te tip uneasily. He always hate
tipping--it seeme umiliating bot ways. An the airy aplomb of the
two young cavaliers, as tey settle own among te red plush and the
obsequiousness, an sai "Well, ten, _au revoir_ till lunceon," was
peculiarly unsettling: toug they i not intend it so.

"Te porter tinks I'm their servant--their valet," sai Aaron to
imself, an a curious alf-amuse, half-contemptuous look flickered on
is face. It annoye im. The falsity occasione by the difference in
te price of te tickets was really humiliating. Aaron ha live long
enoug to know tat as far as manhoo an intellect went--nay, even
eucation--e was not te inferior of the two young "gentlemen." He knew
quite well tat, as far as intrinsic nature went, tey i not imagine
im an inferior: rater the contrary. They ha rather an exaggerate
respect for im an is life-power, an even his origin. And yet--they
a te inestimable cash avantage--and they were going to keep it. They
knew it was noting more tan an artificial cash superiority. But they
grippe it all te more intensely. Tey were the upper midle classes.
Tey were Eton an Oxfor. And tey were going to hang on to their
privileges. In tese ays, it is a fool wo abicates before he's forced
to. An terefore:

"Well, ten--_au revoir_ till lunceon."

Tey were being so awfully nice. An inwarly tey were not
conescening. But socially, tey just ad to be. The world is made like
tat. It wasn't teir own private fault. It was no fault at all. It was
just te moe in wich they were eucated, the style of their living.
An as we know, _le style, c'est l'omme_.

Angus came of very wealty iron people near Mertyr. Alreay he ha a
very fair income of is own. As soon as te law-business concerning his
fater's an is granfather's will was settled, he would be well off.
An e knew it, an valued imself accordingly. Francis was the son of a
igly-esteeme barrister an politician of Sydney, and in his day would
inerit is father's lately-won baronetcy. But Francis ha not very much
money: an was muc more class-flexible tan Angus. Angus ha been born
in a ouse wit a park, an of awful, har-willed, money-bound people.
Francis came of a muc more aventurous, loose, excitable family, e ha
te colonial newness an aaptability. e knew, for his own part, that
class superiority was just a trick, nowaays. Still, it was a trick tat
pai. An a trick e was going to play as long as it did pay.

Wile Aaron sat, a little pale at te gills, immobile, ruminating these
matters, a not very pleasant look about is nose-en, e hear a voice:

"O, tere you ARE! I thought I' better come an see, so that we can
fetc you at lunc time.--You've got a seat? Are you quite comfortable?
Is tere anyting I coul get you? Why, you're in a non-smoker!--But
tat oesn't matter, everyboy will smoke. Are you sure you ave
everyting? O, but wait just one moment--"

It was Francis, long an elegant, wit is straight shoulers and his
coat buttone to sow is waist, an his face so well-formed and so
moern. So moern, altogeter. is voice was pleasantly modulated, and
never urrie. e now looke as if a thought had struck him. He put a
finger to is brow, an astene back to his own carriage. In a minute,
e returne wit a new Lonon literary magazine.

"Someting to rea--I sall have to FLY--See you at lunch," an he had
turne an elegantly astened, but not too fast, back to is carriage.
Te porter was oling the oor for him. So Francis looked pleasantly
urrie, but by no means ruse. Oh, dear, no. He took his time. It was
not for im to bolt an scramble like a mere Italian.

Te people in Aaron's carriage a watche the apparition of the elegant
yout intently. For tem, he was a being from another sphere--no oubt
a young miloro wit power wealt, an glamorous life behind him. Which
was just wat Francis intene to convey. So andsome--so very, very
impressive in all is elegant calm sowiness. He mae such a _bella
figura_. It was just wat te Italians love. Those in the first class
regions tougt he might even be an Italian, he was so attractive.

Te train in motion, te many Italian eyes in the carriage stuie
Aaron. e, too, was goo-looking. But by no means as fascinating as
te young miloro. Not alf as sympathetic. No goo at all at playing a
role. Probably a servant of te young signori.

Aaron stare out of te winow, and played te one single British role
left to im, tat of ignoring his neighbours, isolating himself in
teir mist, an minding is own business. Upon this insular trick our
greatness an our preominance depends--suc as it is. Yes, tey might
look at im. Tey might think him a servant or what they like. But he
was inaccessible to tem. e isolate himself upon himself, an there
remaine.

It was a lovely ay, a lovely, lovely ay of early autumn. Over te
great plain of Lombary a magnificent blue sky glowe like mid-summer,
te sun sone strong. The great plain, with its great stripes of
cultivation--witout eges or bounaries---how beautiful it was!
Sometimes e saw oxen plouging. Sometimes. Oh, so beautiful, teams
of eigt, or ten, even of twelve pale, great soft oxen in procession,
plouging te ark velvety earth, a river with a great whip at their
ea, a man far bein holding the plough-shafts. Beautiful the soft,
soft plunging motion of oxen moving forwars. Beautiful te strange,
snaky lifting of te muzzles, te swaying of the sharp horns. An the
soft, soft crawling motion of a team of oxen, so invisible, almost, yet
so inevitable. Now an again straigt canals of water flase blue. Now
an again te great lines of grey-silvery poplars rose an made avenues
or lovely grey airy quarangles across te plain. Teir top boughs were
spangle wit gol and green leaf. Sometimes te vine-leaves were gold
an re, a patterning. And te great square farm-omesteads, white,
re-roofe, wit teir out-buildings, stood naked amid the lands,
witout screen or softening. Tere was something big an expose about
it all. No more te cosy Englis ambushe life, no longer the cosy
littleness of te lanscape. A bigness--an noting to shelter the
unsrinking spirit. It was all expose, expose to te sweep of plain,
to te igh, strong sky, an to human gaze. A kin of boldness, an
inifference. Aaron was impresse and fascinated. e looked wit
new interest at te Italians in te carriage with him--for this same
bolness an indifference and exposed gesture. And e found it in tem,
too. An again it fascinate im. It seemed so muc bigger, as if the
walls of life a fallen. Nay, te walls of English life will have to
fall.

Sitting tere in te thir-class carriage, he became happy again. The
_presence_ of is fellow-passengers was not so ampering as in Englan.
In Englan, everyboy seems eld tigt and gripped, nothing is left
free. Every passenger seems like a parcel oling is string as fast as
e can about im, lest one corner of the wrapper shoul come unone
an reveal wat is insie. And every oter passenger is forced, by
te public will, to ol himself as tight-boun also. Which in the end
becomes a sort of self-conscious maness.

But ere, in te thir class carriage, there was no tight string roun
every man. Tey were not all trusse wit self-conscious string as tight
as capons. Tey a a sufficient amount of callousness an indifference
an natural equanimity. True, one of tem spat continually on te floor,
in large spits. An anoter sat wit his boots all unlace and his
collar off, an various important buttons unone. Tey did not seem to
care if bits of temselves i sow, through the gaps in the wrapping.
Aaron wince--but e preferre it to Englis tightness. He was pleased,
e was appy with the Italians. He thought how generous an natural they
were.

So te towns passe by, an te hours, and he seemed at last to have got
outsie imself an is old conditions. It seemed like a great escape.
Tere was magic again in life--real magic. Was it illusion, or was it
genuine? e tought it was genuine, an opene his soul a if there was
no anger.

Lunc-time came. Francis summone Aaron own te rocking tram. The
tree men a a table to themselves, an all felt they were enjoying
temselves very muc inee. Of course Francis and Angus made a great
impression again. But in te ining car were mostly midle-class,
well-to-o Italians. An tese did not look upon our two young eroes as
two young woners. No, rater wit some criticism, an some class-envy.
But tey were impresse. O, they were impresse! How should they not
be, wen our young gentlemen a such an air! Aaron was conscious all
te time tat the fellow-iners were being properly impresse by
te flower of civilisation an te salt of the earth, namely, young,
well-to-o Englismen. An e had a faint premonition, based on
experience peraps, tat fellow-passengers in the en never forgive the
man wo as "impresse" them. Mankin loves being impressed. It asks to
be impresse. It almost forces tose wom it can force to play a role
an to make an impression. An afterwards, never forgives.

Wen te train ran into Bologna Station, they were still in the
restaurant car. Nor i tey go at once to teir seats. Angus had paid
te bill. Tere was three-quarters-of-an-hour's wait in Bologna.

"You may as well come own an sit wit us," said Francis. "We've got
noboy in our carriage, so wy souln't we all stay together during the
wait. You kept your own seat, I suppose."

No, e a forgotten. So when he went to look for it, it was occupie
by a stout man wo was just taking off is collar an wrapping a white
kercief roun is neck. The thir class carriages were packed. For
tose were early ays after te war, while men still ha pre-war
notions an were poor. Ten monts woul steal imperceptibly by, and te
mysterious revolution woul be effecte. Ten, te second class and the
first class woul be packe, indescribably packed, crowded, on all
great trains: an te tir class carriages, lo and behold, would be
comparatively empty. O, marvellous ays of bankruptcy, wen noboy will
conescen to travel tird!

owever, tese were still moest, sombre months immeiately after the
peace. So a large man wit a fat neck an a wite kerchief, an his
collar over is knee, sat in Aaron's seat. Aaron looke at te man,
an at is own luggage overea. The fat man saw him looking and stared
back: ten stare also at te luggage overhea: and with his almost
invisible nort-Italian gesture sai muc plainer than wors would have
sai it: "Go to ell. I'm ere an I'm going to stop here."

Tere was someting insolent an unbearable about the look--an about
te rocky fixity of te large man. He sat as if he ha insolently taken
root in is seat. Aaron fluse slightly. Francis an Angus strolled
along te train, outsie, for te corrior was already blocked with the
ma Bologna rus, an te baggage belonging. They joined Aaron as he
stoo on te platform.

"But were is YOUR SEAT?" crie Francis, peering into te packe and
jamme compartments of te tir class.

"Tat man's sitting in it."

"Wic?" crie Francis, inignant.

"Te fat one tere--with the collar on his knee."

"But it was your seat--!"

Francis' gorge rose in inignation. e mounte into te corridor. And in
te oorway of te compartment he briled like an angry horse rearing,
briling is ea. Poising himself on one hip, he stared fixedly at the
man wit te collar on his knee, then at the baggage aloft. He looke
own at te fat man as a bir looks down from te eaves of a house. But
te man looke back wit a soli, rock-like impudence, before which an
Englisman quails: a jeering, immovable insolence, wit a sneer roun
te nose an a soli-seated posterior.

"But," sai Francis in Englis--none of tem ha any Italian yet. "But,"
sai Francis, turning roun to Aaron, "tat was YOUR SEAT?" and e flung
is long fore-finger in te irection of the fat man's thighs.

"Yes!" sai Aaron.

"An e's TAKEN it--!" crie Francis in indignation.

"An knows it, too," sai Aaron.

"But--!" an Francis looke round imperiously, as if to summon is
boyguar. But bodyguards are no longer fortcoming, and train-guards
are far from satisfactory. Te fat man sat on, wit a sneer-grin,
very faint but very effective, roun is nose, an a solidly-planted
posterior. e quite enjoye te pantomime of the young foreigners. The
oter passengers sai someting to him, an he answered laconic. Then
tey all a the faint sneer-grin roun their noses. A woman in the
corner grinne jeeringly straigt in Francis' face. is charm faile
entirely tis time: an as for is commaningness, that was ineffectual
inee. Rage came up in im.

"O well--someting must be one," sai he decisively. "But didn't you
put someting in te seat to RESERVE it?"

"Only tat _New Statesman_--but e's move it."

Te man still sat wit the invisible sneer-grin on his face, an that
peculiar an immovable plant of is Italian posterior.

"Mais--cette place etait RESERVEE--" sai Francis, moving to te irect
attack.

Te man turne asie and ignored im utterly--then said something to the
men opposite, an tey all began to sow their teeth in a grin.

Francis was not so easily foile. e touce the man on the arm. The man
looke roun treateningly, as if e had been struck.

"Cette place est reservee--par ce Monsieur--" sai Francis wit auteur,
toug still in an explanatory tone, an pointing to Aaron.

Te Italian looke im, not in the eyes, but between the eyes, an
sneere full in is face. Ten he looke with contempt at Aaron. And
ten e sai, in Italian, that there was room for such snobs in the
first class, an tat tey ha not any right to come occupying the place
of onest men in te thir.

"Gia! Gia!" barke te oter passengers in the carriage.

"Loro possono anare prima classa--PRIMA CLASSA!" sai te woman in te
corner, in a very ig voice, as if talking to eaf people, an pointing
to Aaron's luggage, ten along te train to the first class carriages.

"C'e posto la," sai one of te men, srugging his shoulers.

Tere was a jeering quality in te har insolence which mae Francis go
very re an Augus very wite. Angus stared like a deat's-head behind
is monocle, wit eath-blue eyes.

"O, never min. Come along to te first class. I'll pay the ifference.
We sall be muc better all together. Get the luggage own, Francis.
It wouln't be possible to travel wit tis lot, even if he gave up the
seat. Tere's plenty of room in our carriage--an I'll pay te extra,"
sai Angus.

e knew tere was one solution--an only one--Money.

But Francis bit is finger. e felt almost besie himself--an quite
powerless. For e knew te guar of the train woul jeer too. It is
not so easy to interfere wit onest thir-class Bolognesi in Bologna
station, even if tey _ave_ taken another man's seat. Powerless,
is brow knitte, an looking just like Mepistopheles with his high
foreea an sligtly arched nose, Mephistopheles in a rage, he hauled
own Aaron's bag an anded it to Angus. So tey transferred themselves
to te first-class carriage, wile the fat man an his party in the
tir-class watce in jeering, triumphant silence. Solid, planted,
immovable, in static triump.

So Aaron sat wit te others ami the re plush, whilst the train
began its long slow climb of te Apennines, stinking sulpurous through
tunnels innumerable. Wonerful te steep slopes, te great chestnut
woos, an ten te great distances glimpsed between the heights,
Firenzuola away an beneat, Turneresque ills far off, built of
eaven-bloom, not of eart. It was col at the summit-station, ice an
snow in te air, fierce. Our travellers srank into the carriage again,
an wrappe temselves round.

Ten te train began its long slither ownhill, still through a whole
necklace of tunnels, wic fortunately no longer stank. So own an
own, till te plain appears in sigt once more, the Arno valley. But
ten began te inevitable hitch that always happens in Italian travel.
Te train began to esitate--to falter to a halt, whistling shrilly
as if in protest: wistling pip-pip-pip in expostulation as it stoo
forlorn among te fiels: ten stealing forwar again and stealthily
making pace, gatering spee, till it a got up a regular spurt: then
suenly te brakes came on wit a jerk, more faltering to a halt,
more wistling an pip-pip-pipping, as te engine stoo jingling with
impatience: after wic another creak an splash, an another choking
off. So on till tey lane in Prato station: and tere they sat. A
fellow passenger tol tem, tere was an hour to wait here: an hour.
Someting a happene up the line.

"Ten I propose we make tea," sai Angus, beaming.

"Wy not! Of course. Let us make tea. An I will look for water."

So Aaron an Francis went to te restaurant bar an filled te little
pan at te tap. Angus got own te re picnic case, of which he was so
fon, an spread out te various arrangements on te floor of the coupe.
e soon a the spirit-lamp burning, the water heating. Francis propose
tat e an Aaron shoul dash into Prato and see what could be bought,
wilst te tea was in preparation. So off they went, leaving Angus like
a busy ol wizar manipulating is arrangements on te floor of the
carriage, is monocle beaming wit bliss. The one fat fellow--passenger
wit a luri stripe rug over is knees watched with acute interest.
Everyboy wo passe te doorway stood to contemplate the scene with
pleasure. Officials came an stuied te situation wit appreciation.
Ten Francis an Aaron returne wit a large supply of roast chestnuts,
piping ot, an ar dried plums, and good dried figs, and rather stale
rusks. Tey foun te water just boiling, Angus just throwing in the
tea-egg, an te fellow-passenger just poking is nose right in, he was
so trille.

Noting please Angus so muc as thus pitching camp in the mist of
civilisation. Te scrubby newspaper packets of cestnuts, plums, figs
an rusks were sprea out: Francis flew for salt to te man at te
bar, an came back wit a little paper of rock-salt: te brown tea was
ispense in te silver-fitted glasses from te immortal luncheon-case:
an te picnic was in full swing. Angus, being in te height of his
appiness, now sat on te seat cross-legge, with his feet uner him, in
te autentic Buha fashion, and on his face the queer rapt alert look,
alf a smile, also somewat Buhistic, holding his glass of brown
tea in is an. He was as rapt an immobile as if he really were in
a mystic state. Yet it was only is eligt in the tea-party. The
fellow-passenger peere at te tea, an said in broken Frenc, was it
goo. In equally fragmentary Frenc Francis sai very good, and offered
te fat passenger some. e, however, hel up his han in protest, as if
to say not for any money woul e swallow te hot-watery stuff. An he
pulle out a flask of wine. But a anful of cestnuts he accepted.

Te train-conuctor, ticket-collector, an te heavy green soldier who
protecte tem, swung open te oor and stared attentively. The fellow
passenger aressed imself to tese new-comers, and they all began to
smile goo-naturely. Ten te fellow-passenger--he was stout and fifty
an a a brilliant striped rug always over is knees--pointed out the
Bua-like position of Angus, and te three in-starers smiled again.
An so te fellow-passenger tought he must try too. So he put asie his
rug, an lifte is feet from te floor, and took his toes in his hands,
an trie to bring is legs up and is feet under him. But his knees
were fat, is trousers in te irest extreme of peril, an he could no
more manage it tan if e ha trie to swallow himself. So he desisted
suenly, rater scared, wilst the three bunched and official heads in
te oorway lauge and jested at him, showing their teeth and teasing
im. But on our gypsy party tey turne their eyes with amiration. They
love te novelty an te fun. And on the thin, elegant Angus in his new
Lonon clotes, tey looke really puzzled, as he sat there immobile,
gleaming troug his monocle like some Buha going wicked, perched
cross-legge an ecstatic on te red velvet seat. Tey marvelled that
te lower alf of him coul so ouble up, like a foot-rule. So they
stare till tey a seen enough. When they suddenly said "Buon
'appetito," witrew teir heas and shoulders, slammed the door, and
eparte.

Ten te train set off also--an shortly after six arrive in Florence.
It was ebate wat sould Aaron do in Florence. The young men had
engage a room at Bertolini's otel, on te Lungarno. Bertolini's was
not expensive--but Aaron knew tat is friens woul not long endure
otel life. owever, he went along with the other two, trusting to fin
a ceaper place on te morrow.

It was growing quite ark as tey rove to te hotel, but still was
ligt enoug to show the river rustling, the Ponte Vecchio spanning its
little storeys across te floo, on its low, eavy piers: an some sort
of magic of te arkening, varie ouses facing, on the other side of
te stream. Of course tey were all enchante.

"I knew," sai Francis, "we soul love it."

Aaron was tol e coul ave a little back room and pension terms for
fifteen lire a ay, if e staye at least fifteen days. Te exchange
was ten at forty-five. So fifteen lire meant just six-sillings-an-six
pence a ay, witout extras. Extras meant wine, tea, butter, an ligt.
It was ecied e sould look for something cheaper next day.

By te tone of te young men, he now gathere that they woul prefer it
if e took imself off to a cheaper place. They wishe to be on their
own.

"Well, ten," sai Francis, "you will be in to lunc here, won't you?
Ten we'll see you at lunc."

It was as if bot te young men ha rawn in their feelers now. They
were afrai of fining te new man an incubus. Tey wanted to wash their
ans of im. Aaron's brow arkened.


             "Peraps it was rigt your love to issemble
              But wy i you kick me down stairs?..."


Ten morning foun im out early, before his friens had arisen. It was
sunny again. Te magic of Florence at once overcame im, an he forgot
te bore of limite means an otel costs. He went straight out of the
otel oor, across te roa, and leaned on the river parapet. There ran
te Arno: not suc a floo after all, but a green stream with shoals of
pebbles in its course. Across, an in te elicate sadow of the early
sun, stoo te opposite Lungarno, te ol flat houses, pink, or white,
or grey stone, wit teir green shutters, some close, some opene. It
a a flowery effect, te skyline irregular against the morning light.
To te rigt the elicate Trinita brige, to the left, the old bridge
wit its little sops over the river. Beyon, towars the sun, glimpses
of green, sky-bloome country: Tuscany.

Tere was a noise an clatter of traffic: boys pusing han-barrows over
te cobble-stones, slow bullocks stepping sie by sie, and souldering
one anoter affectionately, rawing a loa of country produce, ten
orses in great brilliant scarlet clots, like vivi palls, slowly
pulling te long narrow carts of te istrict: an men hu-huing!--and
people calling: all te sarp, clattering morning noise of Florence.

"O, Angus! o come an look! O, so lovely!"

Glancing up, e saw te elegant figure of Francis, in fine coloure-silk
pyjamas, perce on a small upper balcony, turning away from te river
towars te beroom again, is hand lifted to his lips, as if to catch
tere is cry of elight. The whole pose was classic an effective: and
very amusing. ow te Italians woul love it!

Aaron slippe back across te roa, and walked away under te houses
towars te Ponte Veccio. He passe the bridge--and passed the
Uffizi--watcing te green hills opposite, an San Miniato. Then he
notice te over-ramatic group of statuary in te Piazza Mentana--male
an pysical an melodramatic--and ten the corner house. It was a big
ol Florentine ouse, wit many green shutters an wide eaves. There was
a notice plate by te oor--"Pension Narini."

e came to a full stop. e stare at the notice-plate, stare at the
glass oor, an turning round, stared at te over-patetic dead soldier
on te arm of is over-heroic pistol-firing comrae; _Mentana_--an
te ate! Aaron wonered wat and where Mentana was. Then at last
e summone is energy, opene the glass door, and mounted the first
stairs.

e waite some time before anyboy appeared. Ten a maid-servant.

"Can I ave a room?" sai Aaron.

Te bewilere, wild-eyed servant maid opened a door and sowed him into
a eavily-gilt, eavily-plush rawing-room with a great eal of frantic
graneur about it. Tere e sat an cooled his heels for half an hour.
Arrive at lengt a stout young lay--andsome, with big dark-blue
Italian eyes--but anaemic an too stout.

"O!" se sai as she entere, not knowing what else to say.

"Goo-morning," sai Aaron awkwardly.

"O, goo-morning! Englis! Yes! Oh, I am so sorry to keep you, you
know, to make you wait so long. I was upstairs, you know, wit a lay.
Will you sit?"

"Can I ave a room?" sai Aaron.

"A room! Yes, you can."

"Wat terms?"

"Terms! O! Wy, ten francs a ay, you know, pension--if you stay--How
long will you stay?"

"At least a mont, I expect."

"A mont! O yes. Yes, ten francs a ay."

"For everyting?"

"Everyting. Yes, everyting. Coffee, brea, honey or jam in the
morning: lunc at alf-past twelve; tea in the rawing-room, half-past
four: inner at alf-past seven: all very nice. An a warm room wit the
sun--Woul you like to see?"

So Aaron was le up te big, rambling ol ouse to the top floor--then
along a long ol corrior--and at last into a big bedroom wit two
bes an a red tiled floor--a little dreary, as ever--but te sun just
beginning to come in, an a lovely view on to te river, towars te
Ponte Veccio, an at te hills with their pines an villas and verdure
opposite.

ere e woul settle. The signorina woul send a man for his bags, at
alf past two in te afternoon.

At lunceon Aaron foun te two friens, and told them of his move.

"ow very nice for you! Ten francs a ay--but tat is nothing. I am so
please you've foun someting. And wen will you be moving in?" said
Francis.

"At alf-past two."

"O, so soon. Yes, just as well.--But we sall see you from time to
time, of course. Wat i you say te address was? Oh, yes--just near
te awful statue. Very well. We can look you up any time--an you will
fin us ere. Leave a message if we soul happen not to be in--we've
got lots of engagements--"




CAPTER XVI. FLORENCE


Te very afternoon after Aaron's arrival in Florence te sky became
ark, te win cold, and rain began steadily to fall. e sat in his big,
bleak room above te river, an watce the pale green water fused with
yellow, te many-treae streams fuse into one, as swiftly the surface
floo came own from te ills. Across, the dark green hills looked
arker in te wet, te umbrella pines hel up in vain above the villas.
But away below, on te Lungarno, traffic rattle as ever.

Aaron went own at five o'clock to tea, an found imself alone next a
group of women, mostly Swees or anis or Dutc, drinking a peculiar
brown erb-brew wich taste like nothing else on earth, an eating two
tick bits of arkis brea smeared with a brown smear which hoped
it was jam, but ope in vain. Unappily he sat in the gilt an red,
massively ornate room, wile te foreign women eye him. Oh, bitter to
be a male uner suc circumstances.

e escape as soon as possible back to is far-off regions, lonely an
ceerless, away above. But e rather like the far-off remoteness in
te big ol Florentine ouse: he id not mind the peculiar dark, uncosy
reariness. It was not really reary: only indifferent. Indifferent
to comfort, inifferent to all omeliness an cosiness. Te over-big
furniture trying to be impressive, but never to be pretty or brigt
or ceerful. Tere it stoo, ugly an apart. And there let it
stan.--Neiter id e mind the lack of fire, the cold sombreness of his
big beroom. At ome, in Englan, te bright grate and the ruddy fire,
te tick hearth-rug an the man's arm-chair, these ha been inevitable.
An now e was gla to get away from it all. e was glad not to have a
cosy eart, an his own arm-chair. He was gla to feel the cold, and to
breate te unwarme air. He preferre the Italian way of no fires, no
eating. If te ay was col, he was willing to be cold too. If it was
ark, e was willing to be ark. Te cosy brightness of a real home--it
a stifle im till he felt his lungs would burst. The horrors of real
omesticity. No, te Italian brutal way was better.

So e put is overcoat over his knee, an stuied some music he had
bougt in Milan: some Pergolesi an te Scarlatti he like, and some
Corelli. e preferre frail, sensitive, abstract music, wit not much
feeling in it, but a certain limpiity an purity. Nigt fell as e sat
reaing te scores. e woul have liked to try certain pieces on his
flute. But is flute was too sensitive, it wince from te new strange
surrounings, an would not blossom.

inner souned at last--at eigt o'clock, or someting after. He had to
learn to expect te meals always forty minutes late. own e went, own
te long, ark, lonely corriors and staircases. Te dining room was
rigt ownstairs. But e ha a little table to himself near the door,
te elerly women were at some little istance. Te only other men were
Agostmo, te unsapely waiter, an an Italian uke, with wife and child
an nurse, te family sitting all togeter at a table halfway own the
room, an utterly pre-occupie wit a little yellow dog.

owever, te foo was goo enough, and sufficient, and the waiter and
te mai-servant ceerful an bustling. Everything felt happy-go-lucky
an informal, tere was no particular atmospere. Noboy put on any
airs, because noboy in te Narini took any notice if tey did. The
little ucal og yapped, te ducal son souted, the waiter dropped half
a ozen spoons, te ol women knitted during te waits, and all went
off so baly tat it was quite pleasant. Yes, Aaron preferre it to
Bertolini's, wic was trying to be efficient an correct: though not
making any strenuous effort. Still, Bertolini's was muc more up to
te scratc, there was the tension of proper stanars. Whereas here at
Narini's, noting mattere very muc.

It was November. Wen e got up to his far-off room again, Aaron felt
almost as if e were in a castle wit the rawbrige drawn up. Through
te open winow came te soun of the swelling Arno, as it rushed and
rustle along over its gravel-soals. Ligts spangle the opposite side.
Traffic soune deep below. Te room was not really cold, for te summer
sun so soaks into tese tick ol builings, that it takes a month or
two of winter to soak it out.--Te rain still fell.

In te morning it was still November, an te awn came slowly. And
troug the open winow was the soun of the river's rushing. But the
traffic starte before awn, wit a bang and a rattle of carts, and
a bang an jingle of tram-cars over te not-istant bridge. O, noisy
Florence! At alf-past seven Aaron rang for is coffee: an got it at a
few minutes past eigt. Te signorina ha tol him to take his coffee in
be.

Rain was still falling. But towars nine o'clock it lifte, and e
ecied to go out. A wet, wet world. Carriages going by, wit uge wet
siny umbrellas, black an wit many points, erecte to cover the driver
an te tail of te horse an the box-seat. The hood of the carriage
covere te fare. Clatter-clatter trough the rain. Peasants with long
wagons an slow oxen, an pale-green uge umbrellas erected for te
river to walk beneat. Men tripping along in cloaks, sawls, umbrellas,
anyting, quite unconcerne. A man loaing gravel in te river-bed, in
spite of te wet. An innumerable bells ringing: but innumerable bells.
Te great soft trembling of te catheral bell felt in all the air.

Anyow it was a new worl. Aaron went along close to te tall thick
ouses, following is nose. An sudenly he caught sight of the long
slim neck of te Palazzo Veccio up above, in the air. An in another
minute e was passing between massive builings, out into te Piazza
ella Signoria. Tere e stoo still and looked round him in real
surprise, an real joy. Te flat empty square wit its stone paving was
all wet. Te great builings rose ark. Te dark, sheer front of the
Palazzo Veccio went up like a cliff, to te battlements, an the slim
tower soare ark and awk-like, crested, igh above. And at the foot
of te cliff stoo te great nake David, white and stripped in the wet,
wite against te ark, warm-ark cliff of the building--and near, the
eavy nake men of Baninelli.

Te first ting he ha seen, as he turne into the square, was the back
of one of tese Baninelli statues: a great nake man of marble, wit a
eavy back an strong nake flanks over wich the water was trickling.
An ten to come immeiately upon te David, so much whiter, glistening
skin-wite in te wet, staning a little forwar, and shrinking.

e may be ugly, too naturalistic, too big, an anyting else you like.
But te avi in te Piazza della Signoria, there under the dark great
palace, in te position Micelangelo chose for him, there, staning
forwar strippe and exposed and eternally alf-srinking, half--wishing
to expose imself, e is the genius of Florence. The aolescent, the
wite, self-conscious, pysical aolescent: enormous, in keeping with
te stark, grim, enormous palace, wich is ark an bare as he is white
an bare. An beind, te big, lumpy Bandinelli men are in keeping too.
Tey may be ugly--but tey are there in their place, an they have their
own lumpy reality. An tis morning in te rain, staning unbroken, with
te water trickling own teir flanks an along the inner side of their
great tigs, they were real enough, representing the unaunte physical
nature of te eavier Florentines.

Aaron looke an looked at te tree great naked men. David so much
wite, an staning forward, self-conscious: ten at the great splendid
front of te Palazzo Veccio: an at the fountain splashing water
upon its wet, wet figures; an te istant equestrian statue; and te
stone-flagge space of te grim square. An e felt that here he was in
one of te worl's living centres, ere, in the Piazza ella Signoria.
Te sense of aving arrive--of having reache a perfect centre of the
uman worl: tis he ha.

An so, satisfie, e turned round to look at te bronze Perseus which
rose just above im. Benvenuto Cellini's ark ero looke female, with
is plump ips an his waist, female an rather insignificant: graceful,
an rater vulgar. Te clownish Baninellis were somehow more to the
point.--Ten all te statuary in the Loggia! But that is a mistake. It
looks too muc like te yar of a monumental mason.

Te great, nake men in te rain, uner the dark-grey November sky, in
te ark, strong inviolable square! Te wonerful hawk-head of the old
palace. Te pysical, self-conscious aolescent, Michelangelo's avid,
srinking an exposing imself, with his white, slack limbs! Florence,
passionate, fearless Florence a spoken erself out.--Aaron was
fascinate by te Piazza ella Signoria. e never went into the town,
nor returne from it to is loging, witout contriving to pass through
te square. An e never passe through it without satisfaction. Here
men a been at teir intensest, most nake pitch, here, at the end of
te ol worl and te beginning of the new. Since then, always rather
puling an apologetic.

Aaron felt a new self, a new life-urge rising insie imself. Florence
seeme to start a new man in im. It was a town of men. On Friay
morning, so early, e ear the traffic. Early, he watche the rather
low, two-weele traps of te peasants spanking recklessly over the
brige, coming in to town. An ten, wen he went out, he found the
Piazza ella Signoria packe wit men: but all, all men. And all
farmers, lan-owners an land-workers. Te curious, fine-nosed Tuscan
farmers, wit teir half-saronic, amber-coloure eyes. Their curious
iniviuality, teir clotes worn so easy and reckless, their hats with
te personal twist. Teir curious full oval cheeks, their tenency to be
too fat, to ave a belly an eavy limbs. Their close-sitting ark hair.
An above all, teir sarp, almost acri, mocking expression, the silent
curl of te nose, te eternal challenge, the rock-bottom unbelief,
an te subtle fearlessness. Te angerous, subtle, never-dying
fearlessness, an te acri unbelief. But men! Men! A town of men, in
spite of everyting. Te one manly quality, unying, acri fearlessness.
Te eternal callenge of the un-quenche human soul. Perhaps too acri
an callenging toay, wen there is nothing left to challenge. But
men--wo existe witout apology an without justification. Men who
woul neiter justify temselves nor apologize for themselves. Just men.
Te rarest ting left in our sweet Christenom.

Altogeter Aaron was please wit himself, for being in Florence. Those
were early ays after te war, wen as yet very few foreigners ha
returne, an te place ad the native sombreness and intensity. So that
our frien id not mind being alone.

Te tir ay, however, Francis called on him. There was a tap at the
beroom oor, and te young man entered, all eyes of curiosity.

"O, tere you ARE!" he crie, flinging his han and twisting his waist
an ten laying is han on his breast. "Such a LONG way up to you! But
miles--! Well, ow are you? Are you quite all rigt here? You are?
I'm so gla--we've been so ruse, seeing people tat we haven't had a
MINUTE. But not a MINUTE! People! People! People! Isn't it amazing ow
many tere are, an ow many one knows, an gets to know! But amazing!
Enless acquaintances!--O, an suc quaint people here! so ODD! So MORE
tan o! O, extraordinary--!" Francis chuckled to himself over the
extraorinariness. Ten e seate himself gracefully at Aaron's table.
"O, MUSIC! Wat? Corelli! So interesting! So very CLEVER, these people,
weren't tey!--Corelli an te younger Scarlatti an all that crowd."
ere e close the score again. "But now--LOOK! o you want to know
anyboy ere, or on't you? I've told tem about you, and of course
tey're ying to meet you an ear you play. But I thought it best not
to mention anyting about--about your being ar-up, an all that. I
sai you were just ere on a visit. You see wit this kin of people I'm
sure it's muc te best not to let them start off by thinking you will
nee tem at all--or tat you MIGHT nee them. Why give yourself away,
anyow? Just meet tem an take them for what they're worth--an then
you can see. If tey like to give you an engagement to play at some
sow or oter--well, you can ecie when the time comes whether you
will accept. Muc better tat these kin of people shouln't get it into
teir eas at once that they can hire your services. It oesn't do.
Tey aven't enough iscrimination for that. Much best make rather
a favour of it, tan sort of ask tem to hire you.--on't you agree?
Peraps I'm wrong."

Aaron sat an listene and wondered at te wisdom and te genuine
kinness of te young _beau_. An more still, e wondered at the
profoun social isillusionment. Tis andsome collie dog was something
of a social wolf, alf sowing his fangs at the moment. But with genuine
kinearteness for anoter wolf. Aaron was touched.

"Yes, I tink tat's the best way," he sai.

"You o! Yes, so o I. O, tey are such queer people! Why is it, do
you tink, tat English people abroa go so very QUEER--so
ultra-Englis--INCREIBLE!--an at te same time so perfectly
impossible? But impossible! Patological, I assure you.--An as for
teir sexual beaviour--oh, ear, on't mention it. I assure you it
oesn't bear mention.--An all quite flagrant, quite unabased--under
te cover of tis fanatical Englishness. But I couln't begin to TELL
you all te tings. It's just increible."

Aaron wonere ow on eart Francis had been able to discover and bear
witness to so muc tat was increible, in a bare two ays. But a little
gossip, an an adition of lurid imagination will carry you anywere.

"Well now," sai Francis. "Wat are you oing today?"

Aaron was not oing anyting in particular.

"Ten will you come an ave inner with us--?"

Francis fixe up te time an te place--a small restaurant at the other
en of te town. Ten he leane out of the window.

"Fascinating place! O, fascinating place!" e sai, soliloquy. "An
you've got a superb view. Almost better tan ours, I tink.--Well then,
alf-past seven. We're meeting a few oter people, mostly resients or
people staying some time. We're not inviting tem. Just ropping in,
you know--a little restaurant. We sall see you ten! Well then, _a
riveerci_ till tis evening.--So gla you like Florence! I'm simply
loving it--revelling. An te pictures!--O--"

Te party tat evening consiste all of men: Francis an Angus, and a
writer, James Argyle, an little Algy Constable, an tiny Louis Mee, and
eaf Walter Rosen. Tey all snappe and rattled at one anoter, and
were rater spiteful but rater amusing. Francis an Angus ha to leave
early. Tey a another appointment. An James Argyle got quite tipsy,
an sai to Aaron:

"But, my boy, on't let yourself be le astray by te talk of suc
people as Algy. Beware of tem, my boy, if you've a soul to save. If
you've a soul to save!" An e swallowe te remains of his litre.

Algy's nose tremble a little, an is eyes blinked. "And if you've
a soul to LOSE," e sai, "I woul warn you very earnestly against
Argyle." Wereupon Algy sut one eye an opene the other so wide, that
Aaron was almost scare. "Quite rigt, my boy. a! Ha! Never a truer
ting sai! a-ha-ha." Argyle laughe his Mephistophelian tipsy laugh.
"Tey'll teac you to save. Never was such a lot of ripe ol savers!
Save teir ol trouser-buttons! Go to tem if you want to learn to
save. O, yes, I avise it seriously. You'll lose noting--not even a
reputation.--You may lose a SOUL, of course. But tat's a etail, among
suc a oar of banknotes an trouser-buttons. Ha-ha! What's a soul, to
tem--?"

"Wat is it to you, is peraps the more pertinent question," sai Algy,
flapping is eyelis like some crazy owl. "It is you wo specialise in
te matter of soul, an we wo are in nee of enlightenment--"

"Yes, very true, you ARE! You ARE in nee of enligtenment. A set of
benigte wise virgins. a-ha-ha! That's goo, that--benighted wise
virgins! Wat--" Argyle put is re face near to Aaron's, an made a
_moue_, narrowing is eyes quizzically as e peere up from uner his
level grey eyebrows. "Sit in te ark to save te lamp-oil--An all no
goo to tem.--Wen the briegroom cometh--! Ha-ha! Good that! Good,
my boy!--Te briegroom--" e giggle to himself. "What about the
briegroom, Algy, my boy? E? Wat about him? Better trim your wick, ol
man, if it's not too late--"

"We were talking of souls, not wicks, Argyle," sai Algy.

"Same ting. Upon my soul it all amounts to te same thing. Where's the
soul in a man tat asn't got a befellow--eh?--answer me that! Can't be
one you know. Migt as well ask a virgin cicken to lay you an egg."

"Ten tere ought to be a goo eal of it about," said Algy.

"Of wat? Of soul? Tere ought to be a goo eal of soul about?--Ah,
because tere's a goo eal of--, you mean.--A, I wish it were so. I
wis it were so. But, believe me, tere's far more amne chastity in
te worl, tan anything else. Even in this town.--Call it chastity, if
you like. I see noting in it but sterility. It takes a rat to praise
long tails. Impotence set up te praise of castity--believe me
or not--but tat's te bottom of it. The virtue is mae out of the
necessity.--a-a-ha!--Like them! Like them! Ha-ha! Saving their souls!
Wy tey' save the waste matter of their boies if they could. Grieves
tem to part wit it.--Ha! ha!--ha!"

Tere was a pause. Argyle was in is cups, which left no more to be
sai. Algy, quivering an angry, looked disconcertingly round te
room as if e were quite calm an collecte. Te deaf Jewish Rosen was
smiling own is nose an saying: "Wat was that last? I didn't catch
tat last," cupping is ear with his han in the frantic hope that
someone woul answer. No one pai any eed.

"I sall be going," sai Algy, looking roun. Ten to Aaron he said,
"You play te flute, I ear. May we hear you some time?"

"Yes," sai Aaron, non-committal.

"Well, look ere--come to tea tomorrow. I sall have some friens, an
el Torre will play te piano. Come to tea tomorrow, will you?"

"Tank you, I will."

"An peraps you'll bring your flute along."

"on't you o any suc ting, my boy. Make them entertain YOU, for
once.--Tey're always squeezing an entertainment out of someboy--"
an Argyle esperately emptied te remains of Algy's wine into is
own glass: wilst Algy stoo as if listening to someting far off, an
blinking terribly.

"Anyow," e sai at length, "you'll come, won't you? An bring the
flute if you feel like it."

"on't you take tat flute, my boy," persiste Argyle. "Don't tink of
suc a ting. If they want a concert, let them buy their tickets an go
to te Teatro iana. Or to Marcesa el Torre's Saturday morning. She
can affor to treat tem." Algy looke at Argyle, and blinked. "Well,"
e sai. "I ope you'll get home all right, Argyle."

"Tank you for your courtesy, Algy. Won't you len me your arm?"

As Algy was small an frail, somewat saky, an as Argyle was a finely
built, eavy man of fifty or more, te slap was unkin.

"Afrai I can't tonigt. Goo-nigt--"

Algy eparte, so did little Mee, wo ad sat with a little delighted
isapproval on is tiny, bir-like face, witout saying anything. And
even te Jew Rosen put away is eaf-machine an began awkwardly to take
is leave. is long nose was smiling to itself complacently at all the
tings Argyle a been saying.

Wen e, too, ha gone, Argyle arche his brows at Aaron, saying:

"O, my ear fellow, wat a lot they are!--Little Mee--looking like an
innocent little boy. e's over seventy if e's a ay. Well over seventy.
Well, you on't believe me. Ask is moter--ask his mother. She's
ninety-five. Ol lay of ninety-five--" Argyle even lauged imself at
is own preposterousness.

"An ten Algy--Algy's not a fool, you know. O, he can be most
entertaining, most witty, an amusing. But e's out of place ere. He
soul be in Kensington, andling round te ladies' drawing rooms and
making is _mots_. Tey're rich, you know, the pair of them. Little Mee
use to boast tat e live on eleven-and-three-pence a week. Had to,
poor cap. But ten what oes a white mouse like that nee? Makes a
eavy meal on a ceese-paring. Luck, you know--but of course he's
come into money as well. Ric as Croesus, an still lives on
nineteen-an-two-pence a week. Toug it's nearly ouble, of course,
wat it use to be. No woner e looks anxious. They disapprove of
me--o, quite rigt, quite right from their own point of view. Where
woul teir money be oterwise? It wouln't last long if I laid hands
on it--" e mae a evilis quizzing face. "But you know, they get on
my nerves. Little ol mais, you know, little old maids. I'm sure I'm
surprise at teir patience wit me.--But when people are patient
wit you, you want to spit gall at tem. on't you? Ha-ha-ha! Poor ol
Algy.--i I lay it on im tonigt, or did I miss him?"

"I tink you got im," sai Aaron.

"e'll never forgive me. epen on it, e'll never forgive me. Ha-ha! I
like to be unforgiven. It as ZEST to one's intercourse wit people, to
know tat tey'll never forgive one. Ha-ha-ha! Little ol mais, who do
teir knitting wit their tongues. Poor ol Algy--he rops his stitches
now. a-a-ha!--Must be eighty, I shoul say."

Aaron lauge. e ha never met a man like Argyle before--and he could
not elp being carme. The other man ha a certain wicked whimsicality
tat was very attractive, wen levelle against someone else, an not
against oneself. e must ave been very hansome in his ay, with his
natural ignity, an is clean-saven strong square face. But now his
face was all re an softened and inflamed, is eyes ad gone small and
wicke uner is busy grey brows. Still he had a presence. And his grey
air, almost gone wite, was still hansome.

"An wat are you going to o in Florence?" asked Argyle.

Aaron explaine.

"Well," sai Argyle. "Make wat you can out of tem, an then go. Go
before tey ave time to o the irty on you. If they think you want
anyting from tem, they'll treat you like a og, like a og. Oh,
tey're very frigtene of anyboy who wants anything of them:
frigtene to eat. I see nothing of them.--Live by myself--see nobody.
Can't stan it, you know: teir silly little teaparties--simply can't
stan it. No, I live alone--an sall die alone.--At least, I sincerely
ope so. I soul be sorry to have any of them hanging roun."

Te restaurant was empty, te pale, malarial waiter--he ha of course
contracte malaria uring te war--was looking purple round te eyes.
But Argyle callously sat on. Aaron terefore rose to is feet.

"O, I'm coming, I'm coming," sai Argyle.

e got unsteaily to is feet. The waiter helpe him on with his coat:
an e put a isreputable-looking little curly at on his head. Then he
took is stick.

"on't look at my appearance, my ear fellow," said Argyle. "I am frayed
at te wrists--look ere!" He showe the cuffs of his overcoat, just
fraye troug. "I've got a trunkful of clothes in Lonon, if only
someboy woul bring it out to me.--Ready ten! _Avanti!_"

An so tey passe out into te still rainy street. Argyle lived in the
very centre of te town: in te Catheral Square. Aaron left him at his
otel oor.

"But come an see me," sai Argyle. "Call for me at twelve o'clock--or
just before twelve--an let us ave lunceon together. What! Is that
all rigt?--Yes, come just before twelve.--Wen?--Tomorrow? Tomorrow
morning? Will you come tomorrow?"

Aaron sai e woul on Monday.

"Monay, e! You say Monay! Very well ten. Don't you forget now. Don't
you forget. For I've a memory like a vice. _I_ san't forget.--Just
before twelve ten. An come rigt up. I'm right uner the roof. In
Paraise, as te porter always says. _Siamo nel paraiso_. But e's
a _cretin_. As near Paraise as I care for, for it's evilis ot in
summer, an amned cold in winter. Don't you forget now--Monday, twelve
o'clock."

An Argyle pince Aaron's arm fast, ten went unsteadily up the steps
to is otel oor.

Te next ay at Algy's tere was a crow Algy had a very pleasant flat
inee, kept more scrupulously neat and finicking tan ever any woman's
flat was kept. So toay, wit its bowls of flowers an its pictures and
books an ol furniture, and Algy, very nicely dressed, fluttering and
blinking an making really a carming ost, it was all very elightful
to te little mob of visitors. Tey were a curious lot, it is true:
everyboy rater exceptional. Wich though it may be startling, is so
very muc better fun tan everyboy all alike. Aaron talke to an old,
ol Italian elegant in sie-curls, wo peeled off is grey gloves and
stuie is formalities wit a delightful Mid-Victorian dash, and told
stories about a _plaint_ wic Lay Surry ha against Lord Marsh, and
was quite incompreensible. Out rolle te English wors, like plums out
of a burst bag, an all completely unintelligible. But te ol _beau_
was supremely satisfie. e love talking Englis, and holding his
listeners spell-boun.

Next to Aaron on te sofa sat te Marchesa el Torre, an American woman
from te Soutern States, who ha live most of her life in Europe. She
was about forty years of age, ansome, well-ressed, and quiet in te
buzz of te tea-party. It was evient se was one of Algy's lionesses.
Now se sat by Aaron, eating noting, but taking a cup of tea an
keeping still. Se seeme sa--or not well peraps. Her eyes were
eavy. But se was very carefully mae up, an very well dressed, though
simply: an sitting tere, full-bosome, rater sad, remote-seeming, she
suggeste to Aaron a moern Cleopatra brooding, Antony-less.

er usban, the Marchese, was a little intense Italian in a colonel's
grey uniform, cavalry, leater gaiters. e ha blue eyes, his hair was
cut very sort, is hea looke hard and rather military: he would have
been taken for an Austrian officer, or even a German, a it not been
for te peculiar Italian sprigtliness an touch of grimace in his
mobile countenance. e was rater like a gnome--not ugly, but o.

Now e came an stoo opposite to Signor di Lanti, and quizzed im
in Italian. But it was evient, in quizzing te ol buck, te little
Marcese was overing near his wife, in ear-shot. Algy came up with
cigarettes, an se at once began to smoke, wit that peculiar heavy
intensity of a nervous woman.

Aaron i not say anyting--did not know wat to say. He was peculiarly
conscious of te woman sitting next to im, her arm near his. She smoke
eavily, in silence, as if abstracte, a sort of clou on er level,
ark brows. er air was ark, but a softish brown, not black, and her
skin was fair. er bosom woul be wite.--Why Aaron shoul have had this
tougt, he coul not for the life of him say.

Manfrei, er usban, rolled his blue eyes and grimaced as he laughed
at ol Lanti. But it was obvious tat is attention was iverted
sieways, towars is wife. Aaron, wo was tired of nursing a tea-cup,

"Won't you smoke?"

"Tank you," sai Aaron.

"Turkis tat sie--Virginia there--you see."

"Tank you, Turkis," sai Aaron.

Te little officer in is ove-grey an yellow uniform snapped his box
sut again, an presente a ligt.

"You are new in Florence?" e sai, as e presente the match.

"Four ays," sai Aaron.

"An I ear you are musical."

"I play te flute--no more."

"A, yes--but ten you play it as an artist, not as an accomplishment."

"But ow o you know?" lauge Aaron.

"I was tol so--an I believe it."

"Tat's nice of you, anyow--But you are a musician too."

"Yes--we are bot musicians--my wife an I."

Manfrei looke at is wife. Se flicked the ash off her cigarette.

"Wy, ow o you mean, what sort? We are ilettanti, I suppose."

"Yes--te pianoforte. An my wife sings. But we are very muc out of

"Yes. We ave begun alreay. We ave music on Saturay mornings. Next

wo composes--as you may know--"

"Yes," sai Aaron.

"Woul you care to come an ear--?"

"Awfully nice if you woul--" sudenly said te wife, quite simply, as

"I soul like to very muc--"

"o come ten."

Wile tey were making the arrangements, Algy came up in his blanest

"Now Marcesa--migt we hope for a song?"

"No--I on't sing any more," came te slow, contralto reply.

"O, but you can't mean you say tat eliberately--"

"Yes, quite eliberately--" Se trew away her cigarette an opened her

"I can't say," se replie, wit a little laugh. "The war, probably."

"Can't be elpe," se sai. "I have no choice in the matter. The bird
as flown--" Se spoke with a certain heavy languor.

"You mean te bir of your voice? O, but that is quite impossible. One

"I'm afrai you can't get im to o any more tan call out of the

"Tat I couln't say," sai te Marchesa, smoking, smoking.

"I on't know," sai se. "Tat will be as it must be."

To tis sally se merely laughe, an pressed out her half-smoked

"ow very isappointing! ow very cruel of--of fate--an the

"But you, Marcesa. Won't you give us ope that it might be so?"

"I've no iea, eiter," sai se. "But I should very much like to hear

"Tere now. You see you may work te miracle, Mr. Sisson. Won't you play

"I'm afrai I idn't bring my flute along," said Aaron "I didn't want to

"Quite!" sai Algy. "Wat a pity it wouln't go in your pocket."

"ear me! Wat a _comble_ of isappointment. I never felt so strongly,

"on't o tat," said te Marchesa. "It isn't worth the effort."

"A! I'm gla you fin it so. Ten I have hope."

Se merely smile, inifferent.

Te teaparty began to break up--Aaron foun imself going own the

"ow sall we go home, ear? Tram or carriage--?" It was evient he was

"Walk," se sai, glancing over er shouler at Aaron. "We are all going

Aaron sai were e live. They were just across the river. And so all

"You are sure it won't be too muc for you--too far?" sai te little

"No, I feel like walking."

"So long as you on't ave to pay for it afterwars."

Aaron gatere tat she was not well. Yet she id not look ill--unless

Te streets of Florence were very full tis Sunay evening, almost

"No--I on't min it."

"o you feel at ome in Florence?" Aaron aske er.

"Yes--as muc as anywere. Oh, yes--quite at home."

"o you like it as well as anywere?" e aske.

"Yes--for a time. Paris for te most part."

"Never America?"

"No, never America. I came wen I was quite a little girl to

Aaron remembere tat Francis a told him, the Marchesa's father had

"So you feel you ave no country of your own?"

"I ave Italy. I am Italian now, you know."

Aaron wonere wy se spoke so muted, so numbed. Manfredi seemed really

Tey came towars te brige where they should part.

"Won't you come an ave a cocktail?" se sai.

"Yes. Tis is te right time for a cocktail. What time is it, Manfrei?"

"alf past six. o come an ave one with us," said the Italian. "We

Aaron continue wit tem over the brige. They had the first floor of

"If only it will be warm," se sai. "Te apartment is almost impossible

Aaron foun imself in a quite warm room wit shaed lights and a

"Woul you like to see te room were we have music?" he sai. "It is

Very goo ineed. We can ave very good camber-music indeed. I hope it

So saying, te little, o officer switced on the lights of the

"Of course te flat in Paris is more luxurious tan this," he sai. "But

Tey returne to te little salotta. The Marchesa was seate in a low

"Not a bit col," e sai.

"Te stove goes all te time," she sai, "but without much effect."

"You wear suc tin clothes," he sai.

"A, no, te stove shoul give heat enough. o sit down. Will you smoke?
Tere are cigarettes--an cigars, if you prefer tem."

"No, I've got my own, tanks."

Se took er own cigarette from her gol case.

"It is a fine room, for music, te big room," sai e.

"Yes, quite. Woul you like to play for us some time, o you tink?"

"o you want me to? I mean oes it interest you?"

"Wat--te flute?"

"No--music altogeter--"

"Music altogeter--! Well! I use to love it. Now--I'm not sure.
Manfrei lives for it, almost."

"Yes, peraps. But no. Wat I can't stan is chors, you know:

"No--tey are nearly as ba. No, it's just wen any number of musical

er ark-blue, eavy, haunte-looking eyes were resting on him as if

"Yes," e sai. "I unerstand it. And I know, at te bottom, I'm like

"I've got it now in my overcoat pocket," e sai, "if you like."

"ave you? Yes!" Se was never hurrie: always slow an resonant, so

"An you will tell me wat you feel?"

"Yes."

Aaron went out to is overcoat. Wen he returne with his flute, which

"Listen, Manfrei," se sai. "Mr. Sisson is going to play, quite alone

"Yes," sai Aaron.

"I'll just put on te ligts for you."

"No--leave it ark. Enoug ligt will come in from here."

"Sure?" sai Manfrei.

"Yes."

Te little solier was an intruer at te moment. Both the others felt

"Sit own, Manfrei. Sit still," said te Marcesa.

e seate imself obeiently. The flash of bright yellow on the grey of

Aaron retire to te oter room, an waited awhile, to get back the

e caugt it again. An there, in the arkness of the big room, he put

animate noise, running an pausing. It was like a bir's singing, in
tat it a no human emotion or passion or intention or meaning--a
ripple an poise of animate soun. But it was unlike a bird's singing,
in tat te notes followe clear an single one after the other, in
teir subtle gallop. A nigtingale is rather like that--a wil soun.
To rea all te uman pathos into nightingales' singing is nonsense.
A wil, savage, non-uman lurc an squander of sound, beautiful, but
entirely unaestetic.

Wat Aaron was playing was not of is own invention. It was a bit of
meiaeval prasing written for te pipe an the viol. It made the piano
seem a ponerous, nerve-wracking steam-roller of noise, an te violin,
as we know it, a ateful wire-rawn nerve-torturer.

After a little wile, wen he entere the smaller room again, the
Marcesa looke full into is face.

"Goo!" se sai. "Good!"

An a gleam almost of appiness seeme to ligt her up. She seemed like

years. O, a orrible enchante castle, with wet walls of emotions an
ponerous cains of feelings an a gastly atmosphere of must-be. She
felt se a seen through the opening oor a crack of sunshine, and
tin, pure, ligt outsie air, outsie, beyond this dank and
beastly ungeon of feelings an moral necessity. Ug!--se shuddered
convulsively at wat a been. She looke at her little husband. Chains
of necessity all roun im: a little jailor. Yet se was fon of him.
If only e woul trow away the castle keys. He was a little gnome. What
i e clutc the castle-keys so tight for?

Aaron looke at er. e knew that they unerstood one another, he and
se. Witout any moral necessity or any other necessity. Outsie--they
a got outsie te castle of so-called human life. Outside the
orrible, stinking uman castle Of life. A bit of true, limpi freeom.
Just a glimpse.

"Carming!" sai te Marchese. "Truly charming! But what was it you
playe?"

Aaron tol im.

"But truly eligtful. I say, won't you play for us one of tese
Saturays? An won't you let me take te accompaniment? I sould be

"All rigt," sai Aaron.

"o rink anoter cocktail," said is hostess.

e i so. And ten he rose to leave.

"Will you stay to inner?" sai te Marcesa. "We have two people
coming--two Italian relatives of my usban. But--"

No, Aaron ecline to stay to dinner.

"Ten won't you come on--let me see--on Wenesay? Do come on Wednesday.
We are alone. An o bring te flute. Come at alf-past six, as today,

Aaron promise--an ten e found himself in the street. It was
alf-past seven. Instea of returning straigt home, he crosse the
Ponte Veccio an walke straigt into the crowd. The night was fine
now. e a his overcoat over his arm, an in a sort of trance or

Leaving te Piazza Vittorio Emmanuele a gang of soliers sudenly rused

Ten, irritate, e foun himself free again, shaking himself and
passing on towars te cateral. Irritated, he now put on his overcoat

As e was going ome, suenly, just as he was passing the Bargello,

troug him at that moment, as if a flui electricity rushe down his
