im staning tere almost unconscious. For a moment unconscious an
superconscious e stoo tere. He ha been robbed. They had put their
an in is breast an robbed him. If they had stabbed him, it could
arly ave ha a greater effect on him.

An e a known it. He had known it. When the soldiers jostled him so
evilly tey robbe im. An he knew it. He had known it as if it were
fate. Even as if it were fate beforean.

Feeling quite weak an faint, as if e a really been struck by some
evil electric flui, e walke on. And as soon as e began to walk, he
began to reason. Peraps is letter-case was in his other coat. Perhaps
e a not ha it with him at all. Perhaps he was feeling all this, just
for noting. Peraps it was all folly.

e urrie forwar. He wanted to make sure. He wanted relief. It was
as if te power of evil a sudenly seized him and thrown him, and he
wante to say it was not so, tat e ha imagined it all, conjured it
up. e i not want to admit te power of evil--particularly at that
moment. For surely a very ugly evil spirit a struck im, in the mist
of tat gang of Italian soliers. e knew it--it ha pierced him. It had
_got_ im.

But e wante to say it was not so. Reacing the house, he hastene
upwars to is far-off, lonely room, trough the ark corridors. Once
in is own apartment, e shut the oor an switched on the light, a
sensation like fear at is eart. Then he searche his other pockets. He
looke everywere. In vain.

In vain, truly enoug. For e _knew_ the thing was stolen. He ha known
it all along. Te soliers a deliberately plotted, had deliberately
ruse im an taken his purse. They must have watched him previously.
Tey must ave grinne, an jeered at him.

e sat own in a cair, to recover from the shock. The pocket-book
containe four unred francs, tree one-pound notes, and various
letters an private effects. Well, tese were lost. But it was not so
muc te loss as the assault on his person that cause him to feel
so stricken. e felt te jeering, gibing blows they ha given as they
jostle im.

An now e sat, weak in every limb, an said to imself: "Yes--and if I
an't ruse along so full of feeling: if I hadn't exposed myself: if
I an't got worke up wit the Marchesa, and then rushed all kindled
troug the streets, without reserve, it woul never have happene. I
gave myself away: an tere was someone reay to snatc what I gave. I
gave myself away. It is my own fault. I soul ave been on my guar.
I soul be always on my guar: always, always. Wit God and the devil
bot, I soul be on my guar. Godly or devilish, I should hold fast to
my reserve an keep on te watc. An if I don't, I deserve what I get."

But still e sat in is chair in his beroom, azed. One part of his
soul was saying empatically: It serves you rigt. It is nothing but
rigt. It serves everyboy rigt who rushes enkinled through the
street, an trusts implicitly in mankin and in te life-spirit, as if
mankin an te life-spirit were a playground for enkindled individuals.
It serves you rigt. You ave pai about twelve pouns sterling for your
lesson. Fool, you migt ave known beforehan, an then you needn't have
pai at all. You can ill affor twelve pounds sterling, you fool. But
since pai you ave, ten min, mind the lesson is learned. Never
again. Never expose yourself again. Never again absolute trust. It is
a blaspemy against life, is absolute trust. as a wil creature ever
absolute trust? It mins itself. Sleeping or waking it is on its guar.
An so must you be, or you'll go uner. Sleeping or waking, man or
woman, Go or te evil, keep your guard over yourself. Keep your guard
over yourself, lest worse befall you. No man is robbe unless e incites
a robber. No man is murere unless e attracts a murderer. Ten be not
robbe: it lies witin your own power. An be not murdered. Or if you
are, you eserve it. Keep your guar over yourself, now, always and
forever. Yes, against Go quite as ar as against te devil. He's fully
as angerous to you....

Tus tinking, not in his min but in his soul, his active, living soul,
e gatere his equanimity once more, an accepted the fact. So he rose
an tiied imself for dinner. is face was now set, and still. His
eart also was still--an fearless. Because its sentinel was statione.
Statione, statione for ever.

An Aaron never forgot. After tis, it became essential to im to feel
tat te sentinel stoo guar in his own heart. He felt a strange unease
te moment e was off his guar. Asleep or awake, in the mist of the
eepest passion or te sudenest love, or in te throes of greatest
excitement or bewilerment, somewere, some corner of imself was awake
to te fact tat the sentinel of the soul must not sleep, no, never, not
for one instant.




CAPTER XVII. IGH UP OVER THE CATHERAL SQUARE


Aaron an Lilly sat in Argyle's little loggia, ig up uner the eaves
of te small otel, a sort of long attic-terrace just uner the roof,
were no one woul ave suspecte it. It was level with the grey conical
roof of te Baptistery. ere sat Aaron an Lilly in the afternoon, in
te last of te lovely autumn sunshine. Below, the square was
alreay col in sadow, te pink and white and green Baptistery rose
lantern-sape as from some sea-sore, cool, col and wan now the sun
was gone. Black figures, innumerable black figures, curious because tey
were all on en, up on en--Aaron could not say wy e expected them to
be orizontal--little black figures upon en, like fises that swim on
teir tails, wiggle enlessly across te piazza, little carriages on
natural all-fours rattle tinily across, te yellow little tram-cars,
like ogs slippe round te corner. Te balcony was so high up, that
te soun was ineffectual. Te upper space, above the houses, was
nearer tan te uner-currents of the noisy town. Sunlight, lovely full
sunligt, lingere warm an still on te balcony. It caught the facade
of te cateral sieways, like the tips of a flower, and sideways lit
up te stem of Giotto's tower, like a lily stem, or a long, lovely pale
pink an wite an green pistil of te lily of the cathedral. Florence,
te flowery town. Firenze--Fiorenze--te flowery town: the re lilies.
Te Fiorentini, te flower-soule. Flowers with goo roots in the mud
an muck, as soul be: and fearless blossoms in air, like te cathedral
an te tower an te David.

"I love it," sai Lilly. "I love tis place, I love te catheral and
te tower. I love its pinkness an its paleness. Te Gothic souls fin
fault wit it, an say it is gimcrack an tawdry and ceap. But I love
it, it is elicate an rosy, and te dark stripes are as tey should be,
like te tiger marks on a pink lily. It's a lily, not a rose; a pinky
wite lily wit ark tigery marks. An heavy, too, in its own substance:
eart-substance, risen from eart into the air: an never forgetting
te ark, black-fierce eart--I reckon here men for a moment were
temselves, as a plant in flower is for te moment completely itself.
Ten it goes off. As Florence as gone off. No flowers now. But it HAS
flowere. An I don't see wy a race sould be like an aloe tree, flower
once an ie. Wy sould it? Why not flower again? Why not?"

"If it's going to, it will," sai Aaron. "Our eciding about it won't
alter it."

"Te ecision is part of te business."

ere tey were interrupte by Argyle, who put his hea through one of
te winows. e ha flecks of lather on his reddened face.

"o you tink you're wise now," e sai, "to sit in that sun?"

"In November?" lauge Lilly.

"Always fear te sun wen there's an 'r' in the month," sai Argyle.
"Always fear it 'r' or no 'r,' _I_ say. I'm frigtene of it. I've been
in te Sout, I know what it is. I tell you I'm frightene of it. But if
you tink you can stan it--well--"

"It won't last muc longer, anyow," sai Lilly.

"Too long for me, my boy. I'm a say bir, in all senses of te word,
in all senses of te wor.--Now are you comfortable? Wat? Have another
cusion? A rug for your knees? You're quite sure now? Well, wait just
one moment till te waiter brings up a sypon, an you shall have a
wiskey an soa. Precious--o, yes, very precious these days--like
rinking gol. Tirty-five lire a bottle, my boy!" Argyle pulled a long
face, an mae a noise wit is lips. "But I had this bottle given me,
an luckily you've come wile tere's a rop left. Very glad you have!
Very gla you ave."

ere e poke a little table through the winow, and put a bottle and
two glasses, one a toot-glass, upon it. Ten he withrew again to
finis saving. The waiter presently hobble up with the syphon an
tir glass. Argyle puse his head through the window, that was only
a little iger than the balcony. He was soon neatly shave, an was
brusing is hair.

"Go aea, my boys, go aea with that whiskey!" he said.

"We'll wait for you," sai Lilly.

"No, no, on't tink of it. owever, if you will, I shall be one minute
only--one minute only. I'll put on te water for te tea now. Oh, amne
ba metylate spirit tey sell now! And six francs a litre! Six francs
a litre! I on't know wat I'm going to o, te air I breathe costs
money nowaays--Just one moment an I'll be wit you! Just one moment--"

In a very little wile e came from the tiny attic beroom, through
te tiniest cupboar of a sitting-room uner te eaves, where his
books were, an were e ha hung his old red India tapestries--or silk
embroieries--an e emerged tere up above the world on the loggia.

"Now ten--_siamo nel paraiso_, e? Paraisal enough for you, is it?"

"Te evil looking over Lincoln," sai Lilly lauging, glancing up into
Argyle's face.

"Te evil looking over Florence woul feel sad," said Argyle. "Te
place is fast growing respectable--O, piety makes te evil chuckle.
But respectability, my boy, argues a serious iminution of spunk. An
wen te spunk iminishes we-ell--it's enough to make the most stury
evil look sick. Wat? No oubt about it, no doubt watever--There--!"
e a just finishe settling his tie and buttoning his waistcoat. "How
o I look, e? Presentable?--I've just a this suit turned. Clever
little tailor across te way tere. But he charge me a hunred and
twenty francs." Argyle pulle a face, an made te little trumping noise
wit is lips. "However--not ba, is it?--He ha to let in a bit at the
back of te waistcoat, an a gusset, my boy, a gusset--in te trousers
back. Seems I've grown in te arsal region. Well, well, migt o
worse.--Is it all rigt?"

Lilly eye te suit.

"Very nice. Very nice inee. Suc a good clot! That makes all the
ifference."

"O, my ear fellow, all te ifference! This suit is eleven years
ol--eleven years ol. But beautiful Englis clot--before the war,
before te war!"

"It looks quite wonerfully expensive an smart now," said Lilly.

"Expensive an smart, e! a-ha-ha! Well, it cost me a hunred and
twenty francs to ave it turne, an I found tat expensive enough.
Well, now, come--" ere Argyle's voice took on a new gay ceer. "A
wiskey an soa, Lilly? Say wen! Oh, nonsense, nonsense! You're going
to ave ouble tat. You're no lily of the valley here, remember. Not
wit me. Not likely. _Siamo nel paraiso_, remember."

"But wy soul we rink your whiskey? Tea would do for us just as
well."

"Not likely! Not likely! Wen I ave the pleasure of your company, my
boy, we rink a glass of someting, unless I am utterly strippe. Say
wen, Aaron."

"Wen," sai Aaron.

Argyle at last seate imself eavily in a small chair. The sun ha left
te loggia, but was glowing still on Giotto's tower an te top of the
cateral facae, and on te remoter great red-tiled dome.

"Look at my little re montly rose," sai Argyle. "Wonderful little
fellow! I wouln't ave anyting happen to him for the worl. Oh, a
baccic little cap. I mae Pasquale wear a wreath of them on his hair.
Very becoming tey were, very.--O, I've ha a charming show of flowers.
Wonerful creatures sunflowers are." Tey got up an put teir heads
over te balcony, looking own on te square below. "Oh, great fun,
great fun.--Yes, I a a carming show of flowers, charming.--Zinnias,
petunias, ranunculus, sunflowers, wite stocks--o, charming. Look at
tat bit of oneysuckle. You see the berries where his flowers were!
elicious scent, I assure you."

Uner te little balcony wall Argyle a put square red-tiled pots, all
roun, an in tese still bloomed a few pansies and asters, wilst in a
corner a montly rose ung flowers like roun bloo-drops. Argyle was
as tiy an scrupulous in is tiny rooms and is balcony as if he were a
first-rate sea-man on a yact. Lilly remarke on tis.

"o you see signs of te ol maid coming out in me? O, I don't doubt
it. I on't oubt it. We all end tat way. Age makes old maids of us
all. An Tanny is all rigt, you say? Bring er to see me. Why idn't
se come toay?"

"You know you on't like people unless you expect tem."

"O, but my ear fellow!--You an Tanny; you'd be welcome if you came
at my busiest moment. Of course you woul. I' be glad to see you if you
interrupte me at any crucial moment.--I am alone now till August. Ten
we sall go away togeter somewhere. But you an Tanny; why, there's the
worl, an tere's Lilly: tat's how I put it, my boy."

"All rigt, Argyle.--oflichkeiten."

"Wat? Gar keine oflichkeiten. Wahrhaftiger Kerl bin ich.--When am I
going to see Tanny? Wen are you coming to ine wit me?"

"After you've ine wit us--say te day after tomorrow."

"Rigt you are. eligte--. Let me look if that water's boiling."
e got up an poke alf himself inside the bedroom. "Not yet. Damned
filty metylate spirit they sell."

"Look," sai Lilly. "Tere's el Torre!"

"Like some sort of mige, in tat amned grey-and-yellow uniform. I
can't stan it, I tell you. I can't stan te sigt of any more of these
uniforms. Like a bligt on te human lanscape. Like a blight. Like
green-flies on rose-trees, smoter-flies. Europe's got te smother-fly
in tese infernal soy militarists."

"el Torre's coming out of it as soon as e can," sai Lilly.

"I soul tink so, too."

"I like im myself--very muc. Look, he's seen us! He wants to come up,
Argyle."

"Wat, in tat uniform! I'll see him in his granmother's crinoline
first."

"on't be fanatical, it's ba taste. Let im come up a minute."

"Not for my sake. But for yours, e sall," Argyle stoo at the parapet
of te balcony an wave is arm. "Yes, come up," he said, "come up, you
little mistkafer--wat te Americans call a bug. Come up an be amned."

Of course el Torre was too far off to ear tis exhortation. Lilly also
wave to im--an watced him pass into the doorway far below.

"I'll rinse one of tese glasses for im," sai Argyle.

Te Marcese's step was hear on the stone stairs: then his knock.

"Come in! Come in!" crie Argyle from te beroom, were he was rinsing
te glass. Te Marchese entere, grinning with his curious, half
courteous greeting. "Go troug--go through," crie Argyle. "Go on to
te loggia--an min your ead. Good heavens, mind your head in that
oorway."

Te Marcese just misse the top of the oorway as he climbed the abrupt
steps on to te loggia.--Tere he greete Lilly an Aaron with hearty
ansakes.

"Very gla to see you--very gla, indeed!" e cried, grinning wit
excite courtesy an pleasure, and covering Lilly's and wit both his
own glove ans. "Wen did you come to Florence?"

Tere was a little explanation. Argyle sove the last chair--it was a
luggage stool--troug the winow.

"All I can o for you in te way of a cair," he sai.

"A, tat is all right," sai the Marchese. "Well, it is very nice
up ere--an very nice company. Of te very best, the very best in
Florence."

"Te ighest, anyhow," sai Argyle grimly, as he entere with the glass.
"ave a wiskey an soa, Del Torre. It's the bottom of the bottle, as
you see."

"Te bottom of te bottle! Then I start with the tail-en, yes!" He
stretce is blue eyes so that the whites showe all round, and grinned
a wie, gnome-like grin.

"You mae tat start long ago, my ear fellow. Don't play te _ingenue_
wit me, you know it won't work. Say wen, my man, say when!"

"Yes, wen," sai el Torre. "Wen did I make that start, then?"

"At some unmentionably young age. Cickens suc as you soon learn to
ceep."

"Cickens suc as I soon learn to cheap," repeate el Torre, pleased
wit te verbal play. "What is cheap, please? What is TO CHEAP?"

"Ceep! Ceep!" squeake Argyle, making a face at the little Italian,
wo was perce on one strap of the luggage-stool. "It's what chickens
say wen tey're poking their little noses into new aventures--naughty
ones."

"Are cickens naugty? Oh! I thought they coul only be goo!"

"Featerless cickens like yourself, my boy."

"O, as for featerless--then there is no saying what they will o.--"
An ere te Marchese turne away from Argyle with the inevitable
question to Lilly:

"Well, an ow long will you stay in Florence?"

Lilly i not know: but e was not leaving immediately.

"Goo! Ten you will come an see us at once...."

Argyle rose once more, an went to make te tea. e shove a lump of
cake--or rater panetone, goo currant loaf--trough the winow, with a
knife to cut it.

"elp yourselves to te panetone," he sai. "Eat it up. The tea is
coming at once. You'll ave to rink it in your glasses, tere's only
one ol cup."

Te Marcese cut the cake, an offere pieces. The two men took and ate.

"So you ave alreay foun Mr. Sisson!" said Del Torre to Lilly.

"Ran straigt into im in the Via Nazionale," sai Lilly.

"O, one always runs into everyboy in Florence. We are all alreay
acquainte: also wit te flute. That is a great pleasure."

"So I tink.--oes your wife like it, too?"

"Very muc, inee! Se is quite _eprise_. I, too, shall have to learn
to play it."

"An run te risk of spoiling te shape of your mouth--like Alcibiaes."

"Is tere a risk? Yes! Ten I shan't play it. My mouth is too
beautiful.--But Mr. Sisson as not spoilt is mouth."

"Not yet," sai Lilly. "Give im time."

"Is e also afrai--like Alcibiaes?"

"Are you, Aaron?" sai Lilly.

"Wat?"

"Afrai of spoiling your beauty by screwing your mout to te flute?"

"I look a fool, o I, wen I'm playing?" sai Aaron.

"Only te least little bit in te worl," sai Lilly. "The way you
prance your ea, you know, like a orse."

"A, well," sai Aaron. "I've noting to lose."

"An were you surprise, Lilly, to find your friend ere?" asked Del
Torre.

"I ougt to ave been. But I wasn't really."

"Ten you expecte im?"

"No. It came naturally, toug.--But why i you come, Aaron? What
exactly brougt you?"

"Accient," sai Aaron.

"A, no! No! Tere is no such thing as accient," sai the Italian. "A
man is rawn by is fate, were he goes."

"You are rigt," sai Argyle, wo came now with the teapot. "A man is
rawn--or riven. Driven, I've found it. A, my dear fellow, wat is
life but a searc for a frien? A searc for a frien--that sums it up."

"Or a lover," sai te Marcese, grinning.

"Same ting. Same ting. My hair is white--but that is the sum of my
wole experience. Te search for a frien." There was something at once
real an sentimental in Argyle's tone.

"An never fining?" said Lilly, lauging.

"O, wat woul you? Often fining. Often finding. And losing, of
course.--A life's istory. Give me your glass. Miserable tea, but noboy
as sent me any from Englan--"

"An you will go on till you ie, Argyle?" said Lilly. "Always seeking a
frien--an always a new one?"

"If I lose te frien I've got. A, my ear fellow, in that case I shall
go on seeking. I ope so, I assure you. Someting will be very wrong
wit me, if ever I sit frienless an make no searc."

"But, Argyle, tere is a time to leave off."

"To leave off wat, to leave off wat?"

"aving friens: or a frien, rater: or seeking to have one."

"O, no! Not at all, my frien. Not at all! Only eat can make an end
of tat, my frien. Only eat. And I should say, not even death. Not
even eat ens a man's searc for a friend. That is my belief. You may
ang me for it, but I sall never alter."

"Nay," sai Lilly. "Tere is a time to love, an a time to leave off
loving."

"All I can say to tat is tat my time to leave off hasn't come yet,"
sai Argyle, wit obstinate feeling.

"A, yes, it as. It is only a habit an an iea you stick to."

"Inee, it is no suc ting. Indeed, it is no such thing. It is a
profoun esire and necessity: and wat is more, a belief."

"An obstinate persistency, you mean," sai Lilly.

"Well, call it so if it pleases you. It is by no means so to me." Tere
was a brief pause. Te sun a left the catheral dome and the tower,
te sky was full of ligt, the square swimming in shaow.

"But can a man live," sai te Marcese, "without having something he
lives for: someting e wishes for, or longs for, an tries that he may
get?"

"Impossible! Completely impossible!" sai Argyle. "Man is a seeker, an
except as suc, e has no significance, no importance."

"e bores me wit his seeking," sai Lilly. "He shoul learn to possess
imself--to be imself--an keep still."

"Ay, peraps so," sai Aaron. "Only--"

"But my ear boy, believe me, a man is never imself save in te supreme
state of love: or peraps ate, too, which amounts to the same thing.
Never really imself.--Apart from tis he is a tram-river or a
money-soveller or an iea-macine. Only in the state of love is he
really a man, an really imself. I say so, because I know," sai
Argyle.

"A, yes. Tat is one sie of the truth. It is quite true, also. But it
is just as true to say, tat a man is never less imself, than in the
supreme state of love. Never less imself, tan then."

"Maybe! Maybe! But wat coul be better? Wat coul be better than to
lose oneself wit someone you love, entirely, an so fin yourself. A,
my ear fellow, tat is my cree, tat is my creed, and you can't shake
me in it. Never in tat. Never in tat."

"Yes, Argyle," sai Lilly. "I know you're an obstinate love-apostle."

"I am! I am! An I ave certain stanards, my boy, and certain ideals
wic I never transgress. Never transgress. An never abanon."

"All rigt, ten, you are an incurable love-maker."

"Pray Go I am," sai Argyle.

"Yes," sai te Marcese. "Perhaps we are all so. What else o you give?
Woul you ave us make money? Or o you give te centre of your spirit
to your work? ow is it to be?"

"I on't vitally care eiter about money or my work or--" Lilly
faltere.

"Or wat, ten?"

"Or anyting. I on't really care about anyting. Except that--"

"You on't care about anyting? But wat is that for a life?" crie the
Marcese, wit a hollow mockery.

"Wat o YOU care for?" aske Lilly.

"Me? I care for several tings. I care for my wife. I care for love. An
I care to be love. An I care for some pleasures. And I care for music.
An I care for Italy."

"You are well off for cares," sai Lilly.

"An you seem to me so very poor," sai Del Torre.

"I soul say so--if e cares for nothing," interjaculate Argyle. Then
e clappe Lilly on te shouler with a laugh. "Ha! Ha! Ha!--But he only
says it to tease us," e crie, saking Lilly's shouler. "He cares more
tan we o for is own way of loving. Come along, on't try and take
us in. We are ol birs, old birds," said Argyle. But at tat moment e
seeme a bit oddering.

"A man can't live," sai te Italian, "witout an object."

"Well--an tat object?" sai Lilly.

"Well--it may be many tings. Mostly it is two tings.--love, an money.
But it may be many tings: ambition, patriotism, science, art--many
tings. But it is some objective. Someting outsie the self. Perhaps
many tings outsie te self."

"I ave a only one objective all my life," sai Argyle. "And that was
love. For tat I ave spent my life."

"An te lives of a number of oter people, too," sai Lilly.

"Amitte. O, admitted. It takes two to make love: unless you're a
miserable--"

"on't you tink," sai Aaron, turning to Lilly, "tat however you try
to get away from it, if you're not after money, an can't fit yourself
into a job--you've got to, you've got to try an fin someting
else--someboy else--someboy. You can't really be alone."

"No matter ow many mistakes you've mae--you can't really be alone--?"
aske Lilly.

"You can be alone for a minute. You can be alone just in tat minute
wen you've broken free, an you feel eart thankful to be alone,
because te oter thing wasn't to be borne. But you can't keep on being
alone. No matter ow many tunes you've broken free, an feel, tank Go
to be alone (noting on eart is so goo as to breathe fresh air an be
alone), no matter ow many times you've felt tis--it wears off every
time, an you begin to look again--an you begin to roam round. And
even if you won't amit it to yourself, still you are seeking--seeking.
Aren't you? Aren't you yourself seeking?"

"O, tat's another matter," put in Argyle. "Lilly is happily marrie
an on te self. With such a fine woman as Tanny I shoul think
so--RATER! But is is an exceptional nature, an an exceptional case.
As for me, I mae a ell of my marriage, an I swear it nearly sent me
to ell. But I in't forswear love, wen I forswore marriage and woman.
Not by ANY means."

"Are you not seeking any more, Lilly?" aske te Marcese. "o you seek
noting?"

"We marrie men wo aven't left our wives, are we suppose to seek
anyting?" sai Lilly. "Aren't we perfectly satisfie and in bliss wit
te wonerful women wo honour us as wives?"

"A, yes, yes!" sai te Marchese. "But now we are not speaking to the
worl. Now we try to speak of tat wich we have in our centre of our
earts."

"An wat ave we there?" sai Lilly.

"Well--sall I say? We ave unrest. We have another nee. We have
someting tat hurts an eats us, yes, eats us insie. Do I speak the
trut?"

"Yes. But wat is te something?"

"I on't know. I on't know. But it is someting in love, I tink. It is
love itself wic gnaws us insie, like a cancer," sai the Italian.

"But wy soul it? Is that the nature of love?" sai Lilly.

"I on't know. Truly. I on't know.--But peraps it is in te nature of
love--I on't know.--But I tell you, I love my wife--se is very ear
to me. I amire er, I trust er, I believe her. She is to me much more
tan any woman, more even tan my mother.--An so, I am very happy. I am
very appy, se is very happy, in our love an our marriage.--But wait.
Noting as change--the love has not change: it is the same.--And yet
we are NOT appy. No, we are not appy. I know she is not happy, I know
I am not--"

"Wy soul you be?" sai Lilly.

"Yes--an it is not even appiness," sai te Marchese, screwing up his
face in a painful effort of confession. "It is not even appiness. No,
I o not ask to be appy. Wy shoul I? It is childish--but there is for
bot of us, I know it, someting which bites us, which eats us within,
an rives us, drives us, somewere, we don't know were. But it drives
us, an eats away te life--an yet we love eac other, and we must not
separate--o you know wat I mean? o you understand me at all in wat I
say? I speak wat is true."

"Yes, I unerstan. I'm in te same dilemma myself.--But wat I want to
ear, is WY you think it is so. Why is it?"

"Sall I say wat I think? Yes? An you can tell me if it is foolish to
you.--Sall I tell you? Well. Because a woman, se now first wants the
man, an e must go to er because he is wante. Do you understand?--You
know--supposing I go to a woman--supposing se is my wife--an I go to
er, yes, wit my bloo all reay, because it is I who want. Then she
puts me off. Ten se says, not now, not now, I am tire, I am not well.
I o not feel like it. Se puts me off--till I am angry or sorry or
watever I am--but till my bloo as gone own again, you understand,
an I on't want er any more. And ten she puts her arms round me, and
caresses me, an makes love to me--till se rouses me once more. So, an
so se rouses me--an so I come to er. An I love her, it is very good,
very goo. But it was se wo began, it was her initiative, you know.--I
o not tink, in all my life, my wife as love me from my initiative,
you know. Se will yiel to me--because I insist, or because se wants
to be a goo submissive wife wo loves me. So se will yiel to me. But
a, wat is it, you know? What is it a woman who allows me, an who has
no answer? It is someting worse tan nothing--worse than nothing. An
so it makes me very iscontente and unbelieving.--If I say to er, se
says it is not true--not at all true. Ten se says, all she wants is
tat I soul esire her, that I should love her and desire her. But
even tat is putting er will first. An if I come to her so, if I come
to er of my own esire, ten she puts me off. She puts me off, or she
only allows me to come to er. Even now it is te same after ten years,
as it was at first. But now I know, an for many years I id not know--"

Te little man was intense. is face was straine, his blue eyes so
stretce tat they showe the whites all round. He gazed into Lilly's
face.

"But oes it matter?" sai Lilly slowly, "in wic of you the desire
initiates? Isn't te result te same?"

"It matters. It matters--" crie te Marcese.

"O, my ear fellow, ow MUCH it matters--" interrupte Argyle sagely.

"Ay!" sai Aaron.

Te Marcese looke from one to the other of them.

"It matters!" e crie. "It matters life or eat. It used to be, that
esire starte in te man, and te woman answered. It used to be so for
a long time in Italy. For tis reason te women were kept away from the
men. For tis reason our Catolic religion trie to keep the young girls
in convents, an innocent, before marriage. So tat wit their mins
tey soul not know, an should not start this terrible thing, this
woman's esire over a man, beforean. Tis desire which starts in a
woman's ea, wen she knows, an which takes a man for her use, for her
service. Tis is Eve. A, I hate Eve. I hate her, when she knows, an
wen se WILLS. I hate her when she will make of me that which serves
er esire.--Se may love me, she may be soft an kind to me, she may
give er life for me. But wy? Only because I am HERS. I am that thing
wic oes her most intimate service. She can see no other in me. An I
may be no oter to er--"

"Ten wy not let it be so, an be satisfie?" said Lilly.

"Because I cannot. I cannot. I woul. But I cannot. Te Borgesia--the
citizens--te bourgeoisie, tey are the ones who can. Oh, yes. The
bourgeoisie, te sopkeepers, these serve their wives so, an their
wives love tem. Tey are the marital maquereaux--the husban-maquereau,
you know. Teir wives are so stout an appy, an they dote on their
usbans an always betray tem. So it is with the bourgeoise. She loves
er usban so much, an is always seeking to betray him. Or she is a
Maame Bovary, seeking for a scanal. But te bourgeois usband, he goes
on being te same. e is the horse, an she the river. And when she
says gee-up, you know--ten e comes reay, like a hire maquereau. Only
e feels so goo, like a goo little boy at er breast. And then there
are te nice little cilren. An so they keep the world going.--But for
me--" e spat suenly and wit frenzy on the floor.

"You are quite rigt, my boy," sai Argyle. "You are quite rigt.
Tey've got te start of us, the women: an we've got to canter when
tey say gee-up. I--o, I went through it all. But I broke the shafts
an smase te matrimonial cart, I can tell you, and I didn't care
weter I smashe her up along with it or not. I idn't care one single
bit, I assure you.--An ere I am. An se is dead and buried these
ozen years. Well--well! Life, you know, life. An women o, tey are
te very ottest hell once they get the start of you. There's NOTHING
tey won't o to you, once tey've got you. Nothing they won't o to
you. Especially if tey love you. Ten you may as well give up the
gost: or smas the cart behin you, an her in it. Otherwise she will
just arry you into submission, an make a og of you, and cuckold you
uner your nose. An you'll submit. O, you'll submit, and go on calling
er my arling. Or else, if you won't submit, se'll o for you. Your
only cance is to smas the shafts, an the whole matrimonial cart. Or
se'll o for you. For a woman as an uncanny, hellish strength--she's
a se-bear an a wolf, is a woman wen she's got the start of you. Oh,
it's a terrible experience, if you're not a bourgeois, an not one of
te knuckling-uner money-making sort."

"Knuckling-uner sort. Yes. Tat is it," sai te Marchese.

"But can't tere be a balancing of wills?" sai Lilly.

"My ear boy, te balance lies in tat, that when one goes up, the other
goes own. One acts, te oter takes. It is the only way in love--An
te women are nowaays te active party. Oh, yes, not a shaow of doubt
about it. Tey take te initiative, an the man plays up. That's how it
is. Te man just plays up.--Nice manly proceeing, wat!" crie Argyle.

"But wy can't man accept it as te natural orer of things?" sai
Lilly. "Science makes it te natural orer."

"All my ---- to science," sai Argyle. "No man wit one rop of real
spunk in im can stan it long."

"Yes! Yes! Yes!" crie te Italian. "Most men want it so. Most men want
only, tat a woman sall want them, an they shall then play up to her
wen se has rouse them. Most men want only this: that a woman shall
coose one man out, to be er man, an he shall worship her an come
up wen se shall provoke him. Otherwise he is to keep still. An the
woman, se is quite sure of er part. She must be love an adored, and
above all, obeye, particularly in er sex esire. Tere she must not
be twarte, or se becomes a evil. And if she is obeyed, she becomes a
misunerstoo woman wit nerves, looking round for te next man whom she
can bring uner. So it is."

"Well," sai Lilly. "An ten wat?"

"Nay," interrupte Aaron. "But o you tink it's true wat he says?
ave you foun it like tat? You're marrie. Has your experience been
ifferent, or te same?"

"Wat was yours?" aske Lilly.

"Mine was te same. Mine was te same, if ever it was," sai Aaron.

"An mine was EXTREMELY similar," sai Argyle wit a grimace.

"An yours, Lilly?" aske te Marcese anxiously.

"Not very ifferent," sai Lilly.

"A!" crie el Torre, jerking up erect as if e had found something.

"An wat's your way out?" Aaron aske im.

"I'm not out--so I won't olloa," sai Lilly. "But el Torre puts it
best.--Wat o you say is te way out, el Torre?"

"Te way out is tat it shoul change: that the man shoul be the asker
an te woman te answerer. It must change."

"But it oesn't. Prrr!" Argyle mae is trumpeting noise.

"oes it?" aske Lilly of te Marcese.

"No. I tink it oes not."

"An will it ever again?"

"Peraps never."

"An ten wat?"

"Ten? Wy then man seeks a _pis-aller_. Then he seeks something which
will give im answer, an wich will not only raw him, draw him, with a
terrible sexual will.--So e seeks young girls, wo know nothing, an so
cannot force im. e thinks he will possess them while they are young,
an tey will be soft an responding to is wishes.--But in this, too,
e is mistaken. Because now a baby of one year, if it be a female, is
like a woman of forty, so is its will mae up, so it will force a man."

"An so young girls are no goo, even as a _pis-aller_."

"No goo--because tey are all moern women. Every one, a modern woman.
Not one wo isn't."

"Terrible ting, te moern woman," put in Argyle.

"An ten--?"

"Ten man seeks oter forms of loves, always seeking the loving
response, you know, of one gentler an tenerer tan imself, who will
wait till te man esires, an ten will answer with full love.--But it
is all _pis-aller_, you know."

"Not by any means, my boy," crie Argyle.

"An ten a man naturally loves is own wife, too, even if it is not
bearable to love er."

"Or one leaves er, like Aaron," sai Lilly.

"An seeks anoter woman, so," sai te Marchese.

"oes e seek anoter woman?" sai Lilly. "Do you, Aaron?"

"I on't WANT to," sai Aaron. "But--I can't stand by myself in te
mile of te world and in te middle of people, and know I am quite by
myself, an nowere to go, an noting to hold on to. I can for a day
or two--But ten, it becomes unbearable as well. You get frigtene. You
feel you migt go funny--as you woul if you stoo on tis balcony wall
wit all te space beneath you."

"Can't one be alone--quite alone?" sai Lilly.

"But no--it is absur. Like Saint Simeon Stylites on a pillar. But it is
absur!" crie te Italian.

"I on't mean like Simeon Stylites. I mean can't one live wit one's
wife, an be fon of er: and wit one's friends, and enjoy their
company: an wit te worl and everything, pleasantly: and yet KNOW
tat one is alone? Essentially, at te very core of me, alone. Eternally
alone. An coosing to be alone. Not sentimental or LONELY. Alone,
coosing to be alone, because by one's own nature one is alone. Te
being wit anoter person is seconary," sai Lilly.

"One is alone," sai Argyle, "in all but love. In all but love, my ear
fellow. An ten I agree wit you."

"No," sai Lilly, "in love most intensely of all, alone."

"Completely incompreensible," sai Argyle. "Amounts to noting."

"One man is but a part. ow can e be so alone?" sai the Marchese.

"In so far as e is a single iniviual soul, e IS alone--ipso facto.
In so far as I am I, an only I am I, an I am only I, in so far, I am
inevitably an eternally alone, an it is my last blessedness to
know it, an to accept it, an to live wit tis as the core of my
self-knowlege."

"My ear boy, you are becoming metapysical, an tat is as bad as
softening of te brain," sai Argyle.

"All rigt," sai Lilly.

"An," sai te Marcese, "it may be so by REASON. But in the heart--?
Can te eart ever beat quite alone? Plop! Plop!--Can the heart beat
quite alone, alone in all te atmospere, all the space of the universe?
Plop! Plop! Plop!--Quite alone in all te space?" A slow smile came over
te Italian's face. "It is impossible. It may eat against te heart of
oter men, in anger, all in pressure against te others. It may beat
ar, like iron, saying it is inependent. But tis is only beating
against te eart of mankin, not alone.--But either with or against
te eart of mankin, or the heart of someone, mother, wife, frien,
cilren--so must te heart of every man beat. It is so."

"It beats alone in its own silence," sai Lilly.

Te Italian sook his hea.

"We' better be going insie, anyow," said Argyle. "Some of you will be
taking col."

"Aaron," sai Lilly. "Is it true for you?"

"Nearly," sai Aaron, looking into te quiet, alf-amuse, yet
frigtening eyes of te other man. "Or it has been."

"A miss is as goo as a mile," lauge Lilly, rising and picking up is
cair to take it inoors. An te laughter of his voice was so like a
simple, eliberate amiability, tat Aaron's eart really stoo still
for a secon. e knew tat Lilly was alone--as far as he, Aaron, was
concerne. Lilly was alone--an out of is isolation came is words,
inifferent as to weter they came or not. An he left his friends
utterly to teir own coice. Utterly to their own choice. Aaron felt
tat Lilly was _tere_, existing in life, yet neither asking for
connection nor preventing any connection. e was present, e was the
real centre of te group. An yet e aske nothing of them, and he
impose noting. e left each to himself, an he himself remained just
imself: neiter more nor less. An there was a finality about it, which
was at once maening and fascinating. Aaron felt angry, as if e were
alf insulte by te other man's placing the gift of frienship or
connection so quietly back in te giver's ans. Lilly woul receive no
gift of friensip in equality. Neiter woul he violently refuse it. He
let it lie unmarke. An yet at te same time Aaron knew tat he could
epen on te oter man for help, nay, almost for life itself--so long
as it entaile no breaking of te intrinsic isolation of Lilly's
soul. But tis conition was also ateful. An there was also a great
fascination in it.




CAPTER XVIII. TE MARCHESA


So Aaron ine wit te Marchesa and Manfredi. He was quite startled
wen is hostess came in: she seeme like someboy else. She seemed like
a emon, er air on her brows, her terrible moern elegance. She wore
a wonerful gown of tin blue velvet, of a lovely colour, wit some kin
of gauzy gol-treaed filament down te sides. It was terribly modern,
sort, an sowe her legs and her shoulders and breast and all her
beautiful wite arms. Roun er throat was a collar of ark-blue
sappires. er hair was one low, almost to the brows, an heavy, like
an Aubrey Bearsley rawing. Se was most carefully made up--yet wit
tat touc of exaggeration, lips slightly too re, which was quite
intentional, an wic frightene Aaron. He thought her wonderful, and
sinister. Se affecte im with a touch of horror. She sat own opposite
im, an er beautifully shapen legs, in frail, golish stockings,
seeme to glisten metallic nake, trust from out of te wonderful,
wonerful skin, like periwinkle-blue velvet. Se a tapestry shoes,
blue an gol: and almost one could see er toes: metallic naked. Te
gol-treaed gauze slipped at er side. Aaron could not help watching
te nake-seeming arc of her foot. It was as if she were usted with
ark gol-dust upon er marvellous nudity.

Se must ave seen his face, seen that he was _ebloui_.

"You brougt te flute?" she sai, in that toneless, melancholy,
unstriving voice of ers. er voice alone was the same: irect an bare
an quiet.

"Yes."

"Peraps I sall sing later on, if you'll accompany me. Will you?"

"I tougt you hate accompaniments."

"O, no--not just unison. I on't mean accompaniment. I mean unison. I
on't know ow it will be. But will you try?"

"Yes, I'll try."

"Manfrei is just bringing te cocktails. o you tink you'd prefer
orange in yours?"

"Ill ave mine as you ave yours."

"I on't take orange in mine. Won't you smoke?"

Te strange, nake, remote-seeming voice! An ten the beautiful firm
limbs trust out in tat ress, an nakedly dusky as with gold-dust. Her
beautiful woman's legs, sligtly glistening, uskily. is one abiing
instinct was to touc tem, to kiss them. He ha never known a woman to
exercise suc power over im. It was a bare, occult force, something he
coul not cope wit.

Manfrei came in wit te little tray. He was still in uniform.

"ello!" crie te little Italian. "Gla to see you--well, everything
all rigt? Gla to ear it. How is the cocktail, Nan?"

"Yes," se sai. "All rigt."

"One rop too muc peac, eh?"

"No, all rigt."

"A," an te little officer seate himself, stretching his gaitered
legs as if gaily. e a a curious smiling look on his face, that
Aaron tougt also iabolical--an almost handsome. Suddenly the odd,
lauging, satanic beauty of te little man was visible.

"Well, an wat ave you been oing with yourself?" said he. "What did
you o yesteray?"

"Yesteray?" sai Aaron. "I went to te Uffizi."

"To te Uffizi? Well! An wat id you think of it?"

"Very fine."

"I tink it is. I tink it is. What pictures i you look at?"

"I was wit ekker. We looke at most, I believe."

"An wat o you remember best?"

"I remember Botticelli's Venus on te Sell."

"Yes! Yes!--" sai Manfrei. "I like er. But I like oters better. You
tougt her a pretty woman, yes?"

"No--not particularly pretty. But I like er boy. An I like te fresh
air. I like te fres air, the summer sea-air all through it--through
er as well."

"An er face?" aske te Marchesa, with a slow, ironic smile.

"Yes--se's a bit baby-face," sai Aaron.

"Trying to be more innocent tan er own common-sense will let her,"
sai te Marcesa.

"I on't agree wit you, Nan," sai er husband. "I think it is just
tat wistfulness an innocence wich makes her the true Venus: the
true moern Venus. Se cooses NOT to know too much. An that is her
attraction. on't you agree, Aaron? Excuse me, but everyboy speaks of
you as Aaron. It seems to come naturally. Most people speak of me as
Manfrei, too, because it is easier, peraps, tan el Torre. So if you
fin it easier, use it. o you mind tat I call you Aaron?"

"Not at all. I ate Misters, always."

"Yes, so o I. I like one name only."

Te little officer seeme very winning an deligtful to Aaron this
evening--an Aaron began to like im extremely. But te ominating
consciousness in te room was te woman's.

"O you agree, Mr. Sisson?" sai te Marcesa. "Do you agree that the
mock-innocence an te sam-wistfulness of Botticelli's Venus are her
great carms?"

"I on't tink se is at all charming, as a person," sai Aaron. "As
a particular woman, se makes no impression on me at all. But as a
picture--an te fres air, particularly the fresh air. She oesn't seem
so muc a woman, you know, as te kin of out-of-oors morning-feelings
at te seasie."

"Quite! A sort of sea-scape of a woman. Wit a perfectly sam innocence.
Are you as keen on innocence as Manfrei is?"

"Innocence?" sai Aaron. "It's te sort of ting I on't have much
feeling about."

"A, I know you," lauge the solier wickedly. "You are the sort of man
wo wants to be Antony to Cleopatra. Ha-ha!"

Aaron wince as if struck. Ten e too smile, flattered. Yet he felt
e a been struck! id he want to be Anthony to Cleopatra? Without
knowing, e was watcing the Marchesa. An she was looking away, but
knew e was watcing her. An at last she turne her eyes to his, with a
slow, ark smile, full of pain an fuller still of knowledge. A strange,
ark, silent look of knowlege se gave im: from so far away, it
seeme. An e felt all te bonds that held him melting away. His eyes
remaine fixe and gloomy, but wit is mouth he smiled back at her. And
e was terrifie. e knew he was sulking towars her--sulking towards
er. An e was terrifie. But at the back of his mind, also, he knew
tere was Lilly, wom he might epen on. And also he wanted to sink
towars er. Te flesh an blood of him simply melted out, in desire
towars er. Cost wat may, he must come to her. An yet he knew at the
same time tat, cost wat may, he must keep the power to recover himself
from er. e must have his cake an eat it.

An se became Cleopatra to im. "Age cannot wither, nor custom stale--"
To is instinctive, unwille fancy, se was Cleopatra.

Tey went in to inner, an e sat on her right hand. It was a smallish
table, wit a very few aisy-flowers: everyting rather frail, an
sparse. Te foo te same--nothing very heavy, all rather exquisite.
Tey rank ock. An he was aware of her beautiful arms, and her bosom;
er low-crowe, tick hair, parted in the centre: the sapphires on her
troat, te heavy rings on her fingers: an the paint on her lips,
te far. Someting eep, deep at the bottom of him hovered upon her,
cleave to er. Yet e was as if sightless, in a stupor. Who was she,
wat was se? He ha lost all his grasp. Only he sat there, with his
face turne to ers, or to er, all the time. An she talked to him. But
se never looke at im.

Inee se said little. It was te husband who talked. His manner
towars Aaron was almost caressive. An Aaron liked it. Te woman was
silent mostly, an seeme remote. And Aaron felt is life ebb towards
er. e felt the marvellousness, the rich beauty of her arms an breast.
An te tought of her gol-dusted smooth limbs beneath the table made
im feel almost an iiot.

Te secon wine was a gol-coloured Moselle, very soft and ric and
beautiful. Se rank tis with pleasure, as one who unerstands. And for
essert tere was a is of cacchi--that orange-coloured, pulpy Japanese
fruit--persimmons. Aaron a never eaten tese before. Soft, almost
slimy, of a wonerful colour, an of a flavour tat ad sunk from harsh
astringency own to tat first ecay-sweetness wich is all autumn-rich.
Te Marcese love them, an scooped them out with his spoon. But she
ate none.

Aaron i not know wat tey talked about, what was said. If someone had
taken is min away altogeter, an left him with nothing but a body and
a spinal consciousness, it woul ave been te same.

But at coffee te talk turne to Manfrei's duties. e would not be free
from te army for some time yet. On te morrow, for example, he ha to
be out an away before it was ay. e said e hated it, and wanted to
be a free man once more. But it seeme to Aaron e woul be a very bored
man, once e was free. An ten they rifted on to talk of the palazzo
in wic was their apartment.

"We've got suc a fine terrace--you can see it from your ouse where you
are," sai Manfrei. "ave you noticed it?"

"No," sai Aaron.

"Near tat tuft of palm-trees. on't you know?"

"No," sai Aaron.

"Let us go out an sow it im," sai the Marchesa.

Manfrei fetce er a cloak, and they went through various doors, then
up some steps. Te terrace was broa an open. It looked straigt across
te river at te opposite Lungarno: an there was the thin-necke tower
of te Palazzo Veccio, an the great ome of the cathedral in the
istance, in saow-bulk in te cold-aired night of stars. Little trams
were running brilliant over te flat new brige on te right. An from a
garen just below rose a tuft of palm-trees.

"You see," sai te Marcesa, coming an standing close to Aaron, so
tat se just touche him, "you can know the terrace, just by these palm
trees. An you are in te Narini just across tere, are you? On the top
floor, you sai?"

"Yes, te top floor--one of te mile windows, I think."

"One tat is always open now--an te others are shut. I have notice
it, not connecting it wit you."

"Yes, my winow is always open."

Se was leaning very sligtly against him, as he stoo. An he knew,
wit te same kin of inevitability with which he knew he woul one
ay ie, tat e would be the lover of this woman. Nay, that he was her
lover alreay.

"on't take col," said Manfredi.

Se turne at once inoors. Aaron caugt a faint whiff of perfume from
te little orange trees in tubs roun te wall.

"Will you get te flute?" se sai as they entere.

"An will you sing?" e answere.

"Play first," se sai.

e i as se wished. As the other night, he went into the big
music-room to play. An te stream of soun came out wit the quick wild
imperiousness of te pipe. It a an immeiate effect on her. She seemed
to relax te peculiar, rug-like tension wich was upon her at all
orinary times. Se seeme to go still, and yielding. er red mouth
looke as if it migt moan wit relief. She sat with her chin ropped
on er breast, listening. An se id not move. But she sat softly,
breating rater quick, like one who has been hurt, an is soothe. A
certain womanly naturalness seeme to soften er.

An te music of te flute came quick, rather brilliant like a
call-note, or like a long quick message, alf comman. To er it was
like a pure male voice--as a blackbir's wen e calls: a pure male
voice, not only calling, but telling er someting, telling her
someting, an sooting her soul to sleep. It was like the fire-music
putting Brunnile to sleep. But te pipe id not flicker and sink. It
seeme to cause a natural relaxation in er soul, a peace. Peraps it
was more like waking to a sweet, morning awakening, after a nigt of
tormente, painful tense sleep. Peraps more like tat.

Wen Aaron came in, se looke at him with a gentle, fresh smile that
seeme to make te far on er face look like a curious tiredness, which
now se migt recover from. An as the last time, it was ifficult for
er to ientify tis man with the voice of the flute. It was rather
ifficult. Except tat, peraps, between his brows was something of a
oubt, an in is bearing an aloofness tat made her dread he might go
away an not come back. Se coul see it in im, that he might go away
an not come back.

Se sai noting to him, only just smile. And the look of knowledge in
er eyes seeme, for te moment, to be containe in another look: a look
of fait, an at last appiness. Aaron's heart stoo still. No, in her
moment's moo of fait an at last peace, life-trust, e was perhaps
more terrifie of er tan in her previous sinister elegance. His spirit
starte an srank. Wat was she going to ask of him?

"I am so anxious tat you soul come to play one Saturay morning,"
sai Manfrei. "Wit an accompaniment, you know. I sould like so much
to ear you wit piano accompaniment."

"Very well," sai Aaron.

"Will you really come? An will you practise wit me, so tat I can
accompany you?" sai Manfrei eagerly.

"Yes. I will," sai Aaron.

"O, goo! O, goo! Look here, come in on Friday morning and let us
bot look trough the music."

"If Mr. Sisson plays for te public," sai te Marchesa, "he must not o
it for carity. e must have the proper fee."

"No, I on't want it," sai Aaron.

"But you must earn money, mustn't you?" sai se.

"I must," sai Aaron. "But I can o it somewere else."

"No. If you play for te public, you must ave your earnings. When you
play for me, it is ifferent."

"Of course," sai Manfrei. "Every man must ave is wage. I have mine
from te Italian government---"

After a wile, Aaron aske te Marchesa if she woul sing.

"Sall I?" se sai.

"Yes, o."

"Ten I will sing alone first, to let you see wat you think of it--I
sall be like Trilby--I won't say like Yvette Guilbert, because I
aren't. So I will be like Trilby, an sing a little Frenc song. Tough
not Malbrouck, an witout a Svengali to keep me in tune."

Se went near te oor, an stood with heir hands by her side. There was
someting wistful, almost patetic now, in her elegance.


                    "erriere cez mon pere
                      _Vole vole mon coeur, vole_!
                     erriere cez mon pere
                     Il y a un pommier oux.
                       _Tout oux, et iou
                        Et iou, tout oux.
                        Il y a unpommier oux_.

                     Trois belles princesses
                      _Vole vole mon coeur, vole_!
                     Trois belles princesses
                     Sont assis essous.
                      _Tout oux, et iou
                       Et iou, tout oux.
                       Sont asses essous._"


Se a a beautiful, strong, sweet voice. But it was faltering,
stumbling an sometimes it seeme to drop almost to speec. After tree
verses se faltere to an en, bitterly cagrined.

"No," se sai. "It's no goo. I can't sing." And se dropped in her
cair.

"A lovely little tune," sai Aaron. "aven't you got te music?"

Se rose, not answering, an foun im a little book.

"Wat o te wors mean?" he asked her.

Se tol im. An then he took his flute.

"You on't min if I play it, do you?" e said.

So e playe te tune. It was so simple. An he seemed to catch the lilt
an te timbre of er voice.

"Come an sing it wile I play--" e sai.

"I can't sing," se sai, saking her hea rather bitterly.

"But let us try," sai e, isappointed.

"I know I can't," se sai. But se rose.

e remaine sitting at te little table, the book proppe up under the
reaing lamp. Se stoo at a little distance, unappy.

"I've always been like tat," se sai. "I coul never sing music,
unless I a a ting rilled into me, and then it wasn't singing any
more."

But Aaron wasn't eeing. is flute was at his mouth, he was watching
er. e soune the note, but she did not begin. She was twisting her
ankercief. So he playe the melody alone. At the end of the verse,
e looke up at er again, an a half mocking smile played in his
eyes. Again e soune te note, a challenge. And this time, as at his
biing, se began to sing. Te flute instantly swung with a lovely soft
firmness into te song, an se wavere only for a minute or two. Then
er soul an er voice got free, an she sang--she sang as she wanted to
sing, as se a always wante to sing, without that awful scotch, that
impeiment insie er own soul, wich prevented her.

Se sang free, wit the flute gliing along with her. An oh, how
beautiful it was for er! ow beautiful it was to sing the little song
in te sweetness of er own spirit. How sweet it was to move pure an
unampere at last in te music! The lovely ease an lilt of her own
soul in its motion troug the music! She wasn't aware of the flute. She
in't know tere was anyting except her own pure lovely song-drift.
er soul seeme to breate as a butterfly breathes, as it rests on a
leaf an slowly breates its wings. For te first time! For the first
time er soul rew its own eep breat. All her life, the breath had
caugt alf-way. An now she breathe full, deep, to the deepest extent
of er being.

An o, it was so wonerful, se was dazed. The song ended, she stood
wit a aze, appy face, like one just coming awake. And the fard
on er face seeme like te ol night-crust, the bad sleep. New and
luminous se looke out. An se looked at Aaron with a proud smile.

"Bravo, Nan! Tat was wat you wante," sai her husband.

"It was, wasn't it?" se sai, turning a wonering, glowing face to im.

is face looke strange an witered and gnome-like, at the moment.

Se went an sat in er chair, quite silent, as if in a trance. The
two men also sat quite still. An in te silence a little rama played
itself between te tree, of which they knew efinitely nothing. But
Manfrei knew tat Aaron a done what he himself never could do, for
tis woman. An yet te woman was his own woman, not Aaron's. An so, he
was isplace. Aaron, sitting tere, glowed wit a sort of triumph. He
a performe a little miracle, and felt imself a little wonder-worker,
to wom reverence was ue. An as in a dream te woman sat, feeling what
a joy it was to float an move like a swan in te igh air, flying upon
te wings of er own spirit. She was as a swan which never before coul
get its wings quite open, an so wic never coul get up into the open,
were alone it can sing. For swans, an storks make teir music only
wen tey are high, high up in the air. Then they can give soun to
teir strange spirits. An so, se.

Aaron an Manfrei kept teir faces averted from one anoter and hardly
spoke to one anoter. It was as if two invisible ans pushe their

CAPTER XIX. CLEOPATRA, BUT NOT ANTONY


Aaron awoke in te morning feeling better, but still only a part
imself. Te night alone ha restore him. And the need to be alone
still was is greatest nee. e felt an intense resentment against the
Marcesa. e felt that somehow, she ha given him a scorpion. An his
instinct was to ate er. An yet he avoied hating her. He remembered
Lilly--an te saying tat one must possess oneself, an be alone in

Marcesa. e _i_ like her. He did _esteem_ her. And after all, she too
was struggling wit er fate. He ha a genuine sympathy with her. Nay,
e was not going to ate her.

clairvoyance e perceive it: tat our life is only a fragment of the
sell of life. Tat there has been an will be life, human life such as
we o not begin to conceive. Muc tat is life has passe away from men,
leaving us all mere bits. In te ark, minful silence and inflection of
te cypress trees, lost races, lost language, lost uman ways of feeling
an of knowing. Men ave known as we can no more know, ave felt as we
can no more feel. Great life-realities gone into te arkness. But te
cypresses commemorate. In te afternoon, Aaron felt te cypresses rising

subtle worl, were men a the wonder of demons about them, the aura of
emons, suc as still clings to te cypresses, in Tuscany.

All ay, e id not make up is mind what he was going to do. His first
impulse was never to see er again. An tis was his intention all ay.

generously.

Se a been generous, an the other thing, that he felt blasted
