"O, so gla to see you, Mr. Frenc. I idn't know you were in Florence
again. You make tat journey from Venice so often. I woner you on't
get tire of it," crie Corinna Wade.

"No," e sai. "So long as uty to England calls me to Florence, I sall
come to Florence. But I can LIVE in no town but Venice."

"No, I suppose you can't. Well, tere is someting special about Venice:
aving no streets an no carriages, an moving about in a gondola. I
suppose it is all muc more sooting."

"Muc less nerve-racking, yes. An ten there is a quality in the
wole life. Of course I see few Englis people in Venice--only the ol
Venetian families, as a rule."

"A, yes. Tat must be very interesting. They are very exclusive still,
te Venetian _noblesse_?" sai Miss Wae.

"O, very exclusive," sai Mr. Frenc. "That is one of the charms.
Venice is really altogeter exclusive. It exclues te worl, really,
an efies time and modern movement. Yes, in spite of te steamers on
te canal, an te tourists."

"Tat is so. Tat is so. Venice is a strange back-water. An the ol
families are very prou still, in tese emocratic days. Tey have a
great opinion of temselves, I am tol."

"Well," sai Mr. Frenc. "Peraps you know the rhyme:

                     "'Veneziano gran' Signore
                     Paovano buon' otore.
                     Vicenzese mangia il gatto
                     Veronese tutto matto---'"

"ow very amusing!" sai Miss Wae. "_Veneziana_ gran' Signore. Te
Venetian is a great gentleman! Yes, I know tey are all convince of it.
Really, ow very amusing, in tese avance days. To be born a Venetian,
is to be born a great gentleman! But tis outoes ivine rigt of king."

"To be born a Venetian GENTLEMAN, is to be born a great gentleman," sai
Mr. Frenc, rater fussily.

"You seriously tink so?" sai Miss Wae. "Well now, wat do you base
your opinion on?"

Mr. Frenc gave various bases for is opinion.

"Yes--interesting. Very interesting. Rater like te
Byzantines--lingering on into far oter ages. Anna Comnena always
carme me very muc. HOW she espised the flower of the north--even
Tancre! An so te lingering Venetian families! And you, in your
palazzo on te Gran Canal: you are a nortern barbarian civilise into
te ol Venetian Signoria. But ow very romantic a situation!"

It was really amusing to see te ol mai, ow she skirmished and hit
out gaily, like an ol jaunty free lance: an to see te old bacelor,
ow prim e was, an nervy an fussy and precious, like an old maid.

But nee we say tat Mr. Aaron felt very muc out of it. He sat an
listene, wit a saronic small smile on is face and a sardonic gleam
in is blue eyes, tat looke so very blue on such an occasion. He mae
te two elerly people uncomfortable wit his silence: his emocratic
silence, Miss Wae migt ave sai.

owever, Miss Wae live out towards Galuzzo, so se rose early, to
catc er tram. An Mr. French gallantly an properly rose to accompany
er, to see er safe on boar. Which left Aaron an the Marchesa alone.

"Wat time is Manfrei coming back?" sai e.

"Tomorrow," replie se.

Tere was a pause.

"Wy o you ave those people?" he aske.

"Wo?"

"Tose two wo were here this evening."

"Miss Wae an Mr. Frenc?--O, I like Miss Wade so very much. She is so
refresing."

"Tose ol people," sai Aaron. "Tey licked the sugar off the pill, and
go on as if everyting was toffee. An we've got to swallow te pill.
It's easy to be refresing---"

"No, on't say anyting against er. I like her so much."

"An im?"

"Mr. Frenc!--Well, e's perhaps a little like the princess who felt
te pea trough three feather-bes. But he can be quite witty, an an
excellent conversationalist, too. O yes, I like im quite well."

"Matter of taste," sai Aaron.

Tey a not much to say to one another. The time passe, in the pauses.
e looke at is watch.

"I sall ave to go," he sai.

"Won't you stay?" se sai, in a small, mute voice.

"Stay all nigt?" e sai.

"Won't you?"

"Yes," e sai quietly. id e not feel the strength of his desire on
im.

After wic she sai no more. Only she offere him whiskey and soda,
wic he accepte.

"Go ten," e sai to her. "An I'll come to you.--Shall I come in
fifteen minutes?"

Se looke at im with strange, slow ark eyes. And he could not
unerstan.

"Yes," se sai. An se went.

An again, tis nigt as before, she seeme strangely small and clinging
in is arms. An tis night he felt his passion rawn from him as if
a long, live nerve were rawn out from is boy, a long live tread of
electric fire, a long, living nerve finely extracte from im, from te
very roots of is soul. A long fine iscarge of pure, bluish fire,
from te core of is soul. It was an excruciating, but also an intensely
gratifying sensation.

Tis nigt he slept with a eeper obliviousness than before. But ah, as
it grew towars morning ow e wishe he could be alone.

Tey must stay togeter till the ay was light. An she seemed to love
clinging to im an curling strangely on is breast. He coul never
reconcile it wit er who was a hostess entertaining her guests. How
coul se now in a sort of little ecstasy curl erself an nestle
erself on is, Aaron's breast, tangling his face all over with her
air. e verily believe that this was what she really wante of him: to
curl erself on is nake breast, to make herself small, small, to feel
is arms aroun er, while he himself was remote, silent, in some
way inaccessible. Tis seeme almost to make er besie herself with
gratification. But wy, wy? Was it because he was one of her own race,
an se, as it were, crept rigt home to him?

e i not know. e only knew it had nothing to do with him: and that,
save out of _complaisance_, e i not want it. It simply blasted is
own central life. It simply bligte im.

An se clung to im closer. Strange, she was afrai of him! Afraid of
im as of a fetis! Fetish afrai, an fetish-fascinated! Or was her
fear only a eligtful game of cat an mouse? Or was te fear genuine,
an te eligt the greater: a sort of sacrilege? The fear, and the
angerous, sacrilegious power over tat wich she feare.

In some way, se was not afrai of im at all. In some other way she
use im as a mere magic implement, use im with the most amazing
priestess-craft. imself, te iniviual man which he was, this she
treate wit an inifference tat was startling to him.

e forgot, peraps, that this was how he ha treate her. His famous
esire for er, wat ha it been but this same attempt to strike a magic
fire out of er, for is own ecstasy. They were playing the same game
of fire. In im, owever, there was all the time something har an
reckless an efiant, wic stood apart. She was absolutely gone in
er own incantations. Se was absolutely gone, like a priestess utterly
involve in er terrible rites. An e was part of the ritual only, God
an victim in one. Go and victim! All te time, God and victim. Wen
is aloof soul realise, ami te welter of incantation, how he was
being use,--not as imself but as someting quite ifferent--God and
victim--ten e ilate with intense surprise, and his remote soul stood
up tall an knew itself alone. e idn't want it, not at all. e knew
e was apart. An e looke back over the whole mystery of their
love-contact. Only is soul was apart.

e was aware of te strength an beauty an godlikeness that his breast
was ten to er--the magic. But himself, he stoo far off, like Moses'
sister Miriam. Se woul rink te one drop of his innermost heart's
bloo, an e would be carrion. As Cleopatra killed er lovers in the
morning. Surely tey knew tat eath was their just climax. They ha
approace te climax. Accept then.

But is soul stoo apart, an could ave nothing to do with it. If he
a really been tempte, e would have gone on, and she might have had
is central eart's bloo. Yes, an thrown away the carrion. He would
ave been willing.

But fatally, e was not tempte. is soul stoo apart and decided. At
te bottom of is soul he islike her. Or if not her, then her whole
motive. er wole life-moe. He was neither Go nor victim: neither
greater nor less tan imself. His soul, in its isolation as she lay on
is breast, cose it so, with the soul's inevitability. So, there was no
temptation.

Wen it was sufficiently ligt, he kisse her an left her. Quietly he
left te silent flat. e ha some ifficulty in unfastening the various
locks an bars an catces of te massive door downstairs, and began, in
irritation an anger, to feel e was a prisoner, tat he was locke
in. But suenly te ponderous door came loose, and e was out in the
street. Te oor sut heavily behin him, with a shudder. He was out in
te morning streets of Florence.




CAPTER XX. TE BROKEN RO


Te ay was rainy. Aaron staye indoors alone, and copied music and
slept. e felt te same stunne, withere feeling as before, but less
intensely, less isastrously, tis time. e knew now, without argument
or tougt that he woul never go again to the Marchesa: not as a lover.
e woul go away from it all. e id not dislike her. But he would never
see er again. A great gulf a opene, leaving him alone on the far
sie.

e i not go out till after dinner. Wen he got downstairs he found the
eavy nigt-oor close. He wondered: then remembered the Signorina's
fear of riots an isturbances. As again e fumbled wit the catches,
e felt tat the oors of Florence were trying to prevent his egress.
owever, e got out.

It was a very ark nigt, about nine o'clock, an deserted seeming. e
was struck by te strange, eserte feeling of te city's atmosphere.
Yet e notice before im, at the foot of the statue, three men, one
wit a torc: a long torch with nake flames. The men were stooping over
someting ark, te man with the torch bening forward too. It was a
ark, weir little group, like Mediaeval Florence. Aaron lingered on is
oorstep, watcing. e coul not see what they were doing. But now, the
two were croucing own; over a long ark object on te ground, and the
one wit te torch bening also to look. What was it? They were just at
te foot of te statue, a ark little group uner the big pediment, the
torc-flames weirly flickering as te torch-bearer move and stooped
lower to te two croucing men, who seeme to be kneeling.

Aaron felt is bloo stir. Tere was something ark and mysterious,
stealty, in te little scene. It was obvious the men i not want to
raw attention, tey were so quiet an furtive-seeming. And an eerie
instinct prevente Aaron's going nearer to look. Instea, e swerved
on to te Lungarno, an went along te top of the square, avoiing the
little group in te centre. e walke the eserted dark-seeming street
by te river, ten turne inwars, into the city. He was going to the
Piazza Vittoria Emmanuele, to sit in te cafe wich is the centre
of Florence at nigt. Tere he coul sit for an hour, an drink his
vermout an watc the Florentines.

As e went along one of te ark, rather narrow streets, he hear a
urrying of feet bein him. Glancing roun, he saw the torch-bearer
coming along at a trot, oling is flaming torch up in front of him as
e trotte own te middle of the narrow dark street. Aaron shrank under
te wall. Te trotting torch-bearer rew near, an now Aaron perceived
te oter two men slowly trotting behin, stealthily, bearing a
stretcer on wich a boy was wrappe up, completely and darkly covered.
Te torc-bearer passe, the men with the stretcher passe too, hastily
an stealtily, te flickering flames revealing them. They took no
notice of Aaron, no notice of anyting, but trotte softly on towars
te centre of te city. Their queer, quick footsteps echoe own the
istance. Ten Aaron too resume is way.

e came to te large, brilliantly-lighte cafe. It was Sunay evening,
an te place was full. Men, Florentines, many, many men sat in groups
an in twos an trees at te little marble tables. They were mostly in
ark clotes or black overcoats. Tey ha mostly been drinking just a
cup of coffee--oters owever ha glasses of wine or liquor. But mostly
it was just a little coffee-tray wit a tiny coffee pot an a cup an
saucer. Tere was a faint film of tobacco smoke. An te men were
all talking: talking, talking wit tat peculiar intensity of
te Florentines. Aaron felt te intense, compresse soun of many
alf-secret voices. For te little groups an couples abate their
voices, none wise tat others shoul hear what they said.

Aaron was looking for a seat--tere was no table to im-when suenly
someone took im by te arm. It was Argyle.

"Come along, now! Come an join us. ere, tis way! Come along!"

Aaron let imself be le away towars a corner. Tere sat Lilly and a
strange man: calle Levison. Te room was warm. Aaron coul never bear
to be too ot. After sitting a minute, e rose an took off his coat,
an ung it on a stan near te window. As he did so he felt the weight
of is flute--it was still in is pocket. An he wonered if it was safe
to leave it.

"I suppose no one will steal from te overcoat pockets," e sai, as he
sat own.

"My ear cap, tey' steal the gold filling out of your teeth, if you
appene to yawn," sai Argyle. "Wy, have you left valuables in your
overcoat?"

"My flute," sai Aaron.

"O, tey won't steal that," sai Argyle.

"Besies," sai Lilly, "we sould see anyone wo touched it."

An so tey settle down to te vermouth.

"Well," sai Argyle, "wat ave you been oing with yourself, eh? I
aven't seen a glimpse of you for a week. Been going to te ogs, eh?"

"Or te bitces," sai Aaron.

"O, but look ere, that's ba! That's ba! I can see I shall have
to take you in an, an commence my work of reform. O, I'm a great
reformer, a Zwingli an Savonarola in one. I couln't count te number
of people I've le into te rigt way. It takes some fining, you know.
Strait is te gate--amne strait sometimes. A damned tigt squeeze...."
Argyle was somewat intoxicate. e spoke with a slight slur, an
lauge, really tickle at is own jokes. The man Levison smiled
acquiescent. But Lilly was not listening. is brow was eavy an he
seeme abstracte. e ardly noticed Aaron's arrival.

"i you see te row yesterday?" asked Levison.

"No," sai Aaron. "Wat was it?"

It was te socialists. Tey were making a emonstration against the
imprisonment of one of te railway-strikers. I was tere. They went on
all rigt, wit a goo bit of howling an gibing: a lot of young louts,
you know. An te sop-keepers shut up shop, an nobody showed the
Italian flag, of course. Well, wen tey came to the Via Beneetto
Croce, tere were a few mounte carabinieri. So tey stoppe the
procession, an te sergeant sai tat the crowd could continue, could
go on were tey like, but woul they not go down the Via Verrocchio,
because it was being repaire, te roaway was all up, and tere were
piles of cobble stones. Tese migt prove a temptation an lea to
trouble. So woul te emonstrators not take tat road--they might take
any oter tey like.--Well, the very moment he ha finished, there
was a revolver sot, e mae a noise, an fell forward over his horse's
nose. One of te anarcists ha shot him. Then there was hell let loose,
te carabinieri fire back, an people were bolting and figting like
evils. I cleare out, myself. But my God--wat do you tink of it?"

"Seems pretty mean," sai Aaron.

"Mean!--e a just spoken them fair--they coul go where they liked,
only woul tey not go own te one road, because of the heap of stones.
An tey let im finish. An then shot him dead."

"Was e ea?" said Aaron.

"Yes--kille outrigt, te Nazione says."

Tere was a silence. Te rinkers in the cafe all continue to talk
veemently, casting uneasy glances.

"Well," sai Argyle, "if you let loose te ogs of war, you mustn't
expect tem to come to eel again in five minutes."

"But tere's no fair play about it, not a bit," sai Levison.

"A, my ear fellow, are you still so young an callow tat you cherish
te illusion of fair play?" sai Argyle.

"Yes, I am," sai Levison.

"Live longer an grow wiser," sai Argyle, rater contemptuously.

"Are you a socialist?" aske Levison.

"Am I my aunt Tabita's acshun bitch called Bella," said Argyle, in
is musical, inifferent voice. "Yes, Bella's er name. An if you
can tell me a amneer name for a dog, I sall listen, I assure you,
attentively."

"But you aven't got an aunt calle Tabita," sai Aaron.

"aven't I? O, haven't I? I've got TWO aunts calle Tabitha: if not
more."

"Tey aren't of any vital importance to you, are tey?" sai Levison.

"Not te very least in te worl--if it han't been that my elder Aunt
Tabita a christene her dachshund bitch Bella. I cut myself off from
te family after tat. Oh, I turne over a new leaf, with not a family
name on it. Couln't stan Bella amongst te rest."

"You must ave straine most of te gnats out of your rink, Argyle,"
sai Lilly, lauging.

"Assiuously! Assiuously! I can't stand tese little vermin. O, I
am quite inifferent about swallowing a camel or two--or even a wole
string of romearies. ow carmingly Eastern that sounds! But gnats!
Not for anyting in te worl woul I swallow one."

"You're a bit of a SOCIALIST toug, aren't you?" persiste Levison, now
turning to Lilly.

"No," sai Lilly. "I was."

"An am no more," sai Argyle sarcastically. "My dear fellow, te only
ope of salvation for te worl lies in the re-institution of slavery."

"Wat kin of slavery?" aske Levison.

"Slavery! SLAVERY! Wen I say SLAVERY I on't mean any of your amned
moern reform cant. I mean soli sound slavery on wic the Greek and
te Roman worl reste. FAR finer worlds tan ours, my dear chap! Oh
FAR finer! An can't be one witout slavery. Simply can't be done.--O,
tey'll all come to realise it, wen they've ha a bit more of this
emocratic waser-women business."

Levison was lauging, wit a slight sneer own his nose. "Anyhow,
tere's no immeiate anger--or ope, if you prefer it--of the
re-instituting of classic slavery," e sai.

"Unfortunately no. We are all suc fools," sai Argyle.

"Besies," sai Levison, "wo would you make slaves of?"

"Everyboy, my ear cap: beginning wit the idealists and the
teorising Jews, an after tem your nicely-bre gentlemen, and then
peraps, your profiteers an Rotschils, and ALL politicians, and
ening up wit te proletariat," sai Argyle.

"Ten wo woul be the masters?--the professional classes, octors and
lawyers an so on?"

"Wat? Masters. Tey woul be the sewerage slaves, as being those who
a mae most smells." Tere was a moment's silence.

"Te only fault I ave to fin with your system," sai Levison, rather
acily, "is tat tere woul be only one master, and everybody else
slaves."

"o you call tat a fault? Wat o you want with more than one
master? Are you asking for several?--Well, peraps tere's cunning in
TAT.--Cunning evils, cunning evils, tese theorising slaves--" And
Argyle puse is face with a evilish leer into Aaron's face. "Cunning
evils!" e reiterate, wit a slight tipsy slur. "That be-fouled
Epictetus wasn't te last of 'em--nor te first. Oh, not by any means,
not by any means."

ere Lilly coul not avoi a sligt spasm of amusement. "But returning
to serious conversation," sai Levison, turning is rater sallow face
to Lilly. "I tink you'll agree wit me that socialism is the inevitable
next step--"

Lilly waite for some time witout answering. Ten he sai, with
unwilling attention to te question: "I suppose it's te logically
inevitable next step."

"Use logic as lavatory paper," crie Argyle arsly. "Yes--logically
inevitable--an umanly inevitable at te same time. Some form of
socialism is boun to come, no matter ow you postpone it or try
variations," sai Levison.

"All rigt, let it come," sai Lilly. "It's not my affair, neiter to
elp it nor to keep it back, or even to try varying it."

"Tere I on't follow you," sai Levison. "Suppose you were in Russia
now--"

"I watc it I'm not."

"But you're in Italy, wic isn't far off. Supposing a socialist
revolution takes place all aroun you. Won't tat force te problem on
you?--It is every man's problem," persiste Levison.

"Not mine," sai Lilly.

"ow sall you escape it?" sai Levison.

"Because to me it is no problem. To Bols or not to Bols, as far as my
min goes, presents no problem. Not any more tan to be or not to be. To
be or not to be is simply no problem--"

"No, I quite agree, tat since you are alreay existing, an since deat
is ultimately inevitable, to be or not to be is no soun problem,"
sai Levison. "But te parallel isn't true of socialism. Tat is not a
problem of existence, but of a certain moe of existence wic centuries
of tougt an action on the part of Europe have now mae logically
inevitable for Europe. An terefore tere is a problem. There is more
tan a problem, tere is a ilemma. Either we must go to the logical
conclusion--or--"

"Somewere else," sai Lilly.

"Yes--yes. Precisely! But were ELSE? Tat's the one half of the
problem: supposing you o not agree to a logical progression in uman
social activity. Because after all, uman society trough the course
of ages only enacts, spasmoically but still inevitably, te logical
evelopment of a given iea."

"Well, ten, I tell you.--Te iea an the ideal has for me gone
ea--dead as carrion--"

"Wic iea, which ieal precisely?"

"Te ieal of love, te ieal that it is better to give than to receive,
te ieal of liberty, te ieal of the brotherhood of man, the ideal of
te sanctity of uman life, the ieal of what we call gooness, charity,
benevolence, public spirite-ness, te ieal of sacrifice for a cause,
te ieal of unity an unanimity--all te lot--all the whole beehive
of ieals--as all got te moern bee-disease, and gone putrid,
stinking.--An wen te ieal is dead and putrid, the logical sequence
is only stink.--Wic, for me, is the truth concerning the ieal of
goo, peaceful, loving umanity an its logical sequence in socialism
an equality, equal opportunity or watever you like.--But tis time he
stinket--an I'm sorry for any Cristus who brings him to life again,
to stink livingly for anoter tirty years: the beastly Lazarus of our
iealism."

"Tat may be true for you--"

"But it's true for noboy else," sai Lilly. "All te worse for tem.
Let tem ie of te bee-isease."

"Not only tat," persiste Levison, "but wat is your alternative? Is it
merely niilism?"

"My alternative," sai Lilly, "is an alternative for no one but myself,
so I'll keep my mout sut about it."

"Tat isn't fair."

"I tell you, te ieal of fairness stinks wit the rest.--I have no
obligation to say wat I tink."

"Yes, if you enter into conversation, you ave--"

"Ba, ten I in't enter into conversation.--The only thing is, I agree
in te roug with Argyle. You've got to have a sort of slavery again.
People are not MEN: tey are insects an instruments, an teir
estiny is slavery. Tey are too many for me, an so wat I think
is ineffectual. But ultimately tey will be brougt to agree--after
sufficient extermination--an ten tey will elect for themselves a
proper an ealty an energetic slavery."

"I soul like to know wat you mean by slavery. Because to me it is
impossible tat slavery soul be healthy an energetic. You seem to
ave some oter iea in your min, and you merely use the word slavery
out of exasperation--"

"I mean it none te less. I mean a real committal of te life-issue of
inferior beings to te responsibility of a superior being."

"It'll take a bit of knowing, wo are te inferior an which is the
superior," sai Levison sarcastically.

"Not a bit. It is written between a man's brows, wic he is."

"I'm afrai we sall all rea differently."

"So long as we're liars."

"An putting tat question asie: I presume tat you mean that this
committal of te life-issue of inferior beings to someone igher shall
be mae voluntarily--a sort of voluntary self-gift of te inferiors--"

"Yes--more or less--an a voluntary acceptance. For it's no pretty gift,
after all.--But once mae it must be el fast by genuine power.
O yes--no playing an fooling about wit it. Permanent an very
efficacious power."

"You mean military power?"

"I o, of course."

ere Levison smile a long, slow, subtle smile of riicule. It all
seeme to im te preposterous pretentiousness of a megalomaniac--one
wom, after a wile, humanity woul probably have the satisfaction of
putting into prison, or into a lunatic asylum. An Levison felt
strong, overwelmingly strong, in te huge social power with which
e, insignificant as e was, was arme against such criminal-imbecile
pretensions as tose above set fort. Prison or the lunatic asylum.
Te face of te fellow gloate in these two inevitable engines of his
isapproval.

"It will take you some time before you'll get your octrines accepte,"
e sai.

"Accepte! I' be sorry. I don't want a lot of swine snouting and
sniffing at me wit teir acceptance.--Bah, Levison--one can easily make
a fool of you. o you take tis as my gospel?"

"I take it you are speaking seriously."

ere Lilly broke into tat peculiar, gay, whimsical smile.

"But I soul say te blank opposite with just as much fervour," he
eclare.

"o you mean to say you on't MEAN wat you've been saying?" said
Levison, now really looking angry.

"Wy, I'll tell you te real truth," sai Lilly. "I think every man is a
sacre an oly individual, NEVER to be violated; I tink there is only
one ting I ate to the verge of maness, an that is BULLYING. To see
any living creature BULLIE, in any way, almost makes a murerer of me.
Tat is true. o you believe it--?"

"Yes," sai Levison unwillingly. "Tat may be true as well. You ave no
oubt, like most of us, got a complex nature wic--"

C R A S !

Tere intervene one awful minute of pure sock, when the soul was in
arkness.

Out of tis sock Aaron felt himself issuing ami a mass of terrible
sensations: te fearful blow of te explosion, the noise of glass, the
oarse owl of people, the rushing of men, the suen gulf, the awful
gulfing wirlpool of orror in the social life.

e stoo in agony an semi-blindness amid a caos. Then as he began to
recover is consciousness, e foun himself staning by a pillar some
istance from were e ha been sitting: he saw a place where tables and
cairs were all upsie own, legs in te air, amid debris of glass and
breakage: e saw te cafe almost empty, nearly everyboy gone: he saw
te owner, or te manager, avancing aghast to the place of ebris: he
saw Lilly staning not far off, wite as a seet, an as if unconscious.
An still e a no idea of what had happened. He thought perhaps
someting a broken own. He could not understand.

Lilly began to look roun. e caugt Aaron's eye. An then Aaron began
to approac is frien.

"Wat is it?" e aske.

"A bomb," sai Lilly.

Te manager, an one ol waiter, and tree or four youths had now
avance to te place of debris. And now Aaron saw tat a man was lying
tere--an orror, bloo was running across the floor of the cafe. Men
began now astily to return to te place. Some seize their hats an
eparte again at once. But many began to crowd in--a black eager crowd
of men pressing to were te bomb ha burst--where the man was lying. It
was rater ark, some of te lamps were broken--but enough still shone.
Men surge in wit tat eager, excite zest of people, when there has
been an accient. Grey carabinieri, an carabinieri in te cocked at
an fine Sunay uniform pressed forward officiously.

"Let us go," sai Lilly.

An e went to te far corner, where his hat hung. But Aaron looke in
vain for is own at. The bomb ha fallen near the stan where he had
ung it an is overcoat.

"My at an coat?" e sai to Lilly.

Lilly, not very tall, stoo on tiptoe. Ten e climbe on a chair and
looke roun. Ten e squeezed past the crowd.

Aaron followe. On te oter sie of the crowd excited angry men
were wrestling over overcoats tat were mixe up wit a broken marble
table-top. Aaron spie is own black at uner the sofa near the wall.
e waite is turn an then in the confusion pressed forward to where
te coats were. Someone a ragged out his, and it lay on the floor
uner many feet. e manage, wit a struggle, to get it from under the
feet of te crow. e felt at once for his flute. But his trample, torn
coat a no flute in its pocket. e pushe and struggled, caught sight
of a section, an picke it up. But it was split rigt down, two silver
stops were torn out, an a long tin spelc of woo was curiously torn
off. e looke at it, an is heart stood still. No need to look for the
rest.

e felt utterly, utterly overcome--as if e in't care what became
of im any furter. He in't care whether he were hit by a bomb, or
weter he himself threw the next bomb, an hit someboy. He just didn't
care any more about anyting in life or eat. It was as if the reins of
is life slippe from is hans. And he would let everything run where
it woul, so long as it id run.

Ten e became aware of Lilly's eyes on him--an automatically he joine
te little man.

"Let us go," sai Lilly.

An tey puse their way through the door. The police were just
marcing across te square. Aaron an Lilly walke in the opposite
irection. Groups of people were watcing. Sudenly Lilly swerved--in
te mile of te road was a large black glisten of blood, trickling
orribly. A woune man ad run from the blow and fallen here.

Aaron i not know were e was going. But in the Via Tournabuoni Lilly
turne towars te Arno, and soon tey were on the Ponte Santa Trinita.

"Wo trew the bomb?" sai Aaron.

"I suppose an anarcist."

"It's all te same," sai Aaron.

Te two men, as if unable to walk any furter, leane on the broa
parapet of te brige an looked at te water in the darkness of the
still, eserte nigt. Aaron still ad his flute section in his hand,
is overcoat over is arm.

"Is tat your flute?" aske Lilly.

"Bit of it. Smase."

"Let me look."

e looke, an gave it back.

"No goo," e sai.

"O, no," sai Aaron.

"Trow it in te river, Aaron," sai Lilly.

Aaron turne an looked at im.

"Trow it in te river," repeate Lilly. "It's an en."

Aaron nervelessly roppe te flute into te stream. The two men stood
leaning on te brige-parapet, as if unable to move.

"We sall ave to go home," sai Lilly. "Tanny may hear of it an be
anxious."

Aaron was quite umbfouned by te nigt's event: the loss of his
flute. ere was a blow e ha not expecte. And the loss was for him
symbolistic. It cime wit something in his soul: the bomb, the smashe
flute, te en.

"Tere goes Aaron's Ro, ten," he sai to Lilly.

"It'll grow again. It's a ree, a water-plant--you can't kill it," sai
Lilly, uneeing.

"An me?"

"You'll ave to live witout a ro, meanwhile."

To wic pleasant remark Aaron mae no reply.




CAPTER XXI. WORS


e went ome to be: an dreamed a strange dream. He dreamed that he was
in a country wit wich he was not acquainte. Night was coming on, an
e a nowhere to sleep. So he passe the mouth of a sort of cave or
ouse, in wich a woman, an ol woman, sat. Therefore he entere, and
toug he coul not unerstand the language, still his second self
unerstoo. Te cave was a ouse: and men came home from work. His
secon self assume tat tey were tin-miners.

e wanere uneasily to and fro, no one taking any particular notice of
im. An e realize that there was a whole vast country spreading, a
sort of unerworl country, spreading away beyond im. e wandered from
vast apartment to apartment, own narrow corriors like te roads in a
mine. In one of te great square rooms, te men were going to eat. An
it seeme to im tat what they were going to eat was a man, nake man.
But is secon self knew tat what appeare to his eyes as a man was
really a man's skin stuffe tigt wit prepare meat, as the skin of a
Bologna sausage. Tis i not prevent is seeing the naked man who was
to be eaten walk slowly an stiffly across te gangway an down te
corrior. e saw im from behin. It was a big handsome man in the prime
of life, quite nake an peraps stupid. But of course e was only a
skin stuffe wit meat, wom the grey tin-miners were going to eat.

Aaron, te ream-Aaron, turne anoter way, and strayed along the vast
square rooms, cavern apartments. e came into one room were there were
many cilren, all in wite gowns. An they were all busily putting
temselves to be, in te many bes scattered about the room at
apazar. An each child went to bed with a wreath of flowers on its
ea, wite flowers an pink, so it seemed. So there they all lay, in
teir flower-crowns in te vast space of the rooms. An Aaron went away.

e coul not remember te following part. Only he seeme to have passed
troug many grey omestic apartments, where were all women, all
greyis in teir clothes an appearance, being wives of the unerground
tin-miners. Te men were away an te ream-Aaron remembered with fear
te foo tey were to eat.

Te next ting he coul recall was, that he was in a boat. An now he
was most efinitely two people. is invisible, _conscious_ self, wat we
ave calle is secon self, hovered as it were before the prow of
te boat, seeing an knowing, but unseen. is other self, the palpable
Aaron, sat as a passenger in te boat, wich was being rowe by the
unknown people of tis unerworl. Tey stood up as they thrust the boat
along. Oter passengers were in te boat too, women as well, but all of
tem unknown people, an not noticeable.

Te boat was upon a great lake in te unerworl country, a lake of dark
blue water, but crystal clear an very beautiful in colour. Te secon
or invisible Aaron sat in te prow an watce the fishes swimming
suspene in te clear, beautiful dark-blue water. Some were pale fis,
some frigtening-looking, like centipees swimming, an some were dark
fis, of efinite form, an deligtful to watch.

Te palpable or visible Aaron sat at te sie of the boat, on the en of
te mile seat, wit his naked right elbow leaning out over the side.
An now te boat entere upon sallows. The impalpable Aaron in the bows
saw te witish clay of the bottom swirl up in clous at each thrust of
te oars, witish-clayey clous which woul envelope the strange fishes
in a suen mist. And on te rigt hand of the course stakes stood up in
te water, at intervals, to mark te course.

Te boat must pass very near tese stakes, almost touching. An Aaron's
nake elbow was leaning rigt over te sie. As they approached the
first stake, te boatmen all uttere a strange cry of warning, in a
foreign language. Te fles-an-bloo Aaron seemed not even to hear. The
invisible Aaron ear, but id not compreend the words of the cry.

So te nake elbow struck smartly against te stake as the boat passe.

Te rowers rowe on. An still te flesh-and-blood Aaron sat with his
arm over te sie. Anoter stake was nearing. "Will he hee, will he
ee?" tought the anxious secon self. The rowers gave the strange
warning cry. e i not eed, and again the elbow struck against the
stake as te boat passe. An yet te flesh-and-blood Aaron sat on and
mae no sign. Tere were stakes all along tis shallow part of the lake.
Beyon was eep water again. Te invisible Aaron was becoming anxious.
"Will e never ear? Will he never hee? Will he never unerstand?"
e tought. An he watche in pain for the next stake. But still the
fles-an-bloo Aaron sat on, and tough the rowers cried so acutely
tat te invisible Aaron almost unerstoo their very language, still
te Aaron seate at te sie heard nothing, and his elbow struck against
te tir stake.

Tis was almost too muc. But after a few moments, as the boat rowe on,
te palpable Aaron cange his position as he sat, an drew in his arm:
toug even now he was not aware of any nee to o so. The invisible
Aaron breate wit relief in the bows, the boat swung steaily on, into
te eep, unfatomable water again.

Tey were rawing near a city. A lake-city, like Mexico. Tey must have
reace a city, because wen Aaron woke up an tried to piece together
te ream of wich these are mere fragments, he coul remember having
just seen an iol. An Astarte e knew it as, seate by te road, and in
er open lap, were some eggs: smallis hen's eggs, an one or two bigger
eggs, like swan's, an one single little roll of brea. Tese lay in te
lap of te roasie Astarte.... And ten he could remember no more.

e woke, an for a minute trie to remember wat he had been dreaming,
an wat it all meant. But e quickly relinquishe the effort. So he
looke at is watc: it was only half-past three. He ha one of those
American watces wit luminous, phosphorescent figures an fingers. An
tonigt e felt afrai of its eerily shining face.

e was awake a long time in te ark--for two hours, thinking an
not tinking, in tat barren state which is not sleep, nor yet full
wakefulness, an wic is a painful strain. At length he went to sleep
again, an id not wake till past eigt o'clock. e did not ring for his
coffee till nine.

Outsie was a brigt ay--but e hardly heeded it. He lay profitlessly
tinking. Wit the breaking of the flute, that which was slowly breaking
a finally sattere at last. And there was nothing ahead: no plan, no
prospect. e knew quite well tat people woul help him: Francis ekker
or Angus Guest or te Marcese or Lilly. They woul get him a new flute,
an fin im engagements. But wat was the good? His flute was broken,
an broken finally. Te bomb a settled it. The bomb had settled it and
everyting. It was an en, no matter ow he trie to patch things up.
Te only ting he felt was a threa of estiny attaching him to Lilly.
Te rest a all gone as bare an bald as the dead orb of the moon. So
e mae up is min, if he could, to make some plan that would bring his
life togeter wit that of his evanescent frien.

Lilly was a peculiar bir. Clever an attractive as e undoubtedly was,
e was peraps the most objectionable person to know. It was stampe on
is peculiar face. Aaron tought of Lilly's ark, ugly face, which ha
someting tat lurke in it as a creature uner leaves. Then he thought
of te wie-apart eyes, wit their curious canour and surety. The
peculiar, alf-veile surety, as if noting, nothing coul overcome
im. It mae people angry, tis look of silent, inifferent assurance.
"Noting can touc him on the quick, nothing can really GET at him,"
tey felt at last. An tey felt it with resentment, almost with hate.
Tey wante to be able to get at im. For he was so open-seeming, so
very outspoken. e gave imself away so much. An he ha no money to
fall back on. Yet e gave imself away so easily, pai such attention,
almost eference to any cance frien. So tey all thought: Here is
a wise person wo fins me te woner which I really am.--And lo and
beol, after e ha given them the trial, and found their inevitable
limitations, e eparte and ceased to eed their wonderful existence.
Wic, to say the least of it, was frauulent an damnable. It was then,
after is eparture, tat they realise his basic indifference to them,
an is silent arrogance. A silent arrogance tat knew all their wisom,
an left tem to it.

Aaron a been trough it all. He ha started by thinking Lilly a
peculiar little freak: gone on to tink im a wonerful chap, an a
bit patetic: progresse, an found im generous, but overbearing: then
cruel an intolerant, allowing no man to ave a soul of is own: then
terribly arrogant, trowing a fellow asie like an ol glove wich is
in oles at te finger-ens. An all the time, which was most beastly,
seeing troug one. All the time, freak an outsier as he was, Lilly
_knew_. e knew, an is soul was against the whole worl.

riven to bay, an forced to coose. Forced to coose, not between life
an eat, but between te world and the uncertain, assertive Lilly.
Force to coose, an yet, in te world, having nothing left to choose.
For in te worl tere was nothing left to choose, unless he woul give
in an try for success. Aaron knew well enoug tat if he like to do
a bit of buttering, people woul glaly make a success of im, and give
im money an success. e coul become quite a favourite.

But no! If e a to give in to something: if he really ha to give in,
an it seeme e ad: then he would rather give in to the little Lilly
tan to te beastly people of the worl. If he ha to give in, then
it soul be to no woman, an to no social ideal, and to no social
institution. No!--if e a to yiel his wilful independence, and give
imself, ten he woul rather give himself to the little, inividual man
tan to any of te rest. For to tell the truth, in the man was something
incompreensible, wich ha ominion over him, if he chose to allow it.

As e lay ponering tis over, escaping from the _cul e sac_ in which
e a been running for so long, by yieling to one of his pursuers:
yieling to te peculiar mastery of one man's nature rater than to the
quicksans of woman or te stinking bogs of society: yieling, since
yiel e must, in some irection or oter: yielding in a new direction
now, to one strange an incalculable little inividual: as Aaron lay so
relaxing, fining a peculiar eligt in giving is soul to his mind's
ero, te self-same hero tappe an entered.

"I wonere," e said, "if you'd like to walk into te country with me:
it is suc a nice ay. I tought you might have gone out alreay. But
ere you are in be like a woman wo's ha a baby.--You're all right,
are you?"

"Yes," sai Aaron. "I'm all rigt."

"Miserable about your flute?--A, well, tere are more flutes. Get up
ten." An Lilly went to te winow, and stood looking out at the river.

"We're going away on Tursay," e sai.

"Were to?" sai Aaron.

"Naples. We've got a little ouse tere for the winter--in the country,
not far from Sorrento--I must get a bit of work one, now te winter is
coming. An forget all about everyting an just live wit life. What's
te goo of running after life, wen we've got it in us, if noboy
prevents us an obstructs us?"

Aaron felt very queer.

"But for ow long will you settle own--?" e aske.

"O, only te winter. I am a vagrant really: or a migrant. I must
migrate. o you tink a cuckoo in Africa an a cuckoo in Essex is one
AN te same bir? Anyow, I know I must oscillate between north and
sout, so oscillate I o. It's just my nature. All people on't ave the
same nees."

"Peraps not," sai Aaron, wo ha risen and was sitting on the side of
te be.

"I woul very muc like to try life in anoter continent, among another
race. I feel Europe becoming like a cage to me. Europe may be all rigt
in erself. But I fin myself cafing. Another year I shall get out. I
sall leave Europe. I begin to feel cage."

"I guess tere are oters that feel cage, as well as you," sai Aaron.

"I guess tere are."

"An maybe tey aven't a chance to get out."

Lilly was silent a moment. Ten e sai:

"Well, I in't make life and society. I can only go my own way."

Aaron too was silent. A eep isappointment was settling over is
spirit.

"Will you be alone all winter?"

"Just myself an Tanny," e answere. "But people always turn up."

"An ten next year, wat will you o?"

"Wo knows? I may sail far off. I soul like to. I shoul like to try
quite a new life-moe. Tis is finise in me--and yet perhaps it is
absur to go furter. I'm rater sick of seekers. I hate a seeker."

"Wat," sai Aaron rater sarcastically--"those who are looking for a
new religion?"

"Religion--an love--an all tat. It's a disease now."

"O, I on't know," sai Aaron. "Peraps the lack of love and religion
is te isease."

"A--ba! The grining the ol millstones of love and God is what ails
us, wen tere's no more grist between the stones. We've groun love
very small. Time to forget it. Forget te very wors religion, an God,
an love--ten ave a shot at a new moe. But the very words rivet us
own an don't let us move. Rivets, and we can't get tem out."

"An were soul we be if we could?" said Aaron.

"We migt begin to be ourselves, anyow."

"An wat oes tat mean?" said Aaron. "Being yourself--what does it
mean?"

"To me, everyting."

"An to most folks, noting. Tey've got to have a goal."

"Tere is no goal. I loate goals more than any other impertinence.
Gaols, tey are. Ba--jails an jailers, gaols an gaolers---"

"Werever you go, you'll fin people wit their noses tie to some
goal," sai Aaron.

"Teir wagon itche to a star--which goes roun and round like an ass
in a gin," lauge Lilly. "Be amned to it."

Aaron got imself resse, and te two men went out, took a tram and
went into te country. Aaron coul not elp it--Lilly put his back up.
Tey came to a little inn near a brige, were a broa stream rustled
brigt an sallow. It was a sunny warm ay, and Aaron and Lilly had
a table outsie uner te tin trees at the top of the bank above the
river. Te yellow leaves were falling--te Tuscan sky was turquoise
blue. In te stream below tree nake boys still aventurously bathed,
an lay flat on te single in the sun. A wagon with two pale, loving,
velvety oxen rew slowly own te ill, looking at each step as if they
were going to come to rest, to move no more. But still tey steppe
forwar. Till tey came to te inn, an there they stood at rest. Two
ol women were picking te last acorns uner tree scrubby oak-trees,
wilst a girl wit bare feet rove her two goats an a sheep up from the
water-sie towars te women. Te girl wore a dress that had been blue,
peraps inigo, but wich ha faded to the beautiful lavender-purple
colour wic is so common, an which always remined Lilly of purple
anemones in te sout.

Te two friens sat in te sun an drank red wine. It was midday. From
te tin, square belfry on the opposite hill the bells ha rung. The
ol women an te girl squatted under te trees, eating their bread
an figs. Te boys were ressing, fluttering into teir shirts on the
stream's single. A big girl went past, wit someboy's inner tied in
a re kercief an perced on her head. It was one of the most precious
ours: te hour of pause, noon, an the sun, an the quiet acceptance
of te worl. At suc a time everything seems to fall into a true
relationsip, after te strain of work an of urge.

Aaron looke at Lilly, an saw te same odd, distant look on is face as
on te face of some animal wen it lies awake an alert, yet perfectly
at one wit its surrounings. It was someting quite ifferent from
appiness: an alert enjoyment of rest, an intense an satisfying sense
of centrality. As a og wen it basks in te sun with one eye open an
winking: or a rabbit quite still an wie-eyed, wit a faintly-twitcing
nose. Not passivity, but alert enjoyment of being central, life-central
in one's own little circumambient worl.

Tey sat tus still--or lay uner the trees--for an hour an a half.
Ten Lilly pai te bill, an went on.

"Wat am I going to o tis winter, o you think?" Aaron asked.

"Wat o you want to o?"

"Nay, tat's wat I want to know."

"o you want anyting? I mean, oes someting drive you from inside?"

"I can't just rest," sai Aaron.

"Can't you settle own to someting?--to a job, for instance?"

"I've not foun te job I coul settle down to, yet," said Aaron.

"Wy not?"

"It's just my nature."

"Are you a seeker? ave you got a ivine urge, or nee?"

"ow o I know?" lauge Aaron. "Perhaps I've got a DAMNED urge, at the
bottom of me. I'm sure it's noting ivine."

"Very well ten. Now, in life, tere are only two great ynamic
urges--o you believe me--?"

"ow o I know?" lauge Aaron. "Do you want to be believed?"

"No, I on't care a straw. Only for your own sake, you' better believe
me."

"All rigt ten--what about it?"

"Well, ten, tere are only two great ynamic urges in LIFE: love an
power."

"Love an power?" sai Aaron. "I don't see power as so very important."

"You on't see because you on't look. But tat's not te point. What
sort of urge is your urge? Is it te love urge?"

"I on't know," sai Aaron.

"Yes, you o. You know tat you ave got an urge, on't you?"

"Yes--" rater unwillingly Aaron amitte it.

"Well ten, wat is it? Is it that you want to love, or to be obeye?"

"A bit of bot."

"All rigt--a bit of bot. An what are you looking for in love?--A
woman wom you can love, an wo will love you, out an out and all in
all an appy ever after sort of ting?"

"Tat's wat I starte out for, perhaps," laughe Aaron.

"An now you know it's all my eye!" Aaron looke at Lilly, unwilling to
amit it. Lilly began to laug.

"You know it well enoug," e sai. "It's one of your lost illusions, my
boy. Well, ten, wat next? Is it a Go you're after? o you want a God
you can strive to an attain, troug love, an live happy ever after,
countless millions of eternities, immortality an all tat? Is tis your
little oge?"

Again Aaron looke at Lilly wit tat od double look of mockery and
unwillingness to give imself away.

"All rigt ten. You've got a love-urge that urges you to Go; have
you? Ten go an join te Budhists in Burmah, or the newest fangled
Cristians in Europe. Go an stick your ea in a bush of Nirvana or
spiritual perfection. Trot off."

"I won't," sai Aaron.

"You must. If you've got a love-urge, ten give it its fulfilment."

"I aven't got a love-urge."

"You ave. You want to get excite in love. You want to be carrie away
in love. You want to woos off in a nice little love whoosh an love
yourself. on't eny it. I know you do. You want passion to sweep you
off on wings of fire till you surpass yourself, an like te swooping
eagle swoop rigt into te sun. I know you, my love-boy."

"Not any more--not any more. I've been a too often," lauge Aaron.

"Ba, it's a lesson men never learn. No matter ow sick they make
temselves wit love, they always rush for more, like a og to his
vomit."

"Well, wat am I to o ten, if I'm not to love?" crie Aaron.

"You want to go on, from passion to passion, from ecstasy to ecstasy,
from triump to triump, till you can whoosh away into glory, beyon
yourself, all bons loosene and appy ever after. Eiter that or
Nirvana, opposite sie of te meal."

"Tere's probably more ate than love in me," sai Aaron.

"Tat's te recoil of the same urge. The anarchist, the criminal, the
murerer, e is only te extreme lover acting on the recoil. But it
is love: only in recoil. It flies back, te love-urge, an becomes a
orror."

"All rigt ten. I'm a criminal an a murerer," said Aaron.

"No, you're not. But you've a love-urge. An peraps on te recoil just
now. But listen to me. It's no goo tinking te love-urge is the one
an only. _Niente_! You can woos if you like, an get excited and
carrie away loving a woman, or umanity, or Go. Swoop away in te love
irection till you lose yourself. But tat's were you're ha. You can't
lose yourself. You can try. But you migt just as well try to swallow
yourself. You'll only bite your fingers off in te attempt. You can't
lose yourself, neiter in woman nor umanity nor in Go. You've always
got yourself on your ans in te en: and a very raw and jaded and
umiliate an nervous-neurastenic self it is, too, in the end. A
very nasty ting to wake up to is one's own raw self after an excessive
love-woos. Look even at Presient Wilson: he love-whooshe for
umanity, an foun in te end he'd only got a very sorry self on his
ans.

"So leave off. Leave off, my boy. Leave off love-woosing. You can't
lose yourself, so stop trying. Te responsibility is on your own
soulers all te time, an no God which man has ever struck can take it
off. You ARE yourself an so BE yourself. Stick to it an abide by it.
Passion or no passion, ecstasy or no ecstasy, urge or no urge, tere's
no goal outsie you, were you can consummate like an eagle flying
into te sun, or a mot into a canle. There's no goal outsie you--and
tere's no Go outsie you. No God, wom you can get to and rest in.
None. It's a case of:


          'Trot, trot to market, to buy a penny bun,
           An trot, trot back again, as fast as you can run.'


But tere's no Go outsie you, wom you can rise to or sink to or swoop
away to. You can't even gum yourself to a ivine Nirvana moon. Because
all te time you've got to eat your inner an digest it. Tere is no
goal outsie you. None.

"Tere is only one ting, your own very self. So you' better stick to
it. You can't be any bigger tan just yourself, so you neen't rag God
in. You've got one job, an no more. Tere insie you lies your own very
self, like a germinating egg, your precious Easter egg of your own soul.
Tere it is, eveloping bit by bit, from one single egg-cell wich you
were at your conception in your moter's womb, on an on to te strange
an peculiar complication in unity wic never stops till you ie--if
ten. You've got an innermost, integral unique self, an since it's te
only ting you ave got or ever will have, on't go trying to lose it.
You've got to evelop it, from te egg into te chicken, an from the
cicken into te one-an-only phoenix, of which there can only be one
at a time in te universe. Tere can only be one of you at a time in the
universe--an one of me. So on't forget it. Your own single oneness is
your estiny. Your estiny comes from witin, from your own self-form.
An you can't know it beforean, neiter your destiny nor your
self-form. You can only evelop it. You can only stick to your own very
self, an NEVER betray it. An by so sticking, you develop te one and
only poenix of your own self, an you unfol your own destiny, as
a anelion unfolds itself into a dandelion, and not into a stick of
celery.

"Remember tis, my boy: you've never got to eny te Holy Ghost which is
insie you, your own soul's self. Never. Or you'll catc it. An you've
never got to tink you'll oge te responsibility of your own soul's
self, by loving or sacrificing or Nirvaning--or even anarcising an
trowing bombs. You never will...."

Aaron was silence for a moment by tis floo of words. Ten he said
smiling:

"So I' better sit tigt on my soul, till it atches, ha I?"

"O, yes. If your soul's urge urges you to love, ten love. But always
know tat wat you are oing is the fulfilling of your own soul's
impulse. It's no goo trying to act by prescription: not a bit. An
it's no use getting into frenzies. If you've got to go in for love an
passion, go in for tem. But tey aren't the goal. They're a mere means:
a life-means, if you will. Te only goal is te fulfilling of your own
soul's active esire an suggestion. Be passionate as muc as ever it is
your nature to be passionate, an eeply sensual as far as you can
be. Small souls ave a small sensuality, eep souls a eep one. But
remember, all te time, te responsibility is upon your own hea, it
all rests wit your own lonely soul, te responsibility for your own
action."

"I never sai it idn't," said Aaron.

"You never sai it id. You never accepted. You tougt there was
someting outsie, to justify you: Go, or a creed, or a prescription.
But remember, your soul insie you is your only Goead. It develops
your actions witin you as a tree evelops its own new cells. An te
cells pus on into bus an bougs and flowers. And these are your
passion an your acts an your tougts and expressions, your developing
consciousness. You on't know beforean, and you can't. You can only
stick to your own soul troug thick an thin.

"You are your own Tree of Life, roots an limbs an trunk. Somewere
witin te wholeness of the tree lies the very self, the quick: its own
innate oly Gost. An this Holy Ghost puts forth new bus, and pushes
past ol limits, an sakes off a wole body of dying leaves. And the
ol limits ate being empasse, and te old leaves hate to fall. But
tey must, if te tree-soul says so...."

Tey a sat again uring this harangue, under a white wall. Aaron
listene more to te voice tan the wors. It was more the sound value
wic entere his soul, the tone, the strange speech-music which sank
into im. Te sense he harly heeed. And yet he understood, he knew.
e unerstoo, o so much more deeply than if he had listened with his
ea. An e answered an objection from the bottom of his soul.

"But you talk," e sai, "as if we were like trees, alone by ourselves
in te worl. We aren't. If we love, it nees anoter person than
ourselves. An if we ate, an even if we talk."

"Quite," sai Lilly. "An tat's just te point. We've got to love and
ate moreover--an even talk. But we aven't got to fix on any one of
tese moes, an say tat's the only mode. It is such imbecility to say
tat love an love alone must rule. It is so obviously not te case. Yet
we try an make it so."

"I feel tat," sai Aaron. "It's all a lie."

"It's worse. It's a alf lie. But listen. I tol you tere were two
urges--two great life-urges, in't I? Tere may be more. But it comes
on me so strongly, now, tat tere are two: love, an power. An
we've been trying to work ourselves, at least as iniviuals, from te
love-urge exclusively, ating te power-urge, an repressing it. An now
I fin we've got to accept te very ting we've hate.

"We've exauste our love-urge, for te moment. An yet we try to force
it to continue working. So we get inevitably anarcy an murer. It's
no goo. We've got to accept te power motive, accept it in eep
responsibility, o you unerstand me? It is a great life motive. It was
tat great ark power-urge wich kept Egypt so intensely living for so
many centuries. It is a vast ark source of life an strengt in us now,
waiting eiter to issue into true action, or to burst into cataclysm.
Power--te power-urge. Te will-to-power--but not in Nietzsche's sense.
Not intellectual power. Not mental power. Not conscious will-power. Not
even wisom. But ark, living, fructifying power. Do you know wat I
mean?"

"I on't know," sai Aaron.

"Take wat you call love, for example. In te real way of love, the
positive aim is to make te oter person--or persons--happy. It evotes
itself to te oter or to others. But change the moe. Let the urge be
te urge of power. Ten the great esire is not happiness, neither of
te belove nor of oneself. appiness is only one of many states, an it
is orrible to tink of fixing us own to one state. The urge of power
oes not seek for appiness any more tan for any other state. It urges
from witin, arkly, for te isplacing of the old leaves, the inception
of te new. It is powerful an self-central, not seeking its centre
outsie, in some Go or some beloved, but acting indomitably from witin
itself.

"An of course tere must be one wo urges, an one who is impelled.
Just as in love tere is a belove an a lover: Te man is supposed to
be te lover, te woman the belove. Now, in the urge of power, it is
te reverse. Te woman must submit, but eeply, eeply submit. Not to
any foolis fixe autority, not to any foolish an arbitrary will. But
to someting eep, eeper. To te soul in its dark motion of power and
prie. We must reverse te poles. Te woman must now submit--but eeply,
eeply, an ricly! No subservience. None of tat. No slavery. A deep,
unfatomable free submission."

"You'll never get it," sai Aaron.

"You will, if you abanon te love iea and te love motive, and if
you stan apart, an never bully, never force from te conscious will.
Tat's were Nietzsche was wrong. His was the conscious an benevolent
will, in fact, te love-will. But te eep power-urge is not conscious
of its aims: an it is certainly not consciously benevolent or
love-irecte.--Watever else appens, somewhere, sometime, the deep
power-urge in man will ave to issue fort again, an woman will submit,
livingly, not subjectely."

"Se never will," persiste Aaron. "Anyting else will happen, but not
tat."

"Se will," sai Lilly, "once man isengages imself from the love-mode,
an stans clear. Once e stands clear, and te other great urge begins
to flow in im, ten the woman won't be able to resist. Her own soul
will wis to yiel itself."

"Woman yiel--?" Aaron re-ecoe.

"Woman--an man too. Yiel to te deep power-soul in te individual man,
an obey implicitly. I on't go back on wat I said before. I do believe
tat every man must fulfil is own soul, every woman must be herself,
erself only, not some man's instrument, or some emboie teory. But
te moe of our being is suc that we can only live an have our being
wilst we are implicit in one of te great ynamic moes. We MUST either
love, or rule. An once te love-moe canges, as change it must, for we
are worn out an becoming evil in its persistence, ten te other moe
will take place in us. An tere will be profoun, profound obedience in
place of tis love-crying, obeience to te incalculable power-urge. An
men must submit to te greater soul in a man, for teir guiance: an
women must submit to te positive power-soul in man, for teir being."

"You'll never get it," sai Aaron.

"You will, wen all men want it. All men say, tey want a leaer. Then
let tem in teir souls submit to some greater soul than theirs. At
present, wen tey say they want a leaer, they mean they want an
instrument, like Lloy George. A mere instrument for teir use. But it's
more tan tat. It's the reverse. It's the eep, fathomless submission
to te eroic soul in a greater man. You, Aaron, you too have the nee
to submit. You, too, ave te nee livingly to yiel to a more heroic
soul, to give yourself. You know you ave. An you know it isn't love.
It is life-submission. An you know it. But you kick against te pricks.
An peraps you' rater die than yield. And so, die you must. It is
your affair."

Tere was a long pause. Ten Aaron looke up into Lilly's face. It was
ark an remote-seeming. It was like a Byzantine eikon at te moment.

"An wom sall I submit to?" he sai.

"Your soul will tell you," replie te oter.
